The Lightshy Crow
Book One of the Scarab Cycle
John R Raymond
/Did gods say let there be darkness?/
Shall I close my eyes and remember? Remember standing on the balcony outside my room? And see how my vision came to pass! How my uncle ignored my warnings and brought down his fire and ice—and sowed the gods’ own wrath. Shall I close my eyes and see the flames? How I see them rage. And how I see my city crumble and fall into the sea? Oh Deleset? How long must I weep your broken towers, now lost to the eastern sea?
—From The Fall of Delaset by Farseer Illidus Terrikon, the last great son of the House of the Fey Serpent, circa 15 AC.

Chapter 1: Let There Be Light
	Only stories, told in song, live forever.
		—From The Living Book of the Dead

The sun had long since set in the hours since leaving the Clivents’ farmstead. Shadows now hung in the air above the ledge separating Gaz from his bed. Ghosts were said to inhabit such cracks in the Worldegg. Now in the dark, Gaz was too afraid to disbelieve. “Luckily, I been smart,” he muttered to himself. He had used the last of his coin to buy a whiff of tarsk to keep him company on the way home. Uncorking the bottle cradled in his left arm, he took a quick sip. “Hah!” he said as he slammed the cork back down the throat of the jug. Emboldened, he took a quick step forward.
A shadow loomed up in front of him, and he clutched the jug to his chest with both arms. “Ah!”
“I see you, Gaz,” the shadow said.
Gaz stepped backwards squeezing the bottle. “Wh-who is it?”
The creature resolved into Rennly Rickot. “Don’t you recognize me?”
“Rennly?” Gaz said. The moon had blinded him a second. “You’re back? And you are like… You. The moon really sells it… I guess!” Gaz said, stepping forward, pushing his jug of tarsk towards Rennly’s dark outline.
“I’m not here for visiting either,” Rennly said.
He seemed stiffer now, yet the same Rennly as ever. “I heard you were a censor now,” Gaz said. He struggled not to slur his words. “And I know I have to do better when there are others, but look at you--...all in black.” Gaz wanted to be proud but Rennly wasn’t really  his friend, was he? “Why are you here?” Gaz added. “It’s the middle of nowhere.”
“To tidy up some loose ends,” Rennly said as he finally relaxed his stance. 
A spotted Orioles hand Gaz knew too well reached for the jug he held out, but Gaz snatched it back. “Let me--...get the cork.” He popped the top and took another quick slug himself. “Look at me, drinking with a censor.” He handed the jug to Rennly. “Who would have thought it?”
Rennly sniffed the lip of the jug. 
“What loose ends?” Gaz asked, remembering Rennly wasn’t here to steal his tarsk.
“Remember that time about Tomrin?” Rennly said slowly.
Gaz knew the time. “You still want to kill him?” Gaz said, after a moment’s hesitation.
Rennly’s green eyes glinted like dark flint. “I didn’t say that.”
“Whatever you say,” Gaz said, using his small voice. “I never told him. I never said anything, to no one.”
“The plan is to destroy him.”
“I forgot.” Gaz said. Even on tarks he kept everyone’s secret because he couldn’t remember to blab them. Tomrin’s own secret he’d learned at the end of last year’s fair. It was a real doozy. “But…” He let his question trail off. How could Rennly know when not even Tomrin knew nothing of his own knowing? But then Gaz saw it in Rennly’s eyes. He saw why he’d come. You’re the loose end, a deadly serious little voice whispered in his head. Then like a dream, Rennly reached down and wrapped his spotted Orioles fingers around the rod at his waist and unhooked his nightstick. “Just as I destroy you now,” he said as he raised the weapon over his head.
Gaz backed away. “I—” A stray rock caught a heel and he tried to catch himself on the ledge but he careened over the side. “Ahhh!”
The night went black. 
Coiled on a Branch [?]
Rennly frowned at the jug in his hand.
“Why? Because I never really liked you, Gaz. And I never liked the fact that you have our father’s eyes.” He tossed the jug over the side, splattering tarks on the rocks below. “There can be no doubt. I should be the Ocrin great son. The family fortunes should go to me, not to useless slaves. Now, that would be a crime,” he added to himself as he made the sign of the tree. “One against the gods and against men.”
The night’s work done, Censor Rickot headed back home to Menad.
Chapter 2: Infernal Hooks
	I too wish to go to heaven.
		—From The Book of Light 

The phoenix has had a thousand names spoken in a million tongues across the protracted ages. Letting her core halt but a moment, she cut the current age short as she verged on nova. Her surface cracked and streamers of pure light escaped from deep within. Then around the molten rents, fiery loops tore free and flared out. But her greatest loops erupted and sped off in all directions.
The light, like wide earth below, was green in apprehension.
Ribbons of it whipped past Serathus, Gearthrum’s moon and sliced into the high atmosphere above the Empire of Corundum. Unlike the other starlight from the sun, this light was subtle, and ray-upon-ray struck the earth unnoticed.
A small glinting, however, pierced the airy dome above Menad, a small backwater town of the empire. The light quavered gently down through the hot air to an unassuming, two-story home.
Entering a pane of glass, the light bent virtually undetected then bent again and exited the pane on the other side before entering a small rented room—a repurposed attic bedroom. In less time than a bug’s blink, the light stabbed through the air and struck Tomrin Ocrin on the chest. A ring of metallic scales growing over his heart lit up. Instead of glinting or fracturing like a bit of glass, the scales gleamed. For a second, Tomrin froze, deathly still, his shirt held lank in his hand. His breath caught in his mouth. His mind emptied in shock, and refilled in horror. Anyone looking up from the street below could easily see him glowing unnaturally.
He slapped the shirt over his heart and covered the scales on his chest. Turning his head he glanced out the window. Menad darkened and brightened both as the whole of the city came into focus in an instant. “Ugh!” Every muscle in him hummed. His heart thudded in his ears as they strained to hear every sound. “Just what you need: more feathered trouble,” he breathed.
Menad [?]
Tomrin pulled his shirt over his head as the tall white temple at the center of town accused him of living an untruth.
The scales were a problem he still needed to solve, but from his covert studies Tomrin learned they were once called the Mark of the Chosen. Now, the gift was a curse. Any man so marked was to turn himself over to the Censors or to the Daughters of Heaven’s Glory for Purification. As far as Tomrin could make out, Purification was painful, likely fatal. Dying was a fate he sorely wished to avoid with his current disobedience. And despite what The Book of Light said about the subordinate races, he wasn’t that corrupt to deserve death yet. His shirt safely on, he bit a nail as he glanced back out the window.
Nothing, then suddenly, a solitary face turned and looked up at him from the street below. Tomrin recognized the greedy green eyes and face. They belonged to Gaz, his old childhood friend. Stunted ears poked through the greasy mat of hair on his head. Wearing his rough leather jerkin, Gaz looked every bit the Orish slave he was. He squinted up at Tomrin. Work had cut lines in his old friend’s sun baked face. The boy shook his head and abruptly turned and disappeared from view without a second glance. 
Tomrin ripped his gaze from the window and his breathing came deep and rapid. How much had Gaz seen? Had he glanced up before? Had he witnessed the green glow, and seen the scales? When Tomrin dared another glance out the window, Gaz was gone and safe old Menad had returned.
Yet stark green eyes stared back from the window.
A Peregrine [?]
He thought to rush outside and track down Gaz as he quickly tucked in his shirt. Then could ask, obliquely of course, what his old friend knew—and what he’d seen. “So what are you doing in Menad, Gaz?” Tomrin said to himself as if asking Gaz. What Gaz might say to a Censor, Tomrin didn’t want to know. Luckily no one believed a slave. A green glow? There was not much a Censor could make from that, especially if there was no gold in it for them. With any luck, that was.
Rubbing his clammy hands on his shirt, he took a moment to rub the wound on his chest where his scales regrew around an angry button of flesh. Burning the Mark off was better—less painful than cutting—but the burn still itched to the seventh heaven while healing. And yet again, he had waited too long to burn the scales off. Work! He needed to get to work. 
He could solve his problems with gold. Even holy creatures could be bribed for a price, though Tomrin suspected the Daughters of Heaven’s Glory might take umbrage at the thought they too could be so easily purchased.
Tomrin Ocrin [?]
Floorboards squeaked outside his room; there was movement in the hallway.
Tomrin spun around. Just Mistress Carl, he told himself as he tried to calm his jitters. He hadn’t heard her come up the steps, but now his landlady’s heavy footsteps tracked past his room and down the hallway before dying out at the stairs. Those, the heavyset woman took cautiously. 
Cinching his belt, Tomrin wondered if he was getting fat. You are clearly getting sloppy, a nasty voice in his head chided. He swore at the voice as he shook his head at his shirt. Feathers. Going forward I’m going to keep you on, he promised. Even while sleeping, in case I wake groggy, and stupid again.
He grimaced at his own savagery and bloused his shirt.
Despite his efforts, the shirt remained wrinkled. The tan color also did not hide all the grime of work. His last pair of pants, likewise. Running his hand through his hair he pushed his dark, oily locks back. That would have to do. “You look good enough,” he told himself, but he felt like himself, a man going to a funeral, his own.
Ben [?]
Outside his room, the hallway smelled of maple bars and pastries frying; Mistress Carl cooked for him, for herself, and for her ladies meetings.
For those meetings she cooked each day and stacked the pantry full—more than full. Today was maple bars. The idea made Tomrin want to laugh. Why am I getting pudgy again? No mystery there. A dull hunger gnawed his belly, but the thought of Gaz speaking with a Censor killed his appetite.
If Gaz spoke to a Daughter of Heaven’s Glory this was the first place they’d search. 
Tracking down the spiral steps with a sinking heart, Tomrin pondered the green light.
Ben, Mistress Carl’s cat, sat on the banister on the landing. He turned and Tomrin ran a hand through his light gray belly fur. He purred and tried to pull Tomrin’s hand back down when he tried to pull away. “Sorry, little guy,” Tomrin told him. “I can’t pick you up. Gotta get to work.” [?]
Tomrin stepped off the landing. If he’d headed out the door quickly, he might make it before Mistress Carl poked her head out the kitchen way, then he could leave before she realized he was gone.
It was a good and solid plan.
Chapter 3: A Disgrace to His Race
	“... and is free to disregard any master who does not sit upon the Crystal Throne itself.”
		--From the Slave Tax, Lot 25

“Look at you,” Mistress Carl said, stepping out of the kitchen, forcing Tomrin to stop. “Late for work again. I can’t see how you pay the rent. Not this way.”
“I had trouble sleeping,” Tomrin said. He paid for the room, yet his landlady Mistress Carl acted like a greedy aunt who needed his every penny. Why? To give to needy children? No. Or to the poor?  No, to give to the Daughters of Heaven’s Glory--like they needed more. And the more he let on, the more she taxed him.
“Oh? You had trouble sleeping?” she said, raising her eyebrows at him. “You woke me with your late comings and goings last night again.”
“I’m sorry, I-it’s just that last night—” Tomrin had a lie prepared, but she was faster than him this morning.
“And you woke me again in the middle with your screaming.” She wiped her fleshy Rubish hands on her apron. “Who says that kind of stuff anyway?” 
“Screaming?” Tomrin said.
“And banging. I can’t have that. I can’t have any of that, Master Tomrin. If the ladies in my ladies meeting heard half the stuff… No, I’m afraid I’m sorry, Master Tomrin. I’ve made a decision. You can only stay week to week from now on.”
“Week to week?” Tomrin said. “That is nearly twice as much money.”
“Move out if you have to—and the gods help whoever rents to you, carrying on late into the night like you do.” 
“I don’t have that kind of money.” The lie came easy.
“Money isn’t the issue. It sounded like you were trying to raise the dead.”
The Book of Light [?]
Tomrin glanced from Mistress Carl to the painting of Seasargus on the wall above her living room couch. 
Money is always an issue with women like you, Tomrin thought, but a dream had brought him awake the night before. He reached up to scratch his chest, but caught himself. “I was trying to sleep,” he said, his eyes still on the painting. The high god’s orange eyes stared down disapprovingly.
“You were summoning demons. The stuff you were yelling...”
“Hmm,” Tomrin mused, not wanting to remember. 
He studied The Book of Light on the coffee table. The red bookmark lay across the open page, the book’s midpoint, a different place than the night before. Mistress Carl had also moved the green tassel to the beginning, and the blue to near the end. Tomrin knew well the two passages the ribbons likely marked. One referenced men marked by demons, the the treatment of slave men you did not own. And in the middle, the book spoke of a manbeast whom the Peregrine from the far south called the Lightshy Crow. 
Across a painted rendition of a man twisted with the animals, the red tassel firmly lay.
A Dream [?]
Tomrin blinked. 
The dream from the night before replayed itself in his mind. 
The pain was one thing, but watching his body blacken as he was chopped to bits was quite another. The pieces of him then pulled away and the charcoaled shards arranged themselves seemingly at random under a force, chinking together. Cheeng, cheeng, cheeng. The shards stacked, growing into the likeness of a tall bird. The great crow burned on a crooked crossbar held up by a wooden pole alight. Cheeng. 
Then everything was silent. The wooden pole, though burning, held the crow deathly still. 
Haloed by a burning circle of wood, the burning crossbar pinioned open the glass crow’s wings. Its beak hung open and its lifeless head stood stiff, looking up and off and to the right—to the open sky, all white. The bird wasn’t real and it wasn’t alive, and the field it guarded stretched out in harrowed rows of black dirt, all barren to the pale horizon.
Suddenly, the crow’s eyes opened and glowed red. Then turning its head, it shouted. “I am the Lightshy Crow!” That was when Tomrin came awake screaming, his hands numb and his body covered in sweat. He was unsure then whether he’d screamed those words aloud. Now, under Mistress Carl’s disapproving gaze, he surmised he may well have. He sucked in a breath and let it out, saying not a word.
Mistress Carl scowled at him. “You have until next week for this week’s and next’s. Or I want you gone.” 
Tomrin stepped around her and, reaching the door, opened it. “I’m busy. I told you that when I moved in.” He stepped out into the morning sun. 
“You wouldn’t be in such a hurry if you went to bed at a decent hour. That’s half the reason I can’t have you here anymore.” Mistress Carl said as he closed the door. Her muffled voice came through its wooden slats. “I swear the demons have their hooks in you, boy.” 
Tomrin angrily scanned the street. I’m not the feathered Lightshy Crow! It was just a nightmare.  He certainly didn’t want to move, just because of it. 
The idea of having to find a new place put a pressure in his skull.
In the Light Air [?]
The Gods Have Plenty of Gold, an overnight tract left on the porch read.
Tomrin eye’s flittered over it as he headed down the steps. Out in the street, he found himself alternately scanning the way ahead as he trudged along. Mistress Carl would burn the tract left on her porch, but then again maybe she was not all that she seemed. Tomrin tried to laugh at the idea of Mistress Carl being a Sister of the Dark. 
As he walked, an eerie feeling grew in the back of his mind as it buzzed in the fore.
Last night was the first time in years he had raised the covers over his head to ward off the dark. 
Now, he thrust down memories of his life in Woolad and tried to quash a feeling of being watched. Just my imagination, he told himself. The Lightshy Crow was a fairytale cooked up by his mother’s race. Only fools worried the Lightshy Crow walked among them. 
Because if he did, there was no hope left.
A Golden Feather [?]
An Orioles man in black silks and a golden feather badge on his chest, stepped into the street ahead.
The man was like another boy Tomrin had grown up with in Woolad and looked just like Rennly Rickot, his jaw flat and concocted of granite. Orioles each had their own skin grain which never changed. But Rennly a censor? That made no sense. Rennly couldn’t be a censor but his grain had not changed. 
Tomrin slipped off the road into the alleyway just before the corner of Chestnut Avenue and the Eastern Highway. Rennly clenched his jaw tight and hurried up Chestnut, then turned down at the corner. Tomrin tried to act natural as he waited, resisting the urge to touch the itching flesh on his chest. Couldn’t have been Rennly. 
“Jarvis weak on Zircos,” a nearby Rubish man said to a woman on the street.
“No,” the woman said, bumping into Tomrin as she stepped into the alleyway herself and walking on. The man followed into the alley with a stack of papers in his hands. “Jarvis weak on Zircos,” the man said, but now pushing his pamphlet on Tomrin. 
“No. No, thanks,” Tomrin said, trying to smile away a grimace. The tract was standard warmongering, and he had no time for it, having read it once before, and disagreeing with every point.
“The Orioles are sneaking across our borders,” the man said, still pushing his tract. “The emperor needs to do something.” 
“Fine, I’ll take one,” Tomrin said to shut the man up.
Holding it up, he pretended to read it. The man walked off. 
Tomrin waited several moments then scratched his chest with the tract, dropped it thoroughly unread in the corner bin. 
Out onto the street, as if this was his normal, everyday path to work, he glanced in the direction of the glassworks in the valley on Arcade Avenue, trying to see if Rennly walked that way, but at this angle only Goodly Glasswork’s smokestacks leaking their wavy heat into the dusty air were visible.
A Feathered Fire [?]
Feathered Rennly! Tomrin thought as he headed down the avenue to Third Street, feeling foolish. 
The Orioles censor hadn’t even seen him, and the man hadn’t come this way but walked the other. 
Rennly’s father had died in a fire in Woolad, the same one that destroyed the Ocrin carpentry shop. Tomrin knew it was a tragic waste now, but he had said things to Rennly at the time, things he now regretted. Feathered feathers! Tomrin swore, trying to force the memories back down. 
Rennly had been too afraid to enter the flaming building to save his own father. The smoke was too much for Tomrin ultimately, and he had left Davit dying unconscious on the floor of the shop’s main room. Tomrin had hated Rennly, and called him a coward, but Rennly was no coward from a fight after that.
Chapter 4: The Scarab
	Yes. There is a word for that, just as there is a word for every tool in every workman’s toolkit, and yes, there is a new one for how he uses that word on every random puzzle he encounters.
		—From The Living Book of the Dead

Outside the glass shop, Non stood staring up at the Seasargus worked in glass in the window, an effigy to the Great Gods of the Sky, cut with Seasargus sparkling, his primordial wings arrayed out, open in esoteric glory somehow almost filling the whole front window as the morning’s sun glinted off the patchwork of red and black and blue. The idea of destroying the window flashed through Tomrin’s mind. But what to replace it with? Only the image of the solitary black bird burning in his mind came to the fore. Blinking away the thought, he stepped off the street and onto the walkway. “What’s going on?” he asked Non. “Shouldn’t you be working?”
The boy stepped over to the door. “A Censor came by looking for you and Robles. They tossed me out.” Non put his hand on the door’s handle, but did not open it. 
Tomrin ignored the churning in his stomach and tried to act casual. “A Censor? Here for me?” He let his eyes peel off Non to the gray glass door. The cuts had been arranged by Master Laz. The worldtree on Serathus above was done double-height and massive for a work of glass.
Non seemed part of the tree’s base. “The Censor left,” he said. “But what I can’t figure out is why he wanted to see you.”
“Why wouldn’t he want to see me?” Tomrin asked. “And shouldn’t you be working?”
“Should,” Non replied, sounding more dour than usual.
“Then why are we standing out here?”
“You think we can make the Scarab work?” Non asked, not answering.
The Scarab was Master Lazoloth’s big idea. When Tomrin joined the glass shop, the compound opera-glasses Master Laz had devised were but bits of glass laid out on a bench. Tomrin recently turned the mess into an almost-working prototype. Now the Scarab, as they called it, was Tomrin’s project more than Lazoloth’s. 
“Of course I can. What kind of question is that?”
“I was thinking about quitting and heading north,” Non replied. 
“To Corundum? I thought you hated Corundum?” Tomrin said. The idea of anyone quitting now though twisted his stomach.
“Not to Corundum. The Adamant,” Non said. “I’ve been thinking. And talking.”
“And talking?”
“And reading.” Non raised a finger. “They pay the free Peregrine from Corundum up there well. We’re novelties. Free slaves." 
“Novelties? Come on, let’s go inside,” Tomrin said. Despite the shop’s current financial troubles, no one could ever want for a better place to work than here. “There will be time enough to go to the Adamant and be curios when we’re filthy rich.”
“That would be fine too,” Non said. “Then you really think you can make the Scarab work?”
“Why are you asking?” Tomrin asked. “And not opening the door? You are always a pest, but today… Really, Non?”
 Finally, the boy turned to the door and opened it wide to the shop. “The Scarab’s all broken,” he said loudly for him, and for the gods, and for all the shop to hear.
“The Scarab is perfect,” Tomrin replied, lying smoothly. 
In a week the shop needed to demonstrate the strange handheld telescope to the Sapphire Front. Master Laz was the master of promises, and it had in the years since joining the shop fell to Tomrin to deliver.
“Perfect?” Non asked skeptically. 
“Don’t worry,” Tomrin said. “And the Censor was probably here just for glass for the temple.”
“Ah! There you are, Tomrin!—but no such luck,” was the first thing out of his friend Robles’s mouth.
Godly Glassworks [?]
The door closed and sealed street sounds out while locking the drowsy hum of torches in. A welcoming scent of pines hung in the air like incense.With its high doors and ceilings, the glass shop was a temple indeed, Tomrin’s. Here, he was a high priest, and he glanced at the old ratty chair belonging to the back blocking his way. Non stepped around the chair, but Tomrin paused next to it. Fifteen feet away, Robles stood, his back to the inner door. Hidden behind him, the furnace minders made the shop’s colored glass, unlike Robles who stood idle with his knife held high in his hand while Tiff and Ken worked their respective flames.
Despite being Orioles, Robles was Tomrin’s oldest friend, and yet his eyes and face were impossible to read. Tomrin didn’t even want to guess what Robles was up to now.
Dancing Bears [?]
Robles whipped his wrist. The knife sailed through the air, spinning. Tomrin started. “Ah!” The point stuck into the chair’s wooden back. Chenh! Tomrin swore. “What the feathers, Robles? You almost hit me!” 
Robles grinned; he was just messing around. “Not even close,” he said. “Nice of you to actually show up to work, though. Now grab my little doggy, Ang, for me,” he added, making grasping motions with his hands.
Tomrin glanced down at the knife. A doghead grinned up at him from the carved bone pommel. Levering the knife point free of the chair, he walked it over to Robles. “I see you are hard at work yourself,” Tomrin said as he slapped the knife into his friend’s open hand. 
“That I am,” Robles said in his most agreeable voice. “Can’t win the knife throw at the fair if I don’t practice.” 
Tomrin shook his head. “You could have waited until I was out of the way.” 
Robles slipped the knife into the leather holster at his side until a grinning white dog peeked free. “I could have, but then you wouldn’t have been able to save me the trip. Plus, sadly there was not much else I could do until you got here.” 
Robles’s words rang ominously. 
“Non said a Censor came for you and me,” Tomrin said into void.
“He didn’t find you?” 
“No.” 
“Well, that’s too bad. I might have given our old friend Rennly bad directions to your place. I know how much you love him.” 
“Rennly? I thought I saw him… He really is a Censor now then?” Tomrin said, shaking his head. 
“That he is.” Robles patted the head of his knife and walked over to his workstation along the back wall, hidden away in the darkest corner. “He insisted. He wanted to deliver the news himself.” 
Tomrin followed his friend. “The news that he is a Censor?” 
“No, not that.” From his cluttered workbench, Robles picked up an envelope. Tomrin had seen the look of it before, a letter from Robles’s aunt living in Woolad. “You know,” Robles said, holding the letter to his chin. “You should join me in the double’s throw. I bet I could win both that way.”
Flaming Bears [?]
“I’m not going to Sheep Shearing Days this year. Just tell me the news.” 
“Oh, you’re going home. Read it. You’ll see why.” Robles held out the envelope to Tomrin. “It’s about Gaz.”
Tomrin stared at the cream-colored envelope. “Gaz? What about him? I just saw him this morning.” 
Robles scowled. “I’m pretty sure you didn’t.” 
Sometimes Tomrin swore his friend was a master of the obtuse. “What do you mean?” 
“Just listen. I’ll read it for you.” Robles opened the envelope and pulled out a small sheet of thin yellow paper and unfolded it. “I saved this one because I knew you would want to read it, to hear what Aunty had to say directly.”
“Your aunt wrote to you about Gaz?” Tomrin asked. The woman who Robles called his aunt wasn’t his real aunt but his adoptive mother. Tomrin thought her a busybody, always sticking her nose into other people’s business. But she knew what was going on in Woolad, and the disparate parts of the world—or claimed to—and she had an opinion on all of them.
“I said listen.” Robles said, gone solemn. “There is some preamble.” 
Tomrin swore under his breath. “Feathers, fine. Read it already.” 
Robles glanced up from the letter. “Calm down. I am.” He cleared his throat. 
The dear gods in heaven, Tomrin thought. His friend loved his own theatrics too much. “I am calm.” 
“Dear Robles,” his friend started. “Your uncle’s second cousin-once-removed, Ebus, you remember him, the roof-man. His mother’s joints are loving this weather, the poor woman.” 
Tomrin groaned.
Robles stopped reading and looked up. “Old Ebus fixed my auntie’s roof.” 
“I’m glad for her...and for him,” Tomrin said. “But what about Gaz?”
“I’m coming to that,” Robles said, continuing to read. “Well, regardless of what the real demons are up to with this weather, your grandfather’s brother’s youngest, Babbs, wrote to me.” Robles had clearly skipped well ahead of the roof-fixing to the doings of distant cousins. To hear the woman go on, Tomrin swore Cordelia thought herself and Robles nobility, related to all the families of note throughout the world. “The slave trouble in Zircos is getting out of hand. Ha! Ha! Ha!” Robles looked up. “She wrote that.” He flipped the letter at Tomrin, only long enough for him to see that a flowing script in blue ink filled the fine yellow paper. 
“Gaz?” Tomrin demanded, losing patience.
“This is it. I’m coming to it,” Robles said. “Slaves and censors are running east, tracking slaves like headless chickens. Freed slaves in the east are getting caught up in it. But I’m afraid your friend Gaz has no more earthly worry of that. The gods took him to the second heaven yesterday. Seems he stumbled off a cliff and fell. His poor mother. She--” 
“No, no… No. I saw Gaz this morning. I swear I did.” Tomrin turned and thumbed the spines of  books on the bookself along the back wall. “I don’t believe this. Gaz can’t be dead.” He turned around and studied Robles. His friend didn’t look to be joking. 
“I don’t think my aunt would lie about it. You are not saying that, are you?” Robles asked.
“No. Of course not. It’s just… I swear, he walked down my street.” 
“But, did you talk to him?” 
“No. But I did see him through the dormir.” 
“Maybe it was someone else?” offered Non, turning from his own work area on the middle bench. The boy lost focus easily and when Tomrin and Robles talked he always needed to add his two pennies worth.
His butting in now irked. “You don’t know what I saw, Non. Maybe it wasn’t Gaz. But, feathers, it looked like him!”
“Settle down, settle down, Tomrin,” Robles said. “You hardly say a word to him.” 
“I know,” Tomrin said, calming himself. He and Gaz had been close once, but not anymore. “Once, we were better friends; you were the outsider.” 
“Things change. At least you haven’t gone completely daft. Not yet,” Robles said. “I almost believe you saw Rennly—as you say.” 
“Unless you believe in ghosts,” Non added. 
Tomrin ran his hand through his hair and sighed. “I feel like the world has gone crazy, but I am not Marvin. I don’t believe in ghosts.” 
“You see now why we have to go home tomorrow?” Robles said as he eyed Tormin suspiciously. “Gaz’s service is in the morning, then his spirit will be at rest.” 
“I’m not saying I saw a ghost. I’m sure that stuff only happens in stories.” 
“From the sound of it, your aunt is right; Gaz is the lucky one,” Non said as Robles folded the letter and placed the sheet back in it’s yellow envelope. “It’s those poor devils with the Mark I worry about, and with Censors running amok we Peregrine are going to be harassed day and night. Take off your shirt!” Non added in a commanding voice as if he were a Censor himself. He proceeded to take off his shirt, pulling it off over his head without unbuttoning first. His ribs stood out. Obvious from a glance—no scales grew on his chest. 
“Non, put…put your shirt back on,” Tomrin told him. 
“Non could be right,” Robles said as he opened the valves to his torch. 
“It makes sense,” Non said as pulled his shirt back over his head.
“No, it doesn’t,” Tomrin said. 
“Don’t you see it? This is how a new census can start,” Non insisted. 
“Because of a slave uprising in Zircos?” Tomrin said. “That’s a stretch.” 
“It’s always the slaves first,” Non replied. “I’m telling you. Same thing happened in what? Four-oh-one? In the Adamant under Colagon.” 
“You’ve been reading too many tracts, Non,” Tomrin said. “He was called Colagon the Mad...and how many years ago was that? That was like two hundred and um...” 
Non interrupted Tomrin’s mental math. “He was only called Colagon the Mad because he thought to disregard the Serath.”
Robles opened the gas valve to his torch.
“No. It was because...” Tomrin let his words trail off and stared at Robles’s hissing torch.
It was unlit but still burning money.
A Trick of Light and Fire [?]
Robles struck a lighter to the hiss of his torch. Fawt! A dark red flame belched from the nozzle. 
“Don’t think they believed in the Lightshy Crow up there,” he said as he opened the air. The flame warmed to a light blue at its base. The spiral of black smoke disappeared.
“He was a fool trying to use the demon-poisoned gift,” Tomrin said. The wound on his chest itched. The harder he tried not to scratch it the worse it seemed.
“The demons are locked away on Enfurnus,” Non said. “They can’t reach us here. I’m telling you. Don’t you find it strange how everyone blames them?” 
“Don’t tell us again how you think it’s our spooky gods,” Robles said. He held the envelope with his aunt’s letter near the torch head. “You might… get yourself caught by a Censor and… disappear yourself.” The envelope and paper suddenly disappeared in a poof. “Like magic.” 
“Like Censor Magic,” Non said, smiling at the idea of Censors having magic even if it was the Daughters of Heaven’s Glory who were the spelldancers, not Censors. “Maybe we should add a hidden compartment on the Scarab?” 
“A Censor Magic compartment, where we make things disappear,” Robles said. 
“No,” Tomrin said. “That is not happening. The Scarab has to be perfect.” 
Robles squinted over at Tomrin’s workbench. “Your Scarab looks like it needs a little Censor Magic to me,” he said, turning his head to make a sidelong face at the compound telescope.
Tomrin had only spared a glance at his own workbench when he’d entered. Containers filled with bits of glass and tools cluttered the space. Together with the Scarab, they covered most of the tabletop. The Scarab sat on six legs like a bug and its compound lenses formed its insect-like eyes. “What?” Tomrin demanded as he stared. 
“See it?” Robles said.
“See what?” Tomrin said loudly, growing irritated. Finally he saw what his friend ment. One of the Scarab’s armatures had been bent, then rebent like someone had thought to mess with it, and then had tried—and failed—to make good their mistake. The mirrors and lenses were now misaligned. “Feathers! You broke it!” 
“I didn’t break it,” Robles said. “I didn’t even touch it. I mean… I mean I touched it, but it wasn’t me.”
Tomrin bent over the Scarab and frobbed its fine-control dial.“You tried to focus it?” The fine control had never worked properly.
“No. Does work any better than you left it last night?” 
“Worse.” 
“Great,” Non said.
“Who the feathers broke it?” Tomrin demanded. “I had it mostly working! The Scarab needs to be perfect!”
Chapter 5: Into the Furnace
	And remember not to say or to show too much.
		—On spelldancing, from The Living Book of the Dead

“Woah! Woah! Woah! You’re losing it, man,” Robles said.
“Mostly working isn’t as good as actually working,” Non said morosely. 
Tomrin ignored him. “Of course I’m losing it. We demonstrate the Scarab in less than a week.” 
“And mostly working isn’t make us rich,” Non added. 
Tomrin sat and stared at the mess. 
His plans for the day evaporated. His housing situation would have to wait. New plans formed in his head as the Scarab’s gray eyes stared up at him blankly. Robles and Non continued to chat about hidden compartments—their censor magic. Tomrin idly told them they could add it to the leather carry case if they wanted a secret compartment so feathered badly. “The Scarab can’t have anything wrong with it. It has to be perfect.”
“Actually,” Robles said. “I already added a second pouch inside the carry case—for the extra lenses. It makes the case even better than the Scarab as far as I can tell. I’d say the poor little guy could use a little censor magic.” 
“Really could,” echoed Non. 
Tomrin rubbed his forehead and closed his eyes. “Please… I’m trying to think.” 
Robles barked a laugh. “Just saying, Censor Magic isn’t a flaw.” 
“Getting serious,” Non said. “What are we going to do about Fredick?” 
“Yes, Tomrin, what are we going to do?” Robles added, needling him. 
“What do you mean me?”
“You are a founder, now. That was what we were celebrating last night. You’re making all that extra money.”
Tomrin scoffed and wiped a cold spot of sweat on the back of his left right. “My promotion has not brought more money. As far as I can tell, it was in lieu of a raise Master Las promised me last month. Fredick is Master Laz’s problem to deal with,” Tomrin said. “He’s been here as long as anyone. If Master Lazoloth wants to stay loyal to Fredick that is his choice and I respect that.”
“A fact he is rubbing in Master Laz’s nose right now,” Non said. “That’s my bet.”
“Maybe,” Robles said. “But there is no bigger suck-up. He’s probably telling Big Laz just how great he is and how he needs to be getting more money, not less.” 
“Yes,” Robles agreed. His knife appeared in his hand. “Sending Fredick back to Zircos would be like sending them quisling. Seems it would hardly be fair play.” With a flip of his wrist the knife flew over the Scarab and workbenches and sank point first into the chair. Thunch. “But it would be a nice trick,” he added with a grin. 
“Careful,” Tomrin said. “That was close. You almost hit the Scarab.” 
Robles stepped close but didn’t push past. “Yes, I might break it. Were it not tempered to the heat of the sun.” 
“And you are too close to me. I can see your little spots.”
“I know,” Robles said. “And I can smell the ones on your shirt.” Robles moved past and walked down the aisle. He turned and added, “I thought your landlady does your laundry for you?” 
“She does.”
“Doesn’t look like it.” 
“Feathers! Could you just get your knife and get back to work?” 
“I could,” Robles said. “As could you. Looks to me like your precious Scarab needs you.” 
Down to Work [?]
His friend extracted the knife from the chair as Tomrin glanced at the glass Seasargus hovering in the window. The red-colored glass at the tips of his wings made it look like his feathers were dipped in burning ash. “I guess I do have to go home.” 
Robles stepped around him, knife in hand.
“You can watch me win the knife throw.” 
“I think I’d rather miss it.” 
Robles brandished his dagger in his face. “Your own royal appointments, my goodman, are looking less and less likely every day.”
Tormin picked up his flint striker. “Good!” he said and opened the gasflow to his torch and struck the flint. With a small explosion, his torch roared to angry life and began to burn up the shop’s money, adding an trickle of waste to the evergrowing hemorrhage of gold. 
It couldn’t be helped. The Scarab could not be broken, not when it could have no flaws. Finding a new place to live, or worrying about Rennly’s golden feathered badge, or even Gaz being dead--all would all have to wait.
Without thinking, Tomrin scratched the itch on his chest.
New Lenses [?]
Tomrin finished tearing down the Scarab and handed Non one of the mirrors  “It needs resurfaced.” 
One day someone would find a way to harden the non-silver coatings, but for now they had to handle them with care. The silver smudged at the slightest touch—the reason he had disallowed Fredick from touching the Scarab in the first place. 
“Fredick’s fat fingers?” Non asked. 
“Not mine,” Tomrin said. He turned to Robles. “I need a new batch of glass.” 
“Wouldn’t it be easier to fire Fredick?” Non asked. 
“Just fix that one,” Tomrin said impatiently. “Robles, let’s go make that glass.” 
“Just waiting on you,” Robles said. 
His friend followed him through the back door to the backshop to where workers minded the furnaces. Most of the different glasses were colored, but lenses required clear glass. Each glass, colored or not, was more than just quartz and soda. Besides the bins holding different grades of chert, more bins held a variety of sands and powders. 
Tomrin ran the scales. “A penny of White Death,” he said.
Robles ran the scoop. “White Death?” he asked, sounding surprised.
The white powder softened glass, making it bad for production lenses. “I want to rough a new Scarab out this morning.” 
“The morning is almost gone,” Robles said, scooping a small teaspoon of the white powder from the bin. He tipped it onto square weighing paper on the scale. “Too little?” he asked when the pennyweight didn’t rise. 
“Yes, a little more,” Tomrin said.
Robles made a face which tightened the skin on his neck in lines. “You’re the boss,” he said and heaped another scoop on the scale. The scale tipped. “Too much?” 
“No, mix it and heat it,” Tomrin said. “I need it quick.” 
“So quick that I do this?” Robles said as he pretended to slip as he dumped the powder from the weighing paper into a crucible. 
“Just melt it,” Tomrin said. Playing with any of the powders wasn’t funny. He swore sometimes his friend was mildly touched. “I hear the first thing people lose when poisoned by it is their balance.” 
Robles grunted, almost growling as he picked up the crucible. 
Tomrin left him frowning into it, and made his own way back to the front.
Dirty Work [?]
“Done,” Non said. 
Tomrin looked up from his own work and wiped at the bead of sweat on his nose that had been there for some time as he crafted a new graphite mold. 
Non held the mirrored glass piece for him. 
“I don’t need it now,” Tomrin said. “Let it dry more.” He could smell the acid used to eat the silver. “Or do a second coat.” 
Non groaned and turned back to the small tubs with washes he had out on his own workbench. “This stuff is dirty.” 
“I don’t want to hear it,” Tomrin said. “My hands are going to be stained as well.” 
The Pour [?]
When Robles walked in from the back, Tomrin was finishing the mold. “You are a genius, Tomrin,” Robles said over his shoulder. “A perfect cylindrical lens should bend light not at all!” 
“It will. You just don’t know what you are looking at. Is the glass  melted?” 
“I ran the bellows like a dog.” 
“Good.” Tomrin picked up a crown glass lens with his left hand and stood with his new mold in his other hand. With them and Non and Robles in tow, he headed to the back. 
In order to melt the new glass mix, Robles had chosen the furnace near the shop’s door opening into the back alley. When he flipped up the metal plate covering the shop’s smallest smelter, a rich glow of orange and a blast of heat burst out. Tomrin set the mold on the nearby metal workbench and picked up a pair of glass sheers. The study lens he handed to Non to hold. 
“You trust me to do the pour?” Robles asked as if there was any question who had the stabler hands. If there was one thing Robles had, it was steady hands. He had mastered pouring glass his first day. 
Tomrin pointed with the shears to the lens Non held. “Yes, you pour. I need only so much glass so as I can press the lens in.” 
“Ahh,” Robles said and picked up a large metal ladle from the table and closed his eyes and opened them quickly, then thrust the big spoon deep in the orange glow coming from the furnace-throat. He shook the handle with a sudden jarring motion before extracting the half sphere, just a quarter full of molten glass. The glass glowed a clear orange. Swinging around, he began pouring the hot, glowing fluid into the graphite mold. As he poured he raised the spoon and a fine filament formed between ladle and mold. 
Tomrin spread the glass shears and pushed the blades forward ready to snip the filament.
“Lower, lower—lower!” Robles said in a panicked voice. Tomrin eased the shears lower as Robles raised the spoon an inch, maybe an inch and a half. “N--n--now!” 
Tomrin snipped the glass. Chish. The snipped strand sunk into the pool of glass cooling in the mold. Tossing the shears aside he snatched the fat, round crownglass lens from Non’s hands. He held it above the warm glass in the mold, waiting for the glass to gel. He waited a second. Two. A second more. Slowly then he pushed the lens into the mold and down against the hot glass until it was snug. 
He peeled the warming lens off the cooling one. “Uhuh,” he said, letting out his breath. The two glasses, the cold and the hot, had not fused. “It worked.”
Robles ladled the remaining glass into the table.
Tomrin put on a glove and carefully dumped his new lens from the mold into his gloved hand. “We need to make another,” he said. 
“This lens is weird,” Non said, picking it up from Tomrin’s hand. “You think this is something?” 
“Just put it in the kiln,” Tomrin told him. “And get ready for the next. I want to use all the rest.”
More Than Gems [?]
“None are going to be perfectly perfect,” Robles said. 
“That doesn’t matter, yet,” Tomrin said. “We just need one that is good enough for now. I want the rest tempering.”
“Let’s get this done then,” Robles replied, messing up his voice as he did when hungry. “So we can go eat!”
“This time, Non, you press the lens in,” Tormin said. Before long, five new lenses sat cooling in the kiln. “That should be good.”
Robles eyes were wide and he wore a serious look on his sweaty face. “Food! Now!” 
Tomrin wiped sweat from his own forehead. “Yes. Fine.” He was hungry too, and his stomach grumbled from the lack of breakfast. “We have to let them cool anyway.”
I need my pack,” Robles said, and they headed up front instead of slipping out the back door. His friend kept the silliest things in his backpack, never going anywhere without it. Why he needed a dog harness when he didn’t own a dog, he’d never say. 
Tiff must have caught Tomrin staring at Fredick’s workstation. “He went out with his friend.  That Censor from this morning.” She made a face.
Tomrin just grunted though he knew exactly what she meant.
“He said he would be back,” Tiff added. 
“Too bad,” Non muttered under his breath. 
“Let’s go-o-o-o!” Robles said, as he slung his backpack over his shoulder. “You know we’d asked you and Ken to come, but I know you like to go home for lunch.” 
“Thank you, Robles,” she said. 
Ken just nodded. 
“Food!” Robles mooed again, then herded Tomrin and Non for the door.
Chapter 6: The Winged Helm Inn on Third
	And yet this crow shall fear the light, as men are afraid of the dark.
		—Translated from The Dark Verses

In the rafters, pigeons cooed as they spattered the helm on the sign with their dirty business. A porter who guarded the Helm’s open door recognised Tomrin, Non, and Robles right away. At least the man knew Robles well. He had worked the door at the Inn himself for a few months before joining the glass shop. In truth everyone in Menad seemed to know Robles, a fact no longer surprising to Tomrin. 
The porter flourished his hands and bowed to Robles. “The house, Master Robles, is always open to you—and yours.” 
“Why thank you, Felding. Thank you,” Robles said, bowing with a flourish of his own. “Keeping cool, I see?” 
“As always,” Felding replied. “And you, as always, a gentleman.” 
Again, Robles flourished his hand. “And ever at your service.” A penny between his fingers fell into Felding’s open palm.
“Why, thank you, Master Robles—the same.” 
Inside, clear windows in the roof filled the floor with yellow bars. “A booth,” Tomrin said to a server as where they wished to sit. The Rubish woman led them to a corner of the common room near the intersection outside. 
A singer, an Orioles girl his age, began a song on the small dais in the center of the room. Tomrin scooted in and sat with his back to the wall so he could see her. She was easily the most interesting thing here. Other than her it was just the regular clients and the various helms and mugs on the shelves. The whole room was coated in them; mugs, helms, mugs shaped like helms, even a helm shaped like a mug. 
The singer on the dias, however, treated his ears and eyes well. Unlike Tiff who was lovely in a soft Rubish way, the girl who sang, her voice high and pure, was beautiful in her own proud Orioles one. Her dark hair caught the light of the noon sun streaming in. When her song finished, Tomrin felt himself breathing again as he clapped with the other patrons. 
With a curtsy and smile, the girl stepped off the dias. 
Gambling Bears [?]
Robles turned back to the table. “I saw her first.”
“Whatever,” Tomrin said. 
“Whatever? She is better looking than a silver penny, I’d say.”
Tormin grunted. With the Leviathan’s Mark on his chest, he dared not say more. This was not a conversation he wanted, not with Robles, not with anyone. Maybe with a Serath feather or a thousand golden ducks in the bank he could find love, but until glass made him filthy rich, love was beyond him. “Women are too expensive.”
“You don’t have to buy her,” Robles said with an evil grin. “Maybe just rent her?”
Non laughed and Tomrin grinned wryly himself. “She doesn’t look like the type. And neither am I,” he added.
A new singer stepped onto the small stage and started singing a simple song they had all heard many times before. The man’s voice rolled from note to note, from word to word, but starting slowly. Soon another voice from the hall chimed in and Tomrin was sure he had heard the voice before. Rennly?
He sat near the dais adding his voice to the chorus as the singer sang about a family of sleeping mice headed for doom as they floated a stream.
Rennly’s dark silk suit glowed from the sun shining down the skylight above. His golden feather badge glinted, keeping rhythm with his breathing. He looked as if he were truly blessed by the gods. 
Robles added his voice to the mix. The three of them—the singer, Rennly and Robles—sung a three-part canon. Robles dropped his voice to a rich bass while Rennly sang tenor. Together with the singer’s baritone, they—and lyrics—drove the mice to their merry doom, their parts rotating through the canon, their voices greatly filling the short-ceilinged room, drowning out the sound of lunch being served and eaten as knives went still and cups sat undrank. The servers stopped their movements to listen and the patrons, largely Rubish workmen, listened to the choral, seemingly surprised by the sudden harmonization, as the higher notes of the singer’s baritone and Rennly’s tenor were bolstered by the sheer volume of Robles’ low notes. But Tomrin found himself staring at the girl.
“I wager they are up to something,” Non muttered, Tomrin glanced where he stared: Rennly and Fredick.
Armed With Forks and Knives [?]
Blonde Fredick sat at Rennly’s table staring back as Rennly, Robles, and the entertainer rounded down their song about the sleeping mice—who had all died on the rocks after tumbling over the edge of the world. The patrons clapped and without thinking Tomrin added his own brief show of appreciation to the cacophony. Fredick turned away and spoke to Rennly, their words too low under the noise to make out. 
Robles turned back to the table grinning.
“What are Fredrick and Rennly up to?” Tomrin asked him. 
Robles shrugged. “I don’t know. Since they met at the races, nothing good would be my bet.” 
A serving man interrupted them and asked for their orders. Tomrin had the lamb as did Robles, while Non had the humas. “And, a tin of low spirit tarsk for me, please,” Tomrin added before the man left the table. While they were eating, Non made a motion with his head to the door. Rennly and Fredick were leaving the Helm. “I can’t help feeling those two are plotting something,” Non said. 
Tomrin’s eyes followed Non’s. “Maybe.”
Though you sound a little paranoid,” Robles said. “Why don’t we find out?” He turned around and shouted at Rennly and Ferdick. “Hey! Ren! Fred! What were you two doing here sucking up all the clean air?” Fredick grimaced and waved dismissively, but Rennly walked over, leaving Fredick standing in the doorway.
He Who Holds A Feather [?]
The black censor rod waggled at Rennly’s hip.
The gods permitted whatever force they needed to maintain the faith, and that meant bows and arrows and other illegal weapons should they become necessary. Tomrin had read a tract about great barreled guns, but he doubted that the gods would trust Censors with them. Yet, the Serath had entrusted the black silks and golden feather to someone like Rennly, so Tomrin didn’t know anymore. 
Rennly kicked his foot up on the end of the bench next to Robles. He frowned a moment at him. “Your directions were terrible. I can’t trust you for anything.” 
“He knows about Gaz,” Robles said, brushing off Rennly’s criticisms as if praise. “Turns out the letter I received from my aunt this morning also had the news. I read it to him.” 
Rennly glanced at Tormin. “So you made it to work. Sleeping in on a workday...” He smacked his lips and shook his head and turned back to Robles. “As to what my new friend Fredick and I are doing here? Turns out I won the bet the other day. Your colleague was making good on the wager. He doesn’t know horses half as well as he thinks he does.”
“He doesn’t know half the things he thinks he does,” Robles said, as he waved amicably at Fredick.
“Is he wrong about Godly Glassworks?” Rennly asked.
“Depends. What did he say?” Non replied quickly.
“He says it is populated with fools.”
“Master Lazoloth isn’t a fool,” Tomrin said, his anger beginning to rise.
“Sounds like your Master Lazoloth isn’t the only one in charge,” Rennly said pointedly.
“Fredick is just jealous,” Robles said. “I’ve seen it before. Envy and jealousy is enough to turn some men green.”
“And this Lazoloth is handing out promotions when he can’t pay? And to someone who can’t show up on time. That hardly seems like the gods’ work to me.”
“It’s in lieu of more pay, Rennly,” Tomrin said. “That’s how business is done.”
“Is it now?”
“No one expects you to understand,” Robles said easily.
“I understand enough to know that if you and your master defile the name of the gods with your shop I’ll see it’s Censored faster than you can blink an eye.”
“Then we will be sure to be careful,” Robles said.
“See that you are,” Rennly said. “I’ll be watching. But now I must go. So much to do before heading to Woolad in the morning. I’ll see you there—if you manage to find your way for the funeral and the fair. Maybe we will square off for some games—like old times. The fair is going to be in the fairgrounds this year—like always—if you need directions.” 
“I think we all can find it,” Robles said, yawning.
Rennly nodded his head to the left and tapped his rod on the underside of the table. Chunk. He stepped off the bench. “Don’t get lost—or sleep in,” he added. 
He exited the Inn with Fredick, both disappearing into the sun.
The Green Orbs [?]
“With friends like that,” Non said. “I think your friend Gaz is in a better place.” 
“Rennly has a twisted honor all his own,” Robles said, with a shrug. “I guess that is something?” 
“Why does hate me so much?” Tomrin asked. “I mean I know why, but why can’t he just let it go?”
“He’s envious of you, Tomrin,” Robles said.
“Of me?”
“You are well on your way to be your father’s heir and look at Rennly.”
“Me? No. My father loves my brother Hadras too much,” Tomrin said. “And Rennly’s a Censor. I don’t see how he could be jealous.”
“Not jealous,” Robles said as he chewed. “Envy is different.”
“Envy. Jealousy. They are basically the same thing.” 
Robles swallowed. “Words don’t only just mean what you say they mean.” 
“You are just envious of me.”
Robles shook his head. “In your mind. Let’s get out of here,” he said. “Tomrin’s ego is filling the place up.”
Chapter 7: A Light Tap
	For no man lives without guilt. To do so is to be as a tree or an animal.
		--From the Living Book of the Dead

“You should have told your friend, Censor Magic, there, you saw his ghost friend, Gaz,” Non said as they headed out into the sun. “That would have thrown him.” 
Robles chuckled and soon they were back at the glass shop.
Inside Godly Glassworks, the noon heat had not yet penetrated the front. Fredick—with Rennly nowhere to be seen—sat at the desk area along the wall near Master Lazoloth’s office. Tomrin glanced away from Fredick to Laz’s door. It stood ajar. The master was out for lunch himself. Tiff and Ken were gone as well. 
“Now let’s see about getting the Scarab working,” Tomrin said to Non and Robles. Robles punched Non’s shoulder and the two of them left for the back shop. 
Tomrin began piecing the Scarab back together, and Robles and Non returned before Lazoloth did. They were all taking turns looking through the Scarab, when the old man stepped inside the glass shop. 
“Big Laz,” Robles called to the gray haired Peregrine man. Master Lazoloth shambled over as if the weight of the world rested on his shoulders, but as he drew closer, his eyes lit on the Scarab and he brightened. “I see you put it back together, but what is this? This lens is all rough.” 
“It needs grinding and polishing, but—” Tomrin tried to say.
“Just look,” Robles interrupted. “These are prototypes here inside.”
Master Lazoloth bent down and put his eye to the Scarab’s objective. “Interesting,” he said standing up. “The achromatism is gone.”
“Amazing isn’t it?” Robles asked.
“Amazing. Yes? The ramifications? What could they be?” Master Lazoloth mused to himself and patted Tomrin on the shoulder. “Even the fools at the Sapphire Front will have to see what we have here,” he said and headed for his office. 
“The ramifications...” Robles said, as if trying to ponder them himself.
“As long as we won’t be able to count all the ducks…” Non said. “I really don’t care about the ramifications.”
Tomrin pulled the doublet out of the armature. “This isn’t just about the money,” he said.
Master Laz often spoke of the ramifications of their work, but in truth, Tomrin rarely understood half of what he was saying. “Non, this one is yours. Grind it,” he added, keeping focused. If he started thinking about money his mind would wander and nothing would get done.
“Fine,” Non said, and slowly took the lens from Tomrin’s hand. 
“I’m going to look at the new ones you guys made,” Tomrin said, but continued to sit as Non and Robles left. His head was abuzz.
Finally, he stood and headed into the back room.
Fire and Glass and Honest Work [?]
The hot air in the back didn’t help and Tomrin soon retreated to the front of the shop. 
Fredick, his hands stuffed down the front of his pants, was looking at the Scarab. 
Tomrin walked over to him. “We need to talk,” he said. 
The blonde boy glanced up and raised an eyebrow. 
“This doesn’t look like what--” 
Tormin poked his hand into the boy’s face and touched his forehead with the tip of his index finger. “Listen to me,” he said as he pushed his finger against Fredick’s forehead. “You are not to touch the Scarab. I told you this.” Tomrin pulled his finger away from Fredick’s face and waited for him to respond, to acknowledge that he heard, that he understood.
Fredick said nothing, but turned and headed to his work area without a single word. Sweat cool  Tomrin’s hands but his legs boiled in his pants and his face burnt like he had fallen into a glass furnace headfirst.
Breathing heavily through his nose, he sat down at his bench and absently arranged his tools, not making his work area any less cluttered, just changing the shape of the mess. He sighed several times, then stood up and headed through the double doors to the back where it was hotter, but at least the heat made sense, what with the different furnaces for the different colored glass leaking heat into the air. 
He watched Non grind a tool for the new lenses. “Robles is outside,” Non said. 
“Thanks,” Tomrin but headed for the front again.
It would be hotter outside. 
Inside the Oven [?]
Fredick sat at his sidewall desk .
Master Lazoloth stood nearby in his office doorway. “Tomrin,” the old man said. “Can you come in a minute?” 
Tomrin’s walked over. Master Lazoloth held his office door open for him and he could feel Fredick’s eyes on his back, but refused to look at him. Instead he marched inside feeling awkward. “Fredick, come in too,” Master Laz said, then shut the office door behind them.
A pressure built in Tomrin’s skull as he waited for Master Lazoloth to sit while he and Fredick stood in front of the man’s desk, waiting for him to speak. Slowly, Lazoloth rested his tired hands on a stack of scattered papers. “Fredick says you hit him.” 
“What?” Tomrin said, glancing at Fredick. The boy wore no expression at all. “No. I didn’t hit you.” 
“You did,” Fredick said primly. His eyes flashed and he pointed to his forehead. “You hit me right here.” 
“I touched you. I didn’t hit you.” 
“So you did touch him?” Master Lazoloth said. 
“No. Yes. I touched his forehead. Is that what this is about?” 
“You hit me and should be fired.” 
Tomrin scoffed. “Big Laz, I—I touched him. I asked him to use his brain. He’s not supposed to touch the Scarab without my say so. He knows that.” 
“You are not my boss,” Fredick said.
“Yes, you are right, Fredick,” Master Lazoloth said. “But I am, and I don’t want you touching the Scarab either. We’re at a critical point now.” 
“He assaulted me, Master Lazoloth,” Fredick said, drawing himself up. 
Tomrin didn’t like the sound of what he was hearing. “Assault?” 
“Calm you yourself, Tomrin and Fredick,” Master Lazoloth said. “Both of you.”
“I will not calm myself,” Fredick said. “First you stop paying me, now you let this insect abuse me. You think I don’t know you have money in the vaults at the First Farmers Bank? You think I’m stupid?” He reached into his pocket and flashed a scrap of paper before jamming it back into his pants pocket.
“I don’t know what that is,” Master Lasoloth said.
“What money?” Tomrin wasn’t sure what he was hearing. “I thought payroll was late for everyone, because we need the Sapphire Front to pay us.”
“Yes. Yes. Fredick, next week,” Master Laz said.
Pure Lunacy [?]
Fredick began waving his other hand around like a loon. “You two are in this together! I know it. You are stealing from the rest of us!” 
“No,” Tomrin said, pulling back out of the path of Fredick’s errant finger-wagging.
“Calm down, Fredick!” Master Lazoloth all but shouted. 
“First you rob us, now you do nothing while this bug assaults me!” Fredick turned and marched out of the office, pushing the door open so hard it slammed against his outer workstation. He shouted as he rummaged through his stuff on his bench. “Lazoloth is robbing us blind! And I won’t stand for it! I’m taking this theft to the constabulary! There are laws! Even in Corundum!” 
Tormin’s hand went involuntarily to his front right pocket where he kept his papers. The billfold wasn’t there. 
Master Lazoloth began saying something. “I can’t believe I hired Fredick.  Nothing but a feathered—” Master Lazoloth stopped. “Tomrin? What is it?”
“I forgot my papers. They are in my other pants at home, I think.” 
Master Lazoloth’s face turned ashen. His ruddy skin lost several shades of colors as he fished his own billfold out. He opened his wallet and sighed. He looked up. “You better go get them. We can talk when you return. I think I figured out the ramifications.” 
“The ramifications?” Tomrin said.
“If we make the things small enough—” Master Lazoloth said. “But go, we can’t have you in jail over nothing.”
Tomrin blinked. Things? For a split second, he thought he could see what Master Lazoloth meant about the ramifications. An ephemeral image appeared in his mind, complex and colorful, and so intricate that he couldn’t focus on a single aspect as it shifted and disappeared. 
He wanted that picture back but Master Laz was right; he needed his papers. “Feathers, I’ll be back,” he swore and headed out of Laz’s door. 
Exiting the glass shop, he entered the furnace which was the afternoon’s heat. The feathered law was silly, but as a freed Peregrine, without his papers he taunted fate as surely as anything--doubly so with the Leviathan’s Mark growing large on his chest.
Chapter 8: While the Sun Still Shines 
Once you taste the ambrosia, you will realize: you and it are one.
		—From The Living Book of the Dead

Tomrin ran, disbelieving he could be so foolish as to forget his papers. The green glow and the specter of Gaz had distracted him, but still! To forget his papers! “Feathers!” The lamb chop in his belly sloshed in the low-spirit tarsk he’d drank at lunch. The afternoon’s hot air gusted against his face and his head swam with heat and he worried he might fall over if he kept up the pace. You’re free! You’re free! 
Finally he slowed. 
Fredick was somehow already halfway down Arcade Avenue and still running.
As he caught his breath, Tomrin tried to think what he would say if a constable did stop him and demanded proof he was who he claimed to be.  It took no brains to run a billy club. I don’t even have a penny to bribe him. The idea of being tossed in jail for the night because he didn’t have his papers angered him by how much it scared him. They might find out his secret.
At the Winged Helm Inn, he turned up the highway and swore under his breath at Fredick, and at the system. He felt himself going to the dark place where his anger and fear turned to madness, to the place where the evil growing in his chest beneath the Leviathan’s Mark called to him. There was no real reason he had to be the Emperor’s slave.
He had read the tracts. The Emperor had acted creatively in setting the Orish and Peregrine free. His tax and rules concerning patents was that cleverness. But by the rules in The Book of Light—and enforced by the Daughters of Heaven’s Glory and their Censors—none of the lesser races were allowed to be anything but slaves. Tomrin was convinced his own father had transferred his ownership to the Emperor because Dable Ocrin would be feathered if he was going to pay that kind of money. 
Inside the Attic Window [?]
“Mistress Carl! My papers!” Tomrin shouted. 
His clothes were gone.
“Don’t yell in my house!” she called back from the washroom. From the doorway she emerged disheveled, her arms and hands dripping wet. Blinking, she wiped her forehead with a pink arm. “I bet you are looking for your wallet. I found it in your green pants.” 
“Where is it?” he asked.
“On the kitchen table.” She followed him forward. “Your papers were in it.” Tomrin grabbed his billfold. His patents are safe inside. “You could wind up in real trouble,” she said. “I thought you knew you had to keep those on you? And the money.”
“Not now,” Tomrin said and stuffed the bondworks back inside and refolded the leather wallet and jammed it into his pants and tapped it through the fabric. He hadn’t noticed his wallet missing earlier at lunch because Non had paid. “I have to go,” he said, ignoring her question. 
“You shouldn’t be carrying so much money around,” Mistress Carl said. “What if you had been robbed.”
Her tone and pawing around in his billfold irritated him. “As I’ve told you, I’ve been busy.” He wanted to ask about his clothes but only added: “And I got to go.”
“I know I told you week-to-week,” Mistress Carl said.
Tomrin walked around her and to the front door. “Yes, I heard you.”
“Maybe if we bumped the monthly rent up a touch—maybe then I could let you stay month-to-month.”
“We will talk later,” Tomrin said. Opening the door, he stepped back out into the heat.
Not So Artful [?]
Back out front of Godly Glassworks, the glass Seasargus dozed as if it were weary from sun, even though the fiery blaze no longer glinted off the window. Tomrin’s pulled the glass weighted door open and glanced up Arcade to the Hall of Victory. The large dome quavered in the heat. Glancing down the way he’d come, he saw Fredick walking beside a man in brown. The two of them strode up the street with purpose. 
The yellow headed boy wasn’t bluffing about getting the authorities involved. 
Tomrin stepped inside, out of view and pulled the door closed. “Feathered Fredick!” he swore.
Members of the shop, front and back, stared at him. 
A single gas torch burnt unattended on the front bench. Robles sat nearby on a stool with Non standing at his side. Polishing rush dirtied Ken’s hands and fingers. He had more skill with polish than most. Master Lazoloth’s door stood closed. At Fredick’s desk, Frederick and everyone from the back milled about, whispering. 
“Yes? About Fredick?” Robles prompted.
“He’s coming with a constable.” 
“Then it is true,” Jan said. “Master Laz did steal the money?”
“No, it’s not true,” Tormin said.
“Fredick is just a paranoid fool, everyone,” Robles said. “You can see it in his beady eyes.” 
“I need this job,” Antsy, one of the backroom workers, said. 
“We all need this job,” Tomrin muttered, but worried the constable would arrest him for assault, as silly as it sounded. “I could feathered-well kill Fredick.”
Robles pulled out his knife. “Try this. It’s sharp,” he said softly, as a shadow moved in the window.
“Put that away. They are coming.” 
Robles slipped the knife back into its sheath. “Steady now,” he said.
“Me? You are going to get us into further trouble with your antics.”
The tall door of gray and blue glass swung open strong enough to feel it shake the floor as it banged against the wall. Fredick entered. A Rubish man with a gut in a tan uniform entered right behind him. 
The hexagon badge on the man’s chest proclaimed him the law with its gemstone design worked in silver. At his hip rode a tan, not black, nightstick. He enforced the emperor’s laws as the Censors enforced the gods’. 
With Shackles [?]
“You are Master Lazoloth?” the man said, blinking at Tomrin. A greasy sweat dribbled down his flushed pink face.
“No, not him,” Fredick said. He marched over to Master Laz’s door and yanked it open. “This is the thief.”
Master Lazoloth stood up and calmly walked to the doorway. “Is there some problem, Constable--?” 
“Officer of the Crown, Dhom Stabtus,” the Rubish said. “And I am afraid a credible accusation has been made per your character.”
“Really? My character?”
“And as one of the emperor’s bondsmen it can not be ignored.” 
“What are you saying, Officer Dhom?” Master Laz asked. 
The constables’ hands went to his belt and he hitched his pants before pulling out a pair of manacles which had been hiding under his belly. “I mean, I’m afraid, that you will have to come with me.” 
Master Lazoloth stepped from his office. “Freely.” He nodded at the cufts in the constable’s hands. “Those won’t be necessary.” 
“Put your hands out,” Constable Dhom commanded. “I’m afraid that it’s required in instances like this.” The man glanced around the room, almost pleading, he added, “You--you all understand.” 
Master Lazoloth raised his arms in front of himself and waited as Stabtus fastened and adjusted the manacles. “Too tight,” Lazoloth said. 
“That’s how they are,” the officer replied stoutly. He put his hands on Lazoloth’s upper arm and led him to the door. 
“One moment.” Master Lazoloth turned. “You are fired, Fredick. I don’t want to see you here ever again.” 
Fredick grinned his smarmiest grin. “Like I care to work for nothing.” 
“Alright,” Dhom said. “You can worry about all that later. But for now, your accuser has to come with us.” 
“What about us?” Jan asked. “What about us?”
“You a witness?” Stabtus asked?
“Yes, it’s everyone’s money he stole.” 
“I didn’t steal anything,” Master Laz said in a voice pinched as if not trying to show pain. 
Tomrin wanted to shout at Jan and Fredick and everyone. What is wrong with you? But he said nothing, worried Fredick might remember his accusation of assault. 
“That will all be worked out at the courthouse,” Stabtus said. “I don’t want any trouble, you hear? And don’t think about trying to bribe me.” He shot a strange look at Fredick then led Lazoloth outside.
 Fredick stepped behind him, and the door closed.
A House Divided [?]
The backhalfers all started talking at once. 
Robles tossed his knife at the wall. Chunth! Silence fell over everyone. “Now that I have your attention,” Robles said. He glanced at Tomrin. “I’m sure Founder Tomrin has something to tell you all.”
Tomrin blinked at him, wondering if his friend was having a go. “Um. The court will clear Master Laz,” he said. “Don’t worry. The glass shop will stay open.”
"I was thinking more that we needed to go down there and support him as a group,” Robles said. “As his loyal employees."
"I was thinking how nice it would be not having to deal with Fredick anymore," Non said. "Never should have hired him." 
"I think we all wish to know what is really going on,” Jan said.
She gave Non a hairy eyeball as she marched herself and the backhalfers to the front door. Her work crew followed her.
Tomrin turned off the gas torch and turned to Robles. "You think Fredick was stirring up trouble with them?”
“Did you really hit him?” Non asked. 
“No. I barely touched him.” 
“This isn’t your fault,” Robles said. “This is on Fredick.” 
“I think he felt slighted by your promotion,” Ken said. 
Tiff nodded. 
“Maybe.” Robles sounded skeptical. 
“But you hit him, Tomrin?” Non said. “Then he exploded.” 
“No, I don’t think that is it,” Robles said. He walked to the front door.
“You forgetting your pack, Robles?” Non said. “Or are you afraid to bring it to the courthouse?” 
“Censor Magic it over to me,” Robles said. Non picked up and made to toss the pack. “That means bring it to me.” 
Non scoffed. “That’s boring magic.”
True Glasser [?]
The shop’s traitors were well ahead of them on Arcade by the time Robles herded Tomrin, Non, Ken and Tiff out the door. More than half the shop had turned on Master Lazoloth.
The terrible pressure in Tomrin’s skull from the heat was building again and he feared he was about to explode like the initial effluence of a torch. “Let’s keep our distance.”
“Why?” Robles asked, shooting him a rare frown. 
 “They aren’t true glassers, like us,” Non offered.
 Ken grunted noncommittally and asked what they could expect next.
“Less mouths to feed,” Non said. 
“I hope you like working in the back then,” Tomrin said. 
Non groaned. “I was hoping to take over Fredick’s job, and now the Scarab works, you’ll have time yourself.” 
“There is always more to be done on it.”
At the bottom of the valley, Third Street shot straight towards the center of town to the temple. The Hall of Justice sat a block away under watch of the temple overlooking the city and the doves which cooed so freely in the eaves of buildings early that morning nested quietly now while the heat of the stone blocks of the courthouse boiled Tomrin’s eyes in his head. 
The Hall of Justice [?]
Inside, the floors needed polishing. Years of foot traffic had turned the marble into a dirty soapstone. A dirty light filtered down a muted brown through the skylights. Tall wooden doors with bronze plaques lined the two-story building and wooden benches held women with small children, not all Peregrine or Orish. One Rubish mother nursed a half-breed Peregrine child. Workmen—perhaps in the midst of rows with their neighbors—stood around glumly staring at doors closed to them. 
On a stone bench a man sat his sword hilt jutting out. The squires of the surrounding farms had to seek their justice in the courts now, just like all the others, though the right to wear swords was still afforded them. The Emperor’s Restoration had changed many things, but not all. 
Further down in the hallway outside the slave courts, the press of humanity was inhuman. Curse words flew freely. Orish and Peregrine alike bantered coarsely—men and women alike. It almost reminded Tomrin of going home to Woolad. But people in Woolad were more polite. Maybe heading home wasn’t going to be so bad. Cutting off the scales always took something out of him for a couple days and he could use the rest after tonight.
The Slave Courts [?]
Stabtus led Master Lazoloth to the slave court’s doors.
Jan and her group stood huddled on the far wall eyeing them. The slave courts ran with surprising efficiency. When one Rublish sued another, their trial could last years. Slaves however were not afforded such process. Unfair maybe, but Tomrin was glad that this would all be over soon so he could get back to work and away from the grating noise and thick musk.
A tattooed Orish man stepped in front of the constable. “I’ve been here all feathered day,” the man said, loudly. “Why in Seasargus’ Black Beak is this man going first?” 
“Get out of my way and wait your turn,” Officer Dhom said, pushing the man almost twice his size to the side. “Judge Reydal calls you when he calls you.” Officer Stabtus opened the door and waved Fredick and Master Lazoloth inside. “The rest of you wait here.” 
“What about witnesses for the defense?” Tomrin asked.
“You want to testify on the embezzler’s behalf?” 
“No, on Master Lazoloth’s behalf. He’s not an embezzler.” 
“Fine, whatever...but just you.” 
Tomrin glanced at a grinning Robles. “We trust you,” Robles said. But Non’s face contorted, and Tiff squeezed Ken’s hands. 
“Go on,” the officer said and Tomrin stepped through the door thinking it would be better to have Robles go instead. His friend had a way with words and with people, and he was at least Orioles if not Rubish. 
The Great Unwashed [?]
The presiding judge wore a red robe and sat on the high dais near the front of the room, not too high on his throne to look a Serath in the eyes, but high enough for his petitioners to bend their necks to meet his gaze. A brazen plaque on his desk proclaimed him one Jeremy Reydal, Esquire. The gray in his dark hair proclaimed him old enough to be his father, the silver wings at his temples making him look more dignified than any man had the right, as if the robe was not enough. Yet, somehow, he looked familiar… 
He hammering his gavel and shouted order be restored. Tomrin hadn’t heard what had caused the uproar, but from the back of the room men and women, Orish and Peregrine alike, jeered and roared with disdain. The place was mayhem save for one small island, a dark skinned Adaman woman in the back. Her bright blue eyes pierced Tomrin and for a second he wondered if she had done something to make the wound on his chest itch. 
“Councilor Marten!” The judge shouted. “Restrain your charge or I will have him restrained.” 
“Yes, your honor,” a large--even for an Orish man--answered from the banister, the wooden bar separating the judge, recorder, and bailiff from the unwashed masses and where the councilors and advocates stood with their clients. 
The Orish counselor, the one that answered to the name Marten, tucked a massive arm around the head and neck of his Orish charge. Dragging the man’s head to his mouth he spoke in the man’s upswept ear. The man tried to resist, but under the lawyer’s brown vest, muscles flexed and buckled his white shirt, quelling the man. “Listen!” Marten hissed loud enough for the court to hear.  Cut in the western style of Zircos, the man’s brown vest would not look out of place—save he was Orish.
Chapter 9: The Gamblers’ Union
Because we desperately need to it rhyme, 
So shall it be written in the annals of time.
		—The last line and verse from The Gambler’s Pledge

While the Peregrine lawyer on the other side chatted quietly with his own Orish client--just two Orish men taking their differences to the bar--a man with a golden hilted sword standing near the door began taking wagers bets on the outcome of the case in whispers. The local gentry were very bored indeed.
“Your Honor, Judge Reydal,” Dhom Stabus said in the descending quiet. “I have a case here of a freesoul versus a bondsman. A priority case.” 
“Are you feathered serious?” the first Orish man said. 
“Well, I think I have heard enough,” Judge Reydal said and slammed his gavel back down. He pointed the wooden thing at the man. “Guilty. Councilor Marten, I find your client guilty. Master Flager, owes the Master Cordwine two silvers. Now get out of my courtroom room before I hear another word! Bailiffs!” 
“Thank you, your Honor,” the Peregrine councilor for the prosecution said over the cacophony of the bailiffs removing the losing side of the argument. 
“So what is this case?” Judge Reydal asked once order was restored and the guilty and innocent parties had left the room and the bet’s paid out in another round of whispers.
“A case of embezzled funds, your Honor. This, Master Lazoloth, here is accused by Fredrick Kistabal here of stealing money from the common fund of the company they work at, Godly Glassworks.”
