“Well, take off his shackles, Dhom,” Judge Reydal said. “No one is to appear before me in chains. You know the rules.” Stabtus undid the manacles and marched back outside the courtroom.
Tomrin tried to smile at Master Lazoloth, but feared all he did was frown. Glancing away, his eyes lit on the court recorder and he tried not to stare. She was the prim and proper Orioles singer from the Winged Helm Inn. While she had smiled then, now she had a severity to her—like she took this job far too seriously. And the judge? Was he the same man? Here in his red silk judge’s gown he looked quite different. In a way it seemed even the girls’ spots might have changed.
A Day In Court [?]
Tomrin pulled his eyes from the girl, but found himself glancing instead at the Adaman women. His eyes glided over everyone else save the large Orish counselor and the judge as the court grew silent under the threat of his gavel.
The prosecuting lawyer stated the charges against Master Lazoloth and the judge handed Big Laz over to the losing lawyer. “Counselor Marten,” Judge Reydal asked. “Does your client understand the charges against him?” 
The lawyer did not even look at Master Laz. “Yes, your Honor.” 
“Then...how does he plead?”
“Not guilty.”
“Very well,” the judge drawled as if he’d said the same so many times he’d lost count. “The court will hear the evidence.” He turned his attention to the Peregrine lawyer conferring with Fredick. “Councilor Divertay, evidence? Arguments? For the the court please.” The man raised a finger and continued to nod his head at Fredick. “Today, Counselor?” 
“Yes, Judge Reydal,” counselor Divertay said, turning his head towards the judges’ high desk. “The accused--”
“Master Lazoloth,” Counselor Marten injected as the man with the gold sword began to whisper.
Divertay swatted a hand at them both as if at a flies. “Master Lazoloth refuses to pay his employees despite having the money in the bank,” he continued. “This is theft and he must pay them their rightful due. To do otherwise is stealing.” 
“I’m not a thief,” Master Lazoloth said. The judge raised an eyebrow at him, and Counselor Marten put a hand on his shoulder and whispered something in his ear.
“Farmers First Bank,” Master Lazoloth said aloud. 
“And?” the judge said. 
“I ask the court that Master Lazoloth’s accounts be subpoenaed without delay,” Counselor Marten said. “Farmers First Bank.” 
“Counselor Divertay?” the judge said. “Will the prosecution stand by those records as proof of guilt or innocence?” 
Divertay glanced at Fredick. The blond-headed boy nodded. “Yes, your Honor.” 
“And you, Master Lazoloth?” The judge said. 
“Yes,” Laz’s lawyer answered for him. “However, we would like to reserve the right to call witnesses.” 
“Naturally,” the judge said. 
“As would we.” 
The judge glanced at Divertay. “So someone has seen the man commit this theft? Is that what I am hearing.”
“No, your honor. Not to my knowledge.” 
“No, I thought not. But, you are well within your rights, I suppose.” The judge then directed one of his bailiffs to go to the Farmers First Bank and to return with the ledgers of interest. “And don’t dally. This should be open and shut. The next order of business?” he said, turning to the girl furiously writing at her own smaller desk below his. “There is still much to do while the sun still shines,” he added with a smile.
The gambler continued his whispering.
The Price of Justice [?]
The girl flipped through her notebook. “You could call up...”
Before she could bring up a name from the list Tomrin supposed she had been compiling all day, and before the bailiff could exit the door, Marten objected. “I’m sorry, your Honor, but I would like to suggest a brief recess….certain...important security concerns...presented by this case.” The counselor glanced from the Adaman woman at the back of the room to the judge. “And what they might pose to the Crystal Throne.” The Orish man added, sounding not the least bit confused now.
The judge frowned. “What considerations?” 
“They are of a nature, Judge, that we shouldn’t disclose them in open court—not without reviewing them with him and you first.” 
“Irregular, Counselor.” The judge glanced at Divertay and Fredick. “But without objections I am obliged to grant everyone a recess. Counselor Divertay?”
Divertay glanced up from his whispered discussion with Fredick. “No. No objections, your honor.” 
The judge slammed his gavel down. “Granted.” He stood up and walked to the door behind his chair and turned. 
The remaining bailiffs began to clear the courtroom as Marten ushered Master Lazoloth through the bar towards the front. Fredick and Divertay followed them out. Lastly came the court recorder. Tomrin’s eyes followed her unconsciously. Her gray dress, cut high enough to be modest but not too low to be staid, flattered her figure. 
I’m married to my work, Tomrin reminded himself when a bailiff instructed him that he had to leave as well.
“I Love Easy,” [?]
Outside the courtroom, Robles wanted to know everything that was going on inside. 
“It was strange,” Tomrin said, searching the crowd for the Adaman woman. She had somehow managed to disappear. 
“What was?” 
“The lawyer knew about our work with the Sapphire Front…I think,” Tomrin suspected the redheaded woman with the bronze skin and blue eyes, but he didn’t know how to explain her part in the matter. “That’s all I can figure.” 
“Master Lazoloth must have told him?” 
“I don’t know. Fredick and his lawyer were chatting like crazy, but Master Laz barely said a word to his.” 
“Sort of sudden like,” Non said. 
“It was sudden...” Tomrin thought to add more, but his eyes followed Robles and Non’s glances to the courtroom door. The door was open and the court recorder stepped out into the hallway. 
“Uhh,” Non gunted.
“Remember, I saw her first,” Robles whispered. 
The girl’s eyes lit on Tomrin and she walked over. “Founder Tomrin Ocrin?” she said, sounding a bit surprised herself at the words. 
“Yes,” Tomrin said. “Uh-I’m Tomrin.”
“And a Founder,” Robles added. “But it sounds so much more official when you say it.”
“That is because it is official,” the girl said, not turning her big brown, and no-nonsense eyes on Robles, but keeping them Firmly locked on Tomrin. “Counselor Marten has requested you.” She turned around and beckoned him over her shoulder with a hand. “This way, please.” 
Robles pushed Tomrin after her. 
“Feathers! Sorry,” he blurted.
Shady Ways Along Shady Rows [?]
“Smooth!” came Robles’ snicker down the hall at him. 
The girl ignored him and led Tomrin back into the courtroom. The bailiff at the door let her through with only a cursory glance. They passed through the banister to the front of the courtroom and headed for the small door the counselors had left though. 
Tormin read the nameplate on her deck. “Elisah Reydal?” 
“Yes?” she asked curtly. 
“You are related to the judge?” 
“He is my father.” 
“Why do you think the counselor wants to see me?” Ideas of Fredick pressing more charges filled Tomrin with a touch of dread.
“I didn’t think to ask and he didn’t think to tell me,” Eliash said. “It’s not my life to know such things.” She led him out into the hallway and down a long aisle of leather bound law books. Ladders reaching up to the highest ones. Such wasted effort on such uselessness. No doubt half the books dealt with owning and punishing slaves.
Elisah stopped and knocked on a door. A muffled call came from the other side. “Come in.” 
She turned the doorknob and ushered Tomrin inside. “Thank you, Recorder Reydal,” the large Orish counselor said. Elisah nodded and shut the door without a word. The man held out a hand towards Tomrin. “Come. Sit.”
Two chairs flanked the man, but he motioned to a smaller one across the table. 
Tomrin walked to the solitary chair. “Where is Lazoloth?” he asked as he sat. “You know he is innocent, right, Councilor?” 
“Of course he is,” the man said. “And call me Marten. That’s what they always called me in the army: Emerald Front. I fought during the Restoration.” 
“Thank you for that,” Tomrin said, “But--uh--?” 
“But why do I want to see you?” 
“Yes, I suppose that, and um--” Tomrin glanced around. “Where is Master Laz?” 
“He is patiently awaiting the account books,” Marten said, folding his massive hands on the table. “This little meeting is not about that. I have someone here who wants to speak with you.” Marten’s eye roved over to a small door in the wall in the corner of the room. 
Marten was not a small man and the chair groaned as he stood up. Even though he wore a vest like a skinny man for Zircos, he was anything but skinny. Regardless, he moved with a dancer’s grace and he acted far more intelligent than most Orish men his size. When he opened the far door, the tall Adaman woman from the courtroom stepped out of the shadowy adjoining room. 
“Lady Margretta,” Marten said. “Tomrin Ocrin—as you well know.”
The Serpent’s Forked Tongue [?]
“Hello,” Tomrin said. 
It was more of a question than anything. How did this woman know him? He had stared at her almost the whole time and now she was here, and knew of him without even having spared him more than a glance.
Her dress swished as she sashayed into the room. She waited until she was on the other side of the table before she said a thing. Speaking to Marten she said, “Thank you, Marten.” Turning she said: “Hello, Tomrin. It is nice to see you again.” Tomrin had risen from his chair and she motioned for him to sit. “But imagine my surprise seeing you here today?” She was pure business, folding her bronze hands in front of herself on the table on Marten’s right. 
“I—I don’t remember you?” Tormin said, wondering how she knew him. Perhaps she was sent by someone in the Saphire Front—or even the Emerald Front. Spies and spies with spies.
“Marten and I are not often in town,” the dark skinned woman said. “Marten more than I.”
Tomrin glanced at the big man. “You came to our attention some time back--” the larger man said.
Margretta held up a hand to silence him and continued. “We are looking for someone much like yourself, someone with talents—your talents.” The tone she took did not make Tomrin think she meant his talents with glass, and his scalp began to buzz.
“I--ah--don’t know if I’m looking for new employment. But, I am-uh—am a loyal subject of the Empire,” he added.
“You have many talents, Tomrin. That I am convinced. Remember Lord Southerly’s farm?”
“Spy glasses then?” Tomrin said. 
“Opera glasses was what I was told, and ostensibly that is all you were led to believe?”
“Everything is connected,” Tomrin said. “I was working on opera glasses but that was just the line we are all fed by the kind of people who answer to Lord Southerly.” 
Living there was the first time Tomrin had removed the scales from his chest.
It had not gone well and he spent several days in the infirmary from a flu a caught after the scales became infected.
Lord Southerly’s [?]
Lady Margretta slowly unfolded her fingers and continued to unravel her lies or half truths, or worse, the truth. “This goes beyond spying,” she said as Tomrin stared at her hands, the color of her skin was lighter on her palms—similar to that of the one dark skinned Silvan he’d once seen—but Margretta didn’t have the upswept ears of that far eastern race.
Tomrin glanced over to Marten. “I don’t know anything about anything else,” he said. “I’m only good with glass. I worked in my father’s carpentry shop, but I was never any good...” 
Marten grinned and cocked his head slightly to the left as Margretta grunted as if the two of them  disbelieved every word together as one. “Let’s talk about what I can offer,” Margretta said.
Tomrin hemmed softly to himself and waited, hoping she had nothing he wanted, because he was beginning to worry she might be referring not to just any old skill at all. He only needed the slightest excuse and this interview was over.
“First I can end proceedings against your Master Lazoloth straight away. I can clear him in Jarvis’ name,” she said, raising her right index finger, then reaching under the table with her left hand she returned it to the top with an ivory colored card. Smiling like a cat with a mouse, she pushed the small rectangle forward, across the table towards him.
The card held the imperial seal, the same as his own patents. “Not bondworks?” Tomrin said.
“Something else entirely,” Margretta allowed.
The peach stamped in black ink was signed in what might have well been the Emperor’s own hand. Tomrin waited, then, glancing up at her and Marten, said: “But you say, there is nothing to worry about. The master will already be cleared.” 
Marten folded his arms across his vest. “Well,” he said. “No one can know for sure.” 
Margretta tapped the card and frowned. “That doesn’t mean you wouldn’t be better off in my care.” 
“I may be bound to the Emperor’s will—” Tomrin said. 
He reached out and picked up the card from the table and read the words aloud. “The bearer of this bond speaks for me, Lord of the Crystal Throne, Champion of the East, and Emperor of Corundum, Lord Jarvis Teaubril. Do as they command, and you do as I command.” He set the card back down on the table, and reached into his own pocket but didn’t withdraw his billfold. “My papers say that I am free to disregard any master who does not sit upon the Crystal Throne itself. It too is signed by Jarvis.” 
“Jarvis!” Margretta scoffed and slid the card back across the table. “It is true, you are free to disregard me. As you are not part of the hierarchy... The Emperor’s generosity sometimes tries my patience,” she muttered more to herself, than to him or Marten. “I have many talents myself, and there are things I would teach you, young Tomrin. This shop of yours, it’s running out of money. Then where will you be?”
Again, Tomrin heard hints. His neck prickled. The woman spoke like a Dark Sister hinting at darker things.
Chapter 10: A New Turn of the Cycle
	The first unwritten rule of power is spy on your neighbor.
		—From The Living Book of the Dead

Lady Margretta made a face, raising her eyebrows and grinning down at him. “The world is a dangerous place, young man, as I’m sure you know,” she said. “We have taken a hands off approach until now, but with Censors sniffing around, things can no longer continue as usual.”
“Censors?” Tormin said. “Hands off? We?”
“Yes, Censors. One false move,” the woman said. “And men who stand tall today, plummet the next.” 
“I’m not sure about that… I’m not trying to plummet,” Tomrin said. “I’m a glass worker. I’m happy to work glass until the day I die.” 
“And when your master runs out of money…or is convicted?” 
“You aren’t going to force the judge—?” 
“I am not here to force him,’ she said.
 Tomrin pushed out his chair. “I think I would like to leave.”
“Wait!” the woman said sharply. “You think everything is just fine, but I can tell you, you are in a precarious spot, young man. You are more like your Master Lazoloth than you may know. You ever wonder why he never married?” 
“I’m sure he has his reasons.” 
“And you?” 
“Still young.” 
“Yes, but you won’t be young forever, yet how much time have you put into thinking about finding a wife?” 
“I’m not carefree about such things. I am married to glass.” 
“Yet you are careless. Not all hidden things are fully hidden, so trust me when I tell you this. Some people can see more clearly than others. You are gossling flying blind.”
“And by going with you to where, exactly, would my eyes be opened? My blindness cured?” 
The woman lowered her gaze a foot and looked right at his chest. “You would have to trust me.” 
“I am not the trusting type,” Tomrin said and turned away. He walked to the door as his scaly wound itched under his shirt. “I will be right here in Menad,” he said before opening the door. “Where I belong. I am a glassworker and nothing more. If you think I want to be something more, you are mistaken. Spying on me changes nothing.”
The First Strand [?]
“I wouldn’t call it spying,” the woman said. 
Tomrin wanted to ask her just how much she knew, but was afraid he might learn she knew far too much. He always feared what would happen if a Censor or Daughter of Heaven’s Glory found him. But dealing with this creature, a Dark Sister? she promised him a far worse fate. Women who sold their souls to the demons were pure evil. He glanced at Marten but the man acted unconcerned by her. Perhaps he knows. But the Emperor?
The woman must have caught the hint of a question in his eyes. “I can clarify many things for you,” Margretta said. “But not here, not in Menad.” 
“I’m not sure I need things cleared up,” Tomrin said. 
“That is because you are closed—and live in shadows,” Margretta said, softly. “The West is in turmoil. That strife is headed this way. Slavers and Censors from the Zircos have already entered Corundum and they are not particular about who they stop, or who they search.” 
“That’s the Emperor’s fault.” 
“Perhaps I should let his Highness know that he should do more?” she said, sardonically. 
“You have his writ.” 
“That’s true, but Censors move freely. Not even Jarvis can do much about them. I may have the Emperor’s ear, but he is in Corundum. And that does not help against overzealous men in black. A whole new slew of them entered town this afternoon. With slavers,” Margretta added, smirking almost as if she were the one commanding this horde she conjured from thin air.
Sweat beaded on Tomrin’s forehead, but he refused to wipe it. “I’m a loyal subject with nothing to fear. The slavers, I leave to you and to the Emperor.” 
“They will be dealt with, and legally. But slavers are nasty and brutish men,” Margretta said. “And one might ask how little you go to temple? And a Censor might just wonder how good of a person you truly are.” 
“If you think I’ve done something wrong, why don’t you call the Censors yourself?” Tomrin said, hoping to bluff his way out of this conversation. “But for now, I think I have heard enough.” 
The Second Attack [?]
“And Master Lazoloth?” Margretta asked.
“What about him?” 
“Would you let him suffer a terrible fate, bankruptcy or worse, when you could end it, just to remain in this crummy little town, always looking over your shoulders, always afraid?” 
“I’m not afraid,” Tomrin said, feeling ice in his stomach again. His heart began pounding. “I am going now, so please consider helping Master Lazoloth—if you can. He is a good person.” 
“So naive,” Margretta said, shaking her head. “Be careful, Master Tomrin. You walk a terrible thin line.” She didn’t say the words “You walk the Shadow Line,” but Tomrin heard them in his head. He wondered if she feared he would trip and fall into the light? He worried that himself. But also that he might slip into the darkness.
“I try to do what is right. You should too,” he said.
“What is right? Our world is filled with only half-truth. And outright lies. Sometimes it is hard to know the difference between truth and lies.” 
“And right and wrong?” 
“Exactly.” 
“I don’t find it hard. You should help Lazoloth because he is a good person. The top is not the bottom. And the left is not the right.” 
“Laugh and make light.” Margretta turned to Marten. “I told you he was a fool and a coward both.”
The First Retreat [?]
“I’ll just let myself out.” The anger in Tomrin’s words sounded harsh in his ears. He didn’t want to be afraid and he didn’t want to act a fool. What could anyone know about him, anyway? Nothing! There was no way she could know. Unless she was a Sister of the Dark and he didn’t want to think about that. However, he couldn’t ignore the fact that she might know things about him… how and what exactly, she kept oblique—probably out of caution? Tomrin opened the door.
“Done?” Elisah asked as he walked past her out of the room. 
“Yes,” Tomrin said. 
“I need to see Master Lazoloth again, Mistress Reydal,” Marten said. 
“Yes, Counselor,” she replied. “And I will let the bailiffs know as soon as I am done with Founder Ocrin.” 
The counselor walked to the door. “Actually, I will do it. You see that Founder Ocrin is taken care of. 
“Yes, Counselor.” 
“And you—” Marten turned to Tomrin. “—take care and good luck.” 
“If Master Lazoloth is innocent it shouldn’t take luck.” 
“Well—” 
“My father will make that determination,” Elisah said. “Come, let’s go.” 
Back Channels [?]
Down the hallway, Rennly Rickot walked towards them. “What’s he doing here?” Tomrin asked. 
Elisah didn’t answer but raised her voice. “No one is allowed back here.”
Rennly strutted forward until his badge was in her face. “I don’t know if you can see, but I am a Censor.”
“Censor or not; only sworn members of the court are allowed back here,” Elisah said.
Rennly jutted a finger around her and pointed at Tomrin. “This is on the court now?”
“He is in my custody, and if you don’t want to create an incident, Censor, I suggest you find your way out the same way you came in. For I am fully within my power to arrest you right here and right now. So please, turn around and exit the hallway the way you came in.”
Rennly eyed her coldly. “As long as you have this little one in hand,” he said, nodding at Tomrin. “I will take my leave.” He whirled around and headed back down the hall. 
Elisah scrunched up her chin and breathed heavily through her nose until he disappeared through a side door without a look back. “Now,” she said and led Tomrin back to the courtroom and into the hallway where Robles and Non waited with the others. Non was busy biting a nail while Robles was confessing to him that he was the one who stole all the glasswork’s gold. “With magic!” 
Tomrin sighed. “Be serious.” 
“What did the lawyer want?” Robles asked. 
“He wanted to tell me he thought Mater Lazoloth was innocent.” 
“Took him long enough to tell you that,” Non said. 
“I know.”
“What now?” Robles asked.
“Now we wait for the bailiff to return to proceed.” 
“So did you see Big Laz?” Non asked. 
“No, just his lawyer.” 
“And the girl,” Robles said. “What about here?”
“Elisah Reydal. She is the judges’ daughter.” 
“Elisah,” Robles mused softly. “But I still saw her first.” 
“She is all yours,” Tomrin said, smiling to himself at how she had told Rennly off.
Lessons Unlearned [?]
The bailiff returned and the court reconvened.
When Tomrin reentered the courtroom he glanced around the spectator seats, feeling watched. The lady Margretta had moved to the opposite corner in the back. She didn’t look at him but when he glanced away he felt her eyes boring into his back. When Judge Reydal entered Tomrin’s surreptitious glances at the dark skinned woman and fair court recorder ceased.
The man began to speak. “Counselors, you’ve seen the evidence. Now I will hear the arguments from the prosecution.” He made no other mention of the secret talks from earlier.
Fredick and Divertay whispered amongst themselves for several seconds. “Um, yes, Judge,” Divertay finally said aloud. “As you could see from the ledgers, there is money in the glasswork’s accounts under Master Lazoloth’s name. If he can’t afford to pay his employees, why is this money there?” 
Master Lazoloth whispered something to Marten on the other side.
“Judge,” Marten said. “It seems it’s the prosecution’s stance that Founder Lazoloth is stealing from his fledgling company by putting monies into his accounts. But to me that seems the opposite of the charge being levied.” 
“This is a symptom, Judge,” Divertay said.
“Of what?” Judge Reydal asked.
“Of corruption, your Honor,” Divertay replied. “If he has money why is he hiding it? Why won’t he pay his employees?”
Tomrin tapped his billfold. It held the undeposited banknotes drawn from the Lazoloth’s account. “How much money are we talking about?” he asked. 
Judge Reydal turned his head towards Tomrin. “This is my court, young man; I ask the questions. So who are you and why do you want to know so badly as to interrupt?” 
Tormrin blinked as he pulled out his pocketbook. “I’m—uh—a Founder as well, of--uh--Godly Glassworks, and I have here--” He began counting out the bills. “—a couple months of pay all drawn from the Glasswork’s account--no—from Master Lazoloth’s account with Farmers First Bank.” Tomrin could feel all the eyes in the courtroom fall on him, not just the judge’s and the Adaman lady’s. 
The judge turned to his nearest bailiff. “I want to see those,” the judge said, pointing vaguely at Tomrin. 
The bailiff pushed his way through the bar and Tomrin placed the small stack of banknotes in the man’s outstretched hands. 
The Balance Money Makes [?]
The man shook his head. “This is crazy.” 
“I believe in the glassworks,” Tomrin said.
“What is going on, Chall?” Judge Reydal asked.
 The bailiff shrugged. “Nothing, your Honor.”
“Will you bring them to me?” Irritation rang in the judge’s question.
“Yes,” Chall said, as if waking himself from a dream. “Yes, your Honor.” 
Tomrin turned his head to see what Fredick and Divertay were thinking. Fredick stared blankly forward. Tomrin hoped this meant the blonde-headed boy knew he was losing. 
The bailiff handed the bills to the judge and one-by-one he listed off the amounts. “Recorder, how much was that?”
“One moment, your Honor,” his daughter said, all protocol.
The judge nodded over the ledger as she read off the cumulative amount. “Master Lazoloth, did you know anything about this?” he asked, finally looking up.
Master Laz glanced at Marten. “No, your Honor,” Master Lazoloth said. “I keep my own records. I thought that account was all but empty.” 
The judge let out a long sigh and rubbed the flowing red sleeves of his robes over his forehead. “Counselor Divertay, have you any further arguments?” 
“Um, your Honor--” 
“Perhaps ones that make some semblance of sense?” Judge Reydal added. The court burst forth with hoots and the judge was forced to bring down his gavel. “Quiet! Feathers! Quiet! Divertay! Counselor, I asked you a question,” he continued when the court had quieted.
Divertay glanced at Fredick then back to the judge. “The prosecution has no further arguments.” 
“Well, Counselor Marten?” Judge Reydal said. “Anything you would like to add before I make my ruling?” 
“No, your Honor.” Tomrin could hear the smile on the man’s face. He had won as he knew he would. 
“Then I am going to sum everything up,” said the judge. “As far as I can tell Master Lazoloth has operated his glass shop with honor and dignity, and based on our side bar it will have money soon to pay all the employees what they are owed. That there is a discrepancy is not his fault, but because one of the employees has not been visiting the bank--for whatever reason--but one can imagine out of loyalty to Master Lazoloth, perhaps something the defense could learn from. So it seems to me that Master Lazoloth has behaved as to reflect well on his own master, the Emperor, and as such I am forced to conclude that he is in fact innocent of the crime of embezzlement. You are free to go. And, you, young man,” the judge said, pointing at Fredick. “Perhaps this has taught you something about the value of loyalty?” 
“Yes, your Honor,” Fredick said, his voice barely audible over the buzz in the court. The man with the golden sword, nearly the loudest of the lot.
“And you, Master Lazoloth, I would suggest you fire him.” 
“I already did.”
“Well, then before I adjourn for the day I would like to thank you on behalf of the Crystal Throne. You are a patriot and an inspiration.” 
“Thank you, your Honor.” 
Judge Reydal turned Fredick. “And you: Please do not darken my courtroom any time soon.” 
“No, Judge,” Fredick said.
Justice Done [?]
The judge glanced at the skylights oozing the last of the day’s light into the room. “This court is now adjourned.” He hammered his gavel down, stood up, and handed the account book to the bailiff and the bank notes to his daughter. He exited to the din of his court rollicking and money changing hands, mostly with the man with the golden sword.
The judge’s daughter motioned to Tomrin and held up his bank notes. A bailiff let him through the bar. The girl slid the bankdraws into Tomrin’s hand. “This was all because you couldn’t go to the bank?” 
“What? No--” 
“It’s probably just as well,” she continued. “Now your master knows who he can trust.” 
Tomrin began putting the bills away. “Thanks, I guess, but I’ve been busy, Court Recorder Reydal.” 
“Don’t get all high and mighty with me,” she replied with a sniff. 
“I’m not,” Tomrin said, glancing away to where Lady Margretta had been sitting. The woman’s seat sat empty. She had to have ran to have left so fast. Tomrin left the girl standing there and went over to shake Master Lazoloth’s hand.
Big Laz thanked him and Marten, and then he and Tomrin headed out into the hallway where everyone should have been waiting for him. Several of the backhalfers were gone and Fredick was nowhere in sight. “Those disloyal dogs are all fired,” Master Lazoloth said of those that didn’t congratulate him on his victory. “And they can go to the feathered Lake of Fire for all I care.” 
Robles laughed but Tomrin was surprised to hear the man swear, true anger in his words. 
“Now what?” Non asked when they reached the front door of the courthouse. Outside the sun hung low over the valleyside. 
“I need to lock the shop for the night,” Master Lazoloth said. 
“I’m already headed back up there,” Tomrin said. “I need to finish up something before I can head home to Woolad tomorrow.” 
“That’s right,” Master Laz said. “You two have lost a friend.” He dug out a brass key. “I guess you can lock the shop, and leave the key under the stone for me tomorrow.” 
“I will,” Tomrin said, taking the key from Lazoloth. He wanted to ask the man about a place to stay, but waited. Tomorrow would be better. He and the others said their goodnights to Master Laz. He looked old and tired--but victorious--and he headed for his house near the center of town. 
“Well, I think this calls for a victory drink,” Robles announced.
“I suppose it does,” Tomrin allowed. He figured a bit of high-spirit tarsk would take the edge off of having to burn the scales on his chest. “I need a strong drink—or three after today.” 
Both Non and Robles agreed.
Chapter 11: The Leviathan’s Mark
 All is not lost, unless the Fates have acted.
		—A Silvan saying lost to the Fifth Age

The Winged Helm Inn’s selection of music throughout the week was usually local singers and players, Rubish men, or Peregrine songbirds and Orish grist, the best of a hard people ground down harder, but tonight the four on the stage were all Orioles. From their dress, they were from Zircos. They and the men  the bar proved lady Margretta’s words true. High-spirit drinking slavers mixed with granite faced Censors in black.
Tomrin and Robles had their backs to the wall. “My aunt’s letter said they were coming,” Robles said.
Tomrin picked up his own tin of high-spirit tarsk—only slightly smaller for it. He slammed the tin down after shooting the burning contents into his mouth and swallowing. “Uhg! Feathers,” he said. The slavers wore vests in the style of the west and their coiled leather strops brushed their neighbors when they moved, but it was the men clad in black silk at the bar who worried Tomrin most. They, like the slavers, were clearly from the Zircos. “We should go somewhere else.”
“It’s going to be the same everywhere,” Non said. 
“You’ll just have to drink harder,” Robles said. “At least Master Lazoloth is cleared. Life will seem better with more tarsk. Be glad we aren’t dead like Gaz.” 
Tomrin raised his other mug filled with low spirit tarsk. “To Menad. To judge Reydal,” he said. “And to not being dead, I guess.” 
“And to his daughter,” Robles added. He raised his own mug and so did Non. They each took a drink. One of the Orioles men at the bar turned and sneered at them and the room. 
“I think I am going to drink this down and head to work,” Tomrin said and finished his low-spirit drink.
In Town [?]
Robles stood. “Let me settle up,” he  said and walked over to the counter with the slavers. When he returned to the booth he drained his mug.
“Leaving so soon, Master Robles?” the doorman asked them on the way out. “Something wrong?” 
“Nothing wrong, Neem. Nothing a little scouring won’t scrub out of the stools.” 
Neem raised his eyebrows. “Oh? Yes. Well, some smells are passing.” The man was Rubish and the way he wrinkled his nose as he glanced inside, it was clear he too had little love for these Orioles invaders. “But gold is gold.” 
“Yes it is, Master Neem,” Robles said, bowing. “Good evening.” 
Neem bowed. “The same to you Master Robles, and your friends.” 
They stepped on to the street.
A distant boom echoed up the valley. Foom!  A light, red fire, burst from a house deep within Menad’s heart. The flames twisted with black smoke, and poured into the night sky. Something had exploded down in Menad.
“Isn’t that where Master Lazoloth lives?” Non asked. 
“I don’t know--” said Tomrin. He had only been at the Master’s house once before. 
“It looks right,” Robles said, glancing over at Tomrin, an unusual seriousness lined his face. “So I would say it is.” 
“No,” Tomrin said. “Don’t try to shine me on. That has to be the apothecary’s again.” 
“He’s not,” Non said. “I can tell it’s Big Laz’s house. I’m sure of it.” 
Robles swung his pack over his back and took off running. Tomrin legs lurched as he hurried after. “I can’t believe it.”
“I know, I know,” Robles said without slowing. 
Tomrin’s body didn’t seem to register anything. He realized that he wasn’t afraid. He was numb. He ran detached, his mind disembodied. It was like this was all happening to someone else.
A Memory of Fire [?]
A memory from his youth, one Tomrin suppressed out of shame, came back to him again. 
He saw Master Davit Rickot, Rennly’s father, dying, burned up in the flames as they consumed the Ocrin carpentry shop. Now any fire burning out of control frightened Tomrin. The thought of it made him feel very uneasy at the smoke dancing up ahead. The billowing smoke glowed as it rose while houses hid the flames as the temple several streets removed towered up while the houses on the street, wooden buildings dry from the unseasonable spring, seemed ready tinder.
Fire bells tolled from the firehouse on Salud Street, and the courthouse bells rang as the street filled with gawkers. Constables ran rope to cordon rope off the street adhead, blocking them. 
“No through!” a constable shouted inarticulately from the line. Tomrin followed Robles closely, as he punched a way through the mass of people. 
“We are firecrew,” Robles told the constable.
The man began to raise the rope, but stopped. “Where are your badges?” he said. “And your gear?” 
Tomrin looked beyond the man to the burning house. Two green fire trucks pulled by massive horses, tall and brown, were pulling up on the southside past the Hall of Victory to a stop in front of the house. 
“The other houses could catch fire and you are asking for your badges?” Robles said. “If that happens, constable--” Robles folded and unfolded his arms. “That’s on you.” 
The man frowned and held up the rope. 
“You two are the firefighters,” Non said. 
Tomrin and Robles ducked under the line leaving him and the constable there. 
Firecrew [?]
Tomrin waited until he and Robles were out of earshot. “I’m not firecrew,” he said. 
“I was,” Robles retorted. “That’s good enough for both of us.” The grin he shot Tomrin was a sad one. As they neared the house, the question of the wisdom of following where Robles led skittered across Tomrin’s brain. They weren’t supposed here, and now they were in a mix of people, official men, constables, a Censor and firecrew.
The Censor was Rennly.  
The itch on Tomrin’s chest screamed at him to get away, to run, to hide. “What the feather are they doing here?” he muttered. When he realized the others included Fredick and Jan with Rennly. The three of them were standing with two Orioles men as they spoke with a stocky Rubish man in a green fire hat. 
“Marshal Tamerton,” Robles said, “What’s going on?”
Other men had jumped from the trucks. In teams of four they began pumping water onto the blaze. Gallons of water spewed from the lines connected to the massive brass pots hauled on the back of the green trucks. Two two-men teams holding the hoses warred with water swords as they sprayed the fire. 
“My men are killing this fire until I hear different—from the Emperor, or from a Daughter of Heaven’s Glory, or from Seasargus himself,” Tamerton said to him, but his words seemed spoken mostly at Rennly.
“I could have your interdicted,” Rennly said. “And you Robles, and Tomrin--I’m glad you’re here for this.” 
“This? What is this?” Robles said. “Is Master Laz inside? Have you two decided to take Fredick’s complaints into your own hands?” 
“He’s in there. He’s started this fire. Not us,” Fredick said. 
“He tried to kill us,” said Rennly. 
“He wouldn’t do that,” Tomrin said. “You’re talking nonsense.”
“Not unless he had something to hide,” Fredick shot back. 
“What does he have to hide?” asked Tomrin. “You attacked him and he beat you, now your revenge is to burn his place? For making you look a fool? That’s more likely what this is.” 
Rennly scoffed. “All I’m saying, Marshal, is exercise caution. Master Lazoloth is a dangerous man.” 
“Noted,” the marshal said. “But only firecrew should be here. I’d feel better if—” 
Fire broke a window and cut off his words. Lines of water shot in through the new hole as smoke boiled out. 
“Tamerton, we need to send someone inside to find Master Lazoloth.” 
The marshal glanced at Robles. “This isn’t a good idea, Robles. I know you’d be fine but—” 
“I’m not asking you to risk your own men, Marshal.” Robles pointed to himself and at Tomrin. “Us.” 
Tomerton nodded slightly, and turned and shouted. “Two coats!”
“I don’t know about this, Robles,” Tormin said. 
“You already saved Laz once today; I understand if you don’t want to have to save him again.”
Fog in the Air [?]
Robles pointed at Fredick. “It might be a good idea if you are not here when I return, especially if I’m a dead man walking.” 
Fredick stepped back at the vehemence of Robles’ words, but a young Rubish boy with the coats arrived.
“I don’t know the first thing about fires,” Tomrin said as he pulled on his coat. 
“You almost saved Rennly’s father,” Robles said and glanced around as if looking for Rennly. But Rennly and Fredick had stepped away to argue more with Tamerton, now retreated to his men.
Tomrin shrugged. He wasn’t sure if he shrugged because he couldn’t believe that or because of how much the coat weighed. “Strange things happen.” That was a statement as true as time and a fact even he could not stand to disbelieve. “I once promised myself I’d never do this again.”
“Feathers,” Robles cursed. “We can’t back out now.”
“I’m not backing out.”
Foregoing the hat offered to him, Robles pulled one from his pack. “Good. Because we really need to save Master Lazoloth now if we are going to.”
“Your own fire hat?” Tomrin asked.
 “Never know when it might come in handy,” Robles said. A fireman held out an ax and Robles and grabbed it and crouched down. “Let’s go; Master Lazoloth is waiting.” 
“I don’t know what I am doing,” Tomrin moaned, slouching down.
“Just stay low,” Robles said. “And don’t breathe the smoke.” 
Into the Fire [?]
Robles attacked the door with his ax. 
Kthug! Kthug! 
“Don’t breathe the smoke,” Tomrin muttered to himself as he followed Robles inside, his words lost in the crackle and howl of the fire. 
Horses, carved in ivory and running, sat displayed on a front dresser by the door. Smoke ran along the ceiling as fire licked the walls. Two painted horses on opposite walls already burned steadily while a painting of a white stallion on the far wall at the end of the hall wasn’t burning yet. It was the only thing not leaking smoke or covered in it.
Robles opened a closet. “Not here!” Robles’ voice came to him in a bit of an echo. 
The oven-like heat in the house beaded sweat on Tomrin’s forehead and the smell of smoke robbed the air of breathability. “We need to hurry,” he croaked. 
“We’re not splitting up,” Robles said. “That’s how people die!” 
“I’m saying we need to hurry!” They were both shouting to be heard over the fire’s roar.
Robles put a hand on Tomrin’s coated shoulder. “Steady, now. We’ll find him. We’ll find Master Laz. Don’t worry.”
As they headed up the flight of stairs to the second level, Tomrin began to lose hope. The smoke was thick and pushed them to their knees. It would certainly overmaster their efforts now as it boiled chest high down the hall, but the third door Robles opened, opened to a bedroom.
Master Lazoloth lay on the floor inside. 
True Death [?]
They crawled over to Lazoloth and crouched down next to him. 
Blood leaked from cuts where bits of steel and wood punctured his skin. A dark pool had formed under the man. Tomrin couldn’t understand it. Master Lazoloth looked dead-eyed up at the smoke on the ceiling. Robles touched his neck and Tormin glanced away as Robles shook his head. 
“Dead? Master Lazoloth dead?” Tomrin asked.
“Maybe, maybe not. That’s how smoke works.” 
“What are we going to…” 
Brown cylinders—the same as at the glass shop—but laying flat, lay stacked in the closet where instead of clothes hanging on hooks or equipment hanging Master Laz had his insurance and a rill of red fire leaked from one nozzle, spewing like blood.
“To what?”
“We should leave,” Tomrin said.
“What about Big Laz?” Robles said. 
“He’s as dead as Davit Rickot was.”
“No, he might not be.”
Tomrin bent and pried a flint striker from Big Laz’s dead hand. “He killed himself.”
“What?” Robles said. 
“We need to go!” Tomrin pulled on Robles’ shoulder and pointed at the cylinders. “We need to get out of here before they all go!” 
“We take him out!”
Robles seemed distracted. Tomrin pulled his face around to see into the closes. “Look!”
“Oh, feathers!” His friend’s voice sounded only a whisper over the flames. “Help me get him on my back!” He knelt down at Master Lazoloth’s head and reached back. “His hands!” Tomrin picked up the master’s arms and handed them to his friend, then like a bag of grain, Robles grabbed and hitched the old man over his shoulder. 
Tomrin wasn’t sure how, but his friend had a feral strength, a brute power of an Orish farmhand and the will of an iron prod to go with it. He stood in the smoke and staggered forward to fall, gasping for breath, but he pulled the master out of the room into the hall and down the steps.
He all but ran with him on his back to the front door.
A Ticking Bomb [?]
Only once on the street did Robles sag and not stand back up quickly. 
Firecrew ran to him as Tomrin bent over and peeled Master Laz’s blood-slicked body off of him. Tomrin shouted in Robles’ ear. “We have to get further away!” 
Tamerton was there with the others. Tomrin shouted at him. “Get away! Everyone get back!” In with the bottled gas there had been bottled air. If both gas and air were to go at once... “The whole block could go!” 
“Wha--?” The fire marshal tried to ask. 
The first explosion knocked the words right out of the man’s mouth. Woomph! 
The blast stiff armed Tomrin in the chest, but it was the second explosion that split the house. KACAHAK! Boards and timbers shot through the air above them. The entire second story disintegrated and the burning bits and shattered pieces rained down.
Robles began shouting and coughing. “Stop the spread! Stop the spread! The whole block might go!” The flaming bits had sailed to other houses and to the nearby apothecary shop.
The fire marshal gained his composure first it seemed, though he had his hands on his head, holding on his hat like a simpleton and shouting the same thing to anyone curious enough to ask for orders. “Don’t let the fire spread!”
Robles, however continued to shout, and cough and yell at everyone, even the marshal if he or they stopped to question him as he and they stomped the  flames and tried to snuff the danger all around the nearby houses.
Everyone but Tomrin and Master Laz.
A Danger Passed On [?]
Tomrin closed the man’s eyes with a finger.
The dead man’s chest where his shirt had blown open exposed an area above his silent heart. A ring of metallic scales glinted red in the firelight. The scales glinted and shined around an angry, half-healed wound. The strange Adaman woman, Margretta, from the courthouse had hinted at something connecting them.
Tomrin brushed the bloody rent in Master Laz’s shirt out of the way, and checked the button of purple flesh. Laz, too, used fire to burn his scales off it seemed. There was much Tomrin wished to ask him, not too late for that. Frustration built up inside Tomrin until he feared he might burst. He wanted to kill Fredick or himself or both. But violence would not make him whole. A bit of himself now lay dead on the street before him.
“What are you doing,” Rennly asked as he pulled Tomrin’s shoulder.
Tomrin snatched his fingers away and grabbed the ax handle as he stood, turning and staring at Rennly—and Fredick. The blond boy had not disappeared as Robles had ordered. Two other Orioles men stood with them now. Robles loped up, behind. The two new men wore vests and whips rested on their hips. Slavers.
Tomrin’s grip vibrated the ax. “You brought slavers into this, Fredick?” he said. An ax wasn’t a civilized weapon, but it was a weapon.
“Our runaways are our own business,” the stockier of the two slavers said.
“Cut off the dead man’s shirt,” the taller said. “We’ll see if he is one of ours.” 
“Master Laz was free!” Tomrin hefted the ax in his hands. “If you touch him...”
The shorter man pulled out his whip. “No Peregrine is free in the eyes of the gods.”
Robles who grabbed his ax. “Tomrin, don’t. “One man is dead here tonight. We want no more.” He coughed almost exaggeratedly into his hand. “I know I don’t.” He coughed again. “We nor our gods are blood thirsty.”
“We are civilized men,” Tamerton said, picking up Robles’s idea and parroting it back like a good leader. “Now that this man is dead, for love of all we find holy, no more must die here tonight.”
“We have the fire under control?” Tomrin asked.
“Yes,” Tamerton said. “The fire is under control.”
A Taste of It [?]
“The rest of you are a bunch of vultures,” Tomrin said.
The fat slaver turned to the taller one. “Wasn’t one of our boys known for his lip?” The man brushed his coiled leather whip against Master Laz’s body. 
“Stop it, now,” Rennly said, judging the Tamerton’s men. “Can’t you see this man was Marked by the demons? His body must be cleansed and disposed of properly. The gods saw a problem and the gods rooted it out.”
“Maybe the boy then?” the stocky slaver said. His mad eyes fixed on the ax in Robles’ hands. He was the kind that hated other Orioles that had gone soft. The man stared as if judging Robles’s will to use it. The taller man slapped him on the arm.
“Look,” he said pointing. “It is the Leviathan’s Mark. The man clearly manufactured this little display.”
“He or Fredick,” Tomrin said.
“I did not! Lazoloth has been manipulating all of us this whole time,” Frederick said.
“You wanted to take the shop—even the stuff that was in Lazoloth’s house?” Tomrin levied. “We’d never know if you planted the bomb.”
The short man’s whip slipped from his hand as he gasped. “Wah!” He was still staring like an idiot at the scales growing on Lazoloth’s chest.
His partner slapped his head. “Run your eyes, and not your mouth for a change, Sam.” 
The man crouched and picking up the whip. The way he clutched it to his own chest was like it now possessed some darker magic--as if the pain was not enough; it had to feel more painful.
Somehow it seemed it might.

By the Power of the Gods [?]
Rennly held out his hands and brushed the two men back. “By the Tree,” he said as if remembering to make the sign of the worldtree. His hand rose as if along its tall main trunk. “And the three, of  Freafrasus, of Resalus--” He swung his open hand around the branches and leaves of the tree in a loop before descending back down the trunk. “And Daedalus, and all those--” He waved his hand out over Master Lazoloth. “--and all those who now go before us into the second heaven. For those departed headed for the Lake of Fire, we pray for their deliverance from the demons and their servants on Enfernus or on Gearthrum, wherever they may be. By the gods.”
“By the gods,” others echoed.
No words came out of Tomrin’s mouth. His eyes locked on the smaller slaver. 
The man stared back with hate in his heart; he wanted dearly to use his whip. “I still think we should check the boy,” he drawled.
A clammy feeling inched up Tomrin’s fingers and headed for his heart. He feared he something was going to happen. 
Rennly turned to the man. “You were told to shut your mouth, Sam? That your name? I don’t care. Shut your mouth,” he said, his voice taking on a lilt. “From what I heard this man was like a father, and I know what a terrible thing it is to lose a father.” Rennly paused a second and glanced at Tomrin. The boy’s green eyes struck him as cruel and callous. “I know. I lost my own in a similar blaze.” 
“But he could be a slave,” the man persisted stoutly.
“You fool,” Rennly said, rounding on him. “I’ve known Master Ocrin since he and I were children and I know for a fact—” He glanced at Robles. “—Tomrin Ocrin is owned by the Emperor.”
“But--” 
“Maybe in other parts of the world!” Rennly said, loudly, “But no! Here the gods’ will be done. And I am their rod and their staff. I am their voice here, and if I say this one is a slave and that he is duly owned by the Emperor, then he is in the eyes of any slaver in this land or any other. Do not forget your place here or anywhere. For I speak for the gods. Their will be done!”
“One of you killed Master Lazoloth,” Tomrin said.
“No one killed Master Lazoloth,” Rennly replied. “He killed himself. I was here, Tomrin! I saw. Now you can take my word for it.” The boy sneered and razor sharp teeth appeared in his mouth like tiny white daggers. Rennly was the same vicious animal all those years ago. 
Tomrin saw it clearly for a moment and shuddered. It didn’t matter was said here. Rennly manipulated every situation and everyone. 
He hates me for knowing it.
Battle Lines [?]
“Men may never know the full story,” Robles said loudly. “And Master Lazoloth can speak for himself in the heavenly courts.”
“After so many years of sin,” said Rennly mused. “His purification will be some ordeal. Easier maybe for a Peregrine to live the years, the decades, with the scales, but now I’m almost tempted to believe maybe he was embezzling funds after all.”
“That was a lie, now as then,” Tormin couldn’t believe what he was hearing. “They are just going to keep coming after us.” 
“Maybe,” Robles said. “But Fredick isn’t that stupid.” He turned to Fredick and ground the ax into the street’s dirt. “He would be wise to let things be.” 
“Are you threatening me again?” Fredick asked. 
“I’m simply cautioning you that I am a citizen of this land. I will not light the gas. Will you? The shop is ours now. With Master Lazoloth dead, control comes to us.”
“You may be a citizen,” Fredick said, but he pointed to Tomrin. “He, however, he is still a slave.” 
“What are you doing?” Rennly said to Fredick. “This is not the time for fighting over the corpse of this man’s company. You are not in Zircos..”
Fredick turn to Rennly. “But this was all--” Fredick said.
“This! This is a mess on my street! On the gods’ street!” Rennly said, harshly cutting Fredick’s words. “I need it cleaned up!”
“But—”
Rennly scowled at Fredick. “But nothing! I’m not going to stand here and listen to you degrade yourself by arguing with a slave. How about you make yourself useful and have a constable send a wagon.” 
“Yes, of course,” Fredick said and bowed. He left for the cordon. 
The marshal clapped his hands and then pretended to take command. Robles and Tomrin were then asked to leave and return to the cordon with everyone else. “I should have never touched Fredick,” Tomrin said as he and Robles waited behind the cordon with Non for the wagon to take Master Laz’s dead body to the temple. “I don’t even know what I was thinking.”
“I don’t know about you, but I need some sleep,” Robles said.
“Yes,” Tomrin agreed. “There is nothing more we can do here tonight.” 
They left the cordon and Robles and Non peeled off until Tomrin climbed the eastern highway alone. When he came to Arcade Avenue, the soft welcoming light of the Winged Helm Inn beckoned with laughter and music, but he walked on, down Arcade.
He had the shop key in his pocket and needed to use it. Master Lazoloth was dead, but he still needed to fulfill the trust the old man had put in him. He needed to lock the shop. Moreover, he still had one last thing he needed to do before he could leave for Woolad in the morning. 
He needed to burn off his feathered scales.
Chapter 12: Out of the Furnace
	The heaven’s have them. Now, so do we.
		—From The Arcanist’s Cookbook, the forbidden precursor to The Arcanist’s Handbook

The shop door closed; from the back, the Scarab’s glass eyes grew dark.
Tomrin walked over and grabbed a length of thumb-width glass and slipped on his goggles over his eyes. Of all the sizes of glass, one size rod worked best. And molten glass worked the best of all the methods he had tried. He needed this glass shop, if for no other reason. 
“Right,” he said, trying to prepare himself.
He hoped one day he would find a better method than burning. Cutting off the scales made him bleed like a stuck goat, and still he needed to cauterize the wound. A bird chirp outside and Tomrin found it hard to focus.
Inside the mass twisted and writhed, like a snake coiled and bursting to get out. It’s not me, Tormin told himself. The evil wasn’t him. The last refrain of a childhood rhyme tracked through his mind as he spun the tip of the rod in the orange flame. Pinioned to the Shadow Line. 
The chime ultimately claimed the shadow line was imaginary but somehow just as real as the dark and light it seperated, an idea which might have made sense in his dreams, but now was as impenetrable as the depths of space above and which he might spy on with the scope when it was working.
“Feathered riddles,” he muttered to himself as he removed the glass rod from the flame, and sighed. “Forgot to take off my shirt.”
A Feeling [?]
He set down the glowing rod and pulled off his goggles. “I am out of it,” he said. His words spoken aloud filled the shop with an eerie presence but he knew that it was just in his mind as stared at the Scarab, the only other thing around, save for the glasswork god in the window. “Robles is going to have nothing to do with you tomorrow,” he told the thing. “And me just sitting here wasting time. I could be sleeping.”
With Master Laz dead his happy life was melting away. It was disappearing into the creeping shadows of the shop, like dripping blood covering his eyes. He let out a heavy sigh. He still had to do what he’d come to do. 
He walked over to the front door and checked it was locked. No one would be able to see exactly what he was doing from outside through the stained glass. The bubbles and waves in the glass were on purpose—to split the light and scatter it without being opaque. 
Tomrin wondered how many times Big Laz had done as he prepared to do now.
The Demon’s Brand [?]
Taking off his shirt he whipped it over his shoulder.
Tomrin could smell the smoke of Laz’ house on it. He walked back to his workstation. The man’s lifeless eyes flashed in the stars of Tomrin’s mind. “Feathers!” he swore to banish the sight. 
Dropping his shirt on his stool, he sat back down and picked up the glass rod with one hand. He lowered his goggles with the other. After a minute the flame heated the rod to a purple glow.
Tomrin’s fingers clamped the rod. His arms shook. “Do it!” he commanded himself. His words cast spit through his clenched teeth and his head wanted to split from the strain. His eyes wandered. This was going to hurt like the seven heavens and the nine hells combined! It always did.
Half-growling, half yelling, he pushed the red hot end of the glass into his chest and seared his flesh and scales.  A screech exited his throat and left his lips. “Eerrerrh!” The reek of burning skin and pain erupted from the wound as he pressed. He wondered if he’d pressed too hard this time.
It felt like the glass rod pierced down to his heart and burned it dead.
Flaming Heart [?]
Tomrin slammed the rod down on the bench. “Feathers!” he cursed as his hands cramped in fists. He stifled a yell as he caught himself finally coming through the initial pain.
A shadow moved outside the shop’s door. Someone knocked. Fump. Fump. 
“Open up!” It was a woman’s voice. A Daughter of Heaven’s Glory had found him. “I know you’re in there, Tomrin Ocrin.” 
Tomrin stood and quickly pulled on his shirt. Feathered feathers!
His pain kept him focused and  tried not to imagine Master Laz trapped like he was now. He pulled his stinky shirt down over his clammy skin, hiding the cauterized wound. He walked slowly to the door and unlatched the lock. Pushing the glass-encased lead with his right hand while his chest screamed at him. 
A hand from outside pulled the door the rest of the way open. “That’s how you open the door?” 
“You?” Tormin said. The Adaman woman from the court stood before him, holding the door open for herself. 
“And that’s how you address a lady?” she said as she stepped inside, brushing past him as if he were but a slave improperly opening the door for her. 
“The shop is not open,” Tomrin said sharply. He needed to kick this woman out so he could massage the area around the burn until the pain subsided. “You’ll have to come back tomorrow.” 
The woman turned to him, her dark figure a darker ghost in the star light. “You are awfully curt with someone who has just helped save you untold trouble, young man.” 
“Is that what you think?” Tomrin said. This woman was becoming quite a nuisance. “And I am not as young as I look, lady Margretta. The shop I’m afraid is closed.” He could be proper if he chose. 
“Ha,” she scoffed. “Is that so?” 
“Yes, when I cut my beard--” He was not the kind to use his scraggly facial hair as a deflection, but in the low light he supposed he looked fine. “We aren’t selling right now and we certainly aren’t buying.”
In the Shadows [?]
“Just close the door,” she commanded. “The hour is late.” 
“Yes, and I was just leaving,” Tomrin stuck his head out the door. Down the street the Orish Counselor waved to him, raising his hand in a gentle greeting. Tomrin waved back before grabbing the door handle and pulling it closed. “Not going to have your lawyer with you?”
“I’m afraid Marten is--” She smacked her lips. “--Overly protective.” 
“A welcome trait in a lawyer, I suppose,” Tomrin said. He felt he was doing well, not quaking in his boots, trapped as he was and in so much pain. “But, you say: The hour is late. You think you helped Master Laz, but no, he is dead.” 
“Dead?” She sounded surprised, and the starlight shifted on her cheeks revealed she was scowling. 
“He killed himself,” Tomrin added. 
“The fire and the explosion?” 
“Yes. And now because you didn’t actually save him, he’s dead.” 
Margretta scoffed again. “That choice was always yours.” 
Tomrin cooled. “You should go.” 
“Oh?” Margretta said. “You think it is that easy to get rid of me?” 
“I don’t know.”
She stepped closer and invaded his personal space. “You don’t know much.” Her eyes were dark pools as she looked down her nose at him. “I bet you don’t even know what your own name means, Master Tomrin.” 
“It means: happy is he who serves the gods,” Tomrin replied.
“Ha. Close, yet no,’’ Lady Magretta said. “The name Tomrin does not come from Old Silvan, but from Old High Orish, from the words: To-merl-in. Literally meaning the wise black bird.” 
An image from his nightmare flashed through his mind, and Tomrin glanced to the dark Seasargus in the window. “Not that one,” the woman said in a low voice. “The other one.”
“I do not believe in fairytales,” Tormin said. “Even if I am just a simple glass man.” 
“Really?” Margretta said and turned and walked over to the back bench where the torch gently purred. “Glass?” She held up the rod he’d used to burn the Leviathan’s Mark from his chest. “This is nothing then?” 
“Glass is not nothing,” Tomrin replied, and walked over to her and snatched away the rod from her hand. He waved it. “Glass is always more than glass to me.” 
“That’s deep,” she said. “And air is more than air, water is more than water.” 
Her insulting tone irked him. “You clearly don’t know the first thing about glass,” Tomrin said.
 Margretta bent and put her eye to the eyepiece. “Spying on Serathus is what you are doing with it?” 
“It’s not pointed at Serathus.”
“No, it’s not. I see nothing.” She bent back up. “But you could use it to spy on the heavens.” She pointed at him, or maybe at his chest. “Think. If you can see them, can’t they see you?”
A Forked Tongue [?]
Tomrin half-hummed, half-groaned; wound felt like she was jabbing him though she wasn’t even touching him. If she was a Dark Daughter she was certainly sent by demons to torment him. “Line of sight?” he said obliquely. “Why are you here?” Tomrin asked. He didn’t want to deal with the woman a minute longer. “You must know I’m not going anywhere with you?” 
“I’ve met fools less stubborn than you, Tomrin Ocrin. Your master is dead...as I hear from you, and now slaver trouble crawls all over Menad. This city is a dead cow rotting in the sun covered in ants, filled to the brim with maggots.”
“You have no argument from me, but I am heading home tomorrow.” 
“Leaving all of it?”
“By the time I return, they should be gone. I plan to make the shop better than ever for those that wish to work here.”
“Dreams are great, but your Master Lazoloth showed what happens when you run out of cold hard currency.”
“The Sapphire Front will pay us. Master Laz wasted money, I won’t.”
“So you are smarter than him?” 
“In some ways, yes.” 
The woman cackled. “And you think that makes you qualified?” 
“Why wouldn’t it?” 
“Look at you. Look at the color of your skin. I would say you have learned nothing. The races may live together in this town, but that doesn’t change the fact that old thoughts and old ways don’t die by themselves--especially when the gods themselves reinforce those ideas.” 
“The shop won’t hire people like Fredick anymore. I will hire people I, and Robles, and Non, and the others trust.” 
“So you do trust people, just not me? Or the emperor?”
Tomrin walked to the door, feeling her close behind him, mimicking his steps in the dark, making him feel awkward and self conscious. “I don’t know you, and I was just leaving.” He reached out to the handle. 
Her hand brushed his.
He pulled away. “Ah.” 
She held the handle as she spoke. “It’s just that easy for them to know,” she said. “Perhaps how they knew about your master?” 
“If they know about me why do nothing until now?” 
“Laz was old. Perhaps they thought to use him as bait.” 
“Great,” Tomrin breathed. 
“I don’t think I need to warn you that you are not as old and as broken down as your Master.”
“What?”
“That fact should factor into your own thinking.”
He rubbed his hand where she touched him, wishing she would leave so he could scream away the pain in his chest. “I don’t know anything about anything,” he said. “Other than this: I think you should go. It is late, and your lawyer friend is waiting for you. I think you said he was protective? He’s probably worried.”
“I said he was protective, yes,” Margretta replied, no menace in her voice, just exasperation as she moved around him. “But not of me. He worries about what will happen to you, should you come before the bar, and they get a load of you… The machinations.” 
Truth? [?]
“What machinations?” Tomrin said.
“There are facts you should at least try to begin to comprehend,” Margretta said slowly.
“A lecture?” Tomrin muttered. “I have a fuzzy memory of my last. Perhaps write it down?”
“Think of it this way: Two...” Margretta paused. “Two men. They were dressed in black and were waiting for you outside at your home. They were hiding, sneaking about. It seems an Orish slave thought he might have seen something earlier today and with a bit of tarks his opened his mouth real wide.” 
Tomrin didn’t want to believe what he was hearing. “Waiting?”
“Not anymore,” Margretta said. “Don’t worry. Marten has killed before.” 
“He said…” Tomrin replied, not knowing where to begin.
“It has been a while for him, though not the first man in black.” 
“He killed Censors before then?” 
“I didn’t say they were Censors,” Margretta replied coolly. “But if you worry what they might know… You might also worry that I and Marten can’t be everywhere. It was only a chance that he thought to check on you. Me, I don’t like to believe in chance. I certainly do not like to rely on it. Do you?” 
“No. I don’t.” 
She opened the door. “That is wise--the first smart thing you said today.” 
Leaving [?]
“And Tomrin,” she said. “Think about what I’ve said. There is much for you to learn. For you, even if you are as smart as you think. Maybe getting out of town will clear your head. As far as I can tell, you shouldn’t wait.” She slipped out of the shop, shutting the door behind her. 
Tomin put his back to the door as her opaque shadow receded through the gray glass of the tree. 
Tomrin rubbed his chest and swore. “Feathers.” 
Finally, he pulled the key from his pocket and he locked the door. Tich. He leaned back against the cool lead and rouch cut glass a moment. After a minute he pushed away and walked over to his bench. His torch still burned bright. He had been staring at it as it burned gas for no reason. Censors. Did they know? Those that did were dead--if the lady wasn’t lying.
Chapter 13: The Road to Woolad
	It is a burden to always keep up the facade, but if you value your life and those you love, you never lower it.
		—From the Living Book of the Dead

Tomrin cut the gas and the room fell into silent darkness as the halo of light on his bench disappeared into an after image. Making blindly for the back door he exited the building. Is this the last time? He pushed the door. Tuk. Double testing the lock, he headed down and around the alley to Arcade Avenue. Margretta and her man were gone. The street lay empty. The street came alive at the corner as patrons, those who lived elsewhere in the city, stumbled from the Winged Helm Inn. Tomrin avoided the light spilling from the door, and walked behind two Rubish men. They complained they’d spent too much money and slurred both the Orioles invaders and their speech. They turned down the highway and Tomrin turned up.
He glanced from shadow to shadow as the highway switched back and forth. The brittle, heat-killed bushes on the side of the road might have hidden anyone. He had sworn it had been Gaz he’d seen earlier in the day on his street, but that mystery seemed solved now. It hadn’t been Gaz. Now black clad men, if Margretta could be believed, had run into a terminal case of counseling. 
As his house neared, his heart began to pound. The gloom started only a few feet in front of him now and he imagined Censors lurking in the dark. His house loomed up over the street. A light flickered in the kitchen window. Why was Mistress Carl up so feathered late? Her shadow—or another’s—moved in the window?
 He touched the step with his boot and stepped up lightly. 
Retired [?]
Opening the door he stepped inside.
Mistress Carl emerged from the kitchen. He considered walking around her without saying a word. “It’s late again, Master Tomrin,” she said. The light from the kitchen illuminated her left side, tossing the right side of her face in shadows. 
“You shouldn’t have waited up for me,” Tomrin said, trying to hide a grimace of pain.
“Are you hurt?” 
“I’m just tired,” Tomrin said and began walking around her.
“Aren’t we all?” she said as he walked past her.
He headed for the stairs in the dark, leaving her watching after him. Entering his darkened bedroom, he closed the door and laid down. His head hit a stack of clothes, his clean laundry. He pushed the clothes onto the floor, before groaning to sit up. Pulling his boots off, he laid back down and undressed down to his small clothes, but left his shirt on. 
For a long while he lay in the dark staring at the black ceiling. Closing his eyes brought memories of Master Lazoloth lay on the ground dead, his gray hair singed and awry, his eyes staring blankly up at the stars…
An Old Darkness [?]
With fingers sharp as glass, a woman poked at him from the darkness. 
She jabbed his arms and stabbed his chest. She knifed him in the legs and he was powerless to move. Her needle-sharp claws stabbed him, and his rope-bound body jerked with each sharp prod, each painful poke. He struggled to find her in the dark, but a dizziness rocked him, like he was falling into an abyss and she jabbed and jabbed, unrelentingly. The darkness around him blinded him. Only shadows met him as he fell and all around, if there were flames, they were dark and if they flickered gave off no heat but sucked in light.
He tried to yell at the woman to stop, but he could say nothing as she cut him with her hardened nails and words honed to cut deeper. “You are a lie,” she crowed as she sank her razor-sharp fingernails into his right hip. 
Tomrin thrashed against the pain. Needles of pain ripped through him forcing him awake. His arms flailed and his knuckles slammed into the wall. Bruk! Of all his dreams this one was the first and worst.
He grabbed his hand. Feathers. The needles of pain were gone, but his barked knuckles hurt.
Morning’s twilight filled his room with a sullen gloom—and Tomrin with irritation. He bit the ripped flesh off one of his knuckles and swallowed the sickly skin after chewing, disbelieving the morning. He wanted to roll over and go back to sleep, but he had to get up.
He kicked his legs off the bed. Pain from his chest wound made him groan. “Gah!”
His eyes hurt from lack of sleep and he rubbed them despite them having no sleep-sand in them. “Feathers. It’s too early to be alive,” he muttered to himself and grabbed a pair of clean pants off the floor, his tan ones, just baggy enough to be comfortable and perfect for travel. 
He glanced at his pack in the corner near the door and sighed. Standing he walked over and grabbed his pack before walking back to his bed and sitting back down. He picked up his clothes and stuffed them all into the leather satchel. Enough for ten weeks. He was only going to be gone two days, maybe three, but he could feel an argument with Mistress Carl coming on and wanted to head it off. If she believed he was willing to move out, maybe she would try harder to keep him. He slipped his feet into his boots and laced up.
Standing, he slung his pack over his shoulder. He adjusted it but it still put pressure on his chest wound. Can’t helped it. He patted the papers in his pocket, checking them. Also in the pocket he found the brass shop key. Memories from the day before flooded back. Stomping out into the hallways, he headed down the stairs. Ben was nowhere to be found but Mistress Carl was in the kitchen. She blinked several times. “Leaving and its early enough for breakfast—” 
“I decided, I am heading home,” Tomrin said. He had no time for breakfast. Not enough to wait around for her to make it. “I forgot to mention.” 
“It was late.” She rubbed her hands on her apron. 
Tomrin ticked off a couple other reasons in his head. Master Laz dying, and Rennly, and Fredick—and Gaz. “Yes. It was late and I have a funeral tomorrow, and Sheep Shearing Days at home,” Tomrin said, changing the subject.
“And are you coming back here?” Mistress Carl said. 
“I don’t know,” he said. “Maybe I will find another place. I was going to ask Master Lazoloth, but he’s dead now.” 
“What?” Mistress Carl said. “Dead?”
“Yes, dead. I forgot to mention. It was late.”
“Dead?”
Tomrin headed for the door and opened it. “Yes, dead.” He left the house and closed the door behind himself, not waiting for another question.
Freedom [?]
A low hanging sun peeked over the rim of the valley. 
It has been months since Tomrin had seen the sun rise, and now the glare was right in his eyes, yet the morning air was brisk and welcome. As he made his way down the street he greeted those who greeted him. They were a fresher bunch than he was used to, men and women heading off to work seemingly happy to be alive at this hour. You should wake up earlier more often. The lack of heat itself was more than welcome. The Winged Helm Inn, however, never slept; now was no different. From the stables to the commons, grooms moved with the slow deliberate pace of too much tarsk the night before. Tomrin walked past, a wave for the porter on duty. 
At the glassworks, Non stood out front, again. Tomrin resisted the urge to call to him as he neared. There were others on the street, men and women, who worked in the various shops on Arcade Avenue. Their strides were filled with purpose. It wasn’t a strange hour for them to be in such a rush, even if it was for him. If only I didn’t hate mornings so much. 
“Morning,” Non said. “You’re here early.”
“Morning,” Tomrin replied, rubbing a bit of sleep from his eyes that he had missed. “I have the key.” He pulled the warm brass from his pocket and Non stepped aside. 
“It’s so strange, Master Lazoloth--and everything,” Non said. 
Tomrin slid the key into the keyhole on the glassdoor. “I know.” He turned the key. Chict. He opened the door looking at the gray tree of glass itself so near the color of lead. “It feels so wrong.” 
“But it will be nice not having to deal with Fredick.” 
Tomrin held the door open and followed Non inside. “True.” 
Non suddenly stopped and bent down. “Umm this doesn’t look good…”
Grave News [?]
Tomrin stepped inside around Non. “What?” he asked, closing the door.
Non handed him a letter. “Post in the middle of the night?” 
The letter had been dropped into the mail slot between the window and door and had missed the bin. Tomrin tore open the envelope and extracted the letter. The greeting was from the undersecretary of the Sapphire Front. The letter went on for a moment before coming to the point at the end:
...as such, we are no longer interested in the opera glasses you are proposing.
Tomrin sucked in a breath and let it out. “I don’t think we can keep the shop open.” He handed the letter to Non. 
“But you got it working?” Non said.
“It doesn’t work perfectly.” 
“But--” 
Tomrin ran a hand through his hair and pulled at the nape of his shirt, his chest tight. “The question is, do you want to cut glass?” 
Non made a face. “No. I’d rather take over Fredick’s work.”
“For no money?” Tomrin said. “Cut glass is all that we have that pays now.”
The door opened and Robles, Ken, and Tiff entered. “The shop is dead,” Non blurted. He handed Robles the letter, who read it quickly and handed it to Ken. 
“Well, Master Lazoloth is dead,” Ken said. 
“We can’t live on no money,” Tiff said.
“You have children,” said Robles. “No one can expect you to work for free.” 
“So so we have jobs or not?” Ken asked. 
“No pay--for now,” Robles said. “But Tomrin and I can fix that. There are golden ducks everywhere.” 
“But I’m afraid it’s going to be awhile,” Tomrin said. “The Sapphire Front isn’t going to pay us not until we find a way to convince them again. That’s going to take time.” 
“We’ll find money,” Robles said. “But for now we need to save as much money as we can.” 
“We need to firedown the forges while Tomrin and I head to Woolad for a couple days. We will look for money there.” 
“We really can’t work for nothing,” Ken said. “Even we are at our limits.”
“Let’s close the shop for a couple days. Then when we return we will see where we are,” Robles offered. 
Tomrin couldn’t help but feel that they were leading the man on. Tiff looked like she didn’t believe it either. “We can’t work for free.” 
“We’ll get the money,” Robles assured her. “Now let’s get the place closed down.” He directed the cleanup.
When Dar and Sean from the back arrived Tomrin explained to them the plan. 
“We just thought that Master Laz was innocent, but--” 
“I understand,” Tomrin said. “They pressured you to go along.” 
The two nodded sheepishly.
Dynamics [?]
“You didn’t need to rag on them,” Robles said after they returned from lunch and it was just the two of them at their workstations.
	“What? Who?” Tomrin said.
	“Sean and Dar.”
“Do you trust them?” Tomrin asked him. Dar and Sean were Peregrine yet…
“Yes,” Robles said.
“Is the shop clean?” Tomrin asked, changing the subject. As far as he was concerned he gave Sean and Dar what they deserved.
“Close.”
“Time, then?” Tomrin said. He and Robles headed into the back. The heat was still there, but the various glows leaking out of the glass furnaces were softer. Rounding everyone up.”
“Come back soon,” Tiff said.
“Yes, with money,” Non added to laughs.
“I’m sure we will have great barrel loads of golden birds on our backs when we return.”
“And the percents?” Sean asked.
“We will figure all that out,” Tomrin said. 
“So we are really doing this then?” Tiff said. 
“Yes, we are doing this,” said Tomrin. The idea of going begging for a new job was no better than begging for investments. He didn’t think he could live with the idea of working in a lesser shop, cutting glass from colored sheets. 
“Then Tomrin is the new master now?” Ken said.
“Yes.” 
“No,” said Robles, turning. “Not yet.” 
“That’s fine,” Tomrin said. “All I care about is the glass.” 
“Of course,” Robles said. “But if any of us leaves the shop, we lose our percents.” 
“That’s only fair,” Tomrin said. “We are all in this together.” He was glad Robles was the one to make that rule. His friend was the one most likely to grow bored and leave.
“Now,” Robles said as he glanced at the door. “Now it’s time for me and Tomrin to hit the road.”
“And be sure to lock the back door and latch it we you leave tonight,” Tomrin added. “We will have the key.”
On the Road [?]
Now was not the time for travel at all.
“I don’t even want to go,” Tomrin said, locking the door gingerly. The handle was blazing hot.
“You sort of have to go…for Gaz’s funeral.” Robles said, taking the key and tucking it into his pack. “So we don't lose it.”
“Don’t you hate the idea of seeing Rennly again?”
“Not at all,” Robles said. His knife flashed into his hands. “Sheep Sheering Days was all fun at the fair.”
“Even if Rennly is there?”
“Yes, I suppose so? Worried for your toes?” Robles turned and took a step up the hill towards Tomrin’s home in the city—and nominally towards Woolad, their hometown.
“Wait!” Non called. “So you are the Flight Leader, Robles?” 
“No, I’m the Pack Leader,” Robles said as he hitched his pack to his shoulder. 
“I guess that makes Tomrin…”
“I am the King Bird of Glass.” 
“And you two are coming right back with money?” 
“We’ll be back as soon as possible,” Robles said.
Non turned and headed back into the ally.
Tomrin shrugged and followed him, hitching his own pack to adjust it.
Fighting Words [?]
“I don’t think this is a such a good idea,” Tomrin said to Robles when they finally crested the valley Menad was in onto the rolling prairie lands. The climb out of the valley was slow, hot work, each switchback dragging them closer to the sun as it beat down. “It will be good not having to deal with dead weight like Fredick dragging us down. But, what of Tiff and Ken? I can’t help feeling we are leading them on. Sean and Dar have families too, you know?”
“I know that,” Robles grunted. “Life is a gamble.”
“For us, maybe,” Tomrin said. “But we can’t do that to them.”
Robles shrugged. “If you are worried so much, ask your father for money.”
“My father? He didn’t become rich giving away money to people--even some he owed.”
“What’s the worst that could happen?” Robles asked.
“I don’t know,” Tomrin said. “I feel like the worst has already occurred.”
