“Master Laz dead?” Robles asked. “You don’t plan on following in his footsteps do you?”
Tomrin’s hand itched to rub his chest. “Hardly. And I also don’t plan to help you lead us all to ruin.”
“Them’s fighting words,” Robles said, stopping, and turned and headed to the side of the road.

Mixed Sticks [?]
The road entered a small patch of conifers, the stand hiding houses and farms piling up against the Eastern Highway--and tired, hot slaves.
Tomrin took the rough hewn walking stick Robles offered him. The dry bark flaked in his grip. Robles raised a similar cutting. He made several playful jabs at Tomrin with his dead branch. “Defend yourself,” he said and forced Tomrin to deflect his next jab with his scaly staff. Clack.
“If Sean’s babies go hungry…” Tomrin said, but Roble’s high swing forced him to focus or be struck in the chest.
“No one is going to starve,” Robles said. “We’ll succeed, as long as you try.” He swung low.
Tomrin pushed his stick down too slowly and Roble’s tick slapped him in the leg. Clack.
“Me?”  Tomrin demanded, backing up, hopping from the smart. “You’re the one who can’t seem to hold down a job for more than a few months.”
Robles pressed his attack. Clack. Clack. “I can hold them just fine… if I want to.”
“And you aren’t going to run out on us?” Tomrin asked, swinging his stick, missing wilding as Robles arced his body lithe as whip.
“No. Are you?” Robles said.
“No,” Tomrin said.
“Good,” Robles said, grinning, dropping the butt of his walking stick to the ground. “And we’re all happy for that--and that you are better at glass than your are at mixing stick like you used to.”
Tomrin flipped the wooden limb in his hands. “This is hardly a proper staff.” He swung low at Robles’ legs.
Robles jumped, pulling his stick up with him. “Now, that’s more like it,” he said. “Now you are getting into the spirit of Sheep Shearing Days.” 
Tomrin scoffed. “It’s been forever since I enjoyed a single thing about that feathered thing.”
A Touch of Tarsk [?]
“What about the food?” Robles demanded, incensed; he swung low now too.
Tomrin blocked him easily. Clack. “Sheep Shearing Days is kid’s nonsense.”
“The childish fun is the point.”
“We’re not children anymore,” Tomrin said whirling. The dry road puffed dust up from where his booted feet slapped the dirt. The bone dry road had been ground to a fine powder, dust even a heavy rain would not easily penetrate. 
“True,” Robles said, swinging his branch at Tomrin’s chest. Tomrin turned and caught the stick with his shoulder instead. Chut. He swung himself and stick, striking Robles. Frack. They both made hits. One point each. Robles took a breath, dropping stance. “And we are going to have to act it now—if we want money.” 
Tomrin scoffed, slapping it from under him. “Like us fighting in the road instead of getting out of this feathered heat?” His chest wound ached and thirst settled now. “We’re sweating for no good reason. We should have brought some water.” 
Robles ignored him and swung. Clack. Clack. “You know what I mean,” said Robles, setting the point of his stick in the dust. “Like being willing to cut glass--and beg for money.” 
Tomrin leaned on his own stick now. His feather chest burned. “And mount the glass too?” 
“Is that where you draw the line?” Robles asked, holding his stick to the ground, but half-cocked.
“I just hope the glass shop will be leaner now.” 
“We’ll still need the money,” Robles said.
“Money is nothing but a pain in the tailfeathers,” Tomrin said, pretending to adjust his pack.
Robles swung his stick up, dragging a cloud of dirt and dust into the air. The dirt, dust, and branch end flew at Tomrin’s middle. His father had wanted him to be good at staves and some of the training kicked in as the stick in his own hand lept to intercept Robles’s. Clack. His staff then smacked him across the waist. Fump. But there was no way of getting out of the dirt’s way. Sand and pebbles pelted Tomrin even as he scored the hit. 
“You dirty dog,” Tomrin said. “You could have hit my eyes.” 
Robles laughed. “Only if you didn’t still have it.” He rubbed himself where the staff had struck him. 
Tomrin dropped his stick into the dust “A nip of your tarsk,” he said. “Then we will call it even.” 
Robles dropped his own stick and shrugged his pack from his back. “I’m not sure that’s how it works,” he said, but began undoing the straps. He reached in and pulled out his glass flask. The bottle was of a brilliant green glass. He unstopped it and took a sip. “Ahh,” he said, offering it to Tomrin. “High-spirit tarsk.” 
Tomrin took the flask. “I thought I meant real tarsk.” 
“Just drink it,” Robles said. “It will warm you up.” 
Tomrin took the bottle. “That’s just what I need.” He put the flask to his lips and took a swig. “Gah! It’s harsh.” 
Robles snatched the bottle away and corked it. “Not harsh. Strong.” He stuffed the bottle back into his pack and rebuckled the straps. Hoisting the pack onto his shoulders, he picked up his stick and stood. “Now let’s go before we’re truly thirsty.”
Tomrin groaned.
“What is this?” Robles asked. 
“Just sore,” Tomrin said. 
“It’s that drubbing I gave you.” 
“I’m sure that’s what it is,” Tomrin said, dismissively. 
“Just the price of my inglorious company.”
A Snake in the Road [?]
The sun began to drop in the west and Tomrin supposed his parents would already be home, returned from the lower steppes lands, back to the last gasp of civilization--if Woolad could be called such.
The Ocrin’s carpentry shop had burned to the ground. It wasn’t much after that, that the family had left Woolad to wander the steppes, off searching for a feather. Herding goats and sheep and oxen was no life for Tomrin. His mother, Janet, believed in the old ways. His husband, Dabel, seemed to accept them too now--perhaps penance for the unseemly wealth made from the insurance.
“You know what I find odd?” Robles said. “Master Laz dies and just like that, the Sapphire Front cancels their order. I can’t help but feel Rennly did something to make that happen. Him, Fredick, both at the Inn, then Rennly was skulking around the courthouse yesterday. Then at Master Lazoloth’s house...”
“You saw him at the courthouse too? And I thought the same, but I don’t know,” Tomrin said. He suspected Margretta, the shadowy lady from the courthouse.
“If it was him, I think—”
Tomrin cut him off. “I know...we need to keep away from him.”
“No. Not at all,” Robles said, giving him a look. “It’s always better to watch your enemies. Never let them stab you in the back.” 
Tomrin pondered his friend’s words as they walked along in silence. 
Woolad appeared in the distance as they crested the hill of Turtle Pass. “Finally,” Tomrin grunted. 
“Welcome to Woolad,” Robles said, sarcastically. “Land of old slaver ways.”
Chapter 14: Woolad
	Never forget these famous last words: I know how fire works.
		—From the Archanist’s Cookbook

A fresh coat of whitewash covered the logs of the Country House Inn. 
The wooden bears out front had been carved from large tree stumps, once charming and rustic, now were also painted white, making them look off and wrong, and less imposing. The common house was detached from the guest rooms which had been built to look like farmhouses, and the false fronts on the stables made them look larger from the highway, while the outhouses were rough-hewn and squat, fashioned long ago to look like slave quarters. 
"Sure you don’t want to stay with me at my aunt’s?" Robles said, eyeing the white bears skeptically.
Tomrin thought about Robles’ question. He didn’t much like Robles’ aunt, not that she was a bad person, she just wanted to know things. “No, I think I will do this,” Tomrin said. "Get a drink with me?” 
“No, I better run home. Aunty will need to prepare herself for sleep. Me being late will tax her heart. You know how she is, how she likes to worry.” 
Tomrin grunted. “See you later tonight?” he asked. 
"I might head down for a drink. If not, I’ll see you at the funeral in the morning.” 
“Then we look for money.”
“Lots of old money comes into town,” Robles said. “Hate to say it but you are right, Gaz died at a good time for us.” Robles made a lopsided grin. "Don’t run off and get the constables called.”
Tomrin tossed his walking stick into the ditch beside the road. “I won’t."
"Well that’s good to know, a real load off my shoulders,” Robles said. “Just what I need: Founder in the jail in Woolad. You know?” 
Tomrin sighed. "I didn’t hit Fredick.” 
Robles tossed his own ratty walking stick into the ditch. “Never said you did. I better go.”
“Same,” Tomrin said and crunched his boot across the gravel walkway to the commons.
The door stood open but no one acted as porter and there was no music, though snatches of laughter from the back reached his ears as he entered. A front room which adjoined the hallway to the right stood empty. Usually someone manned the front counter. Not today. On the counter, a brass bell sat on a wooden stand, a slat of cedar.
Words were carved into it.
RING FOR SERVICE [?]
He knew the sign and stand but Tomrin had never rung the bell. Now, he reached to pick it up, but pulled his hand back as a shadow darkened the door behind him. 
Boots struck the floor and he turned around. “Rennly,” he said. 
“I just rode in,” Rennly said pleasantly. He had a Daughter with him. There could be no mistake.

She was Rubish and wore green silk. Her necklace held three jewels, three clear diamonds as white as stars. Her red face was flush from the sun, and her dress shone like leaves. She was as beautiful as she was dangerous. “Robles---I and Robles—” Tomrin stammered. “We just got here.” 
“You know this boy?” the woman said to Rennly. 
“Yes?” Renely grunted at her dismissively, adding to Tomrin, "Robles is here? I just see you." He walked to the counter and picked up the bell “You rang, right?” 
“Uh, no—was going to.”
Tomrin refused to look at the woman as Rennly ran the bell. Clang. Clang. Clang. The tall Orioles next to him set the bell down. “The new owners,” Rennly said, and shook his head. “Better but—” 
The door to the left opened and a man several years their senior walked out with an apron on. "Censor Rickot,” the man said as he wiped his hands on a towel. "You have arrived." 
"I keep my reservations, Master Fenway.” Rennly glanced at Tomrin. “And my promises.” Renny smiled. “I promise you that.” 
Words spoken long ago skittered through Tomrin’s mind. I promise you that. Rennly had the same gleaming teeth, the same granite face and hands. Tomrin’s chest began to throb.
Rennly glanced to the man behind the counter and Tomrin glanced away to Master Fenway as he flipped through the file in a metal box he retrieved from behind the counter. “Yes of course, Censor,” Fenway said, as he pulled a slip of cardstock from the case. “Yes, I have you for tonight, but it is a single room.” The man glanced at Tomrin. 
“What about me?” Tomrin asked him. 
“No rooms for slaves, I’m not even allowed to put you up in the outhouses.” The man laughed. 
“What?” Tomrin said. “I’m not a slave.”
“Apologies...” [?]
The man thumbed through his files. “...but unless you are Master Ithus Calway...” 
“No, I’m Tomrin Ocrin.” 
“Well in that case, Mister Tomrin,” the man said, looking back up. “No reservation.” He shrugged. “No room.” 
Tomrin began sweating. “What am I supposed to do?” 
“I don’t know,” the man said. “You know Sheep Shearing Days starts tomorrow?” 
“Yes,” he replied. 
“And I’m sure it’s not just because you’re Peregrine,” Rennly said. “Though you are.”
The Daughter tittered at his stupid joke while the man behind the counter sputtered. “No, not at all. I have many Peregrine friends.” 
“Don’t get all bent out of shape, Tomrin,” Rennly added. “It’s not Master Fenway’s fault you were too lazy to send him a letter asking for a reservation.” 
The man behind the counter shrugged, and Tomrin felt a fool in front of him, and the woman. He just wanted to leave, to disappear. “Well, thank you anyway,” he said quickly. “I’m sure I will find something.” He hurried out the door, hoping he would catch Robles. 
A night at his friend’s aunt’s didn’t seem so bad.
Even the food smelled muddled, Tomrin assured himself as he left the Country House Inn. Like heartburn and unfulfilled hopes. 

As he passed by the fairgrounds he searched the wagons and tents for his family, or any familiar face or sigil; the flat flute of the Ocrin family design appeared on one of the wagons. Don’t stop, Tomrin told himself, and walked down the street down towards Robles’ aunt’s place. He didn’t want his friend to come looking for him at the Country House Inn and not find him. 
He headed up a side street, past the row of houses, to the one where she lived and climbed the steps and grabbed the brass knockers. Picking it up, he let it drop. Tunk. Tomrin dropped it twice more. Tunk. Tunk. Motion and boots on floorboards came from inside. 
Robles’ welcome grin peeked through the high placed windows of the door and disappeared.
The door swung open, and Robles bowed. “Welcome, traveler! Welcome,” he said, swinging his hand. “Enter, my goodman.” 
Tomrin entered the house. “Thank you. Thank you.” 
The place was a good deal smaller than the others nearby, but it had more land out back with a shop covered in tin siding. “Aunty, look who’s here—it’s Eggy.” 
“Buggy!” Robles’ aunt said. She was sitting at the dining room table.
A large carpeted chair split the front room from the dining room and was filled with clothes and books and bags overflowing with paper.
The table itself was just as cluttered. A pair of boots stood in the middle of the mess that hid the table top. Eggy, Toady, Buggy, Robles’s aunt seemed to have a new nickname for Tomrin every time they met, but Buggy she had used before. 
“Good evening, Cordelia,” Tomrin said. “Sorry to call so late, but I wasn’t able to find a room at the Country House Inn. No reservation.” 
“Well, of course you couldn’t,” Cordelia said. She launched into an explanation as to who exactly the new owner of the Country House Inn was, and how he came to be so peculiar as to paint the bears white. 
Tomrin groaned to himself as she began mentioning the man’s relations. Tomrin didn’t care a bit, and it wasn’t like he was going to be staying there. “Hmm. Yes. Ah,” he said in all the right spots. “I just came by to let Robles know I wasn’t going to be there.” 
“So where are you staying?” Robles asked. 
“My parents are in town,” Tomrin said. “I will stay with them, I guess.” 
“I saw your sister and father earlier,” Cordelia said. “All I can say is your poor mother, Buggy. Your father is a card. And your sister, Buggy. Your sister?” She didn’t wait for Tomrin to answer but trampled him under the horse’s shod of her words. “Now, there is a fine looking girl. She has turned into an angel from the gods. Like meeting up with an angel and a demon seeing your father and sister together. I swear the years haven’t touched your father a lick, and he hasn’t grown up a bit. Not a bit.” 
“Yes,” Tomrin said, not knowing what else to say. Cordelia wasn’t wrong. His father was a bit of a cad. “I was thinking about—” Tomrin cut himself off. Robles’ aunt didn’t like Robles drinking tarsk. 
“What?” 
“Oh, nothing. I was just thinking I better be going,” Tomrin said. “I haven’t stopped to see my family yet.”
The Kings of Woolad [?]
“We are kings of this place,” Robles told his aunt, planting a quick kiss on his aunt’s cheek. “Who would dare rob us here? And don’t wait up for me.”
“I won’t,” his aunt said. “Just don’t be long. Remember, I am leaving the door unlocked so you can get in.”
Robles shook his head and turned to Tomrin. “I’ll walk down with you.” He left his pack in the corner and now the sun was setting.
 “I need a drink,” Tomrin said.
“True,” Robles said, but that wasn’t what he suggested as they walked. “This is going to be harder than we thought.” 
“What do you mean?” 
“My Aunty says money has dried up with the Woolad creek.” 
“It can’t have disappeared completely.” 
“No, that’s true, but everyone, and not just the misers, are holding on to every copper.”
“Well if we don’t have someone big on board, we are going to have nothing. Does your aunty know anything about that?” 
“Her exact words were: The generous are acting frugal, and the frugal are like misers, and the misers like the sky this year with it’s rain.” 
“So the plan is ruined?”
“No. Not ruined,” Robles said. “Only harder than we expected.” 
A Dangerous Man [?]
Robles waved hello to Master Stockling and his wife sitting on their porch watching the sunset. Tomrin had ignored them on his first time up the street, but waved to them awkwardly now as well. 
“We will have to look further afield is all,” Robles continued as they turned down Fairway Lane. On one side of the street was the city park, and on the other side the fairgrounds, beyond which the campgrounds where the tents and wagons gathered on King Street. 
The park was filled with as many booths and tents as the fairgrounds with its pavilions erected next to the permanent building, all spreading their shadows now. 
“And take more risks,” Tomrin said.
“Perhaps,” Robles allowed. The sound of animals in the pens reached them over the mostly quiet fairgrounds—and the occasional shout. Robles stopped in front of a closed gate. 
“I don’t know,” Tomrin said, as he eyed the closed gate. “I don’t like risk. Risks were what got Master Lazoloth in trouble--and then dead. I don’t want to end up like him.” 
Robles walked to a section of the fence meant to keep carts and wagons out. A simple heavy rope hung between the posts. He ducked under and held it up for Tomrin to do the same. “I doubt we will end up dead. Unless… You aren’t thinking of killing yourself?” 
Tomrin ducked under. “No.”
“Well, good.”
He wasn’t worried about killing himself, but Tomrin understood why Master Laz had; he clearly didn’t want to be purified. Tomrin’s own Leviathan’s Mark was regrowing, slowly, but far too soon the budding evil would necessitate burning the scales off yet again. 
The mass, he could feel it inside his chest. And unless Lady Margretta couldn’t be trusted at all, others could feel it too—if they touched him. He rubbed his chest. “You don’t think we could get into trouble, tromping through here?” he asked. The lanes and paths through the buildings and tents were paved with white quartz gravel and glittered where the sun peeked through. 
Robles grunted. “No. How would anyone know we don’t have animals entered?” 
“That’s true,” Tomrin agreed. Though lying courts an ill fate, the last thing they needed. 
Roblw did not head straight through the fairgrounds to the campgrounds on the other side, but to a long pavilion, the knife toss tent. Inside sat two large bales of golden straw, bound and tied lengthwise. Painted bulls’ eyes spotted each. In front of the round targets, the ground was marked with ropes laid out at ten, fifteen, and twenty paces. “Now, this is probably against the rules,” Robles said. “Practicing here.” 
His knife flashed into his hands and he stepped to the twenty pace mark. Fizt-futh. The blade buried itself, down to it’s hilt in the right-hand bale’s painted bull’s eye. 
“I don’t know how knife tossing is even legal,” Tomrin said as Robles walked down the alley to collect it. 
“Missiles are only illegal, if they have launchers.” 
“I know, but you are just as deadly. Your arm is the launcher.” 
Robles tucked his blade away. “What can I say?” He grinned, glancing at the setting sun. “I’m a dangerous man.”
The Net Closes [?]
“Let’s get out of here,” Tomrin said and turned and made Robles catch up to him. “I need to find a place to sleep tonight with my parent’s still.” 
“Your old tent?” 
“I’ll have to set it up in the dark,” Tomrin said, headed south towards the camp grounds. “That is if they brought it.” 
“Oh, here is the sparring ring for the staff competition,” Robles said as they turned down one of the paths. “They moved it this year.” He walked over to a barrel stuffed with sticks, regulation staves for the matches. He pulled one from the wooden barrel and tossed it to Tomrin, pushing the center, causing it to wobble slightly as it flew. 
Tomrin caught the staff with his right hand but the thing slapped his palm, a painful strike. “Ah!” It was little more than a heavy gray bar in the twilight. If it was like those cut in previous years it had been recently harvested from a grove on the Gorkel’s family farm. They ran the competition for as long as Tomrin remembered, since the start of the festival--or so they claimed. The yew wood was nothing like the bark covered walking stick from earlier. 
He swung the staff through the air. Whoorf. “Now this,” he said. “Is a civilized weapon.” 
A woman’s voice, low and dulcet, spoke from behind him. “Is it, now?” 
Tomrin swung around. Rennly and the woman with him from the Country House Inn walked up an ungraveled sidepath. Tomrin brushed his left hand against his chest as he deftly dropped the butt of his staff to the ground. “I would say it has always been one, Daughter.”
He couldn’t help but feel that Rennly was following him, even though the two of them came from the opposite direction.
A Civilized Weapon [?]
The woman turned to Rennly. “You will fight him in the competition?”
The way she touched his arm, Tomrin could tell their relationship wasn’t strictly professional. “No,” Tomrin said quickly. “I have given up fighting.” 
“We’ll see about that, won’t we?” Rennly said. “There is still plenty of time to change your mind.” He seemed to brush aside the refusal as if it were nothing. “Ah, Robles, here too I see, I don’t think you have met Daughter Calena.”
Robles bowed. “Robles Kodragus, at your service.” 
“Seems you have changed your mind yet again, Robles.” Rennly said, raising an eyebrow.
“I have changed my mind about many things,” Robles replied. “I am not so enamored with any one of my thoughts as to let them control me. But, which of them specifically are you referring to?” 
“That is a staff in your hand?” 
“Oh this?” Robles said, lifting another staff from the barrel. “This is just a stick to me.” He dropped it back down with a thud. Cunk. “I prefer something a little sharper, a knife say? Why do you think I am so drawn to glass? When it breaks it is sharper than any blade or knife. Blunt things are more Tomrin’s area of expertise.” 
“Probably just as well. Were you signed up there would only be a coin-flip’s chance you’d show up.” 
“Never know what else may happen,” Robles said. “One can’t run his fun and games like a slave ship, can he?” 
“Maybe you can’t,” Rennly said, swatting Robles’ words from the air with his right hand. “But Tomrin here is used to being a slave.” 
“Look, you’ve angered him,” the Daughter said, holding out a hand towards Tomrin. In the descending darkness her hand seemed charged with lightning, and his anger at Rennly evaporated. The blood rushed from his face, and his hands both tingled. He couldn’t run, so he stood transfixed holding the staff as he stared fixedly at her hand. 
Gloom darkened the fairgrounds, and the shadows of dusk loomed up the sides of the nearby tents, and suffused the walls with a darkness to cradle the world in a dark hand. At the center, Daughter Calena glowed. 
She knows? But she couldn’t. In the quick pause, he considered her outreached hand, now only a dark shape in the fading daylight. Tomrin gripped his staff with both hands. He bowed, positioning it horizontal in front of himself. “I take no offense at the truth,” he said. “I am but a Peregrine and slave, the Emperor is my master, and after Gaz’s funeral I will be heading home to Menad. No time for fun for me.” 
“You wouldn’t leave town just like that?” Rennly said. “The fair was always Gaz’s favorite time, to leave like that dishonors his memory.” 
“And you say the staff is a civilized weapon?” the Daughter said. “Is that even a thing?” 
“Civilized as weapons go,” Tomrin said. He didn’t want to continue talking to Rennly, and was surprised he prefered speaking with this woman, this Daughter of Heaven’s Glory. 
She made a small noise. “Hmm?”
“He’s always been like this,” Rennly said.
“Like what?” Tomrin asked. 
“Slippery with words—when you are hiding,” Rennly said. “Hard to pin you down; you always seem to have some excuss when you want to get out of something.” 
“I’m not sure what you mean,” Tomrin said. “But I refuse to believe you are calling me a liar. I don’t like weapons, and that’s the truth.” 
“I’m afraid Tomrin is just too idealistic,” Robles said. “I think he likes to use words in ways that don’t make sense...maybe that’s it?” 
“I try to be precise,” Tomrin said. “Not my fault people can’t follow me.” 
“I think you forgot something,” Robles said. “We are also looking for money for the glass shop after Gaz’s funeral.” 
“True,” Tomrin said. He’d forgotten in the moment.
Machinations in the Dark [?]
“So you are looking for money to keep the glassworks open?” Rennly asked, sounding interested. 
“What glassworks?” the Daughter added. 
“Godly Glassworks,” Robles said. “We are taking over where Master Lazoloth left off.” 
“So that glass shop and that Master Lazoloth?” Calena asked pointedly. “Doesn’t sound so godly to me.” 
“Master Lazoloth made mistakes,” Robles said. 
“Mistakes? He was Marked,” Rennly scoffed.
“Yes. But we aren’t,” Robles said. 
“And the work we are doing is important,” Tomrin added. 
“Oh really?” the woman said. “How so?” 
“It’s hard to explain how important glass is,” Tomrin said. “Everyone thinks it is just sand—heated up until it melts—but it’s so much more than that. How far we can take it? What can it do ultimately? No one knows for sure. But I think we have only just begun to scratch the surface.”
“He sounds like an arcanist on Serathus,” Daughter Calena said to Rennly. 
“Yes,” the Orioles censor said. “Very interesting. Glass. And you say you need money? And here is Calena and I. She is the new Vault Keeper.” 
“Congratulations, Vault Keeper,” Robles said. “But I doubt the Golden Hearted would be happy to find his gold with us without his permission.” 
“Seasargus most certainly wouldn’t,” Calena replied. “I’m not sure what you are saying, Rennly.” 
“Just observing,” Rennly said easily. “But I have money of my own. When my...mother died,” he continued. “She left me a sum of money. And the gods pay me handsomely.” 
The idea of taking Rennly’s money, even to keep the glass shop open twisted in Tomrin’s belly. “We wouldn’t want to impose—” 
“How much money are you talking?” Robles said, cutting Tomrin off. 
“Let’s just say more than a few golden ducks.” 
“We are looking for quite a few,” Robles said. 
“And it would be a gamble,” Tomrin said. 
“Then I need to know it will be worth the risk.” 
“Well aren’t you a gambler?” Robles asked. 
“And I like to win more than I lose,” Rennly said. “That’s why I bet on winning horses.” 
“The glass works is a horse that can hardly lose,” Tormrin said. 
“Let me tell you what,” said Rennly. “Calena and I have something we need to do yet tonight. Come with us…  Afterwards I will make you a proposition.” 
Tomrin glanced at a shadowy Robles then back to Rennly. “I don’t—” 
“We’ll go with you,” Robles said. 
Tomrin wasn’t sure how he felt about going anywhere with Rennly or with a Daughter of Heaven’s Glory. “I guess my parents can wait,” he finally said. “Where are we going?” 
“We are going to see Gaz. And it’s a good thing you said yes,” Rennly said, grabbing his nightstick. “Or who knows…”
“We are delighted to join you,” Robles said. 
Tomrin wasn’t sure delighted was the right word. He replaced his staff, doubting Rennly was only joking about using violence to get what he wanted.
Some people never changed.
Chapter 13: A Dark Ritual
	Yet those who lose their sight by eyes, may yet see in riddles.
		—From The Dark Verses

Robles chatted with Rennly and Calena as Tomrin listened on the far side of Robles, away from the them as they all walked together in a line on their small pilgrimage to where Gaz awaited burial. 
An impending dread filled Tomrin’s footsteps however easily Robles chatted.
The evening breeze, the kind that came with the fall of a summer night, harkened an end, as if fall were just around the next street corner. But winter had given away to spring without snow or rain. 
Still a cool air, welcome after a hot day, seeped a chill into Tomrin’s bones. Going to see Gaz meant only one thing with a Daughter of Heaven’s Glory: a chance to say goodbye to Gaz. A rare chance, but not one Tomrin looked forward to. The idea of being around someone willingly spelldancing made his skin crawl, and he sweated despite the cool spring air blowing off the surrounding fields.
When alone, on similar walks, the gloom always pushed in—and so would fear. As a boy Tomrin had run from light to light—when there were any lights. Woolad was tiny, and he could run from one end of it to the other, a thing he’d done often in terror, afraid of imagined bloodbeasts. Now, as he walked down King Street to the charnel house with Robles, Rennly and Calena, a garish light shining down from Serathus, far too bright on the houses growing out of the hill on their left. 
The charnel house and the Woolad temple sat on the right, the tall spire climbing into the sky, towards the starry heavens on Temple Street. Margretta’s warning words played through Tomrin’s mind as he brushed a surreptitious hand across his chest. That’s all it takes! He could almost feel Margretta’s light touch on his hand. He could only imagine what she would say if she knew he was playing at this dangerous game, and for money. Money! Feathered money!
Night Streets [?]
When he could, Tomrin stole glances at Calena in the moonlight. 
She was not unpretty but perhaps a little pump, as was common for her race, Rubish. This close to the border a Rubish and an Orioles was not uncommon, but her relationship with Rennly occupied little of Tomrin’s mind. He was looking for a hint of recognition in her. He couldn’t help but feel that some game was afoot—and wanted to know the rules. Could she see inside him? Could she see the evil mass growing in his heart? 
“No,” he said in answer to one of Calena’s questions. “I don’t miss Woolad.” Robles had tried to weasel out of Rennly’s his proposal, whatever it was, but Rennly was adamant that they had to wait until after they had visited Gaz’s dead body at the charnel house. He led the discussion to the notable and the quaint life and times they had lived in the Woolad of their. 
“Nothing had changed in Woolad since I left,” Tomrin said for himself. “And that’s the problem with it. It’s not that late but already the town is sleeping. Menad would only now be readying itself for dinner.” 
“Well, I grew up in Corundum,” Calena puffed. “It hasn’t changed either. But it never sleeps—and that’s its problem.” 
“I always wanted to go to Corundum,” Robles said. “How was it?” 
Loud and smelly, Tomrin wanted to say, but thought better of it. 
Calena talked about the capital. “The shrine is large, but it’s nothing like on Serathus,” she said. “The Altar Mountain is its own city, its own nation, nothing like it on earth, maybe the Center Lands, but those are spread out. And on the Altar, the Serath are everywhere your turn.” 
“I thought you didn’t want to get into it with a bunch of hicks?” Rennly gently ribbed Calena. 
Tomrin was finding himself liking the young woman. Such a waste.
Magic in the Dark [?]
Calena answered Rennly as the street leveled off and the temple finally loomed up in front of them.
The structure had seemed large once, but now it was just a building that would disappear in the others of Menad. “You said yourself, your friends aren’t the small-town types. They do seem more like men of the world.” 
“That we are,” said Robles. “And here we are; the Woolad Temple; the greatest building in all this vast city.” The steeple cut a dwarfish silhouette in the stars, but Robles actually sounded proud. 
Rennly led them to the back, where the charnel house held the newly departed awaiting burial in the nearby graveyard. The house squatted—only two stories high, enough to let in a Serath, but only barely. The squarish building was connected to the Temple by a small flagstone walkway which they took to the double doors, closed with chains slung between iron handles. The chain was there to keep out wild animals. And bloodbeasts. Or so Tomrin had believed when he was a child. 
Rennly unthreaded the chains. Clig, clig, clig. Pulling open the doors, a yawning darkness peaked out at them. Bad breath, the worst breath Tomrin had ever smelled wafted out. He’d smelled the smell once before—the day after Rennly’s father had died in the fire that consumed the Ocrin carpentry shop. 
“Brings back memories.” Rennly said, swallowing. “Don’t it?” 
Robles answered. “So dark.” His voice was but a whisper. Tomrin felt trapped in a bad dream. 
“I will give us light,” the Daughter of Heaven’s Glory said; her words echoed back out of the dead of the hall. 
Before Tomrin could make sense of her meaning, orange light flared up inside the charnel house.
Shadows lept into existence. Calena’s hands moved with a fluid grace, the light gently quavering with her slow undulating movements. She was spelldancing. 
Tomrin’s belly filled with ice as she glided forward, the ball of orange fire moving forward with her into the vault. 
The Charnel House [?]
Square iron doors lined the walls heading back into the charnel house, storage for the unclaimed dead. In the back, down the aisle, a stone table held a form, draped in red velvet cloth. Gaz! It had to be. His belly had grown overlarge. Tomrin swallowed and strode after a final glance out into the night sky. He wasn’t sure any amount of Rennly’s money was worth this—acting like spelldancing was nothing. You’re crazy!
 Calena stopped five paces from the altar where Gaz’s lifeless body waited. Rennly stopped as well. 
The flaming orb hung in the air over the velvet form on the table. “What are we waiting for?” Tomrin asked. He expected someone to pull back the cover. It was why they were here, wasn’t it? 
“I am about to allow us to commune with your dead friend,” Calena said, “But you need to know not to touch his spirit.” 
“Spirit?” Tomrin said. Communing with the dead sounded like dark magic, like something a Dark Daughter would attempt.
“Why not touch his spirit?” Robles said. 
Calena turned to him as she continued her spelldance. The light glinted off her bright eyes, and she looked like she was bathed in sunlight, her golden hair giving up an inner light. Her placid face spoke of serene contemplation. Her smooth movements made Tomrin think of a swan in flight. “If you touch the spirit at all, his anger, hate, pain and suffering will enter you. It is opposite of the Luster, or so I’m told.”
“The Luster is good?” Tomrin asked. 
“Focus on what she’s saying,” Rennly said. “Unless maybe you think you need to touch Gaz’s shade, go ahead.” 
“We’re focused,” Robles said, and Tomrin added. “And we’re listening.” 
“Even if you don’t touch him, it will be bad, you’ll feel things and smell them.” Calena said, “Unpleasant things.” 
“How unpleasant?” said Robles. 
“You’re not afraid, are you?” Rennly said. “I think Tomrin is turning green already.” 
“I’m not afraid,” Tomrin said, feeling exactly that. This was turning into some strange nightmare where a beautiful creature promised him pain and suffering while Rennly goaded him into something he wasn’t sure he wanted to do. “Let’s just get it over with.” If he had no choice, the sooner it was over, the better. 
“The Luster hurts?” Robles asked.
Calena frowned. “What?”
Tomrin bit his bottom lip to keep from correcting Robles’ misapprehension. “You mean the bond?” Calena said. “You have no idea what pain is until you try to go against the will of the gods?” she asked. 
“That’s why we don’t,” Tomrin said.
“Right,” Robles said, and Tomrin wondered at her words about the bond. It seemed the poor woman was nothing but a slave herself. Just because her masters were the Serath didn’t mean she wasn’t. 
“Good,” she said.
The light winked out.
Through Foul Air [?]
The open doors behind them were gray holes in the darkness, and none of Serathus’ light penetrated the hall this far in. Tomrin questioned the wisdom of what they were doing. He wasn’t sure it was a good idea to disturb the dead, even if they hadn’t yet been buried. “This feels wrong,” he said. 
“She hasn’t even started yet,” Rennly said. His words had a jeering tone, almost laughing from the echoing spread of it. He was enjoying this. 
In the dark, years were stripped away, as if by a foul magic. “Why do we have to do this?” Tomrin said. 
“You’ll see, unless you are too scared?” 
“Just do it already,” Robles said. 
Calena’s voice became harsh and bitter. “Don’t rush me!” 
Maybe it was the darkness but the seconds stretched. The air began to slowly swirl in front of them. It glowed like a mist. Motes of dark swallowed up the light cast by the lighter motes, and the strange mist churned about a center above Gaz’s dead body. 
Tomrin wasn’t sure what he expected as the velvet cover turned from red to gray in the strange and ghastly light. He gasped when a shadowy Gaz emerged from the mist. The stench of corpses and of jellied blood, and death and rot made Tomrin cough. “Chaah!” He wanted to retch and keep on retching. 
Rennly laughed, and Gaz stared blankly forward, seemingly oblivious of the foulness swirling all around him. He was as dead-eyed as Master Laz. A bit of light leaked out what looked like a gash in his forehead. But the specter wasn’t really Gaz, but his shade. 
“I can’t believe I’m seeing this,” Robles said, sounding amazed, not sickened. “This is something I can tell my children; I bet they won’t believe me.” 
Gaz just stood there, and in the strange light four of them, visible, if gray and weird. There were no shadows; it was as if each of them were shades as well. 
“We’re in the spirit world.” Tomrin said. 
“No,” said Calena, “This is just the darklight.” 
Darklight [?]
Tomrin stared at the unblinking shade.
He was too stunned to think and the stench made it difficult for him to focus on anything but drawing in shallow breaths through his mouth. An evil to rival the one growing in him seemed to lodged itself, breath by breath, into his lung. He would never be clean again. Not inside or out.
Gaz always, rather short for an Orish, wasn’t just stocky, but fat. His upswept ears squated on the side of his pale head, and his nose was piggish and small.
It was terrible to see him like this and Tomrin wished he’d never come, but was happy to see that Gaz’s wasn’t as lined and hard as the man’s on the street the previous morning. Still Tormin was afraid this was now how he would remember Gaz, as a fat ugly shade, not as the puggy, slow but good-hearted boy he’d known in his youth. 
“Both of you be quiet,” Rennly said, “I will speak for us.” Satisfied he continued. “How many tins of tarsk did you drink the night you died, Gaz?”
The shade looked at Rennly. Gaz’s mouth moved, but the words did not seem to come from him. The words spoken where in Gaz’s voice, but came from everywhere, from the mist itself, or from inside Tomrin’s head, as if the words were memories. “I’d don’t remember, four or five.” 
“So when you fell down the cliff, you bumped your head and died?” 
A finger went up and touched the gash which bled gray light. “I hit my head.” 
Rennly turned to speak to Calena. “I think I heard enough,” he said. 
“That’s it?” she replied. 
“I don’t need to hear all the Orish details.” 
The ghastly light faded and Tomrin could feel himself breathe again as Calena ended her spelldance. “That wasn’t by the book,” she said. 
“It doesn’t need to be. Gaz was my friend and I knew him to be clumsy, always falling down.” 
Tomrin didn’t remember Gaz always falling down because he was clumsy, but because Rennly pushed him or hit him, and Gaz never stuck up for himself. He tried not to think about the day Rennly was pushing Gaz around, but instead of helping Tomrin had turned and walked away, finding another way home.
The ball of fire which returned and bathed them in golden light.
It was not enough, and Tomrin worried nothing might ever be.
And the Worst of It [?]
Robles turned to Rennly. “I’d like to hear your proposal.” 
“Walk and speak,” Calena said, turning. 
They followed her and the high-flying ball of flame. 
“Of course, my proposal,” said Rennly. “You’ve called me a betting man, and I am, but like I said, I want to know what I’m betting on. I can’t follow all this glass talk.” 
“With us you don’t need to,” Robles said. 
“Or so you say,” Rennly continued. “And I’d trust that, but when Tomrin said the staff was the most civilized weapon and not the baton, I thought...” 
“He was just talking,” Robles said. 
“Maybe, but I think he should show me.” 
“Show you?” 
“Yes, show me.” 
“How would he do that?” Robles said. But Tomrin could think of a few ways. 
“A man with a staff against a man with a baton?” Robles asked. 
“Not a fair fight,” said Tomrin. It was an idea though; he would beat the tar out of Rennly with his baton and him with a staff. 
“I’m thinking. Tomorrow, in the staff competition. Tomrin beats me, I’ll agree to give you the money.”
They exited the vault and the orb of light disappeared.
	Ramifications [?]
By starlight and moonlight Rennly straightened the chain with Robles help. 
“So, what do you say, Tomrin?” Robles said as Rennly tried the doors by jiggling the handles. The sound of the metal bound doors clanging resounded low and muffled echoes seemed to escape from the charnel house. Bonh, bonh. 
“Well, I—” 
“Don’t say, no,” Robles said, putting a hand up. He turned to Rennly. “We will let you know tomorrow.” 
“Fine,” Rennly said. “Think it over. Calena?” 
“I’m not dressed for this cold.” She sounded antsy to leave.
Tomrin wanted to be elsewhere himself. “I need to go to the camp grounds,” he said. He was going to wake his parents up. Robles said goodbye to Rennly and Calena, Tomrin grunting his own. Then, Rennly and Calena took off down the hill. 
Robles and Tomrin walked along Marvin Street parallel to King Street. 
“You need to do the staff competition,” Robles said. 
“Why? So Rennly can beat me up with a stick?” 
“But if you win.” 
“I can’t win,” Tomrin said. “Don’t you see what Rennly’s doing?” 
“Of course I see.” 
“Really. He still blames me for his father. This is his way to get even. He could even kill me.” 
“Or maybe you could beat him and save the glass shop?” 
“I’m not going to do it,” Tomrin said. “I’m not that good at the staff any more. I never really was.” 
“But the money. Think of it.” 
“I am thinking of it.” 
“Not just Rennly’s money. When the glass works—” 
“I know,” Tomrin said. “I know.” 
“Think...what if we knew we’d get the money, even if you lost?” 
“How could that work?” 
“I’m just thinking,” Robles said. “And I think you are right about one thing.” 
“What’s that?” Tomrin wanted to know. He was usually right about nothing anymore it seemed. 
“He does want to beat you with a stick. We can use that to our advantage.” 
They had reached the cross street that led to Robles’ home. Tomrin glanced up the alley and houses standing watch over the top of the small picket fences. The people and houses slept, no lights but the glitter of stars shone from their glass eyes. They were painted varying colors by day, but now stood gray and sleeping. The stone chimneys of the nearest hung quiet. 
Tomrin stopped and turned to Robles. His friends’ words hung in the air. Tomrin wasn’t sure he wanted to know what it was his friend was thinking. Robles stopped as well. 
“Doesn’t it seem odd to you.” Tomrin said. “We just witnessed spell dancing and spoke with the dead, and we are talking about money as if it’s the most important thing in the world?” 
“Not just money,” Robles replied, his teeth glowing as he grinned. “Gold.” 
“I don’t care about gold. But glass.” 
“Glass is more interesting than magic?” 
Tomrin only had to think a moment. “Yes, balls of fire, unfeeling sprites? What are those compared to far sight, and...and?” 
Robles made a noise in his throat. “And Master Laz’s ramifications?”
“Exactly.”
“Maybe Rennly was right about us being men of the world,” Robles offered,
“That’s nonsense.” Tomrin replied. “I’ve seen enough of the world to know that.” 
“Spoken like a true man of it,” Robles said, continuing along Marvin Street. 
Tomrin stepped after him. “Not going home?”
The Journey Home [?]
“Not yet,” Robles said, marching along. “I need to see you safely home.”
Tomrin stepped beside him. “I’ve read tracts on the rudiments of spell dancing,” he said. “There is nothing to it, just channeling anger, fear, and whatnot.” He hadn’t followed it all, but he’d followed it enough to know there was nothing sublime about it despite what the authors of the apocryphal texts had claimed. “The mysteries of the cosmos, of glass, are greater. There is something deeper to it all than even we can see.”
“Sand and heat,” Robles said. “It would be a shame if you threw it all away because you were still afraid of Rennly.” 
“I’m not afraid of Rennly.” 
“Just his staff?” 
“Of it hitting me,” Tomrin said. “Repeatedly.” 
Robles laughed. “Maybe he does need to kill you to get whatever it is out of his system.”
Tomrin shook his head. “He probably just wants to beat the feathers out of me.” 
“Maybe, but things are beginning to look like they have gone beyond just envy,” Robles said. 
“This might be one of those things we talked about?”
“Maybe,” Robles said. “And since Rennly wants this so badly, we can tell him that you’ll do it. But we say, win or lose, he put his money in.” 
“And if he doesn’t go for it?” 
“I can all but guarantee he’ll go for it,” Robles said.
“And if he kills me?” 
“Are you telling me you can’t defend yourself even a little bit?” Robles asked. “I think you’ll do better than you think. Rennly isn’t as disciplined as he thinks he is.” 
They turned down the lane on the side of the fair grounds.
Home [?]
Robles thead the way through the rows of tens. 
“You might as well say, hello.” Tomrin told him as they neared the Ocrin pavilion. He kept his voice low as Robles. The tents on the way sat largely dark. 
“And maybe drink some of that tarsk your father keeps,” said Robles. “It is after sundown.” 
“And then start rumors about Daughters of Heaven?” Tomrin asked. Real tarsk was powerful stuff. 
“There is always talk of False Daughters as well. My aunt hears stories of one coming to town from time to time.” 
“To steal little children,” Tomrin said. “Yet no children here ever go missing. Isn’t that funny?” He tried to keep his voice light and airy but he couldn’t help but wonder if he wasn’t that child Margretta was trying to steal away. He worried what it meant, that he might never be free of her. She knew his secret somehow, and that meant others could. 
They stopped in front of his father’s tent, the orange glow through the canvas walls bringing back memories—some good, others bad. 
Tomrin opened his mouth. “Father,” he said. “It’s me, Tomrin.”
“And Robarthles Kodragus,” his friend added. “And at your service.” 
It was rare to hear Robles use—what he claimed was—his full name. It was said real names had power and knowing someone’s full name gave you power over them. It was nonsense of course but after tonight it seemed almost anything could be true, here in Woolad, in the heart of superstition and fear--here in this kingdom of dark prejudices.
 “We come with blessings from the west,” Tomrin added. “So may the grace of Seasargus be upon you.” It was a ritual greeting for those meeting in the dark of night, and from the southeast, and from steppe lands thereof. Saying the words sent a shiver down Tomrin’s spine. 
Woolad! The place was the land of hypocrisy and lies…his own. 
Chapter 14: A Familiar Game
	For he is the buzzing in your head, the darkling voice within your soul.
		—From The Dark Verses

A shadow moved within the tent and grew, filling it. 
Emy’s tent next door lay dark, but she squealed his name from within the larger one. “Tomrin!” Apparently, she had stayed up too. The large shadow shrunk to the size of a man, not large enough to belong to an Orish man, yet big for a Rubish. The shadow worked the tie strings and the flap flew open. 
Dable Ocrin stood with the lamp to his back, himself still little more than shadows. “Tomrin,” he said, reaching out to shake his hand. “You surprised us.” 
“Sorry to call on you so late, Dable,” Robles said as Dable shook his hand as well. “But I brought your son with me, so I hope all is forgiven.” 
Emy met them both at the flaps. “I was afraid you weren’t going to come,” she said once she’d finished hugging them both. Inside, Janet Ocrin waited. Tomrin’s mother wore a long white robe like Dable and Emy. She politely shook Robles’ hand, and hugged Tomrin. “Your friend, Gaz,” she said. “His mother. You know—” 
Tomrin dropped his pack next.
“Yes, Janet,” Robles said, dropping his pack next to his. “We heard. My aunty sent word right away, but work kept us.” Tomrin admired the remarkable ease with which Robles stretched the truth. Work had kept them, but that was not why they were so late. He himself felt a near stranger in his father’s tent, not wishing to inconvenience. He’d shaken his father’s hand, and hugged his mother and sister awkwardly, and his stance now was one he found he had around strangers. 
“I was just lamenting not having a fourth for a good game of Pincushion.” Dable said. He rubbed his hand together as he eyed the two of them with his green eyes. 
“I don’t know,” Tomrin replied. “It’s late.” 
“I have time for one game,” Robles said. “Single Dip?” 
“Double,” said Dable. “Takes more skill.” 
Robles didn’t argue with him and he, Dable, Tomrin and Emy sat down on cushions around a small lacquered travel table while Janet Ocrin sat on a pillow in the corner, continuing her sewing. Dable removed the pincushion cards from the table’s drawer as Robles sat opposite Tomrin. Emy opposite their father. 
“Want to deal king’s for teams?” Dable said as he shuffled. 
“What I could really go for,” replied Robles, “Is some of that tarsk you are famous for. Built up a thirst walking up here.” 
“This heat is something else,” Dable said as he riffled the cards. “Janet, some tarsk>” 
“None for Emy,” Janet replied standing up. 
Emy frowned. “I’m not too young for tarsk. Not for real tarsk.” 
“She can have a shell,” Dable said. 
“Fine, but only a small shell,” Janet said.
A Touch of Cards [?]
Janet poured them each a shell. 
	Emy’s frown turned to a satisfied grin, which she turned on Robles who winked at her when he spilled a bit of his drink into her anemic cup of the milky fluid.
Tomrin tipped a touch of his in as well and took a sip. The bitterness of real tarsk was quite different from fake tarsk, which most public houses sold from large wooden barrels, not the stone jug Janet had poured the real stuff from. It  numbed his lips and tongue, and didn’t burn. He set his shell down and played his cards. They sadly, unlike the real tarsk, were total garbage.
Robles betted against Dable, as both he and Emy folded early. Pincushion was a game of luck and skill, and guts. Tomrin hated the aspect of luck and he didn’t have the skill or the guts. His father, however, never lacked for guts and won the first bet. They dipped: Dable into Emy’s cards and Robles into Tomrin’s. 
During the play, Emy collected her and Dable’s tricks, and Tomrin took in his and Robles’. The rounds became more serious as they neared the end of the first hand. The playful banter ended, and only quick reminders broke the silence. It’s your play? I know. Who led? I did? Dable wasn’t sweating, but his shell of tarsk sat idle as Robles rolled his own shell in his free  hand. It was Emy’s turn to play a card on Dable’s lead. Robles had sloughed. Now she didn’t know what to do.
“I forgot,” she whined. “I think I miscounted.” 
“No cheating,” Janet Ocrin called from her sewing. Talking about the play was against the rules. 
“I’m not cheating.” Emy said. 
Tomrin quickly counted his and Robles’ stack of cards. It was slightly less than he and Robles needed for an upset. “Dad’s play does look good,” Tomrin said, skirting the rules. It wasn’t a lie. The play was off suit, but the higher cards in it had found their ways into her’s and Dabel’s stack already.
“Think,” Dable commanded Emy. “You know what to do.” 
“No, I don’t, I can’t remember,” she said, soughing. Tomrin was forced to follow suit. 
It was Dable’s trick. Emy scooped it up as Dable dropped the rest of his cards with a holler. “Hah! It didn’t even matter! Tomrin didn’t have the Queen, I did!” 
“And it was already played,” Tomrin said, feeling annoyed. It was the trick he’d messed up, and that had cost them the upset. 
As the Saying Goes [?]
“Sorry,” he said as Dable gloated about making it right on the money. 
“I knew I couldn’t go any higher,” Dable said. “I had that one crow left over. And I snuck it home.” He took a sip of his tarsk. “Ahh!” he said, savoring it. Then setting his shell down, he placed his and Emy’s pins in the cushion to mark their score. “So, how’s Menad? The furnaces still hot?”
“Master Lasoloth is dead,” Tomrin said. 
In the corner Janet made the sign of the tree, silently to herself. 
“Dead?” Dable said. “What does that mean?”
Robles answered as he rifled the deck. Fip, fip, fip. “It means we are looking for money to keep the glassworks afloat.” 
“But without Master Laz?” Dable said. “I thought he was the genius behind the operation?” 
Robles pointed the deck of cards at Tomrin. “We have a new glass master.” 
“Well, I’ll be a feather.” Dable said, swearing. “You really that good?” 
“Dable!” Janet chided. “No swearing. When’s his funeral, Robles?” she asked before her husband could respond. 
“There will be no funeral, I’m afraid,” Robles said. “You can’t imagine—I can’t believe it myself. Big Laz was marked with the Scales of Leviathan...all this time.” 
Janet signed herself again at the mention of the Leviathan’s Mark.
“How?” Dabel asked.
“His house caught on fire,” Robles said.
“Oh, dear sweet Seasargus!” Janet said. 
It didn’t sound like a curse but in Tomrin’s heard it was.
His Life an Open Book [?]
All eyes were on Robles, and Tomrin rubbed his chest, discreetly. It had started itching at the first mention of Master Laz. 
“You would have been proud of us,” Robes said. “Tomrin and I went into the house and rescued him.” 
“But he’s still dead?” Dable said, the cards forgotten. 
Tomrin wished Robles would just hurry up and deal the cards out and forget this conversation. His friend, however, recounted the story, and their heroics from Robles mouth were quite different than Tomrin remembered. Tomrin added but a word or two of his own. 
“And Rennly was there?” Dable asked with interest, not hearing how petty it all sounded to Tomrin now.
“Yes, and he is in town too,” Tomrin said, wishing that he himself wasn’t. Better the conversation returned to him and his mastery of the strange and mysterious substance called glass than to linger on Rennly. “We saw him just tonight.” 
“I always hoped that boy would come to good, unlike his father,” Dable said.
Tomrin had hated Rennly growing up. And now that he was marked and Rennly was a censor, he wanted more than nothing to do with him. Feathered Rennly! 
“Dable, don’t talk ill of the dead,” Janet said. “We’re better than that.” 
“Yes, we are,” Dable agreed, looking at the cards in Robles’ hand. “You going to deal them—or are you afraid?” 
“I think I should be,” Robles said with a grin and began dealing the next hand. “Rennly might be interested in helping finance the glasswork,” he added as he delt himself the final card and looked at it.
“Really?” Dable asked, frowning at his own cards, probably holding junk. 
“Not really,” Tomrin said. His own hand looked decent. “If I beat him at staves tomorrow, he said he’d pitch in.” 
“Really? Now that’s a bout I’d pay money to see,” Dable said. “I taught you both staves, and never did get to see a championship match between you.” 
“I’m not going to do it,” Tomrin said, turning to his sister. “Bet’s to you.” Staves and cards weren’t something he wished to become invested in, but if they were going to play, he wanted to at least see this hand out.
A full suit of hard-faced Adamen stared at him from his hand.
 Money Mints Money [?]
The betting continued around Robles and Dable.
“So broke?” Dable asked.
Tomrin pulled out his billfold. “We’re not broke at all.” He picked the paper bank draws from the billfold and set them down on the table, splaying them out like the card. “At least I’m not broke if the shop is,” he added and reached to pick up the notes.
Dable was faster. “Seventy,” he said as his free hand, he covered the notes.
Tomrin drew back his hand as Dable carefully squeezed the notes into a pile and picked them up. He inspected them one at a time.
“Seventy-five” Tomrin said
Dable set down the banknotes. “Not a small chunk of change,” Dable added. “Master Laz paid well.” 
“Maybe a little too well until the end,” Robles said. 
Tomrin tucked the bills away. “Still less than we could make in Corundum. But it’s not about the pay when you talk about glass. It’s all about the upshot.” The term always struck him as oddly violent.
“That’s right,” Robles said. “And I am still passed.” 
“I’m paseds as well,” Emy added.
It was just Tomrin and Dable in the race, and Dable’s turn.
“I think that is why Rennly wants in. He’s a betting man,” Robles said.
“Gambling.” Janet shook her head. 
Tomrin held her distaste for gambling. “Luckily, the glass shop isn’t a gamble now,” he said. “I’ve managed to make the new telescope work better than ever.” Perhaps he was bragging but his father brought that out in him. It was something Janet liked to call them both on. For now, she let him slide. 
“If you need money, don’t ask your brother Hadras, “Dable said. “He’s had a bad year with this heat.” 
“Where is Hadras?” Robles asked. “I didn’t see his tent. 
Dable passed and Emy answered. “He stayed behind with the flock.” 
“We only brought in the prized ones this year,” Dable said. “And I plan to win.”
Tough Play [?]
“Tomrin versus Rennly would be a good match, wouldn’t it?” Robles said as he played his first card. 
Dable agreed when the play came back around to him. “But gambling is out of the question,” he said, glancing at Janet. The play went forward, and the tricks required their attention as Emy gathered in trick after trick. 
“I think I over-bet my hand,” Tomrin said.
“No table talk,” Janet said from her corner.
“Rennly doesn’t understand glass like us,” Robles injected. “He understands horse flesh.” He glanced at Dable. “Like the way you understand wood and animals. That’s why he wants this bout.” It was a lie, but Tomrin didn’t correct him. He was kicking himself for losing the trick, a he had played two cards out of order. 
Dable cut in with a higher card. “So Rennly knows how to pick them?” 
“He wins at the races all the time,” Robles replied. Tomrin gave up on the hand as Dable cut another trick. He began focusing on what was being said. He couldn’t help but think Robles was going somewhere with his words. The cards, however, were heading for an upset. “I saw his new horse,” Tomrin said. “A grey razor. Looks fast.” 
“Thumper is his name and he is fast,” Robles said. “I’m not saying I’ve been gambling at the races, but I’ve seen him run. The old boy has good temper too.” 
“Good for him,” Dable said as the last trick of the hand was played. “You think Tomrin has a chance against him?” 
“Rennly just wants a good fight is all,” Robles said. “And I’m sure he’ll invest in the glass shop regardless.” 
“Really?” Dable said as Emy counted their. “That’s the upset,” he added. He moved the pins in the pincushion. “You think so?” 
“He goes in—you go in?” Robles said. “Safer money that way. What do you say, Dable?” 
“I’d say I hope Tomrin is a better partner at glass than at pincushion.” 
“I’d say.” 
“Sorry, I totally over-bet my hand, but I haven’t agreed to any of this.” 
“Not even to keep the glass shop going?” Dable asked. “What’s it called Godly Glass Shop?” 
“Works. Godly Glassworks.” Tomrin drank a sip of his tarsk. He hoped he’d start to feel its effects soon. He was annoyed with Robles for forcing the issue when all he wanted was a way out of having to fight Rennly, even if having a censor on board would be good on the surface. “I might not even face him, if I were to enter.”
“How many hours did you train?” Robles said. “You can show up this riff-raff; no question in my mind.” 
“I’m out of practice. Maybe in a glass working competition.” 
“Then there would be no contest,” Robles retorted. 
Dable laughed. “You really that good, Tomrin?” 
“There are masters better than me in the world.” 
Dable drank a self-satisfied sip from his shell. “But not in Menad?” 
“Not in Menad,” Robles said. “Maybe not even in Corundum.” 
They started a new hand and played it out, and another before the game was over. Dable and Emy crushed Tomrin and Robles. 
Their early upset had been the death of their chances. 
“Not All Things Are About Me?” [?]
“I’d love to stay and make it up,” Robles said. “But, one skunk, is enough for me.” 
“The funeral in the morning and Sheep Shearing Days,” Emy said. “I need to go to bed too.” 
“I need to find a bed as well,” Tomrin said standing. “The Country House Inn was full.” 
“You can have my bed,” Emy said. Robles was saying his goodnight to Janet. “I can sleep in here,” Emy added.
“You have a good sister,” Robles said as he shook Tomrin’s hand. 
“She’s not for sale.”
Emy slapped Tomrin’s shoulder. “Tomrin, don’t say stuff like that. You are embarrassing me.” 
“That’s what brothers are for,” Tomrin said. They showed Robles out as Dable began recounting their game to Janet as she worked her sewing needle. 
“Remember what I said,” Robles said as he nudged Tomrin’s backpack with a toe and sniffed the night air. 
“What about?” 
“Having to do the things we don’t want to.” 
“I remember.” 
 Robles bowed towards Emy. “Well, my good lady, against my better judgment I leave you with this lunk. Goodnight.” 
Tomrin and Emy said their own goodnights and Robles tacked into the night.
Tomrin picked up his own pack and Emy led him by the star light to the entrance of her tent. She talked about the books she’d managed to read, and quoted lines at him. He listened only with half an ear as his mind wandered. He pretended to be a good listener—asking questions every so often as he managed a façade of interest. 
“I’m so happy you came, and so sad for you,” she said finally hugging him goodnight. 
“Why sad?” he asked, there was no way she could know what he was thinking. 
“Because your friend Gaz. It’s just so sad, so tragic.” 
“Yes,” Tomrin agreed. “So pointless and tragic.”
A Prelude to the Ode to the End [?]
Emy lit the lamp in her tent while Tomrin waited outside. 
He needed to find a way out of matching staves with Rennly. The Oriloes boy was always stronger, faster, and full of retribution, a troubling combination. I’ve been in Woolad for only a few hours and already things are going south. 
“We missed you so much,” Emy said, then left him for the larger Ocrin tent. 
The lamps had been turned down and Emy disappeared as she entered. 
Tomrin pushed his pack through the flap then ducked inside the smaller of the two Orin tents. Everything neatly arranged, inside it smelled of myrrh from the match she’d used to light the lamp. The match still leaked its smoke and incense into the air, a small gray streamer. The lamp and ashtray sat together on her rainbow colored chest. He recognized the bronze lamp and wooden ashtray. They had both been his when he lived with his family on the steppes. 
Her bed was made prim, as his never was. He saw no point in making his own, but he liked the clean feeling of the sheets when he slid between them after trying the flap and snuffing the lamp. He laid down his eyes open in the dark a moment, then kicked off the top cover and undid the linens. He couldn’t sleep like that. He never could. 
He closed his eyes, exhausted, and drained by the day’s events and those of the previous. He felt both harassed and harried. Robles was nipping at him to fight Rennly, and so was his father in his own way. At the glass shop, Tomrin had worked quite closely with Master Laz and now he wondered what it meant that he felt nothing. 
The glass shop was in trouble; he was in trouble with it. He and it were linked. Now if it failed he didn’t know what he would do.
The idea of having to fight Rennly twisted his guts. There had to be a different way. But what was it? He wrecked his brain wishing he could just tell Robles: No, we’ll find another way. But what? 
If they just had gold, everything else would be easy. With gold they could fly. They could keep the shop in gas. They could ramp up production of the Scarab. New, bigger Scarabs, bigger scopes, and smaller ones for regular use. Glass was the easy part. Money, however, was too easy to spend. There waa a million ways to waste gold, yet so hard to come by. 
Tomrin believed he understood Master Laz just a little better.
The Contamination Leaks Through [?]
Are you contaminating my mind? he asked the mass growing in his chest.
Tomrin wondered if had driven Master Laz to kill himself? Or was just the thought of Purification. As far as Tomrin knew, no man ever returned from Purification and the gods and the censors and the Daughters made it clear: any man who didn’t turn himself in for Purification suffered far worse than the others.
The coiled ball inside even looked evil, but Tomrin felt it more than saw. It pulsed with each heartbeat. Tight coils slipping past another like yarn in skeins, but this yarn was thinner, thes threads so fine he wasn’t sure it wasn’t just a mist, not threads at all. Colorless and dark, the demon’s corruption filled it. He needed to get back to Menad soon before it and the fish-like scale on his chest grew too large. 
He touched the burn under his shirt. After the green light the day before he wasn’t taking any chances, especially not here in Woolad. In Menad Master Laz thought to live out his life like nothing was the matter with him. I have to be smarter than him, he told himself. His run in with Lady Margretta was a wake-up call. More careful. He didn’t know what he would do if she became an even larger problem. He had nowhere to look for help. 
Gold, he thought. It all came down to gold. He hated that money greased the skids, oiled the wheels of fate. He hated that he needed money to make money. Maybe if Rennly had died instead of Gaz. It was a dark thought, but Tomrin was beginning to hate the boy again, and here when he had thought he had finally put all the anger and hate and shame behind him. It was back again, growing.
Why can’t I sleep? An answer floated up to him from the dark embrace of Emy’s tent: Nightmares. 
He feared the otherness buried deep within the machinations of his mind, and he reopened his eyes and stared at the dark gray ceiling of his sister’s tent. 
A crossbeam held the crown of it up. He stared at it. He stared and stared. Ideas and thoughts intruded. One by one he pushed them away and stared some more. He tried not to think about the nightmares which awaited him. Maybe it would be Gaz come back to haunt him, or with his luck the demons from Enfernus. Or visions of Seasargus bent on his Purification. “Feathers,” Tomrin finally swore to himself, becoming frustrated. A man couldn’t live without sleep. I’ll go mad. 
Finally his eyes shut of their own accord.
Chapter 15: A Dirge
	“Thus he spoke, and thus it was written, both on the foundations of the earth and on the bodies of the sky.”
		—From The Book of Light

Tomrin came awake fully as if struck by lightning. For a second he wondered if he was dying. Cold sweat covered his body and the inside of his sister’s tent was dark, but not pitch black. The gray of pre-dawn, the morning twilight outside, blurred his vision as he stared up at the tent’s crossbar. The dying feeling slowly vanished. He couldn’t believe he’d slept. He didn’t remember dreaming, and felt as tired as when he laid down. He closed his eyes. If he had slept, he’d not slept long enough.
“Tomrin!” It was his father’s voice. “Wake up.” Something batted the side of the tent near the flap. Wap. 
Tomrin wanted to yell at his father, to tell him to quit striking the tent, and to go away and leave him alone. “Why?” he demanded instead. His father, Dable Ocrin, had never been one Tomrin could dictate to, nor to his wife, Janet, his mother. Now he was in their tent, and relying on their hospitality and he felt his father was taking advantage of that. 
“You need to get up, we have to hurry.” 
Tomrin sighed, it sounded like a growl, low and disgusted. “The funeral isn’t for hours,” he said. He just wanted to go back to sleep, but Dable rapped the tent wall again. Wap.
“I said get up.” 
“I’m up!” 
The mass in Tomrin’s chest throbbed with irritation. He remembered now why he hated staying with his parents. They never cared what he wanted. His father liked to find him work to do, and his mother always wanted that work to be the gods’ work. 
Tomrin stood up and opened the tent flaps in his small clothes and shirt. “Put some pants on,” Dable said, glancing up and down. His father wore his white robe, and the light of dawn showed his green eyes better than the lamp light. “And your boots.” 
Tomrin suppressed the urge to tell his father to jump in the Lake of Fire. He had sworn at him once before, just before he’d left for the Menad. It left him with a sense of unease for days, for weeks. He often thought of it and hated the idea of such an incident occurring again.
Instead he sat on Emy’s bed and pulled on his pants he’d shucked the night before. With a groan he stomped into his leather boots. “What are we doing up so early?” he asked when he ducked out the tent flap. The sky was growing brighter by the second. The sun rising in the west was hidden by tents, but the orange glow was there. The day would soon be hot. Tomrin suspected his father wanted to have him help him with the animals. “Sheep?”
Dable grabbed a stick from where it leaned with a mate against the side of Emy’s tent, and handed it to Tomrin. “Here you go,” he said. “You remember this staff?” 
Tomrin did. He had made it at the carpenter shop. He’d thought it long lost. “Yes. It’s too heavy and big.” It was made of oak and he had carved some feathers on it, but it remained unfinished, the work far too slow. The staff was useless from the weight, and had all the wrong play and no give.
Dable Ocrin took the other lighter staff in hand. “Follow me.”
“Why?” Tomrin asked, and let his staff’s butt down heavily. “What can we hope to accomplish?” 
“We can knock the dust off.” 
 Mixing It Up [?]
Tomrin followed his father to a corner of the campground where the ground was uneven and scattered with rocks. None of the tents which were now coming alive with people had used the area. Tomrin was almost more afraid his father would hurt himself than anything. “This seems like a bad idea,” he said. “What about your leg?”
“My leg isn’t going to fight Rennly today,” Dable said. “You need to remember the basics.” 
“I haven’t agreed to fight him either,” Tomrin said.
Dable came at him with a slow, lumbering attack. Tomrin would have had no problem defending had his feathered staff not tugged at his hands as if made of lead.
For a minute they sparred.
Dable delivered classic attacks: the Blacksmith’s Hammer, the Swam’s Sickle. Several of the attacks went through but the slap of wood only hurt because flinching wrenched his chest wound.
“It looks like your hinges are totally rusted,” Dable said. He was a large man, and his red face in the dawn glistened from exertion. “Rennly is going to take you apart,” he said as leaned against his smaller staff. 
Tomrin breathed heavily and leaned on his own. “I never even liked mixing staves,” he said. He picked up his staff and headed back towards the tent and wagons. 
The sun’s light pressed down and felt heavy and hot against his back.
Dable caught up with him. “Last night, your mother and I decided we’d help you and the glass shop, but only if Rennly does.”
Tomrin scoffed. “Great.”
“It is called Godly Glassworks, isn’t it? With a censor there, your mother feels there would be nothing wrong with it.”
Tomrin scoffed again but before he could say more, Dable’s was passing out blessings as if he was Seasargus himself.
Greatness Is Earned [?]
Outside the large Ocrin tent, Tomrin asked his father in a low tone. “Why?” 
It was a broad question, but Tomrin thought it was clear enough. Tomrin knew his mother and father. If money was being spent, it was because Dable wanted it such. “You don’t need to be mixed up in this.”
“I want to see you do well,” Dable replied. “And if I make a little more gold because of it, Seasargus won’t mind.”
“Not because Rennly my half-brother?” Rumors had swirled, but Tomrin had always ignored them. His father had always nurtured a competition between them.
“He is,” Dable replied without embarrassment. “But you should not mention that in front of your mother. And, now, you should get dressed.” 
Tomrin glanced down at his clothes—the same as he wore the day before. They were clean enough. “I am dressed,” he said, anger leaking into his voice. “Good enough to dance against Rennly. Like you danced with his mother.”
“Only Seasargus can judge me!” Dable said, heat finally entering his own low voice.
Tomrin gritted his teeth. “Let me have this,” he said. “This is my thing. Keep your money, and if you want to make Rennly your great son, or even Hadras, do it. I’ll even tell my mother to stop lobbying on my behalf--if she does. I don’t even know.” The mass of evil in Tomrin’s chest heaved, pulsed with every angry beat of his heart. “I can make my own fortune--my own way.”
“Your mother doesn’t lobby anyone. She, like Claire Ricot, is a good woman. Now, go change into clean clothes for Gaz’s funeral. You look like a feathered bum, and not my son,” Dable said and stalked off.
As he headed to Emy’s tent Tomrin cursed Rennly, actually wanting to fight him. I can beat him, he told himself as he entered the tent. While he changed into the tan and blue of mourning he fantasized what it would be like to actually win. But who am I kidding? He knew he had lost all finesse with the staff. I apply my mind, not my body. 
He wanted to stay above such base contests of the flesh. Then he could more easily ignore the Luster calling to him even now as it whispered of tainted powers and evil deeds. If he gave in to it he would become nothing but a hollow empty shell, a servant of the demons. Then he would actually need to be purified.
Dressed, he headed back to the large Ocrin tent. The flaps were tied open. He barely had to duck to enter. His mother was serving his Dabel with Emy’s help. They all wore white robes and, on their arms were tied yellow and blue ribbons. His mother eyed him critically. 
“Tomrin, you can’t wear that to the funeral,” she said. 
“The colors are way off,” Emy added.
Tomrin walked over to the squat table. “They are close enough,” he said sitting. “If the ushers don’t let me in, so be it. My world is glass.” He wanted desperately to get back to it, to Menad, and away from here.
“Don’t be arrogant, Tomrin,” said his mother, as she set a tin tray of cut apples and cheese for him. Emy set a cup of water next to it. 
“We have an extra robe for you,” Emy said. “And stripes.” 
“After food,” Dable said. He said a prayer once Janet and Emy sat down to eat. 
The apple tasted old but bursting with flavor, if spongy. The cheese was white and made from goat milk. Both tasted strong and savory, yet not decadent like the breakfasts Mistress Carls served in Menad. 
When they finished his mother found him a robe, one from her own chest. “Put this one,” she said, “I’ll find the extra stripes.” 
Tomrin sighed, but took the robe over his arm. There was no point in resisting. He wasn’t arrogant, he just didn’t care about conforming to all the small superstitious ideas that pervaded the small minds of Woolad and its surrounding environs.
Holding the robe up, Tomrin stepped outside. 
What Comes of Day [?]
Dable headed with him, but went to check on the animals in the pens.
When Tomrin re-entered Emy’s tent, he laid down the white cashmere robe and tied the tent flaps closed. Just what I need, someone sticking in their head with my shirt off. 
He undressed and pulled the robe over his head. After struggling a minute he finally found the hand and arm holes. Tugging the robe down, he tied its rope belt, looping it over and adjusting it the best he could. Untying the tent flaps, he headed outside feeling phony. White was for anointment and atonement.
The pulsing mass of evil gray inside his chest mocked him. You just needed a shepherd’s crook to complete your disguise. Great son? More like false son. Tomrin wondered if maybe Dable wasn’t really his father. It was silly, because of course the old man was. His own green eyes were his, if not his temperament. 
Back in the main tent, Tomrin let his mother and Emy tie the streamers on his upper arms to match their own. “Now, we all look like a family,” Emy said. 
He and Emy sat on the cushions in front of the lacquered table and started up a game of two-handed pincushion while they waited for Dable to return. Emy talked and Tomrin distractedly listened. She had met a boy on the steppes, but it turned out he wasn’t the one. They played two hands, before their mother admonished Emy that she was talking way too much. “Talking poor Tomrin’s ear off.” 
“I don’t mind,” he said. It let him think, to ruminate on his own thoughts. He was trying to think back to the moment when everything seemed to spin out of his control. “The green glow,” he said. 
“What?” Emy asked him, pausing her own chatter a moment. 
“Oh, nothing,” Tomrin replied.
She continued on about her dream from the night before.
To Temple [?]
Dable returned and they headed for the temple. 
Near the temple, the stream of yellow and blue clad mourners pooled up until the flow through the door all but stalled. Orish families, tall, wide and rough looking—even in their mourning garb—loomed over little old Rubish women, their faces ashen with age. A small number of Ruddy skinned Peregrine men and women and several Orioles were amongst the mourners.
A group of censors parted the crowd, Rennly at their head. They didn’t have to say a word. Out of fear or respect people parted before the black clad men in silk suits, batons wagging at their hips. The censors were Orioles to a man and they looked like they had come from Menad and Zircos before that. By some cruel luck they lived when Big Laz and Gaz were dead. 
The injustice of it burnt in Tomrin’s heart as one of the men laughed as they neared the door. Emy spoke quietly in Tomrin’s ear, but he lost the thread of her meaning as his anger simmered, not too hot, but not yet cold. Nothing to be done, Tomrin told himself. Maybe if time was an illusion as the Luster claimed, but Master’s Laz and Gaz were dead, and tragically that could not be undone. Laz, if not Gaz, was a visionary. These men, though, with their bludgeons, what were they? Small minds. If one of them were to die, what loss to the world? Someone would mourn them probably, but just as likely someone would laugh at their funeral too.
Master Laz wasn’t the Lightshy Crow. He was a man, not a carrion eater. You should be the Lightshy Crow. Just the Luster talking and Tomrin pushed the thought down even as his heart quickened. Am I a fool for not taking Lady Margretta’s offer? The raw power of the tainted gift would make him great and terrible. He would not fear Rennly. He would fear nothing. You could live as a god.
Emy touched his arm. “Sad for Gaz?” she said. “We all are.”
