Tomrin wiped the angry tears from his eyes. “Yes,” he said, “And for Master Laz. I could have done more.”
“You did everything you could.” 
Tomrin sniffed, swallowing saltiness. “I know.” He didn’t like lying to his sister. He had no qualms lying to his father or mother, but it was different with Emy. “I could have done more before the explosion.” 
“You can’t beat yourself up over it.” 
“I know,” he said. “That’s Rennly’s job it seems.” 
Emy suppressed a laugh, making a squeaking noise. “You’re going to enter the competition?”
“No,” Tomrin said. “I don’t know...a woman, a lady, she said she could help us.” 
“With money for the shop?” 
“I mean Lazoloth, and the shop when he was still alive.” 
Emy made a face, as if thinking. “He was wrong for not turning himself over for Purification.
“But he didn’t have to die,” Tomrin said. “Life is so fragile,”
“I’m just happy you’re safe with us.” 
Tomrin forced out a smile. “I just hope the new glass shop finally succeeds.” 
“I have faith in you.”
Tomrin wished he shared her faith in that, if nothing else.
The Pilgrimage [?]
They reached the doors and ushers showed Tomrin them to their seats.
Men and women began to pack the Temple full. When Tomrin stole a glance around he realized he recognized many of the people despite it having been years since he’d been inside the Woolad temple. He did, however, remember it being this stuffy.
On the altar, Gaz’s long rectangular coffin lay covered in a red velvet pall. Members of his family and their owners, an older Rubish couple, Master and Mistress Clivent sat in the first row. They looked very much like the farmers that they were, their yellow and blue as drab as burlap itself. 
Behind them were Rennly and the censors. They were less impressive than their counterparts in the cut glass with its gods, Daughters, and glass-censors, yet the gray stone walls radiated a drabness to fill the place—like a pig in a dress. In with the fleshy pig-nosed Dutters and red-headed Clittons from a grandfather who had hailed from the north Adamant, and for families and faces
Tomrin realized he recognized as many he didn’t. The people Tomrin didn’t recognise seemed out of place, their blues and yellows as strained as his tan pants and blue gray shirt. Outsiders, he thought, but remembered he wore the pilgrim white,and he was no resident himself. Sheep Shearing Days.
“Lookers-on,” he said to Emy in a whisper. 
“What?” she asked back, but Tomrin couldn’t respond. Janet hushed him, and on Tomrin’s right Dable gave them both a look. No one should have been talking, yet the temple buzzed with the dull sound of boredom. The doors closed. Minutes passed but no cantor arrived at the front. 
A cough form the balcony turned Tomrin’s head. The choir too was dressed in white, streamers on their arms. In them was Robles. The boy grinned down and flipped his streamers at Tomrin and mouthed something. Tomrin couldn’t understand what he was saying and after shaking his head, he gave up and looked away. 
Tomrin had missed Bethany on his first perusal, but she sat on on his left, beyond his family and hers. He hardly recognized her. She was looking more beautiful than he remembered, her hair long and in a black braid, like Emy’s and Janet’s.
Hers, however, was burnished to an onyx glow.
Up in Smoke [?]
Tomrin glanced away when the cantor entered with two altar boys in white. 
They wore no yellow or blue morning band, but cords of red dyed silk about their waists. The cantor wore yellow and blue vestments. In his right hand he held a gilded shepherd’s crook. Had it been solid gold the thing would weigh a ton and a half. 
Both boys held censers wafting smoke and the smell of incense with each swing at their sides. Tomrin had their job once but he did not know the cantor. Though old, the man moved with a solid grace. Slowly, the mummer of errant whispers ceased. 
The man began to cant, and his voice, low and impressive in its depth, rebounded through the high-ceiled temple. Tomrin drifted in his thoughts, sluggishly keeping his eyes open as sleep tugged at his mind. Nothing the cantor said hadn’t been said before and with more passion, with more emotion. The man began to drone. The service crawled to its inevitable conclusion, the only decent parts were when the choir sang, Robles’ clear voice carrying them.
Then the casket with Gaz’s body was carried out a side door to the burial yard by his surviving relatives. The front of the temple emptied pew by pew as the congregants joined the cantor and the altar boys and Gaz’s family outside on the brown grass. Those who came stood and clustered around an open grave as the casket was lowered into the dry earth. 
Bethany stood so close to Tomrin that her scent cut through the smell of dried earth. Bethany Karlite. She was like a dark presence pushing against his mind.
Rennly and the other censors stood on the other side of Gaz’s grave. Rennly’s bright green eyes flashed with the dark hint of a smile when they lit Bethany. 
She shrugged at him, like he was nothing to her.
A Last Goodbye [?]
Robles and the choir sang as shovel loads of dirt dropped from the dry pile onto the coffin. 
The spade passed from person to person. First Gaz’s mother then step-father and his two-half brothers, both taller and thinner than Gaz, their hair thinning on top exposing their short, upswept ears. Finally, Gaz’s youngest sister tossed on a bit of dirt and handed the shovel to Rennly. Her eyes were red but she moved unblinking as if in a daze and hurried after the others of her clan.
Rennly attacked the dirty pile with the shovel. Chlank. 
Pebbles of rock and dirt clattered the wooden coffin. 
For a second Tomrin and Rennly’s eyes locked. A demonic hatred leaked out of Rennly’s greedy eyes, and Tomrin glanced away. Feathers. Rennly was not going to let things be. Not now, not ever. Insanity had taken his step-brother. Tomrin wondered if Rennly knew they were brothers.
When it came to Tomrin’s turn to shovel dirt on Gaz’s casket, he used both hands but not his feet to sink the spade into the dirt. The undertaker would see the grave filled fully, this was only symbolic work. Goodbye old friend. Levering the shovel, Tomrin dropped his dirt and handed the shovel to Emy then headed inside out of the late morning sun.
Even in white he was beginning to fry.
Then the service was over with a dirge.
Reverberations [?]
Men and women exited with voices raised louder than the earlier clamor. 
The funeral was over. Seasargus be praised. The thought was not entirely ironic. It was just one thing out of the hundreds the cantor had said. Gaz was still dead, still insensate. There was nothing in the old man’s words but empty platitudes and false hope. Sadness finally choked Tomrin as he and Emy and Dable and Janet headed out the same doors they had first entered.
He forced himself to breathe even as Emy cried in the landing area outside the temple. Everyone talked freely now. Dable spoke with Gaz’s family; trite words and Tomrin was happy to be away from their pain.
When Robles joined Tomrin, he and Dable were headed back to Emy and Janet. Dable was talking about the cantor. “The man sermonized worse than ever. I don’t know where they get these men. Their views are so outside, so unorthodox.”
Tomrin wasn’t sure what his problem was with the cantor’s views. Once he might have cared what his father was saying, but Tomrin’s own views had turned from the unorthodox to downright dangerous. “The music was good,” he said, turning to Robles, “You raised a pleasing sound to the heavens.” 
Robles licked his lips like a hot dog. “I can howl with the best of them.” He wore his white choir robe. It was linen, not breathing cashmir, and looked hot. 
“You had some competition on the ground,” Dable said. 
“I did?” 
“He means Rennly,” Tomrin said. 
“He is a decent singer.” 
“He seemed to be good at what he does,” Dable said. “Maybe Tomrin is wise to avoid him in the ring.” 
“Well it’s too late for that,” Tomrin said. “I’ve already decided to enter the competition.”
Dable clapped him on his back. “That’s my boy!” 
“What tipped the scales?” Robles asked. 
“Rennly. He’s determined to do something. I may not be good at the staff anymore—but I once was. I hope that can carry me. And you are right—this is something I have to do that I don’t want to. If I lose, we can always find other money. Or other work if it comes to that. We have the skills.”
“Time to toss the die?” Dable said. 
“Yes,” Tomrin said, pointing his index finger. “I hate gambling, but Rennly is forcing me to it, and not just him, but you two it seems as well.” 
“At least you know staves,” Robles said. “And sometimes when a dog bites you, your only hope is to bite back.”
“You have hope,” Dabel said, lowering his eyebrows at that. It was his tell that he too believed what Robles said but would not admit for fear of Janet, whom he kept happy above all others in outings like this. 
She too frowned.
Chapter 16: Sheep Shearing Days
	Such is the crow and such is the light, and such is the feeling he gets every night!
		—A Rubish nursery rhyme

Tomrin licked sugar and powdered cinnamon from his fingers.
Bachnam’s Bakery ran the pastry tent, and he and Robles had cut a bee line for it. Men and women ate at benches under the tents. Nearby other foods and drink were purveyed at booths and buildings. Many of the people in them still wore their yellow and blue.
A light breeze did little to cool Tomrin. Even under the tents the heat forced sweat from his pores as he ate his fried and sugared scone. Too hot, he thought. Too hot for hot food. “It’s too hot for anything.”
“Not for the first scone of the Sheep Shearing Days,” Robles said, washing his finger with a bit of water from his tin. “Good, but sticky.” His friend hated anything on his fingers and hands. When he wore gloves, he cut fingers out, like a beggar or orphaned pickpocket. 
“Mine was nutty and good,” Tomrin said and washed the pastry and the sweetened spices from his mouth and tongue with a sip of water from his own tin. It was cool from the ice—which had already melted. The iced water had cost them nearly as much as the scones, several coopers each. He set down the tin. “Rennly,” he said, nodding. 
His half-brother and two censors from the funeral approached down the white quartz path between the booths. 
Robles wiggles his fingers to dry them. “If you’re worried, maybe we should practice.” 
“I mean, here he comes,” Tomrin said. “I’ve already practiced.” The workout with his father hadn’t helped, but he didn’t mention that. 
“Time to work our magic,” Robles said, ending his finger dance. 
“Your magic,” Tomrin said. “I leave this to you. It is your idea.” 
“Tomrin Ocrin and Robles Kodragus; Woolad’s prodigal sons,” Rennly said to his two fellow censors. 
Without leaving the bench, Robles bowed, still sitting at the table. “And at your service.”
“Robles, why don’t you show these two around,” Rennly said without bothering to introduce the two censors with him. “Tomrin and I have business to discuss.” 
“But I am his business partner.” 
“And don’t you trust him?” Rennly asked, sweeping his hands in dismissal. “I’m sure he’ll tell you all about our little conversation.” 
“Robles stays,” Tomrin injected. 
Rennly’s bright green eyes gleamed. “Then I guess we have nothing to discuss,” he said, adjusting his shirt, his golden feather badge glinting in the sun.
Robles stood. “Of course I trust him, and I’d be delighted to show your friends around. Maybe a quick game of darts.” Robles led the two censors off down the path, offering to make their game more interesting. “A small wager on the outcome?”
Rules of the Game [?]
A Orish man, counselor Marten, pushed past Robles and the censors. What’s he doing here? Tomrin wondered. If he was here, then the woman with the ruby hair and dark skin had to be close. The idea of a False Daughter here threatened to unnerve him, but the man headed straight for the Bakery’s ordering window. 
Rennly sat down.
“Where was Calena this morning for Gaz’s funeral?” Tomrin asked, thinking to toss Rennly off with his own line of question. “I didn’t see her.” 
“She decided to head home to Menad,” Rennly said. “The food at the Country House Inn disagreed with her this morning.” Rennly picked up Robles’ tin and took a drink. “One of the sauces.” 
“You sick?” 
“If only you were that lucky,” Rennly said. “But no, I didn’t have any.” Rennly’s eyes flashed a cruel green, and for a second they glowed with his insanity.
“I don’t rely on luck,” Tomrin said. “I only bet on sure things.” 
“So, you don’t want the gold?” Rennly said. 
“I didn’t say that,” Tomrin said quickly, wishing Robles had stayed. The Rennly fish was rapidly slipping off the line. “You want this bout—you clearly want to prove something.” 
“I have nothing to prove,” Rennly replied. The large Orish man, Marten, took a scone loaded with jelly from the delivery window and seemingly intent on it sat silently down behind Rennly.
Tomrin pursed his lips. “What is it then, vengeance?” he asked. “Or is it envy?” 
Rennly scoffed, “I don’t even know what you are talking about.” 
“Something is driving you, Rennly? What is it? Whatever it is, it is not going to turn out well. You need to give it up...for your own good.” 
“My own good?” Rennly asked. “Since when have you thought about anyone but yourself?” Rennly’s tone turned menacing, and a seething anger slithering through his teeth like a snake. “You aren’t even sad Gaz is dead.”
“Ah, we shouldn’t do this, Rennly,” Tomrin said and swept his hand towards the path and the way towards the staves. “It’s dangerous, and not just for us, but for others too.”
Rennly didn’t seem to hear. He smacked his teeth and licked his lips. “Say we do this, what’s to keep you from just running off and sticking me with the deal? That’s my concern.”
Riddle [?]
“That you ask proves you don’t know anything about me,” Tomrin said, stalling. Rennly was correct about him being a coward, but Tomrin was not about to let him know that. “I talked to my father this morning.” 
“About what?” Rennly asked and Tomrin was a little surprised how easy it was to distract his step-brother. Maybe he knows?
“We talked about things. He’s watched your progression with interest. Just as he watches mine. And how this—” Tomrin swirled his hand through the air as he amended the conversation. “—All this is not healthy for you. You need to let it go. I can see it inside you. My father thinks you are a good person, but—” Tomrin folded his arms, and tried not to wince too badly as he made his face stern. “—I’m afraid this thing between us is going to lead you to a bad end.” 
“Your concern is most comforting,” Rennly sneered. “So is this tough guy attitude you seem to have adopted now. I have a story you should hear?” 
“Feathers,” Tomrin swore, but didn’t have time to properly answer before Rennly continued, and while Marten’s ears didn’t exactly perk up, the Orish man raised an eyebrow.
“After my father died and my mother and I moved up into the Emerald Forest, she took me to see an old woman. You know the kind?” 
“Do I?” Tomrin asked. He wasn’t sure where Rennly was going with all this but he was afraid where it might be. 
“She was strange, strange beyond weird. Her hut was a wreck of bones and twigs, and she looked like she was on the verge of death. Her bones poked from her papery-yellow skin. She claimed she could see the future in the lines on my hand.” Rennly rubbed a thumb across his palm. “She told me I have one thing and only one thing to fear.” Rennly balled his fists on the table. “Now you see why I have no fear of you or this match?” 
“Really? What did she say?” Tomrin asked. “Without knowing, your story makes no sense. Why tell it?” 
Rennly snorted a laugh, “Ha. She said I only had to fear the Lightshy Crow, you Peregrine’s boogeyman.”
“Makes sense. It’s the only thing I fear—myself,” Tomrin added puzzled at his own slip of the tongue, and for a moment a shadow, like that cast by the Corvine Man himself seemed to hover over him. Marten frowned into the table and into the half-chewed scone in his massive Orish hands. 
“I don’t believe in tales slaves tell,” Rennly said, coming out of reverie. “You’re afraid to fight me. I can see that. You’re afraid I might slip and hurt you? The fear is in your eyes. Who is the coward now?”
Smarts [?]
An unpleasant thrill shot through Tomrin at the words coming out his mouth echoing his own.
“Not I and despite my misgivings,” he said. “I am submitting to you a wager about the outcome.”
“What? Submit to me a wager?” Rennly asked. “What are you even saying?”
“What am I saying?” Tomrin said. “I’m enter the staves competition. But—” He held up a finger. “—Win or lose you buy in.”
“That’s quite the wager. Win or lose!” Rennly spat. “And what guarantee you’ll even try? 
“Barring a dark horse—you know we’ll see each other in the ring.” 
“That’s not good enough.” Rennly’s eyes were as hard as flint glass. “How about this and because I want to fight you...to see what you’re made of, I—” 
“Not to beat the tar out of me because of...what happened?” Tomrin asked. “Or because…? Nevermind. Fine. If for some reason we don’t meet in the champion’s ring. We will have our own bout—just you and me.” 
Rennly made a thoughtful sound in his throat. “No,” he said. “We’ll have our remedial bouts—regardless if we meet.” 
“What do you mean?” 
“I mean, if you don’t win, we will have another bout, and another until you do.” 
Tomrin made a noise. His idea had been good, but Rennly had just twisted it. “That won’t work.”
“Sure, it will. After work—if I will find you or send you word to come to the temple—you could use more prayer in your life anyway.” 
“Only until I beat you?” Tomrin said, stalling and afraid to offer anything more for fear Rennly would twist that too. 
“That’s right.” 
“Fine,” Tomrin said, “And you will cover operations, payable every month. Automatic transfers until the glassworks ends its draws?” 
“Equity for every draw,” Rennly said pointing at the table. 
“Naturally. But no control.” 
Rennly pointed to his badge. “You forget I have powers no matter what. The power of censure.” 
“Only that.” 
“I think every night might be too often,” Rennly mused. “Maybe the first week or so. To whip you into shape. To help you remember your Peregrine blood.”
“Rennly,” Tomrin tsked. “Such petty barbs. Look around you. Your friends aren’t even here to snicker.”
A Contract [?]
Marten stood and walked over as Robles and the censors returned.
The place was filling up as more from the funeral came for food and drink.
“I...you—” Marten said, gesturing at Tomrin. “I saw you in Menad. I couldn’t help but overhear your conversation.” 
“Best you forget it then,” Rennly said, irritated at the interruption at the point of his strongest insult. 
“I will do that,” Marten replied as Robles and the other censors walked into the tents’ shade. “And I can also offer my services...as an advocate. I do contracts on the side.” The man was wearing his vest from the other day. Other than his ancestry, he looked dressed every bit a lawyer. 
Tomrin wanted to ask the man if he was following him. And where is the dark lady? Tomrin glanced at Robles and stood up. “We worked out the details.” 
“Well, not exactly,” Marten said. “If you want, I can help fill them in. I can write and file the contract so it’s official.” 
“What are the details? A deal is struck?” Robles asked.
“Yes, and if—” Rennly raised a hand to point at Marten. 
“Counsellor Marten, no esquire—” 
“Obviously,” Rennly said. The man was Orish and was technically a slave. He could hold no title, not in Corundum nor anywhere. 
“Obviously,” Marten said easily and continued. He outlined the deal, adding dates and party-this and party-that and herefores seemingly at random, but what he said was largely what Rennly agreed to. “And I can write it up for you to sign for a small fee.” 
Robles and Marten huggled over the fee. “And I want it before the competition is over,” Robles added. 
“Done,” Marten replied. “Now if you excuse me, I have a spot of work to do.” He left the pavilion at a dignified pace. 
Rennly wore a wry smile. “You worked him down for my benefit?” 
“You need to know that your money will not be wasted here, at Godly Glasswork.” Robles said with a bow. “But if you will excuse us as well, it seems Tomrin and I have a bit of a bout to prepare for.”
“Yes,” Rennly said with a mocking bow of his own. “And let’s make it a good one.”
Regrets [?]
“I can’t help feeling we’ve gotten into bed with an asp,” Tomrin said as he and Robles headed for the staves booth.
The booths and games were in full swing. The knives and trinkets for sale at one booth caught Tomrin’s eye. It was all junk though; all flash and gilt with no craftsmanship. At the staves pavilion two young boys sparred as judges called out points for foot work as well as blocks and strikes. In the adults’ division only strikes would count. 
Robles signed Tomrin up at the table and paid the woman behind the counter the entry fee, “And, can we borrow a couple of staves to practice with?” he asked, shining her his brightest smile. “Mistress Gorkel.” 
“Against the rules,” she replied. Tight curled hair ringed her stony face. 
“But—” 
“No, Master Robles,” she snapped. “The rules are the rules.” 
“I have staves back at the tent,” Tomrin said. 
“Good, I’d hate to break any rules, or anything,” Robles said darkly. Mistress Gorkel sniffed. 
“She ate a sour apple,” Tomrin offered as they headed out of the pavilion leaving the clack, clack of artlessly wielded staves and generous calling of points—yellow, blue—behind. The score keeper, a member of the Gorkel family, marked each call. Another called the tens.
Down a side path lined with small booths, the games were nonsense games for children. Many of the booths were empty as if the drought had dried them up too. Past the wooden pens with the cows, sheep, goats and other farm animals, he and Robles entered the small sea of tents, wagons, and carts. The visitors who came for Sheep Shearing Days traveled heavy, and heavily. 
Robles said hello to everyone they met. Tomrin just nodded and gave his fellow travelers a pinched smile. Half-deserted, the dirt paths still felt choked with strangers to Tomrin.
One Last Practice [?]
The two staves Dabel had fished up earlier were where he had left them leaned against the large Ocrin tent. “Hello, Janet,” Robles said, ducking his head inside the tent. 
“Hello, Robles. Tomrin is at the fair,” she replied.  
“I have him here with me. He’s going to enter the staff competition. He needs to brush up.” 
“Hello mother,” Tomrin said through the wall and without ducking in. 
“I’m sure your father will be happy to hear it,” Janet said, sounding disgruntled with one or both of them.
Robles pulled his head from the tent flaps, and he and Tomrin headed for the rocky, uneven ground when Tomrin offered it as a practice place. 
“This is great,” Robles said. He held the staff Dable used and Tomrin held his colossal oak one across his chest. “You can work on your footwork.” 
He sounded facetious to Tomrin.
“That or break an ankle,” Tomrin said, and jabbed with his massive staff. It felt like thrusting a log. Robles countered easily, clut, and spun. It was a nonsense move, but it hid his staff from Tomrin and instead of attacking freely he was forced to go on the defensive. Robles’s swing started low—and stayed low. The faint worked. Tuck. What seemed like a quick swing was a trap.
“Point for me,” Robles said as Tomrin rubbed his ankle. “So what did you and Rennly talk about?”
“Nothing. You know he’s my brother, right?”
“Yes, of course,” Robles said. “The poor guy.”
They traded a couple of countered swipes. Clut, clut. 
“It’s like you should be the one to fight him,” Tomrin said as sweet began beading on the back of his hands. He rubbed his sweaty forehead. “I’m going to need a sweatband.” They continued to spar in silence, their sticks clacking; Robles made moves and Tomrin countered. “I feel it coming back to me.” 
“It’s just like riding a horse,” Robles said. 
Tomrin swung at Robles’ hands. “You know what I think about horses.” 
Robles snorted and soon Dable walked over. 
Calls [?]
Robles called a halt. “Whoa!” He leaned on his staff. “What do you think, Dable?” 
“I think you better get your grand champion to the ring. The competition is about to start.” 
Tomrin glanced at the sun. It had moved. Instead of being a hot glare above it was a red glare in the west. “We lost track of time,” he said, handing his staff to his father. “I need water.” 
Dable led him and Robles back to the tent. “You also need something to keep the sweat from your eye’s,” Dable said as Janet poured water for them. “You can’t be swatting sweat and expecting to win.” 
“I know that,” Tomrin said, wondering about his chance of actually winning, of actually beating Rennly. “I just need a handkerchief.” 
“A streamer?” Janet offered, holding up a yellow streamer. 
“No,” Dable said, “Can’t be yellow or blue.” 
“What can’t be yellow or blue?” Emy asked, darkening the tent’s opening. She ducked in. A young Orish girl as big as an ox followed her inside. 
“My head band,” Tomrin said. “I need a handkerchief.” 
“Oh, I have one,” Emy said and headed outside, leaving the large girl standing at the flaps with a “stay here.” The girl glanced awkwardly behind her. Tomrin’s cringed for her. 
Robles spoke. “Doing well, Kam?” He reached out a hand to the girl, and walked over to her. “I heard you singing.” 
Kam took Robles’ hand and shook. “Thank you master Robles. I heard you too.” 
“Everyone heard Robles,” Dable said with a wry grin for Tomrin. 
Tomrin grinned and nodded but hid his smile and drank the last of his water. 
Emy returned with a head scarf. It was white cashmere embroidered with a black swan in flight, its neck bent as if it were looking for a place to land up on some unseen body of water. “I made it myself,” she said, as she handed it to Tomrin. He began folding it up.
Robles snatched it from him. “Fold it right,” he said, snapping the cloth. He folded it so the swan’s head and neck sat centered. “Turn around.” Tomrin complied, and Robles tied the handkerchief around his head. 
“We better hurry,” Dable said, and Emy peppered them all with a million questions as she, Kam, Dable and Robles trudged with Tomrin to the fairgrounds. 
What Matters Now [?]
The final bout of the youth’s competition was just starting as they rounded on the Gorkel tent. 
A crowd had gathered to watch, or to await their own time in the ring. The flinty clerk was calling for entrants to hurry and sign up before this last match was decided. Rennly and his censors were among the crowd watching the bout. The parents of two boys in the ring were cheering for their respective sons. Robles knew them and their parents’ names and mentioned them to Tomrin but he promptly forgot. Who they were didn’t matter, only the boy’s techniques mattered now, not their names.
Both were Rubish, but the boy with the yellow streamer was half a hand taller than the boy with the blue tied around his arm. The taller was faster and had the reach but evident in his gangly moves he lacked a solid technique. Tomrin found himself rooting for the shorter, stockier boy. He was slow and weighted down with fat, but his technique was solid, a true student of the art. In this ribbon-gaining round, only strikes counted; they were adults now. 
As their bout progressed the taller boy grew visually frustrated and angry. 
The chubby boy with the yellow streamer countered his attacks with a practiced grace. But Tomrin could see him wearing down. He was not the only one to see it. The tall boy’s father called out to his son. “Wear him down, Brad! Wear him down! He’s flagging!” 
After that Brad moved with greater speed, less skill, like a viper making wild swings. Clatch, clatch, clatch. Clack, clack, clack. Then his staff stuck the blue boy. Thak. The judge called out. “Yellow point!” The blows kept landing.
the blue boy’s parents cheered him on. “Go, Mathis,” but there was too much heat and his face had gone from pink to red, and sweat mopped down his hair. With the slap of Brad’s staff against his middle the match was called. The ten-point margin had been breached, eighteen to twenty-eight. 
Brad’s parents cheered, and others clapped. The loser exited the ring, and had to be called back. His ribbon was red for second place, and Brad’s was blue for first. “But don’t leave,” the referee said, “We still have to reward the best-in-show after the adults are done. Anyone could win it! Even second place,” the judge said, gaining laughs from the crowd.
With the boys finally sent from the ring, and the rules were explained again. Tomrin had heard them before. 
He once stood in the champion’s ring as a boy, before he’d given up the sport, if it could be called such a thing; boys and grown men beating each other up with sticks. It smacked of barbarism. 
It was after his father had become so worked up over a poor call by the referee that Tomrin had finally given it all up. He’d swatted others with sticks because his father had pushed him to it, and that was the only reason. When Dable had cried angry tears of rage, finally Tomrin grew the nerve to tell him he was through with it. It was only a game. 
Staves, as weapons went, were more civilized than daggers or swords, but they were still weapons.
No Backing Out Now [?]
“I don’t see our Orish counselor,” Tomrin said. 
Robles ignored him. “We should make sure Rennly’s still in.” He headed through the ring to where Rennly was. Tomrin headed after a moment’s hesitation as he thought about the possibility of a dark horse. More time to train, he thought. He wanted to beat Rennly badly for pushing him into this. 
“Don’t tell me you are backing out,” Rennly said. 
“Not at all,” Robles replied. “We wanted to make sure you weren’t.” 
“As if I am the one afraid?” Rennly said with a satisfied grin. His green eyes seemed to let him see inside, and Tomrin felt under a looking glass. 
“I’m not afraid,” Tomrin said, feeling a surge in his heart. He turned to Robles. “I’m hungry.” 
“Yes, food!” 
They headed back through the crowd. Several bouts stood ahead of Tomrin’s first, and two before Rennly’s. “We need to get back to see his bout,” Tomrin said. 
“And I need to make my other tosses in the knife throw too. Rennly entered it as well.” 
“At least one of us will beat him.” 
“Only yours matters to the glass shop.”
A Quick Bite [?]
There was a nervous energy at the Farmers Stock Feed tent. 
The supply stone was hosting for the price of one silver per plate. When Tomrin sat down with his lamb chops, he found himself almost missing Rennly’s company. For all his faults, he was better than the superstitious Orish  man to his left. While Tomrin wished for Robles help, he had his own trouble in the shape of a Rubish matron waiting to discuss the demon’s role in this drought and how it had impacted the price of their meager meal. 
The Orish peasant next to Tomrin spoke of strange going’s-ons. “Just last night—” The man eyed Tomrin with his dull orbs, and lowered his voice. “—a False Daughters came by the graveyard out yonder butte and brought all the dead back, straight to life to roam and haunt. Gives me chills to think of it. I tell you that. It’s what this world is coming to.” 
Tomrin had no option but to agree. When he finished his lamb chop, he barely remembered having tasted it. He headed back to ring at a near run, leaving Robles to fend for himself and to toss his knife. 
Rennly had taken to the ring against an Orioles man who looked like his uglier cousin hardened by the sun and tough living brought out his spots. While older than Rennly, his age had not made him craftier with the staff. 
“Point Blue. Point Blue.” The referee called as Rennly slapped the man around the ring like a simple farmer. The man lost quickly but with a grin on his face. Tomrin heard him tell another man who could have been his dumber brother: “Least I lost to Rennly Rickot, him all dressed in black I was afraid to really open up. Fear of the Gods and all. Better than some puffed up slave.” 
Tomrin scoffed quietly to himself. There wasn’t going to be enough drink left over in the tarsk tent by the time the competition was over. He congratulated Rennly with a nod and went in search of Robles or maybe his sister Emy, anyone with half a brain. 
Or anyone not trying to brain him.
Chapter 17: A Feathered Thing
	And man shall become the one with the Sword, 
 And with it he shall swiftly unravel their cosmos.
		—From The Dark Verses

Robles’s knife sank into the target’s bull’s eye with a hollow thud in the hot afternoon air. 
Tomrin ducked under the cordon while the game master recorded and retrieved Robles’ knife to the polite claps of onlookert. The knife had hit the center but not dead center.” What’s the matter?” Tomrin asked Robles. The toss wasn’t even the long one. “How are you missing?”
Robles grinned at him. “I’m not,” he said, wiggling his eyebrows in his conspiratorial way. He was up to something. “I have an idea. It came to me when I realized I could be up against Rennly as well.” 
“Oh?” Tomrin said, “What are you thinking?” 
Robles didn’t respond instantly but waited for the recorder to return his knife and for the man to let Tomrin know that he needed to move back. “You’ll see, if you lose.” 
“I could still win,” Tomrin replied and headed behind the audience line. Rennly was good, but he was still beatable. Unless he was holding back like Robles. Tomrin dismissed that idea and thought it would be nice to beat Rennly after everything, the petty slights and thrown dirt clods.
Robles tossed his thirty-paces knife throw. Thufh. 
“What are you doing here?” Rennly asked in Tomrin’s right ear. “The Gorkles are calling your name.” 
Tomrin swung about to glance at the man. “Feathers,” he said. “Thank you.” He pushed through the crowd. He didn’t turn around to see if Rennly and his goons were following until he reached the pavilion; they hadn’t. 
Tomrin checked in. “You were supposed to be here when your name was called,” the referee told him as he tied the yellow ribbon on his arm, a breach of protocol but Tomrin wasn’t going to say anything other than his sorries.
He stole a look at his opponent. The man with his wife was comforted his young boy, barely more than a toddler. His blue steamer was already tied in place. The referee called them to the ring and the man handed his unhappy son to the woman and she handed him his staff.
Tomrin squared off with the young Rubish father and bowed over their staves as the man dis the same. The staff felt light after the oak one and he jabbed with the butt, making the first move. The man countered, hesitating slightly. He was not the dark horse Rennly feared. 
The man’s wife called to her man, and though Tomrin had heard his name before, he finally recognized him. Foby Doormaker had been to the carpentry shop on several occasions. Older than Hadrus by a year or two, he had been friendly.
As his staff touched Foby’s chest, legs and arms, Tomrin made sure not to hurt him.
The referee called out the yellow and blue points as they traded blows, but the match grew lopsided with Foby’s staff only striking Tomrin across the forearm and thigh for two points. Foby sweated worse than a pig when the ten point spread was breached.
Tomrin bowed over his staff to Foby and shook his hand. “Good match,” he said and adjusted the sweat band on his forehead. It was soaked with sweat. 
“I don’t know,” Foby wheezed. “You took me apart.” 
Tomrin rubbed his arm. “You got in a couple of good ones too.” 
Foby smiled and then thanked Tomrin for going easy on him. The man walked out the ring rubbing his chest--massaging the only strike that really connected. His wife handed him back his son and Tomrin thought the woman seemed more relieved than anything. 
Tomrin had heard a loud clapping from a section of the crowd and turned to his father, Dable standing with Rennly and Robles. Tomrin returned his streamer and staff then headed over to them as the clerk called out the names of the next two contestants. 
Dable clapped his hand on Tomrin. “That was a good bout!” he said. “Now I need to check on the animals.” 
“I hope you take it that easy on me,” Rennly said. But it was clear what he meant, when he added: “Not sure I have the same level of control.” 
“Just don’t break his fingers,” Robles said. “We all need those working glass.” 
“Don’t you two have knives to throw at each other?” Tomrin asked, not looking forward to his fated match with Rennly. 
“And that’s why we don’t let Tomrin touch sharp things,” Robles said, “He hasn’t quite got the rules down yet.” 
Tomrin jabbed a finger at Robles chest, not touching him, “Pointy end goes in here.” 
“Right,” Robles said, and turned to Rennly. “We should get back to it.” 
“Worried, Robles?” Rennly asked. 
“Worried? Nothing but my pride on the line, so of course I’m worried!” Robles’ hysterics sounded real, but he was up to something. He’d already admitted as much. 
Flash [?] 
At the knife throw competition, others tossed knives while Robles and Rennly watched and waited. 
Rennly acted cool, calm, and in control, confident of success at both, though he did hurry back to the staff competition as it was close to time for his second match. “I don’t want to be late like someone we know.”
“It’s going to be a nightmare,” Tomrin said. “Having Rennly around.” The man was worse than his mother, always finding fault. 
“Hmm,” Robles grunted. “It won’t be that bad. And if it is, we can always kill him.”
“A pleasant thought,” Tomrin said. “But, no. I hope whatever you have planned works, killing a censor, even Rennly, would never do.” 
Robles grinned. “Maybe it won't come to that.”
“It won’t,” Tomrin said, and headed to the Gorkel tent. The throbbing mass of knotted evil growing in his chest wasn’t him. I am above it.
As he walked he hoped on a feather it was true.
A Bug [?]
At the competition pavilion, Rennly made his second win seem so effortless, as if it came naturally. 
The next few bouts flew by:amateur against amateur. 
Robles returned and Rennly walked over when Dable came from the animals, in his hands a blue ribbon, the satin of it shining in the westing sun. “I see the fun’s not over,” he said, beaming, over-pleased with himself. 
“Not yet, Master Ocrin,” Rennly said. 
“First place, best she-goat,” Robles read.
 “Congratulations, Dable,” Rennly said, adding his own warmth.
The announcer called Tomrin’s name, and because he spent an extra moment congratulating his father, he wasn’t able to select the staff he wanted, the same from the first round. The one he picked was nearly identical but he could feel the difference, and it bugged at him. And itt bugged him that it bugged him. Feathered superstitions. The smooth yew staff was every bit as good as his opponent’s.
Capal Tassas swirled the thing for his Orish friends then swung it like a woodcutter chopping wood. The yew howled through the air.  Kwoof. He turned to face off with Tomrin swinging as he stepped into the ring. Grinning wickedly he continued pulverizing the air. Kwoof. Kwoof, kwoof. He paused to speak. “I’ll try not to hurt you—not too bad,” he said. 
“I’ll try not to as well,” Tomrin said, and bowed, unsure of what his own words meant. Capal grunted a laugh and lashed out and struck Tomrin  a quick blow to the arm. “Point blue!” 
Rennly jerked. “Don’t beat him too bad, Capal,” he shouted. “That’s my job.” People several laughed. Tomrin danced to take a glance at who. Dable was one of them. He slap Rennly on the back.
Capal, and his staff, forced Tomrin to pay attention. Woof-flack! 
The Orish man continued to attack, his staff raised like a blacksmith’s hammer. His next swing was off-balance and poorly executed. Woof-tunt. The whiff of his staff rustled the hair on Tomrin’s arm as he let the blow go to his side to the ground. Tomrin, slapped the man’s middle. Fwap, then spun to deflect a counter stroke. Point yellow. The man’s friend laughed. 
Angry strength made no match of it after that and the fight was essentially over then. Clack, fwap. Clack, fwap. “Hold still you little rat,” Capal muttered angrily as Tomrin delivered the tenth point for yellow.
Soon after the referee called “Match. Yellow.” Capal had only touched him once and that was his sneak attack and a glancing blow at best. 
The man would not win best-in-show.
Games Inside Games [?]
“Now, those two fight for third place,” Tomrin said after he joined Dable, Robles, and Rennly. 
“Hmm, yes,” Rennly agreed. “No dark horse to upset our little game.” 
Marten walked over to them. The big Orish counselor held a satchel of clay-red leather. “Gentleman,” he said, nodding. 
“Who’s this?” Dable asked. 
“Dable,” Robles said, “Meet counselor Marten from Menad. Marten, Dable Ocrin, Tomrin’s father.” 
Marten bowed again as Dable’s hand remained at his side. Dable disliked Orish men. “Not from Menad, but still a pleasure,” Marten said. “And gentlemen, your contract is ready for your perusal and signing.” He tapped his satchel with a meaty hand. 
“We need a table,” Tomrin said. 
“I could certainly go for another scone,” Robles said. “Winning is hungry work.”
“We’ll see about that,” Rennly said.
Dable led the way to the baker’s booth as they made quite a parade of it: two Orioles men—one a censor—another man Rubish yet in the pilgrim white with his Peregrine half-breed son, all followed by anlarge Orish man dressed in the latest style from Zircos. 
The Contact [?]
Each paid their own way at the window. 
When it was Tomrin’s turn he ordered his scone with raspberry jelly without currants--unlike his father who asked for extra. 
After they polished off their snacks Tomrin glanced around. It was like he and the others were an exhibit and people goosenecked when Marten pulled out the papers and a small inkwell and quill he had stashed in his satchel. “Read it,” the Orish man said. “But as an impartial source, you can trust everything is in order. And fair.” 
An older Rubish walked over. “And what you fellows up to?”
Tomrin had met the man before, but had since forgotten his name. The man had once wanted some woodwork done by Dable. Tomrin had forgotten what as well. What he did remember was his father calling the man a gossip and a miser. Yet that had stopped Dable from taking on his job if Tomrin remembered correctly. 
“Seems the boys have a bit of a bet going on with the staves, Nuttel,” Dable said. “Big money’s riding on it.” 
“Actually, there’s no money—” Tomrin said, correcting his father.
Robles stopped him, chopping the air and grimacing. “Nothing riding on the staves,” he said. “But this is a big money contract: all on the glass shop in Menad,.” 
“Glass?” Master Nuttel snorted. “Tin’s good enough for me. I see no need for fancy glass. Just going to break anyhow.” 
“That just means repeat business,” Robles replied smoothly. 
“I suppose it does,” Master Nuttel said, thoughtfully. “If only it wasn’t all a bunch of sorcery.” 
The man tottered, pushing through the aisle of benches, muttering to himself.
The Mighty Pen [?]
Tomrin was happy to see him leave.
“Let’s just sign this nonsense and get it over with,” he said. 
“Settle down,” Robles told him. Clearly none of this was getting to him. It was like he enjoyed it. But then again, he wasn’t the one Rennly was learning with the others; he was not the one about to feel the brunt of Rennly’s nasty side.
“If you aren’t going to sign I will,” Tomrin said and snatched up the quill. “Hand me the paper.” 
“The contract,” Marten said, sliding it to him. 
The man’s script was large and flowing; Tomrin couldn’t focus on it. He found his name at the bottom of the page and scratched his signature with is quill then dropped it back into the inkwell. “I’ll meet you in the Gorkel’s ring,” he said to Rennly then turned and left the tent.
As he walked past couple of strapping Rubish bucks he realized he was frustrated and angry. You can’t be angry, he told himself. That was how the demons got inside. Anger he’d read was how they tricked you. It was how their evil became part of the Mark, and once used, there was no hiding from Daughters, censors, or the gods—or so he’d read. I’m a good person. He desperately wanted to believe it. As he walked, he found the anger dissipating.
By the time he sighed in at the staves pavillion, the anger was gone but now a nervous energy filled and he played out scenarios in his head. Some he won, most he lost.
In the ring, the men fought for a shot at third place, possibly even second. They sparred, and though Tomrin watched, he didn’t see them. The fair and its people moved and lived, and enjoyed themselves all around him, their voices and laughter and calls washing over him, unheard. 
Their lives were outside his understanding, and he lost himself in quiet contemplation.
Under a Westing Sun  [?]
“Going to win?” Robles said. 
Tomrin started, his revelry broken. He’d been thinking about glass and wanting to get back to Menad, planning on leaving as soon as the competition was over. “I don’t know,” he said. “But I feel like pounding Rennly into the dirt.” His flat words held no heat. 
“So you do think you can win?” 
Tomrin grunted. “Can you beat him with the knives?” 
“After tossing a sucker or two?” Robles said. “Easy.” 
“Tell me what’s going on.” 
“The backup plan, if you lose?” Robles asked. 
“So confident in me.”
“When robbing a censor, always have an escape route, I say” 
“We aren’t robbing him,” Tomrin said, though it did feel like Rennly was stealing something from him. What exactly Tomrin couldn’t say. Fighting for money was undignified, and gambling went against his belief in his ability to use his mind—not crude muscles and luck. Feathered Rennly, he thought but muttered: “Feathered Woolad.” Somehow the sun burnt hotter now in the west. “Feather them all.” 
“You may not want to say that overly loud,” Robles cautioned. 
“I know,” Tomrin said. The evil was speaking for him and it frightened him. It was like parts of him were flying loose. “This thing between Rennly and I—I thought it was over.” 
“Well, here he comes,” Robles said, not helping.
A Honey Lash [?]
Rennly came from the direction of the knife toss. “I’m going to beat you both,” he said. “I certainly hope you two are better at glass than you are at the martial arts or my golden ducks are going to fly away and never return.”
Tomrin pretended to watch the bout for a minute while he stewed. The artless clack of staff on staff, a discordant tune in his ears. The need to strike at Rennly, to cut him down, to cut down his pride grew too strong to ignore, and Tomrin turned to him filled with a base contempt. “You think you know our skills so well,” he said. “But you are wrong.”
“Oh, really?”
“You will not. And if you think I am wrong, let’s change the wager. Beat us both and you will have a voice.”
“I already have a voice,” Rennly said. 
“Not inside the shop you don’t. We are the creators. We are the artists, the craftsmens. We are the masters and chiefs—we—Robles and I. We see more deeply than you know and not only glass. But with glass we are its gods.”
Rennly grunted.
“Um, I’m not really that good,” Robles said. “And, I think maybe the heat’s getting to Tomrin.” 
Rennly didn’t blink but glared at Tomrin. Tomrin, however, was not finished, and though he didn’t know what Robles planned for the knife toss he was convinced his friend could and would beat Rennly. Armed with Robles’s confidence he slid his barb home. “If you beat us both, you get a voice inside the shop, but if one of us beats you, I decide what night of the week we meet.”
“You boys still making bets?” Dable asked as he stepped from the path. He and Marten walked together like two men had come to some great understanding, friendly yet diffidently on both their parts. The big Orish man didn’t loom over Dable but looked like a larger, more sophisticated man walking with another man who was large in his own right, both seemingly grand in their own individual ways: Marten, neat, and Dable in his white cashmere robe, clean. Both acted satisfied, even Dable, despite his question. 
A group of three Rubish men trailed behind them. T
omrin recognised them as three of the richest men in Woolad. One he was certain was Penn Rejal.
“Amendments cost extra,” Marten said. 
“Just working out some final question of leadership is all,” Robles said, sounding annoyed. He flashed a sidelong look at Tomrin. 
Tomrin shrugged it off. Rennly needed to learn his place. “That is if Censor Richot wants to show us how it’s done.” 
“Fine,” Rennly said, clearly as annoyed as Robles. “I like this. I agree. Knives are first after all.” 
“Now knives?” one of the men asked. He was old, his hair white and he dressed worse than the other two, a real codger, his shirt and pants patched, making him seem all the more miserly. “How uncivilized,” he added.
Tomrin agreed silently to himself, wondering if he’d misjudged the man.
Sleight of Hand [?]
At the knife toss pavilion a crowd had formed. 
The words bet and gold and ducks sailed through in the air. Nuttel spoke loudly and Tomrin didn’t have to wonder how everyone knew about their little wager. 
The knife toss’ grandmaster asked if the staff bout was over. Clearly Robles and Rennly had delayed their final showdown until after the staves’ champion had been chosen. 
“Change of plans,” Rennly explained as he walked up to the first line. “Knives first. After I beat Master Robles here, I’ll be ready to beat Master Tomrin.”
Robles hissed in Tomrin’s ear. “We wanted to wait in case you won.” 
“And If I lose?” Tomrin asked in a whisper. 
“Never mind,” Robles replied. “Too late now.” He waved to two boys calling his name and walked over to the boys and shook their hands as if he was running for mayor. He didn’t seem the least bit worried about planting his knife in his bale’s bullseye.
Rennly tossed his knife and it stabbed his own bulleye. 
Tomrin brushed the wound on his chest. Maybe it was just the heat, but his head was full of pressure and there was nothing he could do for it but scratch his itch. Even that didn’t seem to help. 
Finally, Robles took his position and, pulling out his knife, tossed it in one smooth motion, flicking almost absently. Thuth. Bullseye. 
He and Rennly each retrieved his own knives in this final round. 
The game master gocked with everyone else as they took their positions on the second last line.
Dangerous Men [?]
“After you,” Rennly said, unease in his voice. Perhaps it was dawning on him that Robles was a challenge after all.
“Certainly,” Robles replied. Threw his knife, making small, but still little show of it, lining up for a whole second before sending his knife into the bullseye once more. He was toying with Rennly.
Rennly made two practice motions before his own knife flew the fifteen paces and found a bullseye of its own. For him, as with Robles’ throw, there was a round of polite claps as the record keeper announced the bullseyes. 
Then with their knives in hand, they moved to the far rope strung across the dirt at twenty paces. 
This time Rennly went first. He sized up the target and—for reasons beyond Tomrin’s understanding—his own knife. He wanted to crack a wise one, but ice filled his chest cavity with doubt and he held his tongue with everyone else. 
Rennly lobbed the knife. It spun with a languid arc before stabbing the bale’s outer bullseye. Thurk. 
People clapped. 
“A good throw,” Robles said, then tucked his knife away in its sheath at his hip.
Rennly frowned. “Conceding?”
Tomrin’s own heart skipped and thudded in his chest. What are you doing?
“No, I’m just going to throw the way I’ve always practiced.” Robles replied, turning to the crowd and Tomrin. “Knives may not be as civilized, but they are a lot of fun.” He looked right at Tomrin and he walked over and winked, wearing a large grin on his lupine face. “Say when.”
“What?” Tomrin asked. “When?” He didn’t have time to blink. 
Robles stood ten paces from the throw line, all the way to the ropes that held everyone else out, but his hand went to his hip faster than an adders’ strike. He spun and the knife exited the sheath at his hip. The knife didn’t even seem to enter his hand. It was like Robles slapped it and it flew free of its leather case, button popping. Clith. The knife didn’t arc as it flew, but struck the bale like iced lightning. Cathud. It buried itself past the hilt, a dead bullseye.
Just as suddenly another knife tricked into Robles’ other hand. It glinted and shot through the air and sunk into Rennly’s bale. Thud. Another dead on bullseye. 
“Bam, bam, both!” Robles said as he turned and bowed, his arms held wide open to the cheering and clapping crowd. 
The old codger, however, didn’t seem impressed. “Still don’t know how any of this has a feathered thing to do with founding glass,” he said. 
Tomrin found himself agreeing with the man as Rennly’s eyes bugged, twitching with repressed rage. 
Chapter 18: The Price of Failure
	Even the gods place their trust in gold.
		--A Rubish saying

Robles proudly displayed his first-place ribbon, pinning it on his chest with a bodkin from his pack. 
“I am the hero of the hour!” he said, smiling soaking in the adulation.
“Never has any one competition, in my memory, ever elicited such interest,” Tomrin agreed. “But you are a bit of a showman. I bet you have another silly trick in your pack right now.” Often or not, they were just simple blacksmith’s puzzles, a small sword tapped or threaded into a toy anvil. Or rings—the point of which was to extract one from the others. He would make some off-hand remark about their ultimate meaning, often announcing a silly prize like becoming king. Corundum hadn’t a king in years, not since the Emperor returned. “So hurry now, your hour is nearly up!”
	“True, true,” Robles said.
Rennly, however, was not amused. “I’ve fallen in with a pair of vipers,” he’d said walking past them, stuffing his second-place ribbon in his pocket.
	The crowd at the knife booth followed as they headed for the staves pavilion.
At the Gorkel tent, the referee chided Tomrin and Rennly. “I was going to forfeit the both of you,” he said. “And give first place to Medza.” 
“And have a riot in your hand?” Robles asked loudly to a round of laughter. 
The referee frowned and tossed up his hands. “Well, are you two finally ready?” he said, and stomped over to the scorekeeper, his brother. There was a smattering of cheers, but Medza seemed crestfallen. Tomrin couldn’t blame the man. He also wished their round had been canceled, if for other reasons.
Rennly muttered something as he picked out his staff.
“What what that?” Tomin asked.
“I said Henk and Ressel are probably lost in a shell of tarsk right now, and I have no one to tie my streamer.” 
Tomrin glanced to the crowd, to where Robles and Dable and Marten and the town patriarchs stood chatting as they waited for the bout to start. Tomrin assumed Rennly meant his two censor friends. “Have Robles do it.” Tomrin said. 
“So, kind of you to offer, not your own help, but someone else ” Rennly said and stomped off.
Tomrin picked up his staff feeling annoyed. He tried to make sense of Rennly, but couldn’t. Retrieving his yellow cloth streamer from Mistress Gorkle, he brought it over to his father Dable to tie next to Rennly and Robles. “Thanks, Papa,” he said after Rennly stomped back into the ring. 
“Rennly has his hackles up,” Dable said. “Be on your toes.” 
A Round [?]
Tomrin glanced over at Rennly.
With his blue streamer tied, Rennly had taken up a spot across the ring and stood with his staff in both hands in front of him horizontally, waiting. The referee stood to the side. Both stared at Tomrin. 
Tomrin flexed his arm to make sure his yellow streamer wasn’t tied too tight and he glanced back at Robles.
His Orioles friend raised his eyebrows. “Yes?”
“What bet were you going to make with Rennly?” 
“Don’t worry about that,” he replied. “Just get in there and rearrange his face for me.” 
“I’ll be lucky if he doesn’t rearrange mine,” Tomrin said and turned back to the ring, stepping over the rope circle.
The referee explained the critical rules. Points for strikes only. No grappling. No head shots. If anyone fell, no hitting them until they were back up. Tomrin had heard it all before, plenty of times. They would play to fifty or until the first person had a ten strike lead. 
On the referee’s signal Tomrin bowed to Rennly. Rennly bent slightly. His eyes gleamed a pale green in the deceptively low sun. The first day, the fun day of Sheep Shearing Days, was nearing over. They bowed upright and Tomrin shifted to his left, circling watching Rennly’s staff like a hawk. Rennly circled as his staff probed.
For a moment they traded blows, each catching the other’s staff with their own, deflecting the strikes. Clat, clat! Clat, clat! Tomrin swung his staff high at Rennly’s shoulders, putting power into it while maintaining some semblance of defense. Rennly ducked as his staff flowed up and at an angle. Tomrin’s staff went high, Rennly swung low.
 Tomrin dodged, jumping backwards. “Ha!” Rennly said. Moving backwards was a show of ineptitude. It was the right move though and Tomrin grimaced, reassuring himself as he and Rennly circled tapping staves, each continuing to probe for weakness. Nat, nat. Nat, nat. 
Rennly smiled. He wanted this and was playing before he got on with it. Tomrin struck out with a wild blow. Rennly blocked it with the middle of his staff. Clatch. His counterattack came swiftly. Clack!  With each set of his heavy blows--two one-handed Blacksmith Hammers, and a third set of back and forth Holy Threshers--Rennly’s grin slowly faded. Clack, clack! Flutch, fletch. Flackk! 
The power in his swings sent vibrations into Tomrin’s hands and pain erupted in his knuckles. Slowly, his finger numbed to the pain and his hands merged with the wood of the staff. “Is that all you have?” Tomrin asked through clenched teeth. 
Rennly sneered. “What about you? Are you even remotely trying?”
As they circled Rennly continued to probe. The crowd watched in silence. One face alone stood out, Counselor Marten’s. The look of concern was gone from his eyes. Once a soldier, he now watched with a soldier’s eye, blank of all emotion. A slight nod of his head was his salute. Given what Margretta had said about the man, he had probably come as protection--now powerless in his task.
Rennly’s staff clacked against Tomrin’s. Tomrin lashed out with an open blow, swinging wildly with his staff.
Rennly countered. Clack. His staff stabbed Tomrin in the thigh. “Point blue!” 
The pain was shocking and Tomrin was certain he’d be bruised to the bone.
The Luster [?]
“Give it back to him, Tomrin!” a voice cried out. 
Bethany Karlite was in the crowd. Her presence added a strange nervousness to his anger, and his feet needed his conscious control as Tomrin stumbled around in the dusty ring. 
Rennly swung his staff, and he blocked. Clack. Tomrin stepped. Clack. Again Rennly swung. Clack. Tomrin edged his staff, up and against Rennly’s shoulder as his power sapped his finesse. “Point Yellow,” called the referee. Clack, clack. Tomrin blocked Rennly’s counter attack. Clack.
Their feet pounding at the dirt filled the air with choking orange fume as the sun filtered sideways under the pavilion. Tomrin began to vie for the light against Rennly, each trying to keep it at their backs to the sun and it out of their eyes.
Rennly continued to rain down blows. Each strike he gained, Tomrin repaid—if much softer. When Rennly’s staff found its way against Tomrin’s streamer, he struck Rennly’s ankle. When Rennly smacked Tomrin on the chest, he jabbed him in the leg. 
Finally, Tomrin let himself pant. He was wearing down and he feared he was going to lose after all. Feathers, he swore. His heavy breathing dried his throat and he was sore. His heart pounded and the mass of evil inside was pounding with it, wanting to come out. 
Rennly swung at Tomrin like he was closing a heavy door with both hands. Tomrin stepped in to block him, taking advantage of Rennly’s awkward two-handed motion. But Rennly swing was a trick, worthy of Robles. Their staves connected and Rennly’s staff jammed down on Tomrin’s foot. It crushed his foot through the boot. Cruk. 
“Feather!” Tomrin swore. 
“Hurt?”
“You broke something.” 
“As long as it wasn’t your hands,” Rennly said. “You’ll need those to pay me back.” 
“I’ll pay you back,” Tomrin said, hobbling now to keep up their martial dance. 
“He’s hurt! Call the match!” Tomrin heard the Orish counselor call out, there were hushed noises coming from the crowd. Tomrin could no one now through the glare as he hopped about like a one-legged crow.
“No!” he said, “I’m fine!” He was mad as a hornet, but strangely calm, and when he looked at Rennly he saw what he was looking at. “You are nothing but a boy, Rennly, a bully, because your father beat you.”
“My father never laid a finger on me.”
“You’re a liar and a fraud.”
“And you’re nothing but a Peregrine half-breed slave, overreaching his station.” 
“Me overreaching?” Tomrin replied. “I have created my own fortunes. I’m not trying to buy or inherit one.” 
Rennly struck another blow for blue and laughed.  “Can’t you tell I’m just toying with you now? You have no tricks like Robles.” 
True. Tomrin thought, yet the evil mass inside his chest throbbed, calling to him. He could feel its terrible power. Corrupt though it was—or because of the corruption—the Luster called to him now more seductively than it had before. It shouted now, promising power greater than the Leviathan’s own terrible might. You could do anything! Rennly was a bug compared to the power the Luster promised. Defy the god—and the demons both! Set yourself up as king! No emperor! And not just of Corundum, but Gearthrum itself! And Serathus besides! He was special—not cursed. Gold! Power! Nothing would be denied him. Nothing and no one would stand in his way. A tyrant to end all tyrants. His staff rod for smiting his enemies and correcting his allies. 
Tomrin shook his head and focused on the pain in his foot. The Luster was a demon’s trick.
He smiled to himself. He would not let them or Rennly push him around.
A Moral Victory [?]
“What are you smiling at?” Rennly demanded as he swung his staff high. 
Tomrin ducked. A head strike was illegal if he had let it go through it would be a point for him, not Rennly, but a point was nothing compared to a staff in the face. Tomrin didn’t need points. Now win or lose, he had already won the important concession in the knife throw. He had just beaten the seductive Luster. Now all he had to do was deny Rennly what he wanted most—to hurt him. “Wouldn’t you like to know?” Tomrin said. “But you are not my judge nor my executioner. You are just a couple of golden ducats to me, nothing more. Dress up in black and still you are nothing to nobody.” 
“You don’t know anything,” Rennly hissed as Tomrin blocked a blow with the rattliing end of his staff. 
“I know you think Dabel is your real father,” Tomrin said as Rennly slashed wildly with his staff and Tomrin’s staff struck him in the leg. Point yellow. “You want his fortunes, but as long as I and Hadras stand in your way, you get nothing.” Rennly said nothing but slammed his staff against Tomrin’s as if trying to drive him to the ground, his eyes filled with cold hatred as Tomrin held him with his staff though his bad foot ached like it was carrying all of his and Rennly’s weight together. “And you will never be his great son,” Tomrin continued. “Can’t you see? He loves Hadras most of all?”
Someone in the crowd wondered aloud what the two of them were waiting for.
Tomrin redirected Rennly’s staff and delivered a strike, clipping Rennly’s elbow. Point Yellow.
A pained surprise filled Rennly’s eyes with angry heat.
But the heat cooled quickly and turned mean.
Tomrin fended blow after blow, many aimed at his bad leg. Tomrin was just thankful it wasn’t his chest. He feared the burn wound would split open and begin to bleed.
On One Foot [?]
In Rennly’s single-mindedness, Tomrin easily kept the scoring close.
Soon, however, they both began to tire. Fatigue slacked Rennly’s face. He was finally conserving energy for his attacks. He circled slower and slower, to the point that Tomrin feared a trick. None came as they fought on. Then again it wasn’t beyond Rennly to milk the full fifty strikes. 
I can still win this, Tomrin thought as his and Rennly’s staves clacked against each other in their interminable dance. The crowd cheered each strike, counting up the points for yellow and blue without waiting for the scorekeeper to announce their points.
With a quick blow to Tomrin’s forearm, Rennly broke forty first.
Tomrin returned the strike. One more welt for both was just one more. 
“Yellow point,” the referee called. 
The crowd called out thirty-nine. 
Rennly lashed out with his staff, leaving himself exposed across the chest. His staff struck Tomrin’s shoulder. Fack. Forty. Tomrin returned the favor. Flack. Forty-one for yellow. 
Then Rennly’s staff slapped Tomrin chest and his mind emptied at the pain of the blow even though it had missed a Mark by an inch. “Ahh!” he cried.
Rennly smiled, cruelly, but Tomrin could barely see him as his eyes blurred up.
To Fifty [?]
His step-brother struck another two quick blows: arm, shoulder, but compared to the chest wound, those strikes hurt not a bit.
Tomrin dropped into a defensive stance and he held the staff more in the center. He wiped his eyes between the blows Rennly threw at him as he gave up ground. Clack, clack. The Blacksmith’s Hammer came down over and over. Clack, clack! Clach! Clach! Clach!
Rennly’s staff shattered. Clash! That wasn’t supposed to happen, but before Tomrin could score a strike the referee yelled. “Halt!” 
Rennly tossed what was left of his staff out of the ring, kicking the other half with him as he walked to the barrel to select a new weapon.
Tomrin wiped his eyes fully, breathing heavily as if he were a man surviving some great shock. 
Rennly strode back into the ring, brandishing his fresh staff like a flail. 
Tomrin changed back into the standard foot forward pose like a man serious about staves. When Rennly attacked, all his father’s training came back. Maybe with his fists, Rennly had strength to make up for his lack of skill. But staves were not fists. 
Tomrin turned Rennly’s attacks against him and held his staff like an extension of himself. He struck Rennly, and despite his skill, Rennly struck him too, but the hits no longer hurt. They were desperate strikes from a desperate, grasping man, whose jealous eyes betrayed his own fear of losing. 
They circled the ring, Rennly pushing Tomrin back around the rope with his remaining strength, but what appeared like weakness was strategy. Tomrin feint one way then abruptly change directions; each time he made a strike at Rennly’s legs, Rennly would counter, but many times he missed his strike and their staves clattering together. 
Before long they were tied at forty-nine. 
Over the shouting crowd Robles howled at him. “Finish him, Tomrin!” Robles shouted. “Chew his legs off!” 
Dusk settled as they circled. 
In the descending twilight, irritation settled in Rennly’s green eyes. “You’re messing with me?” he said as he worked his staff more cautiously now. 
Only habit kept Tomrin’s staff from falling out of his hands. Just one move point and he’d be champion. “I am not a bully like you, Rennly,” he said. “If anyone is dragging it out, it’s you.”
Rennly charged and swung with the power equal to any Orish farmhand. It was a wonder the new staff didn’t break like the old one. Slamming it into Tomrin’s staff, clack, he drove it back back against Tomrin’s body, the haft of it just touching him below the jaw on the side of his neck. 
“Point blue!” the referee roared. “Blue the winner!”
Tomrin’s counter hit Rennly in the leg. But the match was over, the crowd cried, “Fifty! Fifty!” drowning out the score keeper.
Best Loser [?]
For a second Tomrin stood confused, thinking he had won. The tap on his neck hadn’t even hurt, he’d blocked it down too little more than a kiss. I lost? 
The crowd flooded into the ring to congratulate them both. Robles was yelling in his ear. “Now that was a fight!” 
The Gorkles however were yelling at everyone. “We’re not done!” Stay back! There is still another bout. We need to decide second place! We still have to decide second place!” 
Tomrin was exhausted; he had no interest in fighting again, not for second place, not even if it were for first now. His foot hurt to put weight on it, and every muscle in his body hurt from one of Rennly’s strike. Tomrin had no reason to care about second place, yet he was grateful that the crowd finally pulled back so he could breathe. Yet when they did, the staff was the only thing keeping him from falling over. 
“I’m not fighting for second place,” he told the referee. 
“What? You want to forfeit?” the man sounded incredulous, as it was unheard of. He clearly didn’t understand the pain in his foot. 
“Well, “Tomrin replied. “All I want is a tall tin of tarsk.” Rennly smirked at him, but Tomrin didn’t care what Rennly thought anymore. He was just a lunk of muscle and not even that good at staves.
The referee frowned and raised his eyebrows in surprise at Tomrin’s acceptance. “I guess you are happy with third?” 
“No, he doesn’t place if he forfeits,” the Gorkel matriarch who took their name and money said. “The rules are quite clear.” 
Dable who had left the ring with the others, spoke from the side-lines. “What are you saying, Carnila?” He was frowning and his eyes glinted darkly in the twilight. He walked over to her as she tumbled through her rule book. Not all games had rules laid out in books but games like the staves competition were institutions going back to the beginning, its rules passed down for years long before being written down. 
Carnila put her finger down on a page and read. “An adult player who forfeits may not place in his competition.” 
“May not. May,” Dable said, as he braved Carnila at her own table. “He may not—but he may as well.” 
“But may not,” Carnila replied stoutly.
Tomrin hobbled over on his staff and good foot. He was afraid his father was going to make a fuss. 
The referee followed at Tomrin’s side. “Let me take a look, Mother,” the man said.
“May means he still could,” Dable insisted. 
“Dable Ocrin,” Carnila said, “I think I know how to need the rule book, it’s been in my family for twenty generations, since before the Leviathan cracked the World Egg.” 
Tomrin doubted that, but instead of arguing with her, he tried to console his father. “It’s just a game. I only entered the competition to satisfy the contract.” It sounded stupid, and his father turned on him. 
“And what about the prize money?” 
“A few silvers, who cares?” 
“You should take on the other challenger, you’ll beat Forny easily.” 
Tomrin shook his head and walked over to the barrel and dropped the stick. Tuntd. He turned and hobbled towards Robles. 
“That’s a forfeit!” Carnila called to the crowd and they started talking more loudly.
“Good work,” Robles said.
“Was it?”
“You survived.” 
“And I guess that’s all I needed to do.” Tomrin replied. “Tarsk?”
“After the ribbons are passed out.”
Rennly walked towards them as he escorted Dable away from Carnila’s table. “Good bout,” Renny said, grinning. “I look forward to our next one.” 
Dable shook his head at Tomrin. “I don’t understand why you didn’t just finish.” 
“Because I’m hurt, and don’t need to be hurt any further,” Tomrin said. 
“But the glory,” Dable persisted. 
“I don’t care about glory.”
The referee raised his voice to ask for silence. “I’m going to announce the winners,” he said, holding up three pouches of leather and ribbons tied to each of the coin purses. As he called out second and third, two winners gathered their prizes in turn. Then Rennly won the large purse and the blue ribbon tied to it. He smiled a gloating smile as he walked back to them, as if his win mattered. “Now,” the referee continued. “For best-in-show!” The crowd clapped heavily. Always one of the children won—a double consolation prize. It was always chosen to drum up interest for the game the following year. He hoped that it went to Mathis; having seen him fight, he had skill and had made a good showing. The crowd grew quiet. 
“For best-in-show.” The referee repeated, then blinking in the dusk he read the final winner’s name. “Tomrin Ocrin!” 
There was a stunned moment of silence while the crowd digested what it heard. 
“That’s feathered right!” Robles shouted, and the crowd erupted in cheering as Tomrin re-entered the ring.
As he left, ribbon in hand, he was thankful the darkness hid the pools of tears eyes.
Chapter 19: Tarsk Town
	For his path is laid clear:
For even in the darkness,
The Mists light the way.
	—From The Dark Verses

Oil torches lit the interior of Tarsk Town, a tent complex run by the proprietors of a local tavern called the Ugly Twin. Tomrin and Robles entered through the Shanty Town entrance where the murmur of happy voices and laughter peeled the loudest. Crude humor flew out of months as fast as the Orish serving girl helped the patrons all but toss tarks down their throats. The tent reeked of bodies and the tins here were tin and the drinks were a copper each, and payable on service. For the patrons, two Orish men were shouting at each other the loudest, each comparing each other’s looks to the posterior of different farm animals as their friends laughed. 
Tomrin glanced at Robles. “Should have gone in through the castle entrance,” he muttered. “My foot is throbbing and the ten penny tax would have been worth it.” He’d never been in the Ugly Twins Public House’s Tarsk Town tents before, nor to the Ugly Twins downtown, either. They didn’t serve food, only tarsk. Growing up, the idea of going to the Twins had been out of the question. Robles grinned wryly and pushed through the rough benches to the Merchants’ Quarter entrance.
The Merchant Quarters served high-spirit tarsk as well as low, and the benches were padded. A sign in chalk on a wall explained each drink’s cost. The serving women here were Peregrine, not Orish. Instead of being dressed in rough bodices and skirts, they wore the demure style that had gone out of fashion with the Emperor’s Restoration and the slave tax. 
 Tomrin found it hard to get into the spirit of the theme. “This place is a sty,” he muttered to Robles. Down only to drink, sloppy people lounged here, slurring their speech. At five coppers a tin of the high-spirit tarsk, the Merchants Quarters was a worse den of thieves than was the Shanty Town. 
“Out-of-Towners,” Robles said, but Tomrin was pretty sure he recognised several of the drunken merchants. He knew one of the prettier of the two women waiting on the tables, though he couldn’t place her name. She knew Robles. 
She smiled from ruddy check to ruddy check. “Master Robles, fancy seeing you back in Woolad, yet again.” 
“I never miss Sheep Shearing Days, Timea.” He gave her a peck on the cheek. “It’s like a bad habit I can’t kick.”
“Will you and your friend be joining us?” 
“I’m afraid my friend has a hankering for real tarsk, Timea, and we must continue on.” 
Tomrin’s foot throbbed. If she offered him seat now, he would have taken it, but the crestfallen Timea shooed them on their way. “I’ll be back this way...soon,” Robles told her.
She turned to a nearby table. A Rubish man with the manners of an Orish farmhand needed more high-spirit tarsk and was demanding it loudly. 
At the entrance of the King’s Quarters, an Orish man dressed in red, green, and blue, barred their way. “The bribe is a copper,” the man said, putting a hand on the wooden sword at his hip. “Gentleman,” he added belatedly.
“Is that a penny each, Randy?” Robles asked as he worked his coin purse open. He extracted two copper coins from his leather pouch, and then a third. 
“Well, you two gentlemen look quite prosperous,” Randy said. 
Robles handed the man the two coins. “One for the Emperor and his beautiful daughter, the princess, and one for their most trusted guardsman,” Robles said and Randy’s fingers around the coins. “That’s you, Randy.” 
“Right,” Randy said, blinking. “Right… Right this way,” He bowed awkwardly around his toy sword. 
Robles bowed in return and Tomrin grunted out a thank you as he hobbled into the King’s Quarters’ tent. Here the tables were actual tables, and men and women sat in actual chairs. The smell of sweat was as strong as the smell of real tarsk coloring the air. “We should have come in the back way,” Tomrin said.
“And miss saying hello to Timea?” Robles asked as if that idea were completely out of the question and that Tomrin was daft to suggest it.
Priorities [?]
A heavy set Rubish serving woman called Clara, “Lady Clara,” sat them at one of the empty tables. 
The chair rocked under Tomrin on the uneven ground until he swore he was dizzy. He could now see the prices marked on a linen worked like a tapestry. Real tarsk was ten coppers for a single shell. As he glanced around he noticed that all the tins were made of glass. They looked a bit crude to him, but glass all the same. “The glass tins are a nice touch,” he admitted to Robles when Clara left them to figure out what they wanted. 
“Real tarsk for both of us,” Robles told her when she returned. “And Lady Clara, my fine feathered friend here is a bit of a glass aficionado so can you see that we receive your finest shells?” He slid a copper across the table to her. 
No small feat for such a heavy set woman, Clara curtsied and scooped the coin.  “Of course, lord Robles. Our finest shells.”
“I need to check my foot,” Tomrin said. Sore and stiff all over, his foot worried him most, maybe broken. He peeled off his boot carefully. In the lamp light he could see the top of his foot. It was blue and swollen. 
“Lovely,” Robles said. “Looks like it hurts.” 
Their tarsk arrived. Tomrin removed his foot from the armrest of Robles’ chair, and Robles paid Clara, while Tomrin quaffed a large quantity of the milky fluid then waited for his tongue, lips, mouth, and throat to numb. The shell didn’t look like a shell at all. The clear glass was rippled with a strong heavy base—an interesting design. Not bad. The tarsk too was nice and strong. “Potent,” he said. 
Robles took a sip and concurred. “Not as strong as your father’s, but it will do.” 
The ground swept with last year’s chaff offered soft ground underfoot and the canteen lamps burned their dirty red flames into the air, the loud sounds in the other tents, unmuted by the walls that separated them, continued.  Tomrin drank another sip of his tarsk, anxious for it to get on with its duty. He just wanted the first wave to wash over him. 
Robles slapped him absently, and stood with his glass shell. “That’s Booker.” He nodded his head to the far table. “We should say, hello.” 
Tomrin pointed through the table. “I’m going to rest my foot,” he said. 
Robles shook his head and walked to Booker’s table.
Stolen Moments [?]
Tomrin sipped his tarsk and stuck to his chair.
Finally, the first wave of tarsk hit him and the rumbling of the revelers grew in a distan murmur of voices, almost pleasant. Taking off his other boot prepared to stand, but behind him, a voice, a woman’s voice rolled smoothly over the back of his neck and for a split second froze him. Bethany Karlite was paying off the man in the back entrance, bypassing the Merchant’s Quarter and the Shanty Town tents. She was on her way inside, but instead of money she paid the man with a kiss. “There is more where that came from,” she said. “After your watch, you know where to find me.”
A second wave of tarsk rolled over Tomrin. His vision dimmed yet his mind cleared. The torches flickered and the people glowed and grew out of the chaff strewn on the ground.
