Tomrin perversely hoped Bethany stopped to say hello, yet feared she might. Better that she ignored him and walked on by. Her bribe paid, the man guarding the tent thanked her and she brushed inside. The murmur of her dress against the canvas sent the hair on Tomrin’s arms on end. He stared at his hands on the glass on the lacquered table as if they and it were the most interesting things in the tent. 
“Tomrin Ocrin,” Bethany said, bending over him as she placed a hand on his shoulder. Her jasmine perfume filled his nose. “Drinking tarsk, I see,” she said as she bent up. “And all by yourself.”
“Yes, tarsk,” he murmured. “But, no. Robles is here.” 
Bethany moved around and blocked his view of the tent’s other not-quite-regal denizens. In her dress, she looked as lovely as any queen, her dark hair glowing in the firelight like a crown. Pulling out a chair for herself she sat down opposite him.
Lady Clara swooped in. “What’s your pleasure, Love?” The buxom serving lady said. Bethany turned her smokey gaze on Clara and the two exchanged something in their glances. Lady Clara amended her question. “What would you like, my lady?”
“Low-spirit tarsk,” Bethany replied. “But I’d like one of these fancy tins.” 
“Only best for royalty,” Lady Clara said and scurried off.
In the Eyes [?]
“Rennly is still giving you bruises,” Bethany said as her eyes lighted on the purpling covering Tomrin’s arms.
He didn’t move, but sat perfectly still, his hands glued to the glass and table. Against the leaden weight of his arms, the world pushed up into them. He hated Bethany knowing Rennly beat him now as then. “This is nothing,” Tomrin said. “You should see Rennly.” 
“Really? You finally knock the wool out of him?” 
“Not a scratch.”
Bethany laughed. “I’m sure next time you will beat him soundly.”
Tomrin rubbed the bruise on his left arm. “Next time.” 
“So what are you two fighting over? I heard talk of money?” Bethany asked without stopping. “If I needed money I’d fly off to Corundum.” 
“I’ve been to Corundum,” Tomrin said, not answering her question. 
“Really?” She seemed surprised.
“Briefly, and I don’t want to go back.” 
“Why not?” 
“It stinks—far too many people.” 
Clara returned with a clear glass filled with a yellow drink. When she left, Tomrin asked Bethany what she and Rennly were talking about at the funeral.
“We didn’t say a word to each other.”
“You don’t like him anymore than I do,” Tomrin said. It wasn’t a question, but a surprising revelation.
Bethany stood up. “I won’t speak ill of a censor, Tomrin Ocrin,” she said, suddenly angry.  “I owe Censor Rickot for everything I am.” She flowed past him towards the Merchant Quarter’s tent and disappeared.
Further Dealings [?]
Lady Clara stepped over. “That Mistress Karlite is no good,” she said. 
Before she could say more a man started shouting at the guard at the entrance.
“Ten pennies just to enter! Each? One should be good for all. Not ten each!” Master Rejal’s loud complaining broke the spell over Tomrin’s hands. Pulling them from the table, he ran his fingers through his hair.
“Just pay him, Rejal.” The voice was one of two other men Master Rejal had been with earlier in the day. “We don’t have forever.” 
Dable, Marten, and Rennly, and three old monied men entered paying their bribe in order, perhaps in the order of age. Such were things done in Woolad. Tomrin set his hands back down and the heaviness pushed his shoulders down. The weight was was just the tarsk, but Tomrin couldn’t help but think that the strange feeling was how an emperor might feel when trapped on his golden, gem encrusted throne. 
“That’s my boy,” Dable said loudly. “Best-in-show! I told you that if he was here, we’d find him here!” He clapped Tomrin on the back. Lady Clara stepped away during the commotion as they sat, dragging over chairs to his table.
Grabbing a chair from a table, Robles loped over. “Gentlemen,” he said, setting the chair down with a bow. “Welcome to the Castle.” He sounded like he owned the place and he was its true monarch. Tomrin found himself breathing easier, the weight was still there, but it was a more pleasant sensation. Tarsk. He wanted another and when Clara came over for the others, he had his shell refilled. 
“Now down to business,” Dable said when everyone had drunk from their shells or in Rejal’s case, his tin. The man ordered a low-spirit tarsk. It made Tomrin want to shake his head. The man claimed no worries of a headache in the morning. He was adamant: only one! 
“Yes,” Robles said, steering the conversation deftly to what mattered. “There is money to be made in glass. Gold ducks aplenty.”
Ambush [?]
Tomrin stared at the men gathering themselves and things around him.
The three miserly men were interested in two things: Robles and the talk of gold. Rennly’s face, however, was a mask of pleasant charm, while Dable appeared so satisfied with everything he looked like he might split. Marten though glanced from man to man as much as Tomrin did. When the large Orish counselor caught Tomrin staring at him, he nodded thoughtfully. Tomrin nodded back. If he was here, where was the lady Margretta?
“I really think we should have my daughter-in-law’s brother here,” Rejal said. “He knows glass.” 
“Master Talbat?” Robles asked. “He runs a shop here in town?” 
Rejal nodded. “He does.” 
“I thought so,” Robles said. “And he probably knows something, but I can assure you—” 
“Asking him makes a duck’s feather more sense, than knives and slaves.” Rejal said. 
The man had a point. Too true.
“No offense to your daughter-in-law’s brother, Master Rejal,” Robles said, holding up his glass tin. “When it comes to making glasses such as these, it is child’s play compared to what we are doing at Godly Glassworks. Our lowest skilled worker could do this well. I can do better than any Master Talbat can put out—and that’s not bragging.”
“Sure sounds like it to me,” Rejal replied. No one spoke as he sipped his low-spirit tarsk and set it down. “So, you’re good?” 
“Me good?” Robles asked, setting down his own glass undrank. “I’m decent but I’m hardly the glass master. Even before Master Laz passed away, he was surpassed by a glass worker with twice his skill, maybe more, a true genius of glass.” He pointed at Tomrin. 
“Well—I—ah—” Tomrin stuttered. 
“Well, are you good or not?” Rejal demanded.
“I’m pretty good,” Tomrin said. 
“He invented a new lens the other day,” Robles added. 
Dable laughed. “He’s pretty good, just up and invented new things the other day, like it’s nothing!” He took a swig of his tarsk, emptying his glass in a single go and slamming it down. “That’s how you know he’s my boy!”
Tomrin began formulating a plan to leave. He had the perfect excuse. His foot from the drubbing Rennly gave him was hurting—even through the tarsk. 
“I might be mistaken,” Robles said, “But if I had to guess, I would say you fine gentlemen are looking to invest in Godly Glassworks.” He turned to the men in turn. “Please correct me if I’m wrong?” 
To the Heart [?]
“All this sounds good to me, but I need to go, my foot needs salve,” Tomrin said, kicking back his chair to put on his boots. 
“I thought you two were serious,” one of the miserly men said.
Tomrin flushed with irritation. He had months’ worth of pay in his pocket. He didn’t need the man’s money. He didn’t need a salary. “I am serious about glass. If you—” Tomrin stomped on a boot. “—want to put money in, that’s up to Robles. I master the glass, he masters everything else.” In the ensuing silence at the table, Tomrin pulled in the other boot, sucking air as his foot cried out in pain. 
“Is that even legal?” Nuttel asked. 
“I don’t see why not,” Marten offered. “We all heard him. Words spoken are just as good as written when witnessed by so many.” 
Tomrin stood up. “There you go.” He picked up his tarsk, drained it and set the glass back down. Affecting a poor bow, he offered them all a goodnight before heading for the entrance to the merchant tent. He hobbled in pain and went slow from it—and the tarsk. 
Robles caught him at the entrance. “They are going to want control.” 
“I trust you,” Tomrin said, “Whatever you think is right, you know I’ll be happy with it. You’re the Pack Leader. As long as I’m still the kingbird of the glass I’m good with anything.” 
“I can’t believe you are leaving me with these wolves,” Robles said. 
“My foot, man.” 
“It’s not that bad.” 
“It’s not your foot,” Tomrin said. “When I meet with Rennly will I only be hobbling?” 
“Rennly’s not important. He’s a clown in a black dress.” 
“But he’s my clown in a black dress and he’s trying to kill me.”
“That’s fair, but…”
Marten walked over, behind Robles. “I need a quick word with, Master Ocrin.” The Orish man said to Robles. 
“All yours, counselor,” Robles replied, visibly irritated. “I am just going to do what I think is right.” He turned and headed back to the overpopulated table. 
“What is it, Counselor Marten? Everything is fine,” Tomrin said; it wasn’t a question. The man was worried for him, shadowing him like this. 
“Lady Margretta wanted me to remind you—tell you--she forgot to tell you. Whatever you do, don’t drink any from the Wellspring. She needs to train you first.” 
“I know that,” Tomrin said, just as quietly as the man whose voice had turned to a whisper. “I’m not a fool.” 
“She’s worried… Yith the stress you’re under you might do something. You know?” Marten sounded embarrassed. “Sometimes the stress makes it easier to reach the Dark Halcyon, to feel the pull. Sometimes the Pond will sing. Just ignore it. It’s a lie.” 
“I know,” Tomrin said. “Why don’t you let her know warning comes a little late. I’ve already heard and ignored the Luster many of times before. I’m not an unread peasant,” he added. “And you need not follow me.” 
“Of course not. Good. Good,” Marten said. “I’ll let you go. If anyone asks, I was just letting you know your legal obligation still holds with your friend negotiating on your behalf.” 
“Yes, fine,” Tomrin said. “One more thing, counselor?”
“Yes?”
“Tell me. Did Lady Margretta tank our deal with the Sapphire Front?”
“If she did, this is the first I am hearing of it.”
Tomrin sighed. “Thank you.”
“And one last thing,” Marten said.
“My half brother, Rennly?”
Marten nodded. “He is your enemy. And wants you dead.”
“I know,” Tomrin said.
With the grace unusual to men his size, he turned and left Tomrin standing in the way of a new couple as they stumbled into the Castle tent from the Merchant Quarter. Tomrin stepped awkwardly around them as the man remarked on the quality of gentlemen these days, not even holding the door.
Chapter 20: The Edge of the World
	We are inside someone else’s nothing.
		—Head Astronomer Eldon Main, personal notes, circa 326 AC.

Tomrin registered no surprise at flying.
He’d flown above the streets of Woolad so many times before and every time he felt the same exhilaration, the same sense of limitless freedom. At times he flew only five, ten, or fifteen feet above the ground. Now he flew high above his childhood home where he’d been born. This high above the carpentry shop he could see all of Woolad below. Flight was effortless for him, this being who he was; this bird free of all but the highest grandeur!
He soared and the earth spread out before him as Woolad shrunk from view. The world grew blue and green, rich and lush with color and life. The sun shined red, a ruby hanging in the sky. Tomrin thought nothing of it. It gave off no heat, only light…as it should. The cool air did not stir as he rose higher. Mountains shrank, oceans grew, and great clouds of white like the bloom of cottonwood trees soon hid the solid earth from view. Still higher he flew. 
He was at the edge of something. Something great. Like he was flying to a great new revelation. It was as if chance had merged with destiny, and his destiny had gone from good to great, to grand. He turned to look to see where he was flying and recoiled at what he saw. 
The Vast Space
The space ahead was limitless in size, and countless worlds stretched from next door out in all directions.
For as grand as he had felt moments before, Tomrin saw he was nothing now. A great vast alien space loomed in front of him and it, a great, yawning dark abyss. A mindless terror seized him, as the void starred into him with countless glittering eyes. There were worlds upon worlds; stars upon stars—and a great void between, the size and scope defying his imagination. To look was to be lost; to see was to invite blindness, death, destruction. Madness!
In panic he clawed his way back. He awoke, shocked at his fear, surprised to find himself in his sisters’ tent as the dream faded. Of course, he couldn’t fly. At least that part was a shame. He slipped back to sleep, disappointed—the same as always after a flight dream. That and a touch of unease. 
When he woke, it was morning and too hot to roll over on the cot and fall back asleep. He sat up and put his feet on the ground and groaned. His body hurt, but his foot was a fat puffy wad of pain. The color had only deepened. The purple and yellow made it look sick. 
He swore several muttered curses through gritted teeth as he pulled on his pants and put on his boots. “This is feathered ridiculous!” 
No amount of gold was worth this. I need a new line of work, he thought as he left the tent to enter the stirring glare of a Woolad spring morning. But he knew he was being ridiculous. He was a glass man and nothing was going to change that.
High Summer [?]
The day cooked like summer.
A crystalline, morning-blue sky filled the overhead. Not a single cloud. Even the air between the tents was thin. Tomrin walked, avoiding the paths and the people on them. They were laughing and calling to each other. Tomrin had no desire for revelry—not when he was still rubbing sleep from his eyes and they were the ones who woke him.
He limped to his father’s tent. His mother was inside. She was busy putting away the furniture for travel. “Your father’s at the fair,” she told him. “You’ll have to get your breakfast there,” she added as she tucked away a skein of thread. She was saving her sewing to pack last. 
Tomrin headed to the fairgrounds. He needed to find his father, or Robles, or even Marten. As a soldier, Tomrin figured the man had to know something about staves. But definitely his father. I need to train up quickly. He didn’t need Marten’s words of warning to spur him on. Even with a bad foot, he could waste no time. Rennly might use it to train himself. That would be just like him: a feathered menace.
No Help [?]
Tomrin rubbed his chest liberally. No one would think it strange but he glanced around all the same.
Emy walked up the path. She wore what looked like the same white robe as yesterday. He pulled his hand away from his chest, idly adjusted his shirt.
“It’s almost time for me to sing my song.” Emy said. “You are going to go listen right?” 
“I don’t know,” Tomrin said, 
“Oh, you have to!” 
“Well, since you are my favorite sister—” 
“I’m your only sister!” Emy squawked. 
Tomrin touched her little nose with his finger. “Boop. You are correct, but still my favorite.” Tomrin wondered where her friend, the Orioles ox of a girl, was but didn’t ask. 
When they reached the gorge with the stage and a high pavilion raised over it to protect the singers from the sun, no act was singing. A censor had taken the stage. The crowd was not happy. The Orish grumbled the hardest. “What happened?” Tomrin asked, but the censor shouted for quiet. 
“Quiet!” the black suited Orioles man called out. “Everyone quiet down or those blaspheming scoundrels won’t be the only ones arrested!” 
The crowd quieted to a low grumble. Tomrin and Emy stood near the top of the knoll. Tomrin saw where the censor pointed. Ten or more censors with an equal number of Orioles men stood with four Orish boys in custody. Near the foot of the stage. The Orish boys were dressed up in women’s clothing. Part of their act? Tomrin wondered. From the distance Tomrin couldn’t be certain if these Orioles men he’d seen before. Two of the slavers looked like the ones at Master Laz’s the night he’d died. The censor on the far left, maybe could have come from the Winged Helm Inn. 
“They are from Zircos,” Tomrin whispered to Emy. She shushed him with a look. The censor did want quiet. 
The man on the stage continued, raising his voice to a bellow, clearly irritated that he didn’t get his quiet, only a simraculun of it as others whispered and talked in low voices against his will. “It is against the law to harbour slaves in Corundum as well as Zircos. When we find you, and you know who you are, the punishment is lashes—one hundred lashes. So if you harbor a slave stop, turn them over tonight, one night only and you will be free of punishment. I see evil amongst you people! Evil men, Marked men. You harbor them as well, and the punishment will be worse. The gods do not take kindly to disobedience. Perhaps it is your husband, or son? Turn them in. If they are found and you are found to know, the punishment is death! And your soul will forever be cast into the Lake of Fire!” 
“A hundred lashes,” Emy whispered. “So many.” 
“Uh, yes,” Tomrin said. He wasn’t thinking about the punishment for harbouring slaves. He kept his eyes on the censor, afraid the fear would betray him. 
He could trust no one. Not the four boys the censors were making an example of. Not his family. Not strangers. Not even Robles, but he wasn’t here. Nothing has changed, Tomrin told himself. This was the danger he lived with. Slowly, he rubbed his chest with the back of his left thumb. The scales, like those of a fish, were growing back. Soon he’d have to burn them off.
Perfect Exasperation [?]
The loud censor left the stage and he and his cohort marched the four culprits off up the knoll and past Tomrin. 
These boys were clearly nobodies and there women and two men followed begging the censors for justice and mercy, swearing that their boys weren’t runaway slaves. They had the papers. 
“Runaway would avoid notice, not enter a competition,” Tomrin said, loud enough for the censors from the stage to hear. The closest one was only a few yards away. 
The front man halted the procession. “Who said that?” The man demanded to know. “Who here thinks they knew what runaway slaves would do?” He was a whip of a man skinnier than Rennly but as tall. He had a razor-sharp face and his eyes like a razor slid over Tomrin. Fool. Tomrin wasn’t sure how he meant the man or himself for opening his mouth. He worried Emy might implicate him, but she stood strangely quiet. 
“Yes, if they are runaway slaves why risk putting on a show?” One of the two Orishmen asked, taking up the argument. 
Clearly annoyed, the censor restarted the procession. He wasn’t going to find his man and the argument wasn’t making him look wise, or judicious. The crowd seemed more than ready now to turn on at least the slavers. Armed with Tomrin’s words the Orish men were making the black suited man look petty and small for picking on boys, dressed in ladies’ clothes. 
“Are you out of your mind?” Emy asked when the men had tracked down the others from the knoll. 
“I was wondering the same thing,” Tomrin replied.
A Real Glow [?]
“I thought it was brave to say something,” a voice behind him said.
Tomrin had heard it before. And the glow of it made him forget his Mark. It belonged to the girl Elisah, the judge’s clerk and daughter. She was with her father, Judge Reydal. He did not wear his red judge gown but the simple gray brown suit with a small black cravat tied around his throat. It looked hot and suffocating but he seemed fine with the heat. Elisah no longer wore her gray dress of wool, but a blue dress with a high neck, laced at the junction of her chin. Her hair was coiffed without a brown strand out of place. 
“What are you doing here?” Tomrin asked her. 
Elisah smiled at him and answered his artlessly posed question. “I’m going to sing.” 
“Me too.” Emy said. Tomrin had forgotten for a second that his sister was with him. 
The judge eyed them both. “Tomrin Ocrin. And you must be his wife? No sister?” He extended his hand to Emy. “Judge Reydal, and this is my daughter Elisah.” 
“I’m Emy.” They shook hands.
Elisah held her own out to Tomrin. Her fingers were long and slender. He stared at them a split second before taking them. She shook, he just held on. 
“It’s really great that you are working to keep the glass shop in Menad,” Eliash said. “From the sounds of it Godly Glassworks will be pumping new money into the community again soon.” 
“And it’s a shame about your old master,” Judge Reydal added as he took Tomrim’s hand and shook. 
“It’s so sad,” Elisah said, touching his arm. “I’m sorry for your loss. Master Lazoloth seemed like a father to you.”
“Yes,” Tomrin said, feeling mixed. In a way she was right about Master Laz even if he never really thought of him like that. He was like his fathers in many ways. Now he was dead, and Tomrin felt more for him, felt closer because he too had been born with the Leviathan’s Mark.
Eliash removed her hand.
Like the Birds [?]
The master of ceremonies climbed tentatively to the stage, taking each step like a man well chastened.
He announced the next act by reading it woodenly from his program. The man he called climbed to the stage and sang his song while the master of ceremonies waited on the wing watching, listening as if expecting to have to cut him off.
It was awkward for Tomrin standing next to the judge and his daughter.
He could not focus on the machinations on the stage, not until Emy’s name was called and Elisah headed up through the crowd to be closer when it was her turn. Emy’s song was sadly as forgettable as the others, and Tomrin wondered when the competition would get over. The sun was tracking high in the sky, and beat down on his head and shoulders. After her song Emy headed into the crowd to her large friend. Finally, the master of ceremonies called Elisah Reydal’s name from the list.
“I wanted a boy,” Judge Reydal said, almost as if to himself as his daughter climbed up the wooden steps of the stage. She began to sing and the crowd fell quiet. She sang and the crowd applauded when she hit high notes. Tomrin or the Judge, both just listened as Elisah’s voice soared to unspoken heights. The words were a language Tomrin didn’t know, but it sounded like High Silvan. It was as if she were one of the high sprites. When she finished, Tomrin had no doubt that she had won and he clapped with everyone else.
“She’s amazing,” he said, thinking he’d heard the song before. 
“I wouldn’t give her up for anything,” the judge said. He turned to Tomrin and Tomrin saw a tear in the man’s right eye. “It’s funny how the gods work,” the man said, before wiping the tear. 
“Yes,” Tomrin agreed. “Funny.” He glanced around for the censors, remembering Elisah’s song. “Wasn’t that Shelia’s Aria?” 
The judge must have caught Tomrin, meaning. “It’s not banned, and these Zircos censors are just roving bandits. They each think they are destined to catch the Lightshy Crow himself, when they are just thugs,” the man said. “They will move on.”
Tomrin hoped the man was right.
To Bob [?] 
Tomrin was thankful away from under the man’s measuring gaze.
It had turned into a struggle to look at him. He wasn’t sure what the man was really driving at. Only for a moment Tomrin allowed himself to fantasize. A more lovely wife he could hardly imagine. “Us and the glass shop,” Tomrin said, as if adding something.
The master of ceremonies called out Robles’ name but he didn’t arrive. After a quick consultation by the judges on the first row in chairs, the winners were announced. The judge headed down to congratulate his daughter. 
His sister hadn’t won but now chatted contentedly with her friend.
Tomrin left her chatting, and went searching for a barber.
A Hair Cut [?]
The young Rubish barber called him by name and was friendly.
Tomrin, however, found the man’s attempt at conversation awkward. He paid the man for his haircut and shave and a small something for his efforts, happy to be away from the infernal talk of weather or the troubles brewing between Zircos and Corundum, other straight nonsense Tomrin hadn’t wanted to follow but could. So much better if I only knew High Silvan.
Sheep Shearing Days largely over, the campgrounds had cleared out by the time his hair was cut, and Tomrin tramped—his limp only hurting when he jostled it wrong—straight to the Ocrin wagon. 
The tents were down and Tomrin’s father was folding the smaller tent Emy’s
Dable made a question noise like clearing his throat. “You certainly don’t look like a hayseed, now. Go find your sister. We’re ready to leave.”
Heading Out [?]
The oxen, two tired looking beasts, Sally and Milly, were already hitched to the wagon.
Everything but the last tent was loaded.
Tomrin hurried back to the hillock and small gorge with the stage for Emy. Most everyone had cleared out but he found Emy with her friend talking to three boys. One was still in his ladies dress. The Orish boy was checking out the big girl Kam. Love, he sighed. So young and so foolish. 
“Emy,” Tomrin said, interrupting their meeting. “Mom and Dad are leaving.” 
“Already?” Emy said. “Feels like we just got here.” Emy was older than he was when Sheep Shearing Days had lost any meaning to him and Tomrin envied her spirit, her zest. 
“I know,” he said, lying. “Feels like only a single day.” 
Emy said her goodbyes and Tomrin said, then finally she consented to be dragged away. “I think she’s in love,” Emy said when they reached the top of the hill. Tomrin just grunted, and without hearing, Emy began listing all the reasons for her suspicion. 
For himself, Tomrin wondered if a boy like him could find the love of a young woman—one with a voice to make grown men cry. “What do you think of the singer who won?” he asked during one of Emy’s precious breaths. 
“Oh, she, Elly? Whatever her name—she is so lovely. I couldn’t believe how beautiful she was, and her voice. I could listen to her all day. She knew who you were.” Emy stopped. “No,” she said, putting a hand on his arm. “You aren’t in love with her are you, Tomrin?” 
“I think I’d know if I was,” Tomrin said, not lying but skimming truth from his words like cream from cold milk. “When I say she was the best. You were second. I didn’t want you getting mad.”   
“Yes, I was robbed.” Emy said, light and airy, tossing her hands in the air.
They continued on to the wagon. Soon Tomrin was riding with his father into the lower steppes. The conversation turned from the only interesting topic of the deal struck the previous night and everyone fell silent as the sun set.
“It feels like we are at the edge of the world,” Tomrin said of the plains stretching into the twilight. 
“I better walk,” Dable said. “I want you to drive the team as hard as you can. We’ll reach your brothers in a couple hours. It will be too late tonight. And next week...your rematch with Rennly. I wish I could be there.”
Tomrin yawned. He didn’t.
Chapter 21: The Lower Steppes
	When you are in the middle of nowhere, it’s best to keep going.
		—An old Peregrine proverb

They raced away from Woolad as fast as the oxen could go—which was a slow plod at best, one which Tomrin’s parents and sister could easily match on foot. 
Tomrin held the reins, but the oxen knew the way. The one on the left was Sally, the one on the right was Milly. They had the plod of slow beasts overworked and underfed, both tired and old. The wagon’s wheels groaned against the sandy path which was the Eastern Highway. Low and off to the left the moon, Serathus, hung low and waxed gibbous. 
On the road, the Ocrin family was not the only one retiring back to their wandering lives on the steppes. Others raced home with them. All but Tomrin wore pilgrims white as was their way. Tomrin had been one of them once—for a short time, but now this land and its people seemed alien to him. Looking for a feather. So many of them, a train of them and so few feathers. They were all wasting their lives on a doomed quest. 
In the darkness, white stars blanketed a strip in the sky. They and Serathus were the only light and the rolling plains appeared dead, a place where life had fled. The gray land stretched right up to the dome of the sky. Tomrin remembered the final moments of his dream from the night before, that strange smallness, the insignificance, as if he were already dead.
Woolad had the feeling of chaos and darkness, but out here light only shone at all to tantalize and to tease, and to further darken the land with shadows. The air was dry and cooling after a day of baking in the sun. They were entering the heart of superstition and ignorance. And it was a vast heart—the death it offered: eternal.
Only a few days, Tomrin reminded himself; he would return to Menad, and he would defeat Rennly. Only a few days of training then he could leave this arid land. 
His father had let him know nothing had changed with Rennly. Tomrin had only a week to prepare and to square off again. He had to be ready; he had to take Rennly by storm. Had to crush him. Then I can go back to glass. 
“What are you thinking about?” Emy asked him from the driver’s side. Their parents had fallen to the back of the wagon. This was a hard life and Tomrin would not wish it on his enemies. Maybe Rennly or Fredick. 
“Rennly,” Tomrin replied. “I don’t understand him. It’s like he can’t grow up.. The judge said the censors today just want to be the one to catch a Marked Man.” Using the judge’s idea made saying it easier. “But Rennly, I don’t know. He’s different, cracked like untempered glass.” 
“You know his secret?” Emy asked in a whisper. 
“Yes. Still doesn’t explain it. So he’s envious of me. I swear I don’t get it.” 
“I don’t know either,” Emy said, and offered nothing further than a yawn.
What’s in the Head [?]
A quiet solemnity ruled for a time. 
Side roads, just wide enough for wagons, trickled out of the Eastern Highway, and the train thinned as wagon after wagon left the main road for south or north into the steppes. In time the stars and Serathus shifted in the sky. 
Dable spoke. “Turn here,” he said. “Actually, hop down. I’ll drive the team.” Tomrin laid the leather reins across the front board of the wagon and stood up. Grabbing the handle he eased himself down. “And don’t favor your foot. It will just make it worse,” his father added.
“I know,” Tomrin said. “It’s just stiff, and hurts.” 
Dable mounted the wagon and sat in the driver seat before taking up the reins. “A little pain never hurt anyone. I wish a sore foot was the only pain I had now.” Tomrin’s old man’s shoulders were shot from all the years running a saw and hammer, and he’d hurt his knee out on the steppes some years ago. The joint never quite healed right and his gate had a sight list to it ever since. He swung the reins and Callice and Frita plodded forward. After a minute Dable added: “We’re almost here.”   
Tomrin hoped so. He was growing tired of this trek, his foot hurt, and he was growing weary of being reminded why he didn’t like spending time with his family. Tomrin swore his father was driving longer than anyone before turning as punishment for not competing for second or third place at the fair. I won best in show.
As Janet and Emy prayed, Tomrin limped on in a sullen silence. Every hour they spent traveling, and not sleeping or training, were wasted hours.
Hadras [?]
Temporary paddocks held goats, a few sheep, and a handful of oxen and cows. “Hadras!” Dable called out loudly. “The ribbon winners are here!” 
“Tomrin! My brother!” Hadras said as he exited his tent. They shook hands, Hardas’ grip was a vice crushing bone. 
“Careful!” Tomrin said, pulling away. “I need those fingers. But it’s good to see you, Hadras.”
His half brother wore his hair off to the side in some strange style. Being a pilgrim wasn’t what he wanted to do but he and Dable and Tomrin set up the tents. It didn’t escape Tomrin that Hadras did most of the work. He ran the staking hammer, but it was Dable who directed the work when needed, his words a chafe on Hadras and he knew it. He measured them out, letting Hadras work as hard as he wanted in the moonlight. Hadras with his odd hair was like a stallion, one that had not quite been broken. He still required a delicate touch.
When finally Tomrin lay on his old bed in his old tent, he felt the night had disappeared as if he’d been in a fugue. I still need to warn Hadras about Rennly. It was strange how knowledge was mixed with the feeling of the canvas on his hands and in his fingertips. He closed his eyes and morning came. 
Tomrin’s father woke him and a dream faded as he opened his eyes. 
“It’s time to get up.” Dable said, “We’re moving out.” His father started pulling up the tent stakes of his tent. 
“Going?” Tomrin asked as he rubbed his eyes, they itched, irritated like him at being woken up. “I need to practice staves. That’s the only reason I’m out here.” 
“You’ll practice on the way,” Dable replied and pulled up another stake. “We need to get going. Get up and start helping.” Dable batted the tent. 
“I am getting up,” Tomrin snapped back at him. 
“Good,” Dable said and Tomrin heard the stake clutter to the ground. Clanch. “And your tent is your responsibility.”
Tomrin pulled on pants feeling irritated yet somewhat assuaged by his forward thinking. He had kept his shirt on, and now with the tent’s integrity breached he decided not to change it, wrinkled and sweaty though it was. He left his tent tired and annoyed at being woken up so rudely. He grumbled his good morning to his brother, sister, and mother. They were fast at work tearing down the camp while the animals stirred about, bleating their disapproval. They knew they would soon be driven from their paddocks.
“Dable,” Hadras muttered out of their father’s earshot. “I swear.” 
Tomrin could hardly fault him.
On the Way [?]
Hadras pulled the drawstrings of Tomrin’s tent, the last to be rolled and pushed into a canvas sleeve.
Tomrin’s brother had already carried their father’s tent all by himself, now he carried Tomrin’s smaller tent to the wagon and set it in.
“Thanks, Hadras,” Tomrin said, but Hadras just grumbled; he was in a mood. 
“You should wear the white with us,” Emy said as they stopped and ate a quick bite of dried berries and flat bread. She had been chatting away with their mother about who she hoped to meet as they headed deeper into the steppes.   
“Can’t you just be quiet for once in a life?” Hadras asked. He plopped down the last of his bread and stomped, off his mane of hair flopping as he headed for the paddock. 
“He’s such a pig,” Emy said.
“Don’t be mean,” Janet Ocrin berated her. “He can’t help it; you can.” It seemed a double standard to Tomrin, but managing Hadras’ moods had always been a problem. Tomrin had heard some say it was him who burnt the Ocrin Carpentry shop to the ground, not Rennly’s father, and not just Rennly that said that. Tomrin didn’t believe any of it. As far as he was concerned he had caught Master Richot in the act. “Tomorrow, I’ll wear the whites for you, Emy,” Tomrin said, standing. He walked over to Hadras and helped him tear the last of the paddock down. 
Dable limped over and handed Tomrin a shepherd’s staff. 
“How am I to practice with this?” 
“Not with this, and at midday, when the sun is high,” Dable said. “And stop favoring your foot.” 
He limped away, favoring his own.
A Taste of Dust [?]
Soon the family and animals were underway, with Hadras driving everyone to hurry as if he were in charge of the family, not Dable. The oxen pulled the wagons all hatched together in a train. Dable rode in front and Emy and Janet behind in the others. Not needing any help, Hadras led the goats behind him and drove the sheep ahead of him. Tomrin found himself using his staff more as a walking stick than for what it was designed for.
The hot morning sun grew hotter, higher in the sky. The sere land rolled and the goats and sheep killed any green they found in their way, eating and chewing their way forward. Rocks baked in the sun and Tomrin shaded his eyes against the glare in the sky in front of them and over the rocky soil. The Lower Steppes had always been dry, but this was the worst he’d ever seen. 
To New Cold Well Springs [?]
“What the feathers are you doing out here, Tomrin Ocrin?” he asked himself the next day as the sun ascended towards its zenith. “You are no shephard, even dressed in white.”
He’d wasted most of the previous day. Robles could have helped him as much, probably more. Today Hadras drove one of the wagons: Emy’s. She walked with Tomrin as he helped her drive the animals on. As Hadras insisted that today he drive the wagon. No one thought to argue. 
“This isn’t very fun,” Emy admitted to him, rejoining him with one other sheep. 
“No. It has me wondering,” Tomrin said, “Why am I even doing any of it.”
Emy set down the wayward lamb. “What do you mean?” Emy asked. She made no secret that she was unhappy: the New Cold Wellsprings, where they were headed, was likely to be deserted by everyone, even other Pilgrims.
“Why train? Why bother with any of that?” Tomrin said. “Godly Glassworks is hardly the only glass shop in the world. I could go to the capital. In Corundum I would make three or four times as much as I do in Menad. I’m being silly, wanting to stay in Menad.” 
“Oh, don’t go to Corundum!” Emy said. “You would be so far away then.” 
“From this?” Tomrin said, “I want to get as far away from this as possible. That’s why I left. Can’t you see it? All this searching for a feather is silly. Lightning striking the sea in the same spot twice is more likely. You’re wasting your life out here. I can feel mine dripping away.” 
Emy stopped. “You sound like you hate the gods,” she said.   
“I don’t hate them.” Tomrin turned to stare at where New Cold Well Springs should have already appeared. Mirages, the silvery blue spirits which haunted the hot days of the Lower Steppes, lay ahead.
Cold Well Springs [?]
Finally the old well of the Cold Well Springs resolved from the mirages, looking from before the Cracking of the World Egg—at least some said that the Cracking was why the stonewell had run dry. The stone blocks were pitted from age and were gray like those found in Menad and Woolad and the surrounding areas. Rotting planks of wood covered the top, the lip of the well the perfect height for a toddler, or sheep or goat, or even ox to trip and fall in. 
Tormin and Emy followed the wagons, careful to not let any of the willful beasts in their charge head for the old cistern. It still smelled of water. It was said that the dead who fell in still haunted the well—the old and the new.
New Cold Springs [?]
Water ran clear from the moss covered rocks around the new spring. And while two wide-trunked Myrrh shrubs now grew near the old well—those around the new well had long since drowned and fell to decay. 
This was life in the Lower Steppes, and Tomrin couldn’t believe his family still clung to their beliefs that the gods cared at all. He glanced around at them, lost in thought. He thought back to something Margretta had said. Who are you, Tomrin Ocrin? Who was he and what was he doing here? I am the kingbird of glass, he told himself. But that felt empty. 
When, after they watered and fed the animals, and after they watered and fed themselves, Tomrin announced that he was leaving in the morning. “What about your training,” Dable said. 
“I don’t need to be a master at staves, just good enough to beat Rennly.” 
“Sounds like you are going off half-baked again,” Dable said. “You still have a whole day to train, then another to reach Menad.”   
“It’s all coming back to me,” Tomrin said, “Not just the staves, but why I left all this in the first place. This land is a waste. Feathers or no feathers. Can’t you see the gods are liars? Living in Menad has opened my eyes. If they want their feathers back so bad, why not let them find them?” 
“Talk like that,” Dable said. “It’s a good thing your mother doesn’t hear it.”
“So? You don’t believe either,” Tomrin said. “Aren’t you just humoring my mother?”
“You don’t understand sacrifice.” Dable stomped off to his tent and left Tomrin standing in front of his own.
Knife Hunting [?]
Tomrin walked over to his brother’s tent. “Hadras, I need to borrow a knife.” 
“What for?” Hadras asked from inside his tent. “Not going to hurt anyone, are you?”
“Not unless it’s Rennly.”
“What?”
“I angered Dad too,” Tomrin ticked off. “But no.” His brother’s question only cemented his irritation at being out here. “Not hurting anyone but myself.”
The tent stirred with Hadras’ shadow. The flap opened and Hadras handed him his bone handled knife. “Don’t do that either,” he said.
Tormin took the handle and sheath. “If I die and Rennly is still alive, just watch out. He might be coming for you.”
Hadras spied the air and horizon suspiciously. “Thanks.”
Tomrin turned, and in case Hadras called after him still wanting to know what he had in mind, he worked up a lie. Cut my finger nails. But Hadras didn’t ask. The sun was setting and Tomrin headed for his tent. I don’t understand sacrifice?
The animals were bedding down in their paddocks, safe from the coyotes and cats. It wasn’t exactly safe to be out alone on the steppes at night. Or so it was said. Tomrin felt it was just one more superstition, even if the animal tracks lead to the springs. Out here it seemed every nonsense story about bloodbeasts and men-eating, half-man half-beast, could be true. I really need to get out of here. He was starting to see how his parents could come to believe such nonsense in the stark moonlight.
The Spook in the Night [?]
Inside his own tent, Tomrin tossed the knife on his bed and retied the flaps. 
He lit a match. The acrid aroma of the match head burning filled his nostrils and tent as he lit his floor lamp. Dropping the match on the ground, he snuffed it as with his boot, grinding its smoking ember into the dry dirt. Then with Hadras’ knife in hand he sat down on his bed. The the curved thing looked sharp. 
Tomrin sat staring at the knife’s curved blade. It was still too early to make the cut. The mass of evil in him, so large now, was ready to escape his chest. He pulled out some small clothes and tossed them on the knife. He moved around in his tent as if in a dream. He crouched to the ground. He didn’t want to do this here. You should have left last night. Now he had no choice. He had neither good flames, nor glass rods to heat. 
Cutting was so much worse. He’d still have to cauterize the wound or it would bleed and bleed. Even then it would still bleed some before he could cauterize it. 
Tomrin knelt down near the lamp. Its flame would have to serve to heat the knife. He began to dig lightly into the dirt. His blood all over the ground? Better to bury it. The soil was pebbles and sand and dirt and a few roots and streamers sent out by the leathery weeds and grass. Even this close to the springs the steppes grass was sparse and water starved this year.
Tormin waited before the lamp, then moved to his bed and waited again.
He tested the knife’s edge against his finger nail letting the blade catch against the thumb of his left hand.
Hadras kept the thing sharp. 
Confusion [?]
Tomrin set down the knife and kicked the mound of dirt into the hole, and laid down without cutting the scales from his chest.
He still had time. He could train all tomorrow. The Ocrin wagons were here now. They were not going anywhere soon; his family had their lives to find a Serath’s feather.
Tomrin thought back, remembering a moment from his childhood. Rennly had stopped him on the street below the temple. He wanted to fight but Tomrin had run instead. Tomrin wondered if all this was Rennly trying to have his fight? If that fight had happened then, would any of this be happening now? Why can’t he just let it go?
At some point Tormin fell asleep.
“If you are going, it’s time to get going.” Dables voice woke the skittering sleep he had entered after waking him in the morning. “Tear down your tent.” 
“I didn’t sleep.”
Dable batted the side of the canvas tent. “If you are staying, you have to pray. Now get up.” He continued to rap the tent. Bwap. Bwap. Bwap.
“Feathers,” Tomrin swore under his breath. He had no desire to pray. “Fine. Give me a minute.”
Dable gave the tent another good paw. Bwap. “Good.”
Incision [?]
The air seemed cool for the moment, but that would change as the milky sky turned a sapphire blue. The suffocating heat would bring mirages and illusions, phantoms of the mind to the steppes.
Tomrin said not a word at prayers and after breakfast, dried blueberries, Tomrin and Dable set about to practice. Tomrin picked up his practice staff, and followed Dable out beyond the gurgling water of the spring. Dable used the lighter staff to etch a circle in the rocky soil. Tomrin dropped the butt of his staff on the ground and held it with his right hand and waited. 
“You look like you know how to use that thing, standing like that, my brother,” Hadras said standing over him. Tomrin couldn’t understand why his brother would address him like that. Yes, they were step-brothers but it made no sense not to use his name. 
“When I beat the feathers out of Rennly, I’ll be the kingbird of more than glass,” Tomrin said, hefting his staff with both hands and swinging it down like a hammer, mace or scepter. Incompletely carved as it was, it still had the feel of a staff, but definitely no good in a fight. 
Dable finished the circle. “Perfect, yet again,” he announced of the ring drawn on the ground, even though as Tomrin stepped inside he could see his father had slightly corrected the final few feet to make the circle meet. “Now?” Dable asked Tomrin. 
“You go on the defense.” 
“Will Rennly be on the defense?” 
“He will be when I slam him,” Tomrin replied. “I’m done...” He didn’t wait for his father or to say more himself, but advanced swinging.
Dable swatted him after making an easy block.
Training [?]
Tomrin’s attack had no finesse and Dable let him know as he rubbed his arm.
“You will have to do better than that if you want to put Rennly on the defensive.” 
They settled in to spar. After Dable tired, Hadras took his place, and Dable yelled at the both of them. No, Tomrin! Hadras, you wool head. Keep your staff up. Keep your head in it. Patience. Work him, work him! Don’t look there. Just go! Hold that stick. Step. Step. Step! You have to take a feathered step! 
Dable hobled into the ring breaking up the clack, clack, clack, of their staves. “You are slower than a feathered goat,” he said, taking his staff back from Hadras. 
“So?” Hadras asked. “I’m faster than you.” 
Hadras wander off, towards the tents and the fresh water. His brother had the right idea, but instead of following him to the cool of a myrrh shrub Tomrin and his father continued to spar. 
“You could be a grand champion in Corundum, the way you were able to win the yellow ribbon,” Dable said when they both tired. 
“I don’t care about ribbons or staves.” Tomrin said. “I just need to beat Rennly. That only once. Then I can watch him, keep him from poisoning my drink.”
“You really hate him, don’t you?” Dable said.
Tomrin shook his head. “Not me, Papa, not me.”
Time [?]
Back in camp they all played pincushion until the stars came out, all but Hadras who brooded in his tent and had not come out—not even for super. 
When Tomrin begged off another game he went back to his tent. He lit the floor lamp and redug the hole. He waited until he was happy no one was going to interrupt him. The mass was ready to push out of him and be visible to all—or only Daughters? 
He didn’t want to find out the hard way. He pulled off his robe and took the knife from the bed. Kneeling down in front of the lamp, he cut the glinting scales from his chest.
In the morning he would head back to Menad and catch Rennly off guard.
Chapter 22: The Old Dead Well
	And in a darkness as black as holes in space, will he still know our names?
		—A Translation from the Sixth Age

Tomrin dropped the blood soaked smallclothes into the small pit. Sweat coated his body and gave him a chill as he heated the blade of Hadras’ knife in the lamp’s flame. Blood from his chest streamed into his left hand cupped under the wound. The red marble dripped through his fingers into the pit at his knees and splattered over the removed scales.
Gritting his teeth against the heat of the point, Tomrin touched the knife to the wound. The pain of touching hot steel to raw flesh drove breath from his lungs. The smell of blood boiling the air and Tomrin’s nostrils as he breathed in. “Feathers,” he swore as he pulled away the knife.
The flame hadn’t heated the blade hot enough to cauterize the wound. Thrusting the knife back into the flame, the blood of the tip blackened just outside the orange of the flame. The blood from his wound continued to gush. The cup of his hand was full with hot slimy red. 
He shifted his weight on his knees and the blood in his hand leaked, splashing down into his clean smallclothes. This had turned into a feathered mess. Gently rocking the knife in the flame, he kept it in the hottest part. The charcoaled blood on the tip turned red hot and began to glow. No waiting this time, Tomrin told himself. The knife needed to be as hot as possible. 
One fluid motion as he arched his wrist to press the tip into the wound, he pulled the blade to his chest. The round hole in his chest was the size of a silver penny and he would have to hit it twice, maybe three times to staunch the flow. The idea made him winch. More blood splashed. His hand was filling more quickly than he remembered it ever had.
He made only a small squeaking knife as he touched the hot knife to his skin.
He pulled the knife from the wound. The triangle of painful burning flesh gushed blood, just like the rest. His blood leaked through his fingers and over the side of the cup he made with his left hand. The stream tickled as it ran down his chest and belly, ruining his small clothes. He was going to have to throw them in the pit as well. 
Disgusted Tomrin dumped his blood out, flunging it with the shallow pit. 
“Feathers,” he muttered. It was only a flesh wound, but the cut bled like it was a puncture. He clapped his hand over his heart to hold in the leakage. Cauterizing his wound wasn’t working. With his hand held awkwardly on his chest, he stood up and dropped the knife. 
Too sharp? He ran through the possibilities in his mind. Or dull? Maybe it was the tarsk, or maybe the sheer size of the wound. The last time he’d cut the Leviathans Mark off the scales were fewer in number and individually smaller. It was like the feathered thing was getting worse as he got older.
He stood for several minutes holding his chest until his arm grew tired. He held it in place with his other then. He waited several minutes but when he took his hands away the wound continued to bleed as before. You need to stop the bleeding. A clot need to form. He set out to make a bandage. He tore a strip from his shirt, getting blood all over it and on his back pack. He held the strip of cloth to the wound, and it seemed to work but it sopped up the blood and dripped out like water. 
He threw the strip into the pit and Tomrin used the whole rest of the ruined shirt. But it too soon sopped through as blood soaked the fibers. So much blood. When blood began to stream from the bundled up shirt he went back to just holding in his blood in with his bare hands. At least that way he was able to reduce the flow to a small dribble. His small clothes were drenched, but he couldn’t worry about that. First he had to stop the bleeding.
Cauterizing the wound hadn’t worked. I made some mistake? He tried it again and failed again. When he stood up he hoped it was the tarsk making him unstable on his feet and weak as well, but tarsk had never done that. It had cut the pain. Now he feared it was making his blood not clot. “At least its gone for the moment,” Tomrin muttered to himself, knowing he was going to have to face the truth. Sitting down on his bed. It, like him, was spattered in blood. Blood was everywhere.
He was certain to be discovered now, just a matter of time. He tried to think but dizziness distracted him, making it hard to focus on anything. Set the tent on fire? But that wouldn’t fix the fact that he was bleeding and no matter what he tried he couldn’t make the bleeding stop. He looked down and realized he had dropped his hand from the wound. Blood drained out of the cut. His heart beat faster and his focus returned. He was going to die if he just sat here. 
“You need help,” Tomrin told himself and forced himself to his feet. His bloody fingers fumbled at the flap strings. Knife. His fingers had gone numb and the slick blood made it hard to work the strings. The knife seemed dead in his hand, but it cut the strings and he dropped it.
It was as revolting as it was alien.
Help [?]
Tomrin rubbed his hands together trying to bring back feeling in them as he ran. 
The wound! The thought brought his hand to the wound. He stumbled but made his way to his father’s and mother’s tent, surprised when he found himself there. 
“Mom. Dad,” he said. “I need your help.” He wasn’t sure if the warbling voice he heard was his, but those were the words he’d wanted to say. Not wanted—but needed. “I’ve cut myself...bad.” 
The groggy sound of his parents waking reached Tomrin through a fog. He couldn’t believe this was happening. “Yes, it’s me,” he replied to his mother’s indistinct question. 
“Dable, put your robe on,” she said. 
Yet it was the fleshy form of Tomrin’s father that appeared by starlight at the flaps of the tent. 
“Uh,” Dable said. “You look like you were attacked by a bear. Show me your wrist.” Dable took Tomrin’s arm and pulled it from his chest. 
“It’s not my wrist,” Tomrin said as his chest drained blood.
“Oh, Tom, dear gods,” Dable said, letting go. “Janet!” The fear in his voice was enough to scare Tomrin back to his senses. He clamped his hand back over the wound. 
Janet Ocrin pushed out of the tent a moment later. Her robe had been tied and hung loosely over her like a small tent. Tomrin flinched when she grabbed him. “Oh, my poor baby. You are covered in blood!” 
Hotter [?]
“Yes,” Tomrin said. And everything was coming apart. His mother’s face seemed to swim in front of him. “Need to close the wound,” he added, weakly. 
“Get Hadras,” Dable said and Janet hurried off. Dable turned to Tomrin. “We can’t have you bleeding all over the tent. Stay here. I need light.” Dable disappeared and Tomrin swayed on his feet. He hadn’t realized his father had been holding him up. He sank to the ground. The fear of death kept his hand in place on his chest. He could barely feel the weak beat of his heart. He looked at his other hand like he’d never seen it before, then turned his head to the sky. His hand was covered in blood but the sky was clear. Piercing light, the stars, throbbed with his heart. They were beautiful, pristine and pure, everything he was not. He wished he could be with them, not here on the ground, dying. He could see now he wasn’t going to meet Rennly and beat him; he was too weak. He let head roll back and he lay down, catching himself with his right hand. He could barely feel the rocks and lumps of dirt pushing into his back. 
He closed his eyes but clung to life and willed himself to sit up when his father commanded it. The floor lamp pulled from the tent was large and Dable had lit it. Made of brass and heavy, it squanted next to a folding chair, sitting bare of any cushion. “In the chair, I need to look at you.” Dable pulled Tomrin up and dumped him into the slatted chair. He held the floor lamp in front of Tomrin. “Pull your hand away.” 
Tomrin slowly complied, confused. His hand was the only thing keeping his blood in. “I don’t—“ he said, losing his train of thought. Tomrin could see from Dable’s green eyes he was in trouble. He racked his brain for all the problems he now had, but couldn’t. He only knew this was serious. Maybe worse than serious. The worst.   
Dable replaced Tomrin’s hand over the wound. “Did you stab yourself in the heart.” 
“No,” Tomrin replied. “Why would I do that?” 
Janet returned and she brought Emy and Hadras with her. Emy hugged Tomrin’s head but Hadras asked the real question. “What do we do?” His mane of hair was a mess on his head. 
“Why did you hurt yourself, Tomrin?” Emy whined. 
“How did he?” Dable asked. “How did you hurt yourself so bad?” 
“Knife,” Tomrin said. “This wasn’t supposed to happen.” He was supposed to cut the scales off, then close the wound with fire. A thought rushed to him. “Such a fool.” He saw how he should have held the fire directly to his wound. That might have worked. 
“My knife?” Hadras demanded. “You did this with my knife?” 
“Yes,” Tomrin said, he saw no point in lying about that. 
“You said you weren’t going to cut anyone.” Hadras said, “That’s my sacrificial knife. It’s covered in bleeding toxin.” 
“That? It’s not the—no.” The bloody knife wasn’t triangular at all. 
“Get it, Hadras,” Dable said. 
Hadras argued with Dable a minute about the location of the knife but with Tomrin’s help they figured it out. Tomrin groaned at the empty cold inside. He wanted to swear at it but didn’t have the strength. 
His mother held his right hard, kneeling. Her muttered prayer to Seasargus came to him in snatches. “Oh my poor baby,” she took to repeating. 
Hadras returned with the knife. “And look at this,” he said, his breathlessness wasn’t only from running. He held the wet small patch of skin and scales for Dable to see. Emy eked at the sight, and a burning shame pushed sweat out the pores on Tomrin’s face. 
“The Infernal Scales of the Leviathan,” Dable said and Janet jumped to her feet letting go of Tomrin’s hand. 
“Oh, the sweet gods,” she said and began to cry and turned away. “The evil. He tried to hide the evil. I can’t believe my baby is... He used to be such a perfect little baby. He never even cried.”
Dable stopped her hysterics. “We need to sew him up. A thread and needle! Quick, woman!” 
Hadras was inspecting the wound holding Tomrin’s hand away. “I don’t think that’s going to work,” he said, letting go, and Tomrin’s hand slapped his own chest. 
“We need a big bandage,” Emy said. 
“Cauterize it,” Tomrin starred. “You have to burn it shut.” 
When Janet returned with the needle and thread, Dable sent her back into the tent for a spoon. “Spoon! Metal!” he shouted to her. Dable handed him the needle and thread to Emy. “We still might need this.” 
With the small silver spoon Janet brought back outside in one hand, Dable turned up the lamp. “Hotter,” Tomrin said. “It has to be red. Hotter.” His mother had turned away. She couldn’t even look at him, but she muttering prayers to Seasargus, as if the god in heaven could help him now. “Hotter!” Tomrin said when Dable pulled it from the flame. 
“It’s as hot as it’s going to feathered get!” Dable said. 
“Oh, sweet Seasargus,” Janet wailed. 
Dable brought the spoon close to Tomrin’s hand. “Move your hand.” 
“No, it has to be hotter.” 
Dable turned his head to Hadras, “Grab his arms.” 
Tomrin removed his hand. And Dable pushed the spoon’s cup against the wound. “Ahh!” Tomrin cried and Dable removed the spoon. “It has to be hotter. Feathers!” The wound still bled but his head cleared. The pain was as bad as when he’d cut the scales off. Dable frowned and put the bloody spoon back in the flame. “Put it in the blue of the flame, it’s the hottest.” 
Soon the spoon began to glow a dull orange. “It’s hot.” Dable said, pulling the spoon from the flame. Tomrin unclamped his hand. The spoon’s black and orange colors filled eyes and vision completely. Dable pushed the hottest part of the spoon into the wound. It was hot but not as hot as Tomrin would have liked it. Not hot enough to deaden the ring of nerves that always grew back. 
Tomrin smelled burning flesh as pain dimmed the lamp. He heard his brother and sister speaking as if they were getting further away as if from inside a deep cavern. “I can’t believe my brother is the Lightshy Crow,” Hadras said. 
“I think we killed him,” added Emy. Then all Tomrin heard was the screech of someone in pain as darkness overtook him, snuffing the stars and the lamp light.
The First Taste [?]
His hands—left first, then right—turned to glass or something like it, something light and brittle. 
Then his feet, both together, were transmuted into that same brittle substance. The sky had turned pure white and the ground was black as coal. It too may have been made of glass or charcoal, or even strewn with black feathers. 
Red birds with triangular like-fox heads patrolled the skies above. Green snakes slithered in and out of the dark earth. Tomrin tried to run but couldn’t. The snakes slid close. The birds swarmed. As they dived Tomrin tried to bat them away with his hands and arms, but his appendages refused to move. His hands and feet stuck fast to something. 
He turned his head and saw a demon staring at him. Its red face scowled and its leather wings hung open. It looked at him with a curious eye, a dark intelligence in them. “Are you the Crow?” it asked in an old man’s voice, creaking and catching, as if it were itself. “I am here for the Crow.” 
“No!” Tomrin shouted and beat his wings against the pinions fettering him to the cross bar of his stand. He arched his head back to yell--and banged it. 
He came awake with a start. 
Slowly he realized he was on his back and the bright lights in his eyes were stars in the cloudless night. The blur made him think of the compound beetle-like scope. Why can’t I perfect it? He wondered. Why wasn’t he the master of the glass he wanted to be? Feathers! As good as he was, he still lacked the knowledge to be truly great.
“He’s awake,” Hadras said.
Tomrin bent his neck to look at his chest. A white bandage of cashmere was wrapped over him. A drop of blood had seeped through. The muttering sound of prayer ceased suddenly. Tomrin tried to move his arm. But they were tied with coarse tent rope. 
Tomrin sat up as his family huddled around him. “Why are my hands tied?” he asked. His legs were tied as well. He felt thirsty, empty and tired as if he had run all day in the heat without food or water. “Untie me.” His chest itched—and the pain. He rubbed it with his bound hand. 
“Don’t do that. You’ll tear it open again,” his father said, grabbing his arms. “Is that what you are trying to do?” 
“No, I’m not trying to tear it. I’m trying to scratch,” Tomrin said. The rope was cutting into his wrists and he couldn’t understand how Dable was afraid he was going to hurt himself—or even had tried to hurt himself. It was nonsense. He had the glass shop to help run. And he needed sleep and then to hurry back to Menad in the morning. “I’m fine, I don’t see why you had to bind me. Was I thrashing in my sleep?” 
“You’ve been cutting the Mark of the Leviathan from your chest,” his mother said. 
Tomrin closed his eyes. Yes, they knew—of course, they did. He saw no point in lying about it anymore, “Yes,” he said. “Don’t tell anyone.” 
“But you have to be Purified,” Emy said. 
“No, I don’t. Master Laz didn’t; he lived for years.” 
“He killed himself,” Dable said. “In the morning, I’m going to Woolad to get the censors. It is death if we don’t.” 
“You don’t have to tell them anything. How would they find out?” Tomrin reefed against his bonds. “I want to live free. I don’t need Purification. The gods are liars. Are you so blind?” Staying had been a mistake; coming had been a mistake. They stared at him blankly. They were a mistake. 
“Tomrin, you are scaring me,” Emy said as Hadras stood and backed away, confused. 
“I’m sorry, Emy,” Tomrin said. “But I will not be bound like an animal for sacrifice.” He stared at her then glanced at others. He could not read their shadow-darkened faces. “Just let me go, forget me, but let me live as I choose.” 
“But the gods—” Janet said. 
“I don’t give one twisted feather for the gods!” Tomrin roared. “If they are so powerful then why let the Leviathan break the Gaze Between the Worlds? Or Crack the World Egg. Tell me that. You can’t, because the Book of Light is lies.” 
Janet Ocrin made the Sign of the Tree. “You were right,” she said to her husband. “It was wise to bind him. The demons have my baby.”
Ropes [?]
Tomrin began untying his feet.
Dable grabbed him by the hair and snatched his arms away. His father still had a frightening power in his tired old body. “The gods will decide your fate,” he said and let go of Tomrin’s hair and grabbed his hands and arms instead. “Hadras get his feet.” 
Tomrin flopped like a dying fish and yelled at them to stop. “Don’t touch me!” 
“Where are we taking him?” Hadras asked as Tomrin yelled at them to let him go but they didn’t listent/ 
“The old dead well,” Dable said. “Get two shepherds crooks,” he added to Emy and their mother. 
“Why not just tie him to a tree!” Emy asked. 
“Obey your father,” Janet Ocrin said. 
Hadras dropped Tomrin’s feet when Tomrin flopped again. Hadras stared at them. “Seems better than the old well,” he said. 
“And do you want to be out here when Serathus sets?” Dable said. “Now grab his feet we need to hurry.” 
A sense of dread pushed the anger from Tomrin. “You don’t have to do this; I’ll be good I promise.” But his father didn’t seem to hear him as he puffed and huffed. He and Hadras half dragged, half carried Tomrin to the old dead well. Tomrin felt dizzy. The spinning and lurching of the sky made him want to heave whatever was left of dinner and the tarsk in his stomach. 
Emy and his mother arrived with the staves. Tomrin swallowed the bile that came into his mouth as Dable and Hadras pushed the rotting cover off the pitted blocks of well stones. The blocks emerged a foot, and appeared a gateway to a dead, forbidden realm of spirits. 
Tomrin wanted to scramble away but he was drained of strength. His muscle refused to work. Cold poured from the hole in the ground next to him. 
“Now, hold still,” Dable said. “Or you’ll come off and do an injury to yourself worse than you’re already done.” Dable and Hadras lifted him up by the shepherds’ staves, one under each arm and swung him over the edge of the well, pushing his legs in. 
Starlight looked up at Tomrin from the shallow water at the bottom.
Inside the Well [?]
His step brother and father lowered him towards the blackness.
Tomrin his feet hit the inch of water on the bottom. He wrenched against the right staff under his arm pulling it with a falling jarring motion, ripping it from Hadras hands. Both Hadras and Dable cursed. Hadras cursed the staff, Dable his son. “You feathered idiot.” 
Tomrin tried to pull the other staff from Dable’s hands, but like his mind, his grip was more sure. Dable jerked on the staff and Tomrin’s chest wound tore open. “Ahh!” he screamed and the staff slipped under his arm and his father pulled it back up. 
“You got your clever side from me,” Dable said. “Once you are Purified, you’ll understand all this has been for your best.” 
“What about the other staff?” Hadras asked. 
“He can’t do anything with it,” Dable said. “Help me close it up.” 
They disappeared and Tomrin checked his wound. The bandage filled with new blood. “Help me,” he said, trying to shout as the cover eclipsed the stars above. “The wound tore open...” The plink of small bits of wood and sand hitting water was louder than him. 
Then there was darkness.
He sunk to the ground and the cold began to leach into him. He tried to grab the staff, to pull it into his lap but strength escaped him. It was useless. Everything was useless now. He sat for what seemed hours as his thirst built. Finally he rolled over and sucked sandy water from the bottom of the old dead well. The cool water tasted of mold and something else—rotting skeletons?
His thirst slaked for the moment, Tomrin tried to untie his feet and ankles but got nowhere. He couldn’t see a feathered thing. He crawled, dragging himself to the side of the well. A lip existed between the blocks. Bbound as he was there was no hope of climbing out. With the last of his energy, he turned and put his back to the cold brick and sat. His body and joints slowly filled with pain. He hated the heat, but this cold was no comfort.
Down here, in this well with no escape, night grew colder than cold.
Chapter 23: The Lightshy Crow
	And what of him—and what of his companions? 
For they shall shine brightly against the darkness,
And see the heavens? For they shall count them plenty. 
— From The Dark Verses

A brittleness like wrongly tempered glass filled him as he sat in the inch-deep water. 
The brittleness was like he would crack and his teeth would shatter and his bones break. It was a brittleness like his heart would freeze. It stole in, and his blood slipped out, fused with the darkness and fed the tiny creatures of the well. Desperate to slough the brittleness, Tomrin pulled his knees to his chest and reached out with his bound hands to the ropes tying his ankles. Like rotting tree stumps, his fingers worked the ropes, the coarse fibers biting into his fingertips. 
He couldn’t see a feathered thing he was doing. 
Swearing to himself, and afraid the brittleness would consume him, he scooted like a worm into a bar of starlight streaming through the cover’s slats. Here, white starlight streamed, bright as a torch. The light didn’t burn the rough canvas rope on his arm, but Tomrin could see well enough to untie the knot. A Hadras special. His first bit of luck. 
He pried on the knot until it loosened and he was able to work it free, untying a little at a time as the threads of the coarse rope slipped over twists in the braid. When finally free, Tomrin massaged his ankles where the rope had cut into him. His feet splashed up water and echoed as he stood and walked around the well. He searched the walls with his shoulders and arms and face, looking for a way to climb out. The gaps between the blocks made him think he could use them for hand holds if only he could find a way free of the ropes on his wrists.
He tried rubbing the rope on the gaps, the sharpest he could find, but soon tired. He was so tired and weak. It was going to take everything just to climb out. “I still have time,” he told himself, even though it was slipping away. He still needed to fight Rennly to save the glass shop. One thing his father had said of the old money men, they wanted to see that Tomrin and Robles met their obligations; they had made that a stipulation.
Tomrin swore at them and at Rennly as he collapsed to his knees and sat once more in the water. It seemed colder. Tomrin forced himself to his knees and began searching the ground at the bottom of the well for something to cut the rope. Rocks in the silt had long since lost their sharp. When he found a bone, he hoped for a minute that it was the hilt of a knife, a blade still attached, but it was just as useless as the rounded stones. He dropped the bone into the water. Shlunk. Tomrin continued his search. Even rusted, a knife... He came up with nothing.
He sat, kicked out his legs, wanting to lay down. The well was just wide enough for him to stretch out. He stared at the white edges of the hole in the cover above. 
“You don’t know sacrifice,” he muttered to himself. What was Hadras thinking putting bleeding toxin on a non-sacrificial knife? Everyone knew a sacrificial knife had to be triangular; the toxin was to insure that the sacrifice bleed out. “I’m my own feathered sacrifice now.”
Tomrin laughed through chattering teeth.
Bound in the Dark [?]
His dry wit evaporated him as his fear mounted. 
He pulled at his rope until he worried his arms might crack in two. The skin under the rope split. Finally the pain forced him to stop his thrashing. He checked his chest wound. He was still bleeding to death. He tried to put pressure on it, but his father’s knot on his was all but impossible to press hard enough to stem the flow. 
Each breath came more shallow, and Tomrin could tell he was suffocating.
In a puddle of water flavored with his own watered down blood, he tried to let go of breathing. He wasn’t trying to die, but if sleep came and death with it: Life only prolonged the agony. He let his eyes slowly close.
The starlight dimmed and shadows reared. 
For a time he floated above his body then merged with the darkness. This was all there was left of him. Defeating Rennly, running the glass shop, were gone. The dead had no dreams, no hopes, no joys, and that was what he was. He was a spirit filled with fear and anger. He was not the kingbird of glass. He was not the kingbird of anything. And if drug before Seasargus what would he have to show for his disobedience? Less than Master Laz, that was for sure…
And in the darkness, the Luster called to him: Taste me, and you can destroy anyone in your way.
Tomrin forced himself back into his body and willed his heart to beat. Ka-thud. Ka-thud. Ka-thud. He was standing now. He held hands out in front of himself. Burning sunlight drenched the ropes which bound him. Somehow dawn had come and the Luster gone.
Who are you, Tomrin Ocrin?
Tomrin feared he knew the answer.
And He Shall Be Pinioned To The Shadow Line [?] 
He squinted at the braids twisted about his wrists. 
“I,” he muttered, unsure of himself. “I am the Lightshy Crow.” 
His voice echoed in the well—in his ears and in his head--and he became more sure of himself.
The fetters at his wrists were but fibers of braided canvas, crude and flammable. Time itself would rot them to dust. It and them were nothing; nothing compared to the awesome power calling, just out of reach. If he could command the ambrosia in the Wellspring. He could direct it at the knot and it would have no choice but to give way to him. He could pull down the well cover, and cast it into fire, or shatter it with ice. He could set fire to the steppes. Or not. Tear down tyrants and set up empires. The seas would boil at his command. He had the power to crack Serathus open like an egg, to crush his enemies like insects, yet none would dare to oppose him. Magic, fear, and death? The gods would bow to him. He was the Corvine Man. He was the haunter of lore, the dreadnaught known as the Lightshy Crow.
Fire, ice, shadow and something akin to strength or will flowed into him. The four of them chocked his arteries full. 
The mass in his chest under the scales uncoiling, a sword unsheathed in anger or a sail unfurling in the wind of a storm. It expanded out from him. It  had never been small, and now only a tiny core of it stayed with him as the rest expanded out into the outer reaches of earth and space. It covered everything, the earth, the moon, the stars and the vast emptinesses in between. And he stood at the center, a beacon, folding and warping the fluids. It was much deeper than he had imagined glass the day Master Laz died.
The Wellspring lay all around him, up and down and side to side. 
Liquid ice filled it. Where there was ice, there was fire and it burned the ice. Or maybe the ice grew and the fire burned in reverse. It was confusing, and masked in shadows. The shadows cast shadows of their own. And each mote of fire and ice and shadow were woven together by countless threads, or spools of the fourth substance.
It and they were burning in his veins and glowed almost too bright in the Wellspring.
A Simple Matter [?]
He fought the strands of power ripping through him. He glowed brighter as he netted up the ice and shadow in the wilful threads. Willvane. Tomrin remembered its name, and it made sense now. Controlling the willvane, he shrank it and the ice and shadows to nothing, leaving only fire burning in him.
Sweeping his hands, he cast the fire out through his wrists. The fire flared as it burned the canvas rope. The light blinded him. “Ahh!” he cried as it burned more than just the canvas. The smell of burnt flesh and rope filled the well. 
Tomrin’s second sight dissipated, taking the Luster with it.
Free [?]
He pulled free of the burning rope.
The fire at his wrists had shifted to his hands and came out his palms. They burned like he had hammered freezing spikes into them. Despite the fire’s intense power, Tomrin saw it behaved in subtle ways. It coiled and wound in on itself. He blinked at the bright afterimage of coiled fire as he probed his palms with his fingers tips. When he inspected them in the starlight he saw the fire had been hot enough to cook skin to a chalky white ash. The burns themselves were thin as his fingernails but the surrounding flesh was angry from the heat and began to blister. 
“Feathers,” he muttered and picked up his father’s staff and hung its crook over his shoulder. Using the space between the stone block, for handholds, he scaled the stone wall of the well. 
Finally, the cover brushed his head. Holding the wall with one hand Tomrin pushed his other up and under the lid and he wrenched the cover open. Daylight spilled pain into his eyes. He waited for them to adjust before climbing the last bit out. 
He crawled over the lip and onto the ground. Dirt covered the bandage around his chest. His small clothes were as filthy. Both were stained with blood and dirt. Brushing at them he stood with the help of the staff and leaned on it like an old man. The climb had taken the wind out of him and for a second he pondered the crook of the staff. The way the fire curved in the Wellspring, and how it burned and coiled, the staff might prove useful with its crook.
Another Day in the Sun [?]
The sky was a cloudless blue. 
Another hot one, Tomrin thought as he headed for his tent. Emy outside of hers, stared at him. “Good morning, Emy,” he said. “It’s going to be hot,” he added, glancing at the bandage. “I think the bleeding stopped.” When she didn’t say anything, Tomrin leaned the staff on his own tent and ducked inside his own. His hand was sweating and he could feel salt attacking the light burn on his palm. At least it isn’t bleeding, he thought as he sat and began to change. There was so much blood everywhere but the clothes in his backpack were still relatively clean. 
Stripping the bandage off his chest, he poked the sticky scab with his index finger before tucking his feet into his boots. Voices rose outside as he stomped into them. Emy was talking. She certainly liked to talk. The thought brought a smile to Tomrin’s face, but couldn’t understand what she meant. “There is something wrong with Tomrin. I know it.” 
“And he is in his tent?” he heard his father ask.
Tomrin stepped outside, pushing the tent flaps out of the way. Not just Emy and his father, Dable, but his whole family was outside walking towards him. His mother Janet had her hair braided just like Emy. And Hadras, pink and fleshy, with his half a head of hair flung to one side too. 
His father stopped suddenly; his voice said he didn’t believe Emy, but now he spoke almost shrilly. “Where do you think you are going, Tomrin?”
It felt strange not wearing his shirt. The scales were gone—it might take a day or so for them to show, but the wound was there, angry and burnt, proof that they had been cut and burned. Tomrin touched the mass of angry flesh around the scab as he thought about his father’s question. “I guess,” he said looking up. “I’m getting ready to leave.” 
His father eyed the staff. It was as close to him as it was to Tomrin. Dable’s eyes snapped to Tomrin. “Leave for where?” His father’s voice sounded strained and Tomrin squinted at him and the others. He could see fear in everyone but Hadras. His brother’s eyes held curiosity, a rare thing in him.
 “You wouldn't try to stop me now, would you?” Tomrin asked his father. The idea sounded ridiculous. 
“Are you going to leave wearing only pants and boots?” Dable asked. He took a step forward; the step put him closer to the staff.
