Tomrin held up his hand. He didn’t want to have to hurt his father. It was the lies of the gods that had made him do what he had done. “You shouldn’t try to stop me,” he said. “Come with me. You all can come. But we must hurry,” he said. He nodded at the animals. “Someone could stay to help Hadras drive them in, but for now, they would slow us down.” 
“Are you going to turn yourself in now?” Emy asked.
“No,” Tomrin said. “No one is turning anyone in. That is all over now,” he added looking not at her, but at his father. He was beginning to worry someone was about to get hurt. His father and mother both could be so stubborn. 
“Is it really?” Dable asked, squinting.
“Yes. I am returning to the glass shop.”
“Don’t be arrogant, Tomrin,” his mother chided. 
Snapping Point [?]
“No! You listen to me!” Tomrin said. “You’ve tried to tell me what to do all my life. I’m sorry but that time is over. I am no longer a child but a man!” 
“Then act like one,” Dable Ocrin said. “Take responsibility for what you are. The gods will punish us—Us!—if you aren’t Purified.” 
“The gods are nothing but toothless birds worried about their feathers. If you are so afraid of them, hide. Stay here in this waste, but for your own good, don’t stand in my way. I have no more patience for it. I am not evil because I am Peregrine, no more so than for the Mark.” 
Dable went for the staff, grabbing it from where Tomrin had leaned it against the tent.
Tomrin moved without thinking. One second he was a step or two away from the staff, the next he wrapped his hands around the staff. 
His father was bigger and stronger, but when his Dable tried to strike him with the shepherd’s staff, Tomrin shifted and slowed the swing and avoided the staff connecting. Dable puffed and sweated while Janet yelled at the both of them to stop. Tomrin dodged the next dampened swing. The third, Tomrin played into the swing dragging his father off balance and into the tent. 
A tent pole snapped under the weight as Dable crashed into it, collapsing half of Tomrin’s tent. Dable landed on his canvas-tarped bed crushing it. Janet rushed to her husband. “I hope you’re happy! she wailed as she helped him to sit up. “You’ve hurt your father!”
Other Points [?]
Tomrin picked up the staff. 
Tomrin didn’t feel happy or sad, but resigned. Talking, trying to reason with his family, was a waste of time and effort. He was getting nowhere with sense. “Stay here then, waste your lives in search, and never leave until you find a Serath’s feathers.”
“Never to leave until we find a feather?”
“Or come with me now,” Tomrin said, holding out his hand to his father.
Janet helped Dable to his feet and they stepped off the fallen tent. “Who are you to dictate to me?” Dable said, balling a fist and shaking it at Tomrin. “I made you!” 
Tomrin worried what he might do as confusion floated across the surface of his own anger. “Who am I?” he shouted as the Luster gripped him. He quoted. “The wind stops, and mountains kneel to what mists conceal. The earth quakes, and hearts break. The light fades, and the dead walk in his shadows made!”
His mother gasped.
The four ambrosia entered Tomrin as he drank from the Wellspring with fire and ice, shadow and will. They burned. He was great, and they were terrible. And it was glorious. With both hands he held the staff high. “I came into this desert to master the staff,” he said. The ambrosia swirled in his veins. “And I have done it!”
Tomrin wove as he channeled. The ice, shadow and will became as nothing. He cast the fire into the staff as he lowered it by the crook with his right hand. The butt of the staff he hammered into his canvas tent. Fire burst from the staff’s crook and the butt. Flames burst everywhere but on the staff's crook where he held it. The fire roared bright and he shouted as he cast the flaming staff into the inferno that he had made. “Who am I?” he screamed. “Who am I? I am the Lightshy Crow!”
His parents and brother and sister scattered running from him, and from the burning tent.
An Unwelcome Inferno [?]
Tomrin shadowed his face with his arm and stepped back from the burning tent as the other ran.
He called for them to stop as the second sight faded and he lost sight of the Wellspring filled with ambrosia overlying the world. Now I really do need their help. To burn his pack and clothes was foolish. He touched his pocket. The billfold was in his other pants. They and it—and all the bankdraws and patents—were burning. Tomrin only thought about the money for a moment. What he needed was a shirt, and food and water. 
Tomrin tossed the staff into the flames “Now will you help me?” he called out to his family who had gathered.
“What do you want?” Dable asked, turning, keeping his distance. The others hurried over to him. 
“I need a shirt,” Tomrin said. “And a water bag and something to eat. It’s going to be a long day for me, walking out of here.” 
“We don’t wear shirts,” his father said stubbornly. 
“I have a shirt,” said Hadras. 
“Thank you, brother,” Tomrin said. “Go get it.” 
Tomrin continued as Hadras ran to his tent. “And a water bag and something to eat.” Dable nodded to Emy and Janet and they hurried away. 
“And that’s it?” Dable said. 
“No,” Tomrin said. He glanced over to the old myrrh tree. It had a gnarled branch he wanted. “Help me make a walking stick?” Tomrin walked to the tree, it was already smelling strong in the morning heat. “This branch,” he said to his father. “It is almost perfect for my design.” 
“And what design is that?” Dable asked when Tomrin directed him to break it such that its double crook, like that of a cane, was preserved. Dable broke the branch, carach, and began straightening the ends with his knife.
Tomrin answered. “I’m not exactly sure, but it seems the first thing I have to do is fight Rennly and save the glass works from him. Then, who can say?” 
Dable tucked away his knife, and held out the walking stick. “My son, the Lightshy Crow.” There was a touch of pride in his voice. 
Hadras hurried back with a tallow nightshirt. Tomrin didn’t raise the hood over his head when he put it on. The water skin from Emy, he slung over his shoulder. Then with his walking stick in one hand and the dried flat cake his mother gave him in the other, Tomrin said his goodbye. 
“You could stay with us,” Emy said. “You could find a feather. The gods would forgive you too.” Her sadness filled him with his own. 
Tomrin glanced away to the horizon at his left. “I’m afraid you will have to find your feathers out here alone. Now that you have all helped me, you can’t come back without one.”
“What about Rennly now?” Hadras asked.
“Still as true as ever.”
“What’s that mean?” Emy asked, but Hadras hushed her as Dabel began to command them all to try to control the fire.
The Bug, the Bull, and the Human Kind [?]
Tomrin headed west to Woolad.
His foot stopped aching, and after he got moving—and the flatbread was eaten—he was able to walk without a limp. He didn’t have time to limp. By the moment, the sun grew higher in the east. I’m coming, Rennly.
The rolling plains of the lower steppes concealed life and death. Bones washed clean by the scavengers slowly sunk into the soil as they cracked. A buzzard hawk, or maybe it was a turkey vulture, circled wobbed overhead and the insects, scarabs with black shells, worked the scattered dung left by nomad animals.
The burning sun arched overhead, Tomrin realized that that wasn’t it at all. There was something deeper here. Something he couldn’t explain, only that every beetle he saw cleaning the desert might have been any other. Only in a different stage of life. Just like glass, as deep as he could see, there was so much more. All he knew was the surface. 
Just like the Wellspring or Pond as he heard Marten call it. His understanding was intuitive, but under the surface what lurked? How did it really work? Tomrin could see he was a novice, Lightshy Crow or not. He needed to know more. 
His pace lagged as he pondered what he saw in the Pond as he neared the Eastern Highway.
Another Light [?]
There was another beacon in the Pond directly in front of him.
Tomrin stopped when he was still several miles from Woolad, a league or better. The steppes gave way to the farms and he could see the mountain peaks in the far distance. The Emerald Mountains beyond Menad were washed out as if painted gray. 
Was the beacon another person? If I can see them, can they see me? The beacon had to be as far away as Woolad. It seemed below ground? A trick of the mind? Or was it that Woolad was lower than the surrounding area? And the beacon?
It took Tomrin a moment to accept the truth. I’m not alone. 
The idea gave him no comfort.
Chapter 24: Ripples on the Pond
	Avarice is a price a wise man never pays.
		—A Orioles proverb

The rising sun glared at Tomrin’s face and eyes.
The farms near the road squatted, short affairs overlooking rolling fields which should have sprouted wheat and barley, or alfalfa or cabbages, or whatever seeds the farmers had planted. The birds were gone with seeds this year. For the farmers, it was a mercy there was no wind, only the stale hot air of summer came too soon.
Tomrin waited until the dusty strip of road wandering through the gently rolling hills cleared of traffic before breaking from the line of shrubs to enter the highway. He wasn’t exactly sure why he needed to avoid being seen—something from a tract maybe—but he kept his eyes on the road, and his mind on the light in the Pond. 
His own movements seemed to stir up vortices in it--maybe? It was hard to tell with the chaos possessing the willvane. Tomrin switched hands with the walking stick and walked the road, heading east toward Woolad and Menad, towards the other beacon. He wished he knew for sure what it meant, what or who it was, but at this distance he couldn’t quite tell if it was moving about or if it was just him and the road. 
At a couple of miles out, he figured he would have to stop for sure to gain reference. As he approached the town, his unease grew steadily as the farms pushed closer together. Sometimes Tomrin could glance down into them and see lone trees and outhouses, other times there was nothing to see but the embankment. Further south and north the farms were larger, but here the fields were not much larger than three or four of the in-town plots. No one waved or called to him, and he simply walked by trying to remain inconspicuous. 
A mile out, he was certain the light in the Pond was coming for him. What are the chances? 
He was the Lightshy Crow, but what exactly did that mean? If the beacon was something like a False Daughter, or even a real one? Tomrin worried a man from the porch to his left might see him, and he quickened his pace. The old quarry road was just ahead. He turned onto it. Don’t run, don’t run. 
The road carved north around town and for stretches Tomrin snuck peeks into Woolad. He hoped he might be able to see whoever was tracking him. He pulled the hood of his brother’s nightshirt over his sweaty head. If I can see them, they can see me.
The First Ripple [?]
Suddenly there was a stirring in the Wellspring.
It was similar to when he had casted the first two times. He stopped his feet and watched. At the distance of a mile Tomrin wasn’t exactly sure what he saw. For a minute the willvane rippled. Then just as suddenly the casting stopped. The light was gone from the pond. Who or whatever simply vanished. 
Tomrin stood for a moment, leaning on his walking stick, stunned. His unease sprouted stalks of doubt. What just happened? What did it mean? Doubt forced him to question his readiness to return to Menad. Things were happening he didn’t understand. I don’t know anything, he realized. Daughters, False or true, were practiced in their arts.
He ran, not bothering to use his walking stick, as images of Master Lazoloth dead flashed in his mind. I can’t kill a woman, he thought as he ran. Can I? The idea seemed beyond him, but he certainly didn’t want to find out how far he would go if a Daughter or censor tried to take him captive. A single night in the old dead well had been bad enough.
At the first of the natural side gully of the washout that nestled Woolad, the old quarry road circumambulated, but Tomrin was able to glance down into town, towards King Street as it joined the Eastern Highway. The people on the streets were small as ants, but where he looked, Tomrin saw a group of them, three of them in black. 
At this distance he struggled to make out races, but suspected they were Orioles.
He pulled his head lower as he stared. The three appeared to be looking directly at him. A woman with long hair pointed at him. The men were perhaps the same Orioles censors from the fair.
A glint of sunlight caught off one of the black clad men’s chest. Definitely censors, Tomrin thought, and the woman was almost certainly a Daughter. If her pointing was any indication, she could track him!
Fire Power [?]
Tomrin’s mind raced as he turned and ran. 
He would have to lay a trap. He needed to head for the steppes. No, for the mountains. He would have to pick them off, kill them one at a time. No--all at once. Even the woman? I don’t want to kill anyone! The woman had gone invisible in the Pond; he needed to learn her secret. How did she do it?
His heart pounded as the heat began boiling him alive. He flung back his hood and ran, his feet pounding road, the dust behind him leaving an obvious trail. She can see me through the hills! It was a terrifying thought. What do I do? He would have to make a stand. He would have to overawe them. Or learn her disappearing trick. Menad might have to wait. The glass shop might have to wait. Feathers! He would return, to make a new glass shop. A better one.
He swore as he ran. “Some Corvine Man I’ve turned out to be! Already backing out of promises to myself.”
Past the old quarry, a small hill, less than a few wagon loads, made of crushed white quartz had been raised at the mouth of the Old Quarry. The next gap he came to, Tomrin looked down into Woolad, but didn’t see the women or the censors. They could be anywhere in town or chasing after him on the Old Quarry Road. I need to get to the trees. He could slow them down, if not hide in them.
He turned off the Old Quarry Road onto a side road and headed east by north. 
Only farms and timberlands lay between him and the Emerald Mountains.
The Trees [?]
Tomrin left the road and ran straight towards the Emerald Mountains.
All hope of reaching Menad in time evaporated as he hustled through a field. The trees he headed for were some of the oldest and easiest to get lost in in the area, The streams inside the trees were always changing. He stomped through fields he’d only once walked by before, years ago. 
The first wooden fence he scaled without problem throwing his walking stick over first and jumping down from the top cross board. He picked the stick up from his crouch and sped on, cutting through the next field at an angle, its furrows at awkward spacing forcing him to hop and jump as he ran, stutter-stepped to the next field line. An Orish slave yelled at him and for the farmer as he ran.
Tomrin didn’t stop and the slave didn’t follow, a dog all bark and no bite. 
I should steal a horse. The censors and Daughters would have horses, and his single myrrh branch left him unprepared to face them if they were trying to rush ahead of him as he turned north. 
He saw the trees as he bounded over the next fence and landed hard on his foot. He was too winded to swear, but picked up his walking stick and looked around.
A Quick Moment [?]
Several women sat on the porch of the farm house. 
Tomrin pulled the hood of his shirt up and over. With his walking stick he hobbled away from them towards the far fence. “Who are you?” one of the women called out to him. “Who are you, handsome stranger?” She repeated when he only waved with his walking stick in hand, keeping his eyes on the fence.
“No one!” Tomrin called out as he limped using the stick. Handsome? Tomrin shook his head. My tail feathers. He hopped the fence and took the side road and headed north, mostly straight at the woods.
“Hey, stop, stranger!” 
It was the woman, sounding breathless. She had run after him! Tomrin pulled his hood further down as he turned. “Are you hurt, why are you running through our field?” the woman demanded. She was a handsome young Rubish woman, kissed by the sun.
She was a touch more assertive than was healthy.
Tomrin held a hand out to stop her. “Stop!” he said loudly. “To follow me is death!” 
“Don’t be silly, Jal. Why are you playing a prank on me, Jal Clar?” The woman said, stepping closer, but more slowly, a curious cat not sure if the bird it stalked was larger in the claws than it.
Tomrin feared the woman would know him if she got too close and that she might follow Jal Clar if he ran. “No, I am not Jal Clar!” he said. “I am a Feathered Man, woman! Stay back!” 
“Jal!” she said “You sound strange.” The woman hadn’t quite stopped. “What’s going on ? What are you saying?”
The woman’s persistence flipped Tomrin’s frustration at this pointless stop to anger. The Luster greeted him like an old friend when he breathed in a heavy breath. He could raise armies of undead minions up to frighten her. Or rain fire from the clear blue sky. Comets! He could show her death and destruction awaited her in this foolishness. The Pond was all around and it was filled to brimming with sweet, sweet ambrosia. He drank in the tiniest of tiny sips. Holding the walking stick at a slant across his body, he casted and wove as one. “Be gone, pest! Do not bother the Lightshy Crow!” 
he wove willvane to vanquish the ice and shadow as fire exited him into the staff and shot out in a flash of light and fire. The woman squawked and the sweet nectar left Tomrin. 
He tamped the burning end of his walking stick into the dirt to snuff it. 
Wide Eyes [?]
The woman’s eyes went wide and for a second Tomrin worried she might die of fright. 
“Go,” he said. “Run. Hide.” The woman turned and with a scream ran back the way she came, away from him and the scent of myrrh burning. 
Tomrin didn’t wait to see if she decided to turn back around but turned and ran himself, too much precious time lost already. He needed to get to the trees. His stick was shrinking and he cursed it and ever having tasted the ambrosia. Time! That was what he needed. I need time! Then he could find the way to go invisible in the Pond. He could evade his pursuers, he just needed time.
Feathered time!
Borrowed Time [?]
The road he was on ended at the woods near a house in a small hollow and a large elm out front.
With no path in the trees Tomrin would make terrible time through them and be caught on the other side trapped if he didn’t take a path when his pursuers did. Which way? he asked himself, but he couldn’t remember if the Calvera place was west or east of the path. He’d only been out here once before—before the old woman Calvera died. Now the house crumbled in on itself. East or West? Tomrin couldn’t decide. He picked up a flat stone. Dirt stuck to one side. “Dirty side go east, clean go west.” He flipped the stone and caught it, dirty side up. “East.”
Up and down the rolling hills of the tree line, Tomrin hurried, using his walking stick like a cane. Each hill he crested brought him greater trepidation. You should have gone west. East had to be the wrong way, but he kept at it, he couldn’t turn back now. 
Relief flooded through him when he saw a path leading up and into the tree line. 
Dust, however, was coming off the road to the south behind the hillock that hid the road from view. Tomrin hurried down the path. When he entered the trees on the path he glanced back.
Up the road, three censors and a woman rode horses at a trot. Tomrin thought he could see the gleam of the woman’s silk dress. Yellow and golden in the sunlight. Tomrin ran to the path and into the woods. Hooves thundered behind him and men shouted like dogs barking on the hunt. 
They are hunting me down like an animal. And they had horses. The shouts and the sounds of houses grew closer as Tomrin pushed into the forest. The path narrowed further. It was a footpath and might stall them some. Tomrin heard a man with a sharp Orioles accent call out. “Dismount!” 
Another voice called out. The pitch was high and that of a woman. “He went in. Be wary.”
“Wary?” a man asked. “Not a chance!”
“Don’t be a fool! He can dance!”
Yes, he can, Tomrin thought as the Luster called to him. He would not be hunted like a wild animal for sport, not for another man’s glory. And not when the sun and heat had turned the world around him to tinder. The leaves of the trees all but begged for fire. All but begged for a match. And was he not the Corvine Man? A Feathered Man In Black? He pulled in the ambrosia. 
Behind him the woman yelled shrilly to her men. “Watch out! He’s channeling!”
And so he was.
To Be [?]
The Corvine Man encased shadow and ice in a net of shrinking willvane. 
He tossed his walking stick from hand to hand as he switched which side of the path he burned, ambrosia from the pond setting fire to the trees as they stretched out skeletal fingers into the path. Choosing the most curved branches from either side of the path, he lit two columns of flame trailing behind him. Soon branches exploded with fire as sap boiled.
He drank more ambrosia, channeling from the pond the fire, ice and the shadow and will. Then he closed the channel before things got out of hand. He only wanted to light a few trees on fire. Now great shards of ice slicked through him. Death, his and everyone’s, seemed a promise from the shadows. And the willvane was bent on wrestling him to the forest floor. His feet stopped as he wove. The power of the shadow and ice seemed too much but he wove, and slowly the three dissipated.
Was and Am [?]
Tomrin turned around as fire burned inside him. 
He held the walking stick pointed between columns of fame on either side as the trees burned. The men had stopped, afraid of the inferno that separated them from him. The heat was intense, it shined right through his hood. “Stay back,” he said to the men. “I don’t want to have to kill you!” He shouted over the sound of the trees spreading their flames and sap pockets bursting.
The woman pushed forward. When she saw his walking stick pointed at her she hid behind the front man. “No one defies the gods!” she said as she pushed her head up behind a censor’s shoulder.
The fire scorched inside him, and Tomrin reached out with his free hand and grabbed hold of a drooping branch that curved back in towards the trunk of its tree. He pushed the remaining inside himself fire into it. The flaky bark caught fire as raw flame spilled into the tree. He removed his hand and stepped backswards as he held the fire inside the tree, a poor vessel for ambrosia, but a vessel nonetheless. As he spoke he continued to back away. “One does defy the gods!” he said. “The Lightshy Crow does!” 
He wasn’t sure they heard him because he lost control of the fire in the tree, and the it exploded, erupting with fire.
KARCHAK! 
Amongst the Wicked [?]
Tomrin turned and ran swatting at the burning splinters that landed on his arms. It was a marvel he was still alive. He’d gone beyond playing with fire which he feared to playing something he only slightly understood which was clearly way more dangerous.
He was more bothered by that and the fact that he was certain the whole stand, east to west, would burn because of him. He didn’t want to be this destroyer. He wanted to tear down the wicked, but wasn’t he amongst the wicked now? 
As he ran headlong through the wind break and out the other side he pondered what it meant for his humanity. He worried he was falling down to the level of that of a naked beast. He turned to look at the Emerald Mountain as Menad farmland stretched before him. To his left, somewhere was Menad, nestled between the rolling hills. The city was rich partly due to the burning woods at his back. 
Tomrin glanced back only once to see the black and gray of the smoke streaming toward the heavens. He’d bought himself time, but he wasn’t convinced it would be enough. “No,” he scolded himself. “You can’t give up.” He would escape and make it in time to fight Rennly too. “Somehow.” Some-feathered-how! 
He started by putting one foot in front of the other.
Horses [?]
Several horses pastured in the distance.
Tomrin turned towards them. Speed was what he needed. He drank from his waterskin, without slowing. He had fallen to too slow a pace already.  He needed to find water too. And horses meant water too. 
No money and no bank notes--not that he could use them here. He would have to steal one of the horses and ride it bareback. Tomrin wondered if this was what he had become as well: a thief, stealing and taking. I only want to borrow a horse, he reassured himself. And he didn’t even want to do that. He convinced himself as he neared the fence that penned in the horses. A horse would do no good in the rocky crags of the Emerald Mountain, and it wasn’t thieving if the animal would find its way back home. 
Tomrin climbed the fence after slipping his walking stick under the cross boards, hewn from a wavy aspen, white from age as if they had been painted. He half expected some decrepit old farmer to come hobbling out the farmhouse. It also looked like it had just barely survived the Cracking of the World Egg. 
Tomrin crouched as he walked over to the nearest horse, just as he had done as he approached, hiding from the windows of the farm house and from anyone peering out of them. The horse was a bay and she looked young, maybe a few years old, but seemed broken to humans. She didn’t shy away when he reached up a hand as if to feed her. The filly was not impressed and snorted at his hands lack of treats and pulled back her head. 
“Whatever girl,” Tomrin said and moved away. A filly was too flighty for him anyway. What he needed was a nag. Something that could still move but wouldn’t think to test his abilities as a horseman. Keeping low, Tomrin scrambled over the dusty pasture ground to the trough with water in it. Green moss grew on the inside and out. The horse drinking the stagnant water sported a smoky white coat, a gelding of some age. The old boy looked at Tomrin with dull eyes and went back to drinking. “There’s a good boy,” Tomrin said as he patted the pale horse’s front flank. 
Tomrin directed the horse with his walking stick over to the gate. He popped the latch and pushed open the wooden gate. It creaked on its hinges. He had no choice but to brazen it out. Throwing a glance at the farm house, he led the horse outside into the pasture and closed the gate. 
Reaching over the gelding’s back with his walking stick in one hand Tomrin swung up and onto the horse. Tough old bones poked into his back side.
“This is going to be a long ride.”
On a Pale Horse [?]
Adjusting his grip on the horse’s coarse main hair, he gently nudged the beast in the flank with his boots. The old thing turned back and looked at him. “Go,” Tomrin said, kicking the horse harder. The horse made a surly noise and began to plod down and away from his paddock. With several more boots to the ribs, Tomrin convinced the gelding to trot. By guiding the horse with his walking stick, Tomrin finally pointed the tired old thing in the correct direction.
Tomrin threw back the hood of his shirt. The wind stirred up by the horse felt cool. Fathoms disappeared behind him. He was going to make it. He only had to set fire to the windbreak, scare the daylights out of a woman, and steal a horse. But he was going to make it. 
After a few minutes he stole a glance back. A streamer of dust rose up into the clear sky. How could they have made it here already? He kicked his horse as hard as he could. “Go!” he shouted at the pale hack under him. But it was no use, the horse was old and already tired and refused to go any faster. You should have stolen the bay. She had spirit. Probably break my neck on it. But he bet it knew how to gallop. 
The steamer of dust behind him grew closer and closer. Finally, Tomrin pulled on the gelding’s mane. “Woah! Woah! Stop!” he called. There was no point running the poor thing to death. It wouldn’t help him a bit. His pale horse came to a full stop. 
Tomrin slid off the horse’s back and stretched his legs. He was going to be bruised on his back side for days. If he lived that long. He waited with his walking stick in hand and the horse at his back.
The brown cloud topped a rise and two riders drove their mounts straight at him like they wanted to break their necks or their horses. One was larger than the other, a man and his dun coloured horse stood nearly as tall as the other—and the rider too.
Her horse—for sure it was a her—was darker. The two were the lady Margretta and her counselor Marten on the dun. It had to be. The woman’s hair whipped back and forth in ruby currents. Tomrin knew how she had found him. The pond roiled around him, but just like the Daughter which had disappeared, Lady Margretta stirred the Pond not even a ripple. 
Tomrin lowered his walking stick as the two pulled their horses to a stop. The dun looked tired. The old boy was covered in sweat, but the bay seemed hardy and hale. “Hello, Master Tomrin.” Marten called out as he dismounted.
“Good day!” Tomrin replied
“Nice to see you are still alive and not killed on this fine spring day,” Marten said, eying him.
“Yes,” Tomrin replied jovially. “It is a fine day for a nice ride in the country. And a really, really great day to be alive.” 
The man laughed, and Tomrin found himself laughing too.
Chapter 25: The Walking Stick
	So and if you don’t want flies, don’t kill the spiders.
		—From the Living Book of the Dead

Margretta slid off her horse, her blue riding dress split to her hip. Her long shapely legs, and blackbooted feet, reached the ground. The bay’s reigns she let drop. She wasn’t on the ground a second and she was pulling off her leather riding gloves. Those at least she had rolled down. She flung back her hair and handed Marten her gloves. Her piercing blue eyes glinted flinty glass as she stared at Tomrin like a cat wary of its prey. “You look like an eagle looking at a lynx,” Tomrin said, switching the two in his mind in the hopes of keeping her off balance. The way she was staring, he wondered if he was going to have to use his walking stick after all. 
“Tomrin Ocrin,” she said. “You I see you didn’t receive my warning. I knew I should have delivered it myself.” 
“I received it,” Tomrin said, glancing at Marten.The angry woman’s ire seemed misdirected, But the Orish man at her side stood impassive as if this were a court and it wasn’t yet his turn to speak. The man also did not wear his vest, but a leather jerkin, dark pants and boots. It was not a uniform Tomrin had ever seen, but he knew he was seeing Counselor Marten, the pleasant and able lawyer, as the soldier. At his hip a dagger, a large knife or sword hung sheathed in a triangular black leather. It looked like a sacrificial knife, but it is were one it was the largest Tomrin had ever seen. The hilt sported a green gem and looked like an emerald gazing out from an ivory orb. 
“You received it?” Margretta said. “You might have heard it, but you obviously didn’t receive it.” Her voice was not shrill, but there was a harsh edge to it and an inhuman indifference to her ire, almost feral. “Now, I must deal with the consequences.” She stepped towards him.
Tomrin took a step back. The front quarter of his stolen horse stopped him. The horse made a questioning sound and Tomrin raised his stick slightly into the air as if to shade his eyes. “What consequences are those?” 
The woman suddenly looked every bit a lady, a warrior princess, and a Daughter—albeit False. She glared not at the walking stick with an irritation evident. “Well for starters, you need to be warded,” she said. “I’m not sure you realize but every Daughter from here to Menad can see you channeling. Can’t you see it?” 
“I see the churning.”
“Then you know you must be warded, and now! Already Daughters of Heaven’s Glory are looking for you.” 
Tomrin spared a blink towards the west. What looked like a cloud had gathered. It was smoke from the burning break. “One Daughter and three censors,” he said and turned back to Margretta. “And this ward will make me disappear?” 
“Yes, it will,” she said and stepped to within striking distance of his walking stick. “I must cast a web on you.” 
Tomrin held up his hands, and held her off with it. “Teach it to me.” he said, “This web of disappearance” 
“That’s not what it’s called- “ 
“What is it called?” 
Margretta sighed and Tomrin could feel she was going to be a difficult teacher. “It doesn’t matter what its called. We haven’t the time.” 
“Why not?” Tomrin asked. If he disappeared, he could still make it to Menad in time to fight Rennly and save the glass shop. “How long will it take?” 
“Days, weeks, maybe months,” Margretta said. “Maybe you will be a quick study with the willvane as you are with sunfire, but if I guess right that stick is a crutch and in more ways than one.” 
“I am quick,” Tomrin said, resting the end of his walking stick in the dirt. 
“Regardless, the first step is for me to ward you,” said Margretta. “Or do you want to stand here until your new friends arrive?”
“And if I refuse?” Tomrin asked. “Then what?”
The Darklit Pond [?]
Margretta scowled at him. “Once already you have refused my offer of help,” she said. “I don’t know who you think you are, Master Ocrin, thinking you can ignore it now. Gaze into the Darklit Pond. Where can you go that Daughter can’t find you? Already your stunts will bring down Heaven’s fury. The sky will fall on you in a storm of feathers. Meteors of sunfire will burn you to your skeleton, and comets of glacier ice will crush and scatter charred and blackened bones—that is Master Ocrin—if Heaven were that forgiving! No, they would kill you and interrogate your corpse. But don’t think that could ever be your fate. Not now. Not now...that you and I have spoken for freely.” 
Marten stepped forward; his hand was on the hilt at his side. “I’m sorry, Tomrin,” he said. “I tried to warn you, not to give into the Luster. You understand. Secrets must be preserved. Lives are at stakes; the balance of them is immeasurable.” 
“Lives?” Tomrin said. “All she cares about is her own. She didn’t care about Master Laz’s.” 
“What is one life versus thousands?” Margretta asked. 
“I refuse to think like that. Master Laz’s life meant something.” 
“He never gave into the Luster, not even at the end, but you have. In this war with Heaven there can be no rogues, no disinterested parties,” Margretta said. “You are with us or against us… And since you already said you want to live.” She cocked her head and raised her eyebrows at him like he was a nughty boy.
Tomrin raised his myrrh walking stick and scratched the itch on his chest. “Maybe it is you should bow to me,” he said.
Margretta reared back, looking down her skeptical nose at him and laughed. “Ha! Where in the feathers did you get that crazy idea?” she asked, glancing at Marten. 
The lage man blinked blinked. “He thinks he is the Lightshy Crow?” 
Margretta’s icy eyes turned back to Tomrin. “You the Lightshy Crow? Please, Master Ocrin.You start a fire or two and suddenly you think you are living out some legend? Look around you! Do you see palaces and parades? What glory do you have here? Your kingdom of desert and dirt? Do you want me to bow down to you, Master Ocrin? Or will a curtsy do?” The woman curtsied and swore. “Black feathered crows preserve me.” 
“I just...I thought…,“ Tomrin said and lowered his walking stick. 
Willvane [?]
Margretta didn’t have to continue, but she did. “You just thought what? That because you are slightly more precocious than most men, you have to be the Lightshy Crow?” 
“No, because I’m the only one who has drank the ambrosia.” Tomrin said. 
“Really? Is that what you think? You are the only fool man who can dance with a drop of sunfire rushing through his veins?”
“Aren’t I?” 
Margetta scoffed. “No. You are hardly the only one, but you are the most foolish I know who and even they aren’t feathered fool enough to think think I’m bowing to them. You drinking the ambrosia without training, and without wards? What were you thinking? Nothing comes easy. You should know that.”
“We need to hurry,” Marten said. “The recriminations can wait until after we are safely out of this field. Unless we want a battle and this is not good ground.” 
“You’re right, Marten,” Margretta said. “Enough of this foolishness. Now let me ward you.” She stepped forward and touched Tomrin on his shoulder where his neck poked from his brother’s night shirt. “Don’t do anything, don’t talk, don’t distract me. Just hold still.” 
Tomrin found the nearness of her in itself distracting. Her touch was cold and her fingers were clammy from the heat. But he didn’t shy away. What choice did he have? He needed to disappear from those who could see him in the ambrosia. He bet he could learn how to cast the ward faster than Margretta said it took to learn. He wasn’t just slightly more precocious than most men.
The ambrosia around Margretta didn’t seem to stir anymore than the storm already churned, but Tomrin could see tendrils of willvane enter her. These fibers were different, just like the ones that came into him were different when he drank the four ambrosia. But for her, it was only willvane that entered. The fire and ice, and the shadow stayed out. That was a trick he still needed to learn. “Only Willvane?” he said. “How do you do it?” 
“What?” she asked, distracted. “I can only do one at time, just as you can only do all four. Now, hush.”
A Warding [?]
The tendrils from her snaked into Tormin. 
They entered him from her fingertips. Then slowly like a growing cocoon, the tendrils filled him up. It felt like nothing, but Margretta seemed to wrestle with the fibers, moving her body as she struggled to weave the ward. Sweat beaded on her forehead and she blinked drops from her eyes when they ran. Tomrin tried to follow the twists and turns but they were like nothing he had done to control the ice and shadow. His net had been as simple as a spider’s web. Margretta’s ward was an incongruous network of contradictions. It looked like at any moment it would come flying apart, unraveling like a basket full of yarn tossed over a barn.
Then suddenly she stopped pulling in the willvane. She continued casting, continued doing her spelldance. The tendrils of willvane she tried off and finally the last of the ambrosia slipped from her into Tomrin. Then the ambrosia around him shifted. The storm around him subsided. 
Margretta stepped back wiping her hands on her dress before she wiped her forehead of sweat with the back of her forearm. The look she gave Tormin was curious.
“Was that it?” Tomrin asked. “It worked right?” 
“Yes, it worked. It’s just—” 
“What?” Tormin asked. 
“That was harder than it should have been.” 
“What does that mean?” 
“It means,” Marten said. “That we should leave now; the ward is stable—isn’t it? 
“Yes, yes,” Margretta said, shaking herself and blinking. “Marten, take us out of here.” 
“Mount up. We have some riding ahead of us,” the Orish men said. 
“Where are we going?” Tormin asked. 
“Where we have to. To start with the way you were going—” Marten pointed. “—that is until I can find a spot to double back. We might be invisible in the Wellspring, but horses leave tracks, ones still visible in this world.” 
“As long as we make it back to Menad in time to fight Rennly. Today is the last day to save Godly Glassworks.” 
“You are being chased by censors,” Margreta said sounding exasperated. “And how many Daughters? You don’t even know? And you are worried about that glass shop?” 
“Margretta please. You too Master Tomrin. We cannot stand out here wasting time. All I can do is promise I will try, but the safety is my top priority. The more time we waste, the worse the chances any of us will return to Menad intact.”
“You have obligations,” Tomrin said. “I get that. I too have obligations.”
“And we respect that, Master Tomrin. We all have obligations. Mine is to see you two safe out of here… If i can.” 
“Let’s go,” Margretta said. She turned and headed for her bay, snagging her riding gloves from Marten on the way. 
Tomrin turned to his horse. “Sorry old boy, but we ride again.” 
“You really know how to pick a horse,” Marten said, laughing as he mounted his charger.
“I know,” said Tomrin. “Fastest mount this side of Corundum,” Tormin teased. “I don’t even know his name, but I think I’m going to call him Lightning.” 
Marten chuckled. “Let’s hope he still had a little thunder left in him.”
The man led the way, straight towards the nearest peak of the Emerald Mountain. They soon left the current field and came to another, then to a road after the third wide open field. Marten took the road north. At a side road they went east and then doubled back making a circuit but continuing on west until they hit trees. 
Finally after making another circuit and a double back, Marten announced they were heading back to Menad. A road leading south, the old south road, entered Menad from the north, but when they neared Menad, they made another turn, this time back to the east and then Tomrin realized where they were. They were just north of the Eastern Highway. 
Marten called a stop. “We’ll walk from here.” 
Reentry [?]
“I need to get rid of Lightning?” 
“Yes. Can’t really expect to enter Menad on a stolen horse.” 
Tomrin poured out a taster of water in his hand for Lightning. It was a lonely stretch of land ahead and with no water. Then slapping him, chased him off. “Find your way home!”
With the sun in their eyes they walked the Highway into Menad. 
Marten chatted easily and Tomrin learned the name of both his and Margretta’s horses—King’s Gamble and Queen’s Answer. “It’s good to be home,” Tomrin said when he finally glimpsed the Winged Helm Inn.
“Don’t get too comfortable,” Margretta said. “This may have been your home, but not any more.” 
“Why not? You still expect me to leave?” 
“Master Tomrin, I swear. You think everything can just go back to the way it was?” Margretta was shaking her head at him. 
“Why can’t it?” 
“Probably shouldn’t be discussing this out here,” Marten said.
Whatever Margretta wants with me she can have in Menad. The idea of the woman holding something over him rankled and the implied threat made in the field hung between as they walked. 
Home [?]
“We can go faster than this,’’ he said as they neared the Winged Helm Inn. He wanted to go inside and drink tarsk until he couldn’t remember his own name, but even that he could not do.
“I’ll take the horses,” Marten said. “And meet you inside.” 
“This is where you are staying?” Tomrin asked Margretta. 
“Yes. Why?” 
“Let’s just go to your room—and talk,” Tormin said. He was tired of walking, tired of running and he might be able to snag that shell of tarsk if luck turned eyes his way.
“Will you be coming?” Margretta asked Marten.
“I will see you safe to--to where you are going.”
“And where is that?” Tomrin asked. It felt like they had everything planned without him. 
“Inside,” Margretta replied. “I will see to the Queeny myself,” she said to Marten. To Tomrin she said. “Room 204. Go there after me.” 
At the door, Tormin nodded to the door man, but had forgotten his name. Without Robles the man was simple, efficient, and Tomrin hurried inside. The censors had moved on, as had the slavers. Soon the evening crowd would be heading in. When the man behind the bar asked him what he wanted to drink, Tomrin said, “High Spirit tarsk, wait.” He remembered he had no money. “Never mind. I forgot my money in my other pants.” The lie came to his lips. “I’m waiting for a friend.” 
“How about a low spirit? On the Helm,” the man said. “You look like you were chased by the Lighshy Crow himself. Waiting for Robles to return?” 
Tomrin placed his elbow on the bar and ran his hands through his hair. “Thank you; I could. Ah, Yes.” 
The man filled a mug and Tomrin drank it. Robles was back in town, and he wondered what would happen if he were to come through the door. You won’t believe it. I was the Lightning Crow. Then I wasn’t. Now I’m waiting for a False Daughter to take me away, or she’ll kill me.
Robles wouldn’t believe a word of it. 
The evening crowd began filtering in. Tomrin watched for people he knew, like Non and Robles, but it was Margretta, followed by Marten who entered finally. 
Tomrin watched them enter the back hallway and emptied his mug. 
A Word [?]
He stood up and walked around the dias to the hallway. 
The stairs climbed to the top hallway built into the hillside. The free tarsk had twisted in Tomrin’s stomach all the way up the stairs and down the hall. Here the gloom of the lower wall replaced any glimpse of golden sun peaking through skylights. Tormin knocked on the door of 204, nevermind the tap of his walking stick leaving a trail of wump on the floor.
Marten opened the door and Tomrin entered. The man smiled and Tomrin was surprised he was so at ease. The Orish counselor didn’t even glance at the stick. His own weapon seemingly forgotten at his hip. 
Margretta sat at the table, the room’s only furniture save for the bed and dresser and the empty chair opposite her. She closed a small black book the size of a billfold, then set it aside. “Sit,” she told him, the way a queen might command her court. 
Tomrin hesitated. Sitting would disarm him. “Regardless of the price, I am going to be leaving here in a few minutes and heading for the temple,” he said.
“Tomrin, sit.”
Tomrin walked over to the chair, leather padded, with a lat at the top back. He laid his walking stick along it. “I prefer to stand,” he said as he laid his hands on the gnarled stick. 
“No, you intend to be difficult,” Margretta said as Marten rummaged, filling up packs in a hurried way. Margretta glanced at the strange man. Big but agile, smart but deadly, he seemed a strange mix of contradictions, and he was on a mission to see Margretta out the door. 
“Not difficult,” Tomrin said. “But precise as any lawyer. I do not want to leave Menad.” She made to speak, but he plowed over her. “I need to go to the Temple before dark and meet my obligation as a Founder of Godly Glassworks.”
“You think life can continue as it was?” Margretta said, standing. 
Marten zipped a large bag. “I told you he might be difficult about this point,” the big man said without looking up.
Margetta sniffed at him and continued. “We have to leave to continue your training.” 
“If you need to leave then leave,” Tomrin said. “But I want to stay. I will not let you push me around. I was already pushed into something this week I regret. I can’t be helped now, Marten knows, he was there.” 
“Yes? Marten, what is this?” 
Marten looked up from a pack he was stuffing with clothes. “Boys, they like to gamble. It’s in our blood. Can’t you see the boy is gambling now? He’s betting you aren’t going to kill him right here and now. I told you he would be difficult with this glass stuff in some way.”
Margretta sighed. “And no little help from you.” She walked around the table and put a hand on Tomrin’s walking stick. She was so close to Tormin, it made him feel awkward just standing there. “So you want to stay. But the thing is I have to go. I can’t be tied down here.” 
“Then go.” 
“But I can’t leave you here, unattended.” 
“Why not?” Tomrin asked. With her hand on the stick he knew he could not use it fast enough. 
“You need to be warded.” 
“But you warded me.” 
“Yes, and every few days or so you need to be warded again,” Margretta asked. “Did you think it lasted forever?” 
“Well— I—“ 
Margretta took her hand from the stick, and walked back around the table. “Now, sit.” She sat herself. 
Tomrin didn’t move.
By Choice [?]
He considered if she was lying. But Tomrin didn’t know. If he sat his power was diminished. No, maybe it wasn’t. His mind churned as he picked up the stick with one hand. Use it? Don’t? He pulled out the chair. “I will hear you out, then you will hear me out.” Tomrin sat down.
“There are rules.” Margretta began, Marten two carried packs out the door and shut it.   “Rules!” Tomrin demanded, “There is no time for all that. Listen to me, Lady Margretta, I agree I need to go with you, to learn to ward myself. But first...I am going to stand up and walk out of here. I am going to go down to the temple one last time. My brother and I have business we  need to complete.” 
“What business could be so feathered important?” Margretta said, slapping the table. 
“I am a founder of Godly Glassworks,” Tomrin said. “You think I want to go and fight my half-brother? No. I have been avoiding fighting him since I was a child. But he’s found a way to force me to fight him now again. Well, I’m not going to disappoint him.”
“I don’t understand?” Margretta said.
“I have to beat him at staves to complete the terms of our contract. Marten didn’t tell you?” Tomrin added as he stood and began walking to the door.
“It seemed unimportant at the time.” Margretta said. “Same as now.”
“Not to me.”
“Is this money?” Margretta demanded. “You are worried about gold?”
“Gold? This is about lives and livelihoods. I have to save the Glassworks, but not just for myself anymore, but for everyone who depends on it. Rennly is trying to stop me, sure, maybe, but I will stop him first.”
Margretta called after him. “Then what, Tomrin Ocrin?”
Tomrin glanced back at her. “Then I will do what it takes,” he said. “As I always have.” He turned and popped the latch and left the room leaving the tall handsome woman sitting at the table. 
“We leave tonight!” she called out after him.
Tomrin’s walking stick held her answer. Wump, wump.
Chapter 26: The Temple
	And he will burn their temples
Until the ash itself will not mold.
		—From The Dark Verses

Tomrin carried the walking stick up Arcade Avenue at a good clip. One last thing remained before he headed to the Temple to face Rennly: he needed to talk to Robles. The hour was late, but he might catch him still at work—though now it seemed unlikely his friend would still be there. But with the company newly reformed there was no doubt much for his friend to do as Pack Leader. Robles’ll be there, Tomrin told himself as he marched along. He was cutting it close now, himself, with maybe an hour left before the contract’s sunset clause was invoked.
As the silver smiths Tomrin could see the interior of Godly Glassworks stood dark. The glass Seasargus in the window had never flown darker, and the gray tree on the door never blacker. Tomrin hurried up the walkway. The door was locked and he shuffled around the rocks before finally heading back through the dusty alleyway for the back door. It too was locked, but someone had not closed it properly as happened from time to time. Popping the handle, he opened the door to the back room. 
Inside the heat of the kilns filled him with a delight as he passed them on his way to the front. Things were working out at the shop. Though the shop wasn’t being minded as closely as it needed to be: the mid door too was open.
Up front. Tomrin didn’t stop by his old workstation but headed straight to Master Laz’s office. He needed paper and a pen to write with—a note for Robles. Paper he found on the desk, but the inkwell was dry. A graphite rod used for making glass stamps, however, worked just as well. He pulled one from Master Laz’s middle desk drawer and began to write. 
Robles,
I am leaving Menad and the glassworks. I know I will lose my share but I am fine with that. I wanted to see you in person, but you weren’t here. I am going to the Temple to fight Rennly as per our contract. Don’t worry, I will beat him.
Your friend, Tomrin.
P.S. The back door was locked, but not properly latched.
He folded the paper and put a To the Pack Leader on it. Setting down the graphite rod, he stood up and left the office. Back outside the glasswork he closed the door. Lifting the handle the latch clicked. Snick.
The sun had climbed perilously low on the horizon now and Tomrin hurried back to the Winged Helm Inn, and turned down Third Street. The setting sun filled the west with glare against the cart traffic. Tomrin found himself too tired to tell himself he didn’t look out of place wearing his brother’s nightshirt, and hoped people stared at him as blankly as he stared into the glare. 
Finally downtown the shadow of the temple’s spire stretched out over the houses and the road to the east. The tower’s wooden heights scraped the sky as Tomrin passed the courthouse and circled up the stoneway outside the temple. In the shadow of the spire, his walking stick clunked against the pavers. He glanced up at the high balconies where a Serath might land, and felt he understood them better now.
Up there they were safer, far away from the likes of him.
Temple [?]
The wooden doors were carved from oak and locked from the inside. 
Tomrin frowned at the main doors. Curious. The temple doors were never locked save for on special occasions—or when a new Daughter had been found. He wondered if they had been closed because of him. But I’ve not been caught. A new Daughter rarely appeared. Not as rare as a male, but rare. Not that anyone liked to admit that one of their own required purification.
Tomrin pushed through the dead shrubbery and headed around to side doors cantors, censors, and Daughters used. He tried the set. They were also locked. He knocked with his walking stick. Cong, cong. Solid echoes from the inside.
“Feathers,” he swore under his breath and continued on to the next set. 
On the west side the backdoor was locked like the others. Cong! Cong! Cong! No one came running. He thought about going to the south door but continued to smack the door with his walking stick. Konk, konk, konk. Konk, konk, konk. 
“What’s going on down there?” an angry voice called out from above.
It sounded like Rennly.
What It Sounds Like [?]
Tomrin backed away from the door and looked up. His step-brother bent out a window looking down at him. The low sun struck his forehead and threatened his eyes. 
Tomrin raised his voice. “It’s time for our bout,” he said.
“No,” Renny called down. “You are too late.” 
“The sun still shines. That’s what the contract says.” Tomrin looked around for anyone to prove him wrong, to say this once was a lie too and not finally the truth.
“That’s not what I mean. I can’t now. Look,” Rennly said. “Come back next week. You had to show up earlier.” 
“Next week? I pick the night!” Tomrin said. “Me.” 
“But, I can’t now. I’m the only one here,” Rennly said, he said something else, but much more quietly. It sounded like he was saying something like: the vault was unguarded. 
“Then you forfeit,” Tomrin said. “The letter of our contract is fulfilled. Ah ha! I have bested you and you know it.”
“Not fulfilled!” Rennly snarled, slapping at something behind him. “It is not fulfilled,” he added, turning back to Tomrin. “Not without a fight.”
“Right, and I win right now,” Tomrin added. “If you don’t face me. Check the words in the contract. The bout happens and has a winner. Look around you. If I walk away and say I beat you, no one will question it. But everyone knows that you fight how you do and how you would have to punch yourself several times repeatedly in different awkward spots. No one would believe it. But you say come back tomorrow and what are we? You think we are brothers in the eyes of the contract? No. You forfeit and I will claim I beat you because after training I was so much better than you that I was able to best you without even wounding a single hair on your head, only your feathered pride. That is the intent, Rennly!”
“Not the intent,” Rennly whined. “The intent was to have our little bouts.” 
“Why do you think I changed the words at the last second? It’s so I control the when? I am the master of the when! Fight me now or you lose. That is the intent!”
Pluralism [?]
Tomrin lied most of his words, but it was luck now finally playing to his favor.
Fe savored the look on Rennly’s face. Finally his half-brother was getting a little of his own back. “Stay there,” Rennly shouted. “There is no forfeit!” His head retreated back through the window, and the glass and curtains closed.
The dried, tawny colored lawn Tomrin stood on would serve well for their ring. It seemed—for all their powers—the god still refused to control the weather, or the lives of men, despite how they tried. Lies and bonding, gold and greed. Fear and doubt. Time and space. Who can say what is real?
As he waited he felt nothing but the bump of the dirt as he scratched out a ring. No anticipation, no rush, just the same washed out emptiness as when he’d exited the well earlier that morning. When he finished, he stood in the circle, his back to the sun, and his body casting a naked shadow on the temple’s door.
Slowly, Tomrin’s thoughts became as many as the sun’s rays.
Trust or Not [?]
The door opened and Rennly appeared.
He did not have his staff. “Get in here,” he said, impatient, maybe even a little afraid, his eyes darting.
“I made a ring out here,” Tomrin said. “Did you bring your staff?”
 “I’m not supposed to leave her.” 
“Her?” Tomrin said.  He did not want to go inside. He had not stepped inside the Menad temple in years, and didn’t like the idea of being trapped inside now with Rennly.
“We found her today. And this is terrible timing,” Rennly said. “Now get inside. I can’t leave her for a minute.”
Rennly held the door open for him and Tomrin stepped from the ring and five steps to the temple door. “I see no reason we can’t fight inside,” he said as he stepped through the doorway. He just hoped he judged Rennly correctly and that he wasn’t a rabid animal that would end up having to be put down. 
His brother closed the door and it took a second for Tomrin’s eyes to adjust to the dim lamplight. “You thought my baton, against you with that stick?” Rennly said. “And you aren’t even going to probe if I’m trying to kill you?”
“What about the stick?” Tomrin replied. “And are you aren’t going to kill me?”
“Nothing about the stick,” Rennly said and locked the door. “But you won’t believe the day I’m having—because of you. So it would make sense if I was.”
“Because of me?” Tomrin said. “Nice that you feel the need to don’t mince words.”
“Me? You! It’s because you and your late tail feathers. You had to wait until the last feathered minute. Didn’t you?”
“I set the time,” Tomrin reminded him. 
Rennly only scoffed. “This way.” 
Ascent [?]
Rennly led Tomrin through a corridor in the back of the temple where the walls hung with tapestries and held alcoves with statues of different gods painted and lacquered and lamps made of beaten gold. The hallway was tall and wide enough for a Serath to walk. “What exactly did you do today, that you finaly come here with a dirty shirt and a cripples’ stick?” Rennly said.
“Umm,” Tomrin stuttered. “I’ve been busy.”
“You think you’ve been busy? We found this new Daughter, but not only that. A man, think of it! Apparently he’s burning up the countryside. Someone else is going to get all the glory of catching him I because of you.  Can you believe it? It’s like he thinks he’s the Lightshy Crow or something.” Rennly brought stopped at a door, then turning a key stopped into a rampway which led up as it wound around the temple. “And because of you, I stayed back when everyone else left,” he said when they reached a new doors but kept walking.
“I thought you were afraid of the Corvine Man,” Tomrin said. 
Rennly spoke as he tapped each door. “This man is hardly the Lightshy Crow,” he said. “The Lightshy Crow is a fairy-tale your mother told to scare us.” 
“What about the crazy woman in the mountains?” Tomrin asked. 
“That was just a story I told to scare you,” Rennly said, pointedly. “You should have seen your face. I thought you were going to swallow your tongue.” 
His brother chatted easily, and when he was like this Tomrin could imagine enjoying being around him, but Tomrin feared it would not last. The dark steak and no answer to his question of wanting to kill him left a wedge of suspicion in his mind.
“You know we don’t have to fight,” Tomrin said after a moment of quiet, only broken by the cluck, cluck of his walking stick striking the wooden ramp. 
“Turning coward now?” Rennly taunted. 
“It’s not that,” Tomrin said. “We both want the glass shop to succeed don’t we.”
“You think I am going to accept a draw?”
Tomrin grew frustrated. Rennly was being intentionally difficult. “You think beating me will change anything?” 
“Yes. It will make me feel better,” Rennly said at the smallest door they’d come to. “Stop here.”
Onwards [?]
The higher they climbed the smaller the doors and rooms became. 
Rennly turned the handle. “Wait for me,” he said and bowed to enter. “I need to check on her.” 
The door shut, but not before a slurred voice, that of a girl’s, commanded drunkenly: “Get out!” So lucky as to be cursed with the gift, whoever this woman was, she clearly didn’t find Rennly charming.
After a minute, the door opened and Rennly slipped out, straightening his hair. 
His face grew calm, and a smile formed on it, almost as if suddenly painted on, a mask covering up an inner rage. Rennly’s eyes, however, never changed. “Let’s get on with this,” he said.
“Who was that?” Tomrin asked, no longer just worried for his own life. Now when the time came he tried to think about the location of this room in the spiral they were taking to the top.
“Don’t concern yourself with her,” Rennly said. “She’s still impure, but the gods will see to cleansing her spirit of its infernal taint. Now I think we have a contract to fulfill.” A line of blood on Rennly’s neck formed a rill where he’d been scratched. “Come,” he added harshly.
The ramp turned and came to a flat area, outside, but was decorated with heavy fabric. This was the balcony level. Shooting up through the decking, pillars of wood held up the roof. The whole level was made of carved wood. The pillars, the walls, the ceiling, even the ladder leading to the roof had been worked by a carpenter’s chisel. Tomrin thought he recognized the work. This was perhaps some of his father’s earliest carvings. Only the floor and one item on it remained untouched. The item, gray wood twisted, looked like some contraption.
“The temple’s perch?” 
“We will stay away from it,” Rennly said. “We’ll have our match under the red carpet since we’ll not likely have the chance again.”
Tomrin followed him. “True. Our first rematch will be our last.” 
“So confident you can beat me?” Rennly said and opened one of the carved panels and revealed a small closet. “And not afraid I will try to kill you?”
“The thing is, Rennly,” Tomrin said. “I have adjusted for you, but that doesn’t mean I am any less prepared to take a hit, if that is what it takes.”
Rennly made a face and frowned.
To Doom [?]
The sun streamed in across the temple from the western balcony. “I’m sure we can see about that,” Rennly said as he grabbed a pair of staves from inside a side drawer and walked over. He tried to hand Tormin one of the staves. 
Tomrin brandished his own myrrh walking stick. “I brought my own.”
Rennly scoffed. “That piece of junk?” He dropped the second staff and kicked it back towards the ramp. “Of course you were serious,” he muttered.
“I don’t know how much more serious I could be other than to tell you that if you ever do go see an old crone and live to tell tales about it afterwards and they are true, she is long gone,” Tomrin said as he stepped back and took up a stance. “As to the staff? It’s come in useful once or twice today already.”
Rennly raised an eyebrow. “Really?” He asked as his Orioles spots made him look like an animal of some kind
Tomrin nodded. “And win or lose, I’m leaving Menad.”
Rennly grinned. “You think you can just disappear now?” His grin turned to a snarl as he took a fighting stance.
“Not because of you,” Tomrin said. “But since I can’t make you see reason, let’s begin.” Tomin shifted his weight on his staff as he pretended to glance towards the light of the setting sun. “But before we do, you should know that even if you do win, it will change nothing between you and me because I am leaving either way.” 
Rennly pointed to his golden feather badge. “You can’t leave without my say-so.”
Tormin stared into Rennly’s straight green eyes. He saw his brother now more clearly now than he ever had before. “You stole Gaz away from me simply because he was my friend?” Tomrin said and hobbled with his stick as he play-danced to give himself better striking room.
“Friend?” Rennly scoffed. “You don’t know anything do you?”
Tomrin saluted with his walking stick. “I know so little it hurts some times?” 
Rennly did not return the salute. He attacked, swinging his staff. Tomrin parried. Clatch. He countered with his stick. 
His stick was shorter but he could strike at Rennly like an asp with it. 
Rennly grunned. He could see it now too. The pain helped his eyes tell the story as he made several feints. “You’re fast,” he said, “But I am stronger.” 
“But you are only going to wear yourself out,” Tomrin said. 
Rennly swung down with his staff. “I’m not fat like you.”
He caught another swing as he danced out of its way. The crook cradled Rennly’s staff and the walking stick struck Rennly in the chest, a tap with the tip of the haft, the second point. 
“Fat? Can’t you see it, Rennly?” Tomrin asked.
Rennly extracted his staff. “See what?”
“If that is all the better you have, you should forfeit now,” he said and feinted. “We’re not children anymore.”
 Rennly fell for the move and swung high to block, hoping to make a counter to Tomrin’s. Tomrin ducked under the swing and with the crook of his stick snagged Rennly leg. Rennly’s unblocked swing had overbalanced him. Now the leg held all his weight. Tomrin tugged sharply with his walking stick. Rennly fell to the deck. Whump. 
“Maybe,” Rennly said as he scrambled back to his feet. “But it seems we still play dirty.”
“Dirty?” Tomrin laughed. “I think that still counts as the third point.” 
Rennly jabbed his staff at Tormin. 
Using his stick to sidestep, Tomrin pushed the staff to the side and struck an easy fourth point, then struck a fifth when Rennly feinted and Tomrin counter-feinted while pretending to attempt a counter. Instead he blocked Rennly low swipe then struck Rennly’s foreleg. That scored a grunt and Tomrin let Rennly rub his leg.
“Maybe you have some finesse,” Rennly said when he balanced his staff on one open palm. “More than me, but I have power.”
“Finess always beats power,” Tomrin said.
“We will see about that.”
Rennly’s next few swings lost all elegance, all veneer of skill or refinement. Clack! Clack! Clack! The blows sent vibrations and numbness into Tomrin’s hands and Tomrin feared his walking stick would break from the blows as Rennly’s own had in their prior bout. “It’s like you are trying to hammer in nails with a sledge. This is supposed to be a civilized fight or not?” he asked his brother.
“I’m not the one who grew up with animals,” Rennly said as he took a breath and paused his wild attacks. During them Tomrin had only been able to score a single additional point. Six. He was beginning to feel tired now and hoped Rennly was too, but doubted his step-brother had woken up exhausted. 
Running across the fields, and walking so many miles out of the Lower Steppes had drained him, and he spoke to gain a second more breathing room. “You have yet to score a single point, and I only need four to win, to turn you into a skunk.”
“Skunk me? That must be a pleasant thought for you,” Rennly said. “Pleasant for you.”
Tomrin struck him again.
The Count [?]
A massive swing came low at Tomrin’s right leg. Rennly had let the previous point through on purpose, a trade.
“Ah-hah!” Tormin groaned.
“That is going to leave a bruise.” Rennly ginned.
Not holding back his walking stick, Tomrin won the next two points. Cratch, fwap. The myrrh rod struck like a whip. When Rennly tried to repeat his trick from the fair, Tormin was ready for it. After the first counter he whirled out and stuck Rennly low in the ankle. 
“You learn fast,” Rennly said. “But will that be enough?”
“I’m winning fast enough.” 
“But you are getting slower,” Rennly said. “I’m not even breaking a sweat.” It was true. Sweat welled out of Tomrin. It burned his chest where it soaked into his cauterized wound. “And while you’ve been practicing, do you think I have been just goofing off?” Rennly said and again changed his style, his stance looser, not meant for raw power. His staff was like a chameleon’s tongue striking out at a fly. Clack, clack. Clack, clack. Tomrin fell back from the barrage. 
Rennly’s staff was inherently longer and he had newfound speed. 
He might actually win.
Descent into Madness [?]
Circling, they moved away from the hidden closet, spiraling outwards.
The sweat running from his forehead, he dared not brush away. He had no time. Tomrin dared not even blink. The clack of staves continued as they circled and Rennly swatted at him. I just need to bide my time. The sun was low enough to stab Rennly in the eye. I just need that second. A heartbeat of distraction would be all it would offer. Tormin committed to taking advantage of it. It was dirty, but he had come to win, and Rennly didn’t seem to tire even as he swung again and again. High, low, it didn’t matter; his step-brother was in far better shape. 
It seemed like forever, like the sun would set first, but finally Rennly squinted. Tomrin’s feint was already trafficking through the air, stabbing at him, another trick to disjar Rennly from his barrage of snappy attacks. 
Rennly jerked his head, and Tomrin shifted on his feet, and rapped his step-brother on his shoulder. “Seven, one,” Tomrin said.
“The sun was in my eye,” Rennly squawked. “That’s the only reason.” He swung.
“Oh, was it?” Tomrin asked as he blocked a jarring blow. “That’s part of the game,” he added. Part of the game—same as Rennly’s shifting style was part of the game. His brother was back to power. Tomrin scored another hit, but pulled the strike. Control was important as well. He didn’t want to feel the massive strike Rennly’s blows promised if he lost that. “Just three more now,” Tomrin said, quickly removing sweat from his brow, taking advantage of the lull.
The sunset and dusk offered a quick advantage. Rennly wasn’t even trying to win anymore. He was just going through the notions. A quick jab after blocking one of Rennly’s wild body swings and the score tipped, nine to one. 
“It’s too bad this is our final bout,” Rennly said as their eyes finally adjusted. “I … just… don’t want it to end.”
A Sun Sets [?]
The twilight seemed to drag for hours, but somewhere within it Tomrin landed the penultimate strike. 
Ten-one. A strange feeling of floating washed over him again, if not quite so strongly. He was about to win. He could taste it. Winning would mean this charade would be over. 
“It was a good fight,” Tomrin said magnanimously. “Invigorating,” he added. Exhaustion tugged at his arms and legs. “After today he was going to sleep for a week. If I never wake up again I will be happy. After today,” he said. “We both can live free of whatever it was between us. We can live now as our own men, not as Dable Ocrin’s misbegotten sons.” 
“Rousing speech,” Rennly said and lashed out.
Tomrin caught it with his staff and locked it. “I think you see now part of the reason for my confidence.”
“Are you trying to patronize me?” Renny said, pulling back with a force to unlock their weapons, whirling and swinging madly. Swoosh. 
Tomrin ducke. “Not at all. I am just trying to say how nice it will be once things go back to normal.” 
“Normal?” Rennly said. Clack, clack. 
“Yes,” Tomrin said. “Life under the contract gives you plenty of the Glasswork’s profits.” 
The twilight faded and their weapons became silhouettes. 
“Sure,” Rennly said and feinted. He’d done a similar move before in their bout at the fair. 
Tomrin pretended to be taken in and dropped to his knees before the return. He swung his walking stick across Rennly’s thigh. Fap. Eleven, one. He breached the ten point spread. “Now, I must go.”
“And where do you have to be off to soo fast?” Rennly asked. “In such a rush?”
“Here it comes,” Tomrin muttered to himself. “Places,” he said, resting his body on his staff. He could hardly say he needed to catch up with Margretta and Marten so they could run off because he had the Leviathan’s Mark on his chest. 
“What places?”
“New places.”
Chapter 27: The Horror of Fire
	If the gods are so powerful why have they not found me yet?
		—Words scrawled in the margins of Cologon’s Living Book of the Dead

Thought ceased and Tomrin moved by reflex as his muscles tensed themselves and his fingers squeezed his walking stick as he moved it up to block Rennly’s swinging staff. His wrists were at a poor angle as the staff slid through the air, a gray form barely visible. Cathawuck! The walking stick blocked the staff but not completely, not enough to stall it completely before striking Tomrin across the cheek and ear. White points of jagged light filled his vision. “Feathers,” he swore and his instincts took over.
Suddenly, he found himself on his feet, pushing his walking stick so as to catch another of Rennly’s swings. “So this was you all along?” 
“Me?” Rennly spat. 
He began raining down towering blows, pushing Tomrin back. The vibrations of the walking stick rattled all the way down his arms up to his teeth. In the darkness each wild swing was a potential break or fracture. His walking stick couldn’t hold up much longer. The world, more particularly Rennly, had gone mad. His ears buzzed and his cheek burned as if stabbed with red hot glass where that staff hit him.
“Stop this Rennly! Relent and I will let you live!” he shouted as he began to circle. Clack. Clack. He focused on parrying. He couldn’t see for a tinker’s tail feathers.” The balcony, he thought, and fell back under Rennly’s blows, dragging Rennly with him. 
“Let me live?” Rennly laughed. “If this has to be our last fight,” he said as he swung across his body. “Let’s make it a good one!” 
“You aren’t even listening to me!” Tomrin shouted at him. “You’ve had your chance for a good fight.” His feet stepped into a pool of starlight. He backed out into the balcony, into the full light of night.
Rennly followed Tomrin out onto the balcony. “I bet now you wished you had availed yourself of the staff I offered you.” Behind Rennly Tomrin could see the Perch. It seemed for a second that his brother had sat up on it and that he just dismounted it from the shadow as he entered the balcony. “Your problem,” Rennly continued. “You think you are the best at everything.”
Tomrin held his tongue and they circled the balcony until over the low railing the Hall of Victory gleamed against the valley. The houses slept and the stores waited for the morning. 
Rennly swung again and again like a lunatic, crashing his staff against Tomrin’s walking stick, trying to break it as if it were inferior.
The Long Tradition [?]
“There it is!” Rennly said in their weapon clatter. “Everything comes too easy for you Peregrine.”
“Easy?” Tomrin asked, surprised by the verbal attack. “I worked hard to become good at glass. What have you done? Martial training? And yet you can’t even beat me at a child’s game of sticks.”
“Easy for you, a Peregrine slave boy! No responsibility.”
“I’m here aren’t I,” Tomrin growled.
“Look at you! Always judging. It’s easy for you to look good. As a Peregrin half-breed no one expects anything of you. You dress yourself, show up to work, and do half a day’s work, then go home and people sing your praises. I have to work for everything I have! Everyone expects more of me!”
A swing went through and struck Tomrin in the belly, but he had tensed. He swung himself and connected his walking stick with Rennly’s face. The blow stunned his half-brother, and he blinked in confusion. 
“You are envious of me,” Tomrin said. “Aren’t you?”
“What?” Rennly demanded. “No, I am not.”
“If you could see your face right when I struck you, you would know that you should stop,” Tomrin said. “We’ve both had our fun—we’ve each had our say. So now we are done playing. I have to go.”
Rennly lunged at him.
Tomrin side stepped but his foot caught on the railing as he tried to move. Rennly, changed directions and plowed into him, his staff across his body. He pushed the staff and himself onto Tomrin’s chest and onto the crook of the walking stick, trapping it. 
A flood of vertigo washed over Tomrin as Rennly pushed him back over the railing. “It is a long way down,” Rennly sneered. “When you measure yourself against me, now don’t you think?”
Jerking his head as if to headbutt Rennly, Tomrin looped both his feet around the pillars of the railing, the only reason Rennly couldn’t push him completely over to fall on the sun baked ground below. “I don’t measure myself against you, Rennly. I don’t even think about you.”
“I think about you all the time!” Rennly roared.
“Why?” Tomrin said. His back was going to break, arched as it was—or the railing would. “It’s been years since you left Woolad.”
A Spirit of Time [?]
Rennly roared. “Ahieeaahh!” 
It was some terrible beastal roar of an animal in pain, wild and mindless. As he roared, his muscles shook and Tomrin could feel the raw power in his half-brother coming to the fore, but behind him—what seemed like a phantom appeared—holding the discarded staff. 
She walked out into the moonlight, her naked feet padding drunkenly on the wooden balcony decking. Tomrin felt befuddled. He knew the girl. The judge’s daughter’s dress was ripped. “Elisah,” he breathed her name as it finally came to mind. She looked wild-eyed, like she had seen a ghost, even as she seemed a ghost herself. Tomrin couldn’t understand. What was she doing here? Of all people, and her looking like this waif?
She screamed and swung the staff in her hands. “Yuah!” It was an artless swing but the staff clobbered Rennly on the back of the head. Cluth. The blow stunned him a second.
Tomrin pushed off the railing, and Rennly stumbled backwards. His brother spun, and with the back of his hand he slapped Elisah hard across the face. She tumbled and hit her head hard on the wooden decking with her fall. Krath.
Free of Rennly’s weight Tomrin swung his walking stick at Rennly while he was turned away, striking him in the neck with the crook. Rennly staggered and dropped his staff. “What the feathers are you doing, man?” Tomrin asked him. “She’s a girl.” 
Rennly’s hand went to his neck where Tomrin had hit him. It was the same spot where the new Daughter had scratched him. Rennly spoke but Tomrin understood in a flash without needing to hear it from Rennly. “She’s nothing but an unclean gossling awaiting transport.”
“I know her,” Tomrin said. “She’s a good person.” He stepped forward, but Rennly blocked his way. Tomrin raised his walking stick. “Get out of my way, Rennly. I think you killed her.” 
“That would be no great loss.The gods have plenty of Daughters,” Rennly said. “But you aren’t even supposed to be here. Let alone see her.”
“What did you do to her?” Tomrin asked. “She looks afright.” 
“I didn’t don’t do anything,” Rennly said. 
“That’s a lie. Her dress didn’t rip itself!” 
Revelations [?]
“If you must know...” Rennly said, waving his hands. He didn’t complete his sentence but leaped at Tomrin. He moved so quickly Tomrin didn’t have time to stop him. Rennly’s left hand grabbed Tormin by the nape of the shirt and spun him as his shirt ripped open. Rennly’s eyes went wide. “What the feathers?” Rennly let go and grabbed at the baton at his hip.
