Tomrin swatted his wrist, preemptively blocking Rennly’s attempt to use the device and it clattered away. “Why did you do that?” Tomrin demanded, stating stupidly as his own chest.
“You!” Rennly shouted, pointing at Tomrin’s scales. His eyes met Tomrin’s. “You! You are him.” 
“Yes, I’m him,” Tomrin said. “And now don’t you see what a fool you’ve been trying to kill me. You can’t kill me. Only I can me. And you. But I don’t have to. You could come with me.” 
“Come with you?” Rennly asked. “You came here for the girl?” 
“No, I came for our contract,” Tomrin said. 
Rennly stepped back and eyed the girl. “I need you to drop your staff.”
It was as if sudden sanity had overtaken his madness and Tomrin laughed. “Me? I’m not going to drop my walking stick. Think, Rennly. Think! How you’ve been acting. You aren’t thinking straight.” 
“I’m thinking straight now,” Rennly said, swallowing. “I’m interdicting you.” 
“Hear yourself, Rennly,’’ Tomrin said. “I have set fires. I have banished my family to the desert. I have come here through imprisonment. I have stared demons in the face. Do you think I am going to submit to you? Not even with a please and thank you?”
“Demons?” Rennly asked, his cold green eyes glancing around warily.
“Now sadly you have to come with us.”
“Us?” Rennly asked. “You are with her, now?”
“I can’t let them have her, not now.” Rennly reached for his sheathed knife.
“Don’t do it, Rennly!” Tomrin pointed his walking stick at his step-brother. “Don’t make me kill you.”
“Kill me?” Rennly said, pausing his hand. “You kill me and you kill Godly Glassworks.” 
“No, it will survive your death. If I kill you, you’ll be dead. Everyone will assume something happened. An accident, perhaps.” 
“They might, sure,” Rennly replied. “But don’t think I’m so foolish. I remade my will while you were off training. If anything happens to me now, the glass shop will be censored.” 
“No...” 
“Ah! You see it. This is the best! Kill me and kill your darling glass shop. So now you have to submit and undergo purification if that’s really is all you care about.” Rennly laughed and Tomrin shook his walking stick at him. The Luster, however, evaded him. 
Even if he wanted to, Tomrin could not make good his threat. 
Final Partings [?]
“Fine, Rennly, you win,” Tomrin said. “And yet...?”
“Of course I win,” Rennly said. “And yet. I still have yet to kill the girl.”
“What?” 
 Rennly pulled out his knife and stepped over the sleeping form of Elisah. Her chest rose and fell with her breathing. Rennly knelt down over her.
“No!” Tomrin roared as he brought back up his weapon. A fury gripped him—and a calm. He was willing to sacrifice himself for the glass shop, but why this girl? “Why her?” Tomrin demanded as Rennly looked at him. 
“Think of it as your first step,” Rennly said. 
“You would never get away with killing a Daughter.”
“I always get away with everything,” Rennly said. “It’s my charm. And she’s not a Daughter yet.”
“Rennly—brother, think,” Tomrin said, his anger returning.
Rennly laughed. “What is that even supposed to mean?”
“You can’t kill her and get away with it.” 
“Of course I can.”
A sinking lightness filled Tomrin’s stomach as the Luster called to him. “What have you done?” Tomrin asked. “You haven’t rigged it have you?”
“Rigged?” Rennly asked. “No. I am not your dead boss.”
No, Rennly [?]
“You think I killed your father so you want to kill me,” Tomrin said.
Rennly sat back on his heels. The knife lowered but also dropped to the side of Elysah’s throat. “I don’t think about that. I was the who killed Davot.”
“Why?”
Rennly smiled. “When I learned he wasn’t my real father.”
“You?” Tomrin asked. “I knew you were insane. But? I told Hadras. Or do you think I am stupid? We have other brothers or did you forget that?”
“Like Gaz?”
“Gaz?”
“He had our father’s eyes,” Rennly said. “As do all his children.”
Fast [?]
Tomrin saw it now clearly. Rennly was a scorpion. His knife was his stinger and he loomed now over Elisah’s limp body confident in his prey like the animal he was killing was to him. The thought of having to stop him, to lower himself to a level of beast, made Tomrin feel sick. “I think I am going to be sick, Rennly, if you move another inch,” he said. “My parents know what I am. Emy knows. Hadras knows. They know who and what I am. They could not keep me prisoner, as pious as they are. I left them the desert overawed with the fire from the Wellspring of my own damnation. Think! Around me now, you can can kill no more men, women, or children, not this girl, not now, not ever. No more so than I am a prisoner either. I don’t care what righteous god you serve, I am going now with the girl, one choice remains and that is to go with me. So this is your last warning.”
“Warning?” Rennly raised the knife to throw.
“Toss the knife off to the side, Rennly,” Tomrin said, his voice cleared of all emotion. The Luster was as nothing. It spoke for Rennly and the Rennly’s of the world. Tomrin raised his own voice to be heard in its face. “I will not say it again.” 
Rennly wavered. “What are you saying?” 
Tomrin dissolved and the Corvine Man resolved to end it. “I will not found shops and houses.” His words became a tumble. “Don’t you see it, brother? Come!” He reached out his other hand to Rennly. Join with me now and live; cross into history with me.”
“Join you? Never,” Rennly sneered and arched back his wrist with the knife as he stood. “I will see you dead first! And Seasargus will reward me.” 
He winged the knife.
The Will [?]
The Corvine Man was casting. Willvane wrapped the shadows and the ice. The three powers vanished, leaving sunfire, burning through his veins. He cast the fiery ambrosia out by pushing it into the walking stick. As he pointed with one hand, holding it by the double crook, the length of the stick shot brightness on Rennly’s chest.
As the knife spun through the air, Tomrin pulled back his other hand as hard as he could, jerking as if his hand were on fire with deadly flames reaching for him and every point as wicked fire filled the walking stick, and so much more still left over inside Tomrin and the stick, all yearning to be free. Fire bloomed in all directions down the cracks of the bark, then a fire bolt shot down the length of the gnarled branch and burst out the butt in a swirl of yellow and gold. The knife glinted and was engulfed in catastrophic fire which burned and roared like a lit glassworking torch, and like a fist of swirling flames it punching Rennly in the chest, carrying the knife with it. Frawh! 
The excess fire in the air pounced on him and pushed him backwards and frothed, splashing on the wooden floor near Elisah. The blade in Rennly’s chest, stabbed inside the wound as it burned a shocked expression onto his face. 
Then it was only cruel flames which lit up his gastly face, not life.
Fire [?]
Tomrin tossed the remnants of the flaming stick in his hand aside and rushed over where Rennly teetered on his knees over Elisha. Tomrin pushed him backwards to keep him from spreading flames to the girl. “I can’t believe... Elisah Reydal.”
Ridding himself of the rest of the sunfire, he pushed it into Rennly, and with strength of will alone, he held the fire inside his dead stepbrother’s body as he grabbed Elisah by the wrists and dragged her off the balcony into the shadows.
Once her hands slipped through his, Tomrin fell to his knees. He was using all his strength to hold the fire in, and the farther he was from Rennly the harder it became. The fire was beginning to leach out. Tomrin stared into the Wellspring in horror. He’d torn his ward, but he had no time for that. The fire was leaking into the air but also into the wooden balcony’s decking. Fire now soaked into the wooden planks, and crawled up the pillars of the railing and filled them and then skittered under him and Elisah and down into the beams holding up the temple roof. It would burst free and the tower would become an inferno. 
Tomrin pushed his arms under Elisah’s body and legs and picked her up and walked into the darkness as flames began to crackle out of the balcony. The smell of burning chased them into the shadows which flickered like sirens on a distant shore on the twisting wall, a mirage. 
Heat was in the air; too much ambrosia, and all control was fading.
The sunfire began to run ahead of him as the dead weight in his arms rolled back her head. And Looked up and him. “Ahh!”
“Ahh!” he replied and she rolled her drunken head back over.
Everything was coming apart. By the time he made it to the ramps the planks ahead were filled with fire he was holding down with will alone as his arms and legs did the work
Robles did the same for Master Laz…
And still not save him!
The Holies [?]
Elisah finally opened her eyes and looked at him more than a second as he marched awkwardly down the ramp with her.
Behind him the fire roared. He had maybe two levels left to go.
The walls themselves were smoking now and tiny flames came alive as Elisah spoke. “Tomrin Ocrin?” 
“Can you walk,” he asked her, but her head rolled back again. 
You need to let her go, a voice inside his head told him. She was too heavy. His steps were too slow. Like this, they were both going to die. Save yourself! The smoke now choked his breath. Tomrin had heard this voice before, the first time when he had tried to save Rennly’s father. That time he had obeyed. Now he yelled at it. “No! Not her too!” He stumbled on through the blinding smoke and flames. Not her! Not her! Over and over he said it as he descended through the fire. At last he reached the stone of the temple proper. His breathing turned slowed in the cool air. He let himself drop down to his knees and laid Elisah out on the flat stones. 
“Not her?” she asked, opening her eyes once more. Tomrin realized he was still repeating his mantra.
“Nothing,” he said. “You’re alive.” 
Elisah flopped her arms and let them lie on the stone. The fire burned in the rafters above them. “My head hurts,” she said.
“But can you walk?” Tomrin asked. “This place is coming down.” 
“I don’t know,” she said.
Tomrin helped her to her feet and she leaned on him as they moved down the stone ramp to the ground floor. They exited the back into the front of the temple. While it was lit by lamps in the back, here the stars and moonlght shone through the cut glass windows. 
“I need a rest,” Elisah said and Tomrin sat her in the pews facing the altar.
“Stay here,” he said. He hurried to the front door. It was still locked. He pulled the handle, but it was futile. The large door budged not an inch. Tomrin hurried back down the aisle. “It’s locked. I’m going to go try the others.”
“I’m coming with you,” Elisah said, standing. She wobbled like she had been drinking high spirit tarsk all day. Tomrin put out a hand to catch her. She pulled back and nearly fell. “I’m fine. Let’s go.” She held her torn dress while they walked past the altar into the temple holies. 
Final Escape [?]
The fire pulled air in from the cracks like a chimney fire. As soon as they opened any door the temple would burn and the wood walls would collapse and fall into the stones and burn like a fire pit, if the trestling didn’t collapse first. Elisah seemed apprehensive at the howl of the wind through the first door which grew louder as they approached. “It’s just the stove wind,” Tomrin said. 
“I’m not afraid of banshees,” Elisah replied. 
Tomrin thought of Margretta—and her threat to kill him. “Me either,” he said. The wind squalled through the crack of the door, but it was locked and Tomrin felt certain Rennly had the key. “Too late now to go back and get the key from my brother.” 
“He’s your brother?” 
“Half-brother,” Tomrin said, “It’s a long story. Don’t worry, I’m nothing like him. He did all of this?” he added motioning to her torn dress. 
“Maybe we can reminisce when we are out of here, I think the temple is about to collapse.” She didn’t look like the girl he’d just saved from death, a death his carelessness made possible. She seemed willing to take charge. “All the other doors will be locked. I thought you knew this one was open.” 
“Well—ah,” Tomrin stammered. “I had hoped.” 
“Great, so we burn to death?” she said. “You brother was crazy setting the temple on fire. Probably the worst censor in history.”
“Actually, I, uh...”
“What?”
“I was the one who set the temple on fire. It was an accident,” he added when she gave him a withering look. 
“Fires this large aren’t accidents,” Elisah said. “Are you an arsonist or something?”
Tomrin reflected on the wind break he had burned, acres of trees destroyed in one afternoon. “No,” he replied. “I’m not setting fires for fun.” 
“So you didn’t get his key before you started all this?”
“No. I didn’t”
She frowned. “So now what?” 
The Last Door [?]
“Well, I guess—step back,” Tomrin said. He put his hand on the wooden door. It wasn’t like his walking stick and he figured this was going to hurt. He would need to be careful. 
Elisah took a step back and Tomrin tried to feel the Luster. He closed his eyes.
“What are you doing?” she asked him. 
Tomrin squeezed tight his eyes. “Rah!” he growled, but the Luster evaded him. He pulled back from the door. “You try it.” 
“Try it?” Elisah asked. “Try what?” 
“Aren’t you a Daughter?” Tomrin said. “Can’t you see the ambrosia in the Wellspring?” 
“Ambrosia? Well, yes, no, but…” Elisah said. “What does that have to do with anything?” 
“Use the sunfire, or ice, I don’t know. It’s easier for a girl.” 
Elisah pulled herself up. “I am a woman.” 
“Just try it,” Tomrin said, not knowing if he should apologize. Talking to her was like standing on an anthill.
She walked to the door. “Sunfire?” 
“Yes,” Tomrin replied. “Just pull it in. You can see the four ambrosia?” 
“I can’t see anything,” She pulled back from the door. 
“You are a Daughter?” 
“They gave me something. I can’t remember. My mind is fuzzy.” 
“Really? You didn’t even try.” 
“You don’t know anything,” Elisah snapped. “Now, you got me killed.” 
“I saved you,” Tomrin said, growing angry. He was afraid now that he and she would die here after all. “Stand back,” he commanded her as the Luster gripped him. 
A bit of wood would not stand between him and life and freedom. No, he was the Corvine Man, the bugbear of legend, the cloud to blight the sun. He was both the monster and the myth, heaven’s destruction. He was the end to the Serath Dominion, and when he drank a sip of ambrosia, it was the tiniest of tiniest. Arsonist!
The willvane swiftly tangled with him like a thing alive, an animal with its back to the wall. He wrestled with it as he held his hand to the door. Sunfire burned within him promising untold glories. The glacier ice froze him to the quick while the darklight rotted all, corroded all—and it was magnificent! He had nothing left to lose but life, hope, and love. Almost nothing at all!
He wove the willvane, netting and capturing the glacier ice and darklight in its web. The sunfire he saved for last as it threatened to boil him down to a charcoaled corpse. He thrust out his other hand and pushed the fire into the planks of the door, burning his palms as the fire scorched out of him. “Aahh!” The pain was nothing but a sharp light in his head. He pushed away and closed himself off to the ambrosia as the Luster fled. 
“Get back! Get back!” he shouted, running as he held the raw sunfire inside the wood of the door by strength of will. 
“Why?” Eisah demanded as he grabbed her and pulled her down the passage. She pulled free of him and turned to stare at the door. It only looked different if you could see the change in the ambrosia. “Nothing is happening,” she said. 
Her words crumbled Tomrin’s control over the sunfire. The door exploded in a rain of flame and wood. Skrootch!
Air rushed into the passage and the stove wind blew bits of flaming char at them. Tomrin shielded his eyes with this arm. The fire was normal, hot, but natural. “We are free,” he said, turning to Elisah.
She seemed agast what she saw. “Who are you, Tomrin Ocrin?” she asked, her voice a horrified whisper. 
“I don’t know,” he replied softly. A splinter on the floor burned the carpet near his left foot. He gently tapped the flame out with a toe. “Yet sometimes I fear I might be the Lightshy Crow.”
Chapter 28: From the Ashes
	I died long ago when I realized I never truly lived.
		—From Cologon’s Living Book of the Dead

Elisah touched his shoulder and Tomrin turned as she pulled him around to face her. Her eyes, however, were not on his face. She stared instead at the scales on his chest, seeing them for the first time. Her shock and surprise lit up her face at what she saw. The burn still stung from the night before and the tiny regrowing scales glinted red. How she had missed the Mark before was forgivable in his mind. The fire above was still burning. “Hotter now than ever!” Tomrin said as the stove winds rushed by, bellows worth, like bottled air he used at the glass shop. “We need to get out of here before the dressing falls in and traps us inside a sea of flame.”
Elisah voiced something that sounded like a question, but Tomrin could not hear it over the fire’s quickening roar. The winds were picking up. She put a freezing finger on the ring of the scales. It tickled something fierce, like a knife and feather. 
He grabbed her arm by the wrist. “We need to run for it!” he yelled. The bits of burning door were burning sideways at them from the inrushing wind. “Follow me!” But he didn’t give her any choice; he kept hold of her and dragged her towards the open night. Cool air rushed by, waiting for them outside. 
Tomrin kicked the burning splinters of what was left of the door out of the way. The sunfire was gone, but the heat of its passage had shattered the door to burning pieces. A dark shape appeared outside. For a second Tomin’s heart raced. Censors. Tomrin recognized the man, however, and the woman who had come with him.
“Tomrin Ocrin,” Marten said.
“Marten,” Tomrin said as he exited with Elisah in tow. “Margretta.” The woman wore a sour expression on her face. “I believe you both know the judge’s daughter, Elisah Reydal,” he added.
“We thought maybe she was the one,” Marten said gravely. He sheathed the black triangular flange of metal he held, sending it to his hip. His great black riding cloak he unhooked as he stepped forward. He swung the sable swath over Elisah. “It’s unfortunate to see you again like this, Recorder Reydal.” 
“Thank you, Counselor,” Elisah replied, wrapping herself like a caterpillar in a cocoon.   Margretta scowled as she looked Tomrin up and down. She moved to grab his ripped shirt, she held it over his Leviathan’s Mark a moment. “Let’s go,” she said, adding: “Was this your idea all along, Master Ocrin?” 
“No,” Tomrin replied. Behind him the section of the temple crashed in with a wrenching sound. Fratchp. He could hear glass breaking, or thought he could. “Rennly—things got out of control—he’s dead.” 
“Have you thought about how you might look in the Wellspring?” Margretta asked coldly. 
“Yes,” Tomrin replied, looking again but widening his view, not focusing only on the ambrosia churning around him to the wider world. To the south he saw two lights. They were still far away. “Daughters?” he said. 
“Two more on top of those already looking for you,” Margretta said. “Now they know you are right here.” She sighed. “But with the feathered temple on fire, they won’t even need to look will they?” 
“Counselor Marten? Lady Margretta?” Elisah said. “You’re—” 
“In a hurry?” Margretta snapped. “Yes, now you hold still,” she said to Tomrin. She put her hands on his shoulders. “I could give you further warnings, but I swear you’ll just do the opposite.” 
“Margretta, we need to go,” Marten said. “Remember, the crowd building out front?” 
“Yes, Marten,” Margretta replied as willvane entered her from the wildly maddened swirling of the four ambersia in the Spring. “Now—” She said but didn’t finish her thought but wove the willvane as she had before, filling Tomrin with the web. “And hippity-hop goes the horsey’s head.”
“What’s she doing?” Eisah asked, but Marten shushed her. “Not now, Recorder Reydal. She needs to concentrate.” 
Finally Margretta completed her spell dance and pulled back. Tomrin could feel the concentration and will she exerted to wrestle such an abundance of the feathery willvane under control welling off her, and though if he felt no different, his footprint in the ambrosia bubbled and disappeared.
“Me with the girl?” Marten said. “Meet at the horses?” 
“Yes,” Margretta replied. She was sweating and she slumped her shoulders as if exhausted by the weaving of the ward. “Someone will find out something happened here.” 
“The two...in the Wellspring?” Marten said.
Margretta raised her eyebrow at the question. “Call no attention to yourselves.” 
“Right,” Marten said. He led Elisah away around the curve of the temple and towards the front. 
“And you remember,” Margetta said to Tomrin. “You tore your night shirt getting out of bed. Now hold it shut.” 
Tomriin did as he was ordered and followed Margretta without prompting. 
Out In Front Of This [?]
In the front, the courtyard was filled with people. 
Lookie-loos always came out when there was a fire of any magnitude and he and Margretta seemed no different than any of them. The fire trucks arriving garnered all eyes. Even still, Tormin found himself holding his breath. He and Rennly had been out fighting on the balcony. Someone could have seen, yet no one stopped them. The fire trucks, however, stopped right in front of them. Margretta moved to push around as the firemen jumped down, the Fire Marshal with them. 
“Master Ocrin,” Tamerton said. “Will you look at this?” The man pointed disapprovingly at the burning Temple. “I don’t know what to make of it.” 
Tomrin stopped. “I know,” he said. To ignore the man would call more attention to himself. The man knew him. “I have no idea how the temple could catch fire.” 
“It’s wood,” the man said. “But here I have less jurisdiction. The Daughters should take care of it. I wonder if they are alright. I feel I need to do something.” 
Tomrin glanced at Margretta. She glared at him to say: “Let’s go. What are you doing?” 
Tomrin grimaced and turned back to the Rubish fireman. “Too bad my friend isn’t here. He’d go in. Me though? I think I’ve had my fill of fire,” he said. “But I better go. I can’t even bear to see it anymore. I don’t know what I was thinking. I should have just stayed in bed.” 
“Me too,” the Marshall mused. 
“Thing is Marshall,” Tomrin said. “If the temple is burning it’s because Daughters want it too. I personally wouldn’t get involved.” 
The Marshall frowned. “I suppose you are right. Thank you, Master Tomrin.” 
Tomrin walked away. Just how the man was in a position of leadership boggled his mind.
 He put the man aside and followed Margretta to the court’s yard and to the stables. They entered through a side door and met Marten and Elisah already inside.
A lamp hanging from the low rafter cast a dull orange glow on them. 
Life of Death [?]
Elisah looked angry. “I demanded to know what is going on!” she said as soon as Margretta opened the door. 
“Hush!” Margretta said sharply as she waved a hand at Tomrin. He shut the door. “We have no time to explain.” 
“I am a judge’s daughter,” Elisah said. “I know a kidnapping when I see it. I was kidnapped once already today. And Tomrin Ocrin, he can do magic, I saw it. Who are you people? Don’t tell me you are a Daughter, lady Margretta.” 
“Let me make this very easy for you, Elisah Reydal. I know very well who you and your father are. But maybe you don’t think I am a lady of title? Maybe I was not born one and had to earn it. Which I did by keeping my mouth shut and doing what had to be done, and by making the hard choices—choices like the one you are about to make now. Trust me and trust this seal—” Margretta pulled a card from her pocket. On it was stamped the image of a peach and some words Tomrin could not read, but he knew the card. “—trust and live.” Margretta handed it to Elisah.
Elisah read aloud. “The bearer of this bond speaks for me, Lord of the Crystal Throne, Champion of the East, and Emperor of Corundum, Jarvis Teabril. Do as they command, and you do as I command. There is a signature.” She squinted at it. 
Margretta snatched the card back and stuffed it into her dress through one of the hip folds into a hidden pocket. “Yes, that is the emperor’s signature,” she said. “Now are you sworn officer of the court here, or not?” Elisah nodded at Margretta. “Good. Later I can tell you more,” Margretta continued. “But for now, all you need to know is that the Emperor needs you to do as I tell you. Or you could choose your second option.” 
“Death?” Elisah asked in a whisper. One of the horses whickered in the silence. “But…”
A Spring Stone [?]
“Think,” Margretta said and pulled her hand from her pocket. 
A small stone sat in her palm. The ambrosia ever so stirred slightly around Tomrin and he realized it was just Margretta channeling sun fire into the stone. It glowed in the Wellspring with a thousand tiny steamers of fiery ambrosia. A ball of flames appeared in the stillness above them, casting light like the noonday sun. “I would make it painless, but you would still be dead.” 
“No,” Tomrin said. “You wouldn’t.” 
Marten put a heavy hand on Tomrin’s shoulder. “You give Elisah Reydal too little credit, Master Tomrin,” he said. “She is as wise as her father. She will choose correctly.” 
His words hung in a growing silence and the horses in their cramped stalls stood quiet.
King’s Gamble, the tallest of the three, glanced from person to person as Elisah considered her options. She looked as sober as her judge father, not the stumbling mess from before. She pulled Marten’s black greatcloak closer around herself and said, “I want to live—but under protest. I would bring this to the Emperor himself if I have to.” 
“Good. This way you may have that chance,” Margretta said.
“Then what?”
“We only have three horses,” Marten said. “That means you two will have to share.” He indicated Tomrin and Elisah. 
“I am not a very good horse man,” Tomrin admitted, sheepishly. The idea of sitting close to Elisah now even after carrying her embarrassed him. 
“It’s only until we find another horse,” Margretta said, letting go of the channel. “For now we need to get out of Menad. The place is going to be crawling with—trouble.” Tomrin could hear the brief stutter and was sure Elisah could hear it too. But here he dared not say a thing. Not in front of Margretta. Elisah deserved to know what she was getting into. But in truth he wasn’t sure himself. The day had turned into a nightmare, and it wasn’t over yet. 
Heading Out [?]
Marten helped Tomrin and Elisah up onto a gray razor called Misty. The riding coat he took back from Elisah as Margretta produced an overshirt for her. 
“You two will come after us. Stay Close,” Marten said.
The Orish man led his massive dun to the stable’s double doors and opened them and led out his charger. “Stay Gamble,” he said and then led out Queen’s Answer for Margretta. Then finally Misty. After which he disappeared back inside the stable door, closing it.
Margretta made a face but said nothing.
Finally he joined them from the side door. “Had to get the light.”
“Order, nothing out of place, look natural, “Margretta said to Tomrin and Elisah. “That’s how we stay alive. And yes, not talking to the fire Marshall.” 
“Is that what took you so long?” Marten asked. 
“He knew me—I had to stop.” 
“Then you did right,” Marten said.
Margretta grunted. “Let’s just go. We’ll all know if it was right, but later.”
Marten led the way. His massive dun was impossible to miss ahead of them or anyone watching. “So much for not looking conspicuous,” Tomrin said, turning his head to speak with Elisah. 
“Just watch where you’re going,” she said, not finding him funny, adding to the awkwardness. She had her arms around him and he could feel her pressed against him. He tried to ignore her but his body had responded. He seemed to lose attention even as he felt a stiffness in his neck. He snapped his head forward and made sure he followed closely on Margretta and her bay.
Her animal rushed ahead a dark gray splotch. 
They were just three shapes heading west. 
Then north, then west, then south. 
Marten was taking them on a circuitous route. 
Tomrin wondered if the man knew where he was going. If not he pretended he did. The streets he took were void of life and with quiet, empty shops lining them. The houses on the Maple were hidden by a line of shrubs. The man was avoiding the eyes of strangers. But there was an eerie feeling to the streets he chose. Maybe it was just paranoia, maybe Marten’s leaking into him.
Margretta must have felt it too. “What is it, Marten?” she called ahead. The man slowed his mount to a stop. Tomrin nudged Misty close to hear their words as they spoke. 
Marten was saying “….but it’s not a human, it’s an animal.” 
“Why would it be following us?” 
“I don’t know wild animals that well,” Marten shrugged. 
“What’s going on?” Tomrin asked. 
“It’s nothing,” Margretta said, “A dog maybe, probably hungry.” 
The look on Marten’s face did not say he believed it was just a dog, but he reined his dun about and led them on, continuing his random travel through the city of Menad, heading west by starts. Tomrin kept looking around for this wild dog supposedly shadowing them. He saw nothing, but maybe a pair of yellow eyes under a porch which disappeared as soon as he thought he saw them. 
Seeing things. It worried him. The night seemed unreal. He was leaving Menad. The glass shop was ruined. And these people he was with, he didn’t know and certainly couldn’t trust. He thought he could trust Elisah, but everytime he had her figured out, she changed. And Marten—Tomrin wanted to like the man, but now he was seeing things—wild animals in the shadows, or even a heaven hound. Or bloodbeasts. “Don’t even know where you’re going,” Tomrin muttered as they’d reached the western incline of the valley and started up.
What do you see? [?]
“What is it?” Elisah asked. 
 “What do you mean?” 
 “You’re breathing hard,” Elisah said. “Am I squeezing you too hard?” 
“No, It’s nothing,” Tomrin replied. “I never wanted to leave Menad.” 
“Me either,” Elisah squeezed him with her arms. “I can’t imagine. I’m sure it will be fine, now that we are going to see the Emperor.” 
Tomrin made a mollifying sound in his throat. “Yes, but, I don’t think that is where we are going.” 
“I keep forgetting you are not with them,” Elisah said. “Especially after what you said. And what you…are.” 
“I am a good person.” 
“But you killed your brother.” 
“And I’m sorry I did, but he made me do it.” 
“Made you?” Elisah shuddered. “But still, the temple.” She glanced off  to their left where it burned. Here and there were houses and they had come alive with people looking out over the valley towards the burning temple. The road here followed the curve of the valley and was lined with trees on both sides. Holding to the branches of one, a woman sobbed at the sight. A man, her husband most likely, held her. Fear gripped the man’s eyes. Talk of the Enfernii demons wasn’t just in whispers. Another man lamented that the gods had abandoned them. When people asked what they knew, Marten replied for all: “We know only as much as you, my friend.” 
Tomrin was relieved when they finally reached the level and the Menad valley was hidden by the trees. “You wanted to be a judge like your father?” Tomrin asked Elisah. 
“I was spending my free time in the Hall of victory.” 
“Never saw you there; but I stopped going.” 
“I think I can see the ambrosia now,” she said, suddenly distracted. “Faintly…”
At a break in the trees valley-side, Tomrin pulled Misty to a stop. This was the city which was his home. In the dark he could not see the glass shop nor resolve the Winged Helm Inn in the distance. 
Marten and Margretta returned to him. “Do you see it?” Marten said. 
“It?” asked Elisah. 
“The coyote?” Margretta said.
 “You saw a coyote?” Tomrin asked without looking away from them. 
 “It was large for one, but it wasn’t a wolf or a dog.” 
“It’s probably nothing,” Margretta added. 
“On the lower steppes there are stories of a coyote attacking large animals at night. Even horses and cows and oxen. A folk tale to frighten pilgrims,” Tomrin said. Here this Orish man wore one of their sacrificial knives and it made Tomrin wonder though Marten looked concerned...and maybe thoughtful. 
“Some tales have truth baked into them even if the facts are a jumble,” Margretta said. “But we stay here paralized by fear, or leave and hope we make it out alive.”
The Light in the Darkness [?]
“I don’t think anyone is afraid,” Marten said.
“I’m,” Tomrin said. “I’m afraid that the glass shop is totally ruined.” 
“Because of the contract?” Marten asked. 
“No,” Tomrin said. “I killed Rennly.” 
“Ah,” Marten said, raising his head slightly, and making a large looping, and knowing arc of a nod. “His will?” 
“Yes. He said he had it changed. Now the shop will be censored. It is Rennly attacking me from the grave. How did you know?” 
“Well, he wanted it done quickly, so he had done it cheaply.” 
“You?” 
“Yes,” Marten said with a grin to nearly rival Robles’ bests. “I wouldn’t worry.” 
“Why not?” Tomrin was in disbelief. “Tell me.” 
“Well, when you have something done cheaply, sometimes mistakes are made. I’m afraid its for his estate to never learn that certain aspects may not have been filed with the court at all and were instead sent to the burn bin.” 
“Freelancing again, Marten?” Margretta scolded.
Marten shrugged off her reproach. “You know me. Sometimes I make mistakes. That’s all.” 
“That still doesn’t fix the old money problem,” Tomrin said. 
“The three miserly men from Woolad?” 
Tomrin nodded, frowning. “They wanted Rennly and I to fight.” 
“I remember,” Marten said. “But now with you and Rennly gone what are they to think?”
“Exactly. Their money won’t come through. I feel like I’ve betrayed my friends at Godly Glassworks.” 
Marten pointed into the valley. “Look, the temple is on fire and it will have to be rebuilt. And you know what that means.” 
“It means more cut glass,” said Elisah brightly. 
“Yes, and who will be there to cut it? And dye it?” 
“Ahh,” Tomrin said, “But the contract?”
“When there is money to be made, men rarely stand on the forms,” Margretta said. 
“Trust in human greed. I’d almost be willing to bet my knife on it.” The weapon tricked into Marten’s hand. 
“Greed does seem eternal,” Margretta added.
“I don’t know,” Tomrin said, turning away from the flames of the temple. 
“I’m not saying trust men, just their greed,” Margretta said. “Now, let’s go.” Marten tricked home his knife then he and she reined their horses about. Tomrin stole one last glance at Menad before nudging Misty after them. 
“I can’t believe it,” Elisah said. 
“What?” Tomrin asked her. 
“After what you said—earlier, you are only worried about Menad and your glass shop.” 
“Is that so unbelievable? You saw that rock in Margretta’s hands. It was made of something like glass. We haven’t even begun to scratch the surface.” 
“It looked like a spell stone to me,” said Elisah. “What? You think you can make one?” 
“I’d like the chance to try. Who knows?” 
Tomrin contemplated as they rode on in silence. Glass and ambrosia, there seemed no limit to what he could achieve with a mastery of both. I’m well on my way. It was a pleasant thought. But he knew he had so much to learn. The last couple of days had taught him that. He breathed in the cool night air. It was free of smoke. The weight of worry about the glass shop lifted. 
“So ultimately, this fire is good for Menad,” Elisah said. “And your glass shop.” 
“I suppose,” Tomrin said. “But that of course all depends on how the gods take me burning down one of their temples.” 
“If you look straight ahead,” Elisah said to Tomrin. He took her drift to mean Marten and Magretta. “I wish I knew what was going on there,” she added. 
“Margretta and Marten have their own plans,” Tomrin said. “But at least there is hope for the glass shop. For now, that is good enough for me.”
Epilogue: How It Begins
	We hope to see more than just the stars through the trees.
		—From Notes, Digest One of the Society of Astronomers, 
  written by Head Astronomer Kurtus Jay. AtC 239

Headmaster Astronomer Pario pulled down his cap over his ears to cover them. The cap was one of his mothers. She knitted it when he was first elected to the College of Observers some forty years ago, and it helped some, yet the Altar’s observatory was always cold. Carved into a rock his gods called the Altar Mountain, the scopes were out and the platform in the cavernous observatory and stood open to the cold mountain air.
The rotating platform required power. It was a shame more of that heat could not be used to heat the platform. Women, Daughter of Heaven’s Glory, used their sunfire to provide the power. It was said life on Gearthrum was so much different than on Serathus, but this was the only home Pario knew.
He touched his cup of coffee on his desk. The ceramic cup was cold. He had drunk so little of it in the excitement of finding a new Daughter. The small temple in a small town on the edge of the wilderness had laid out the red carpet: New Daughter Here. 
Pario wanted a new cup of warm coffee, but his joints were beginning to feel stiffer by the day. Send Jemmen, he thought but dismissed the idea. He didn’t want anyone to know he was starting to wear down. He’d only been headmaster three years. His predecessor had lasted five. 
Head master Pario stood up and hugged his heavy purple robes around himself before picking up the mug of cold coffee. He heard the hiss of steam as one of his twenty junior astronomers shifted their scopes, scope five, Pario knew it by sound and location. 
Pario walked over to his Jemmen’s desk. They were the only two in the room who rode desks. Everyone else rode their respective scopes, each looking tiny up on their large brass tubes. “I’m going for more coffee, Jemmen,” Head master Astronomer Pario said. “You are in charge while I’m gone.” 
“A second cup?” his assistant asked. “This late?” You won’t sleep for your next shift.”
“I didn’t drink this one,” Pario replied. “The new Daughter sent coffee right out of my mind.” 
Down off the platform, Patio was certain Jemmen coveted his job as much as he had when he was Headmaster Astronomy Talvertey’s assistant. Probably hopes I will trip and fall off the ladder and break my neck. No one wasn’t supposed to have coffee up here, but there were perks to being the Headmaster. Certain rules were forgiven. Perhaps it was an incentive, or something more sinister? Holding the coffee left him only one hand for the rails. 
“Headmaster!” Tillus called out from scope five. “It’s Menad again. It looks like a fire.” 
“Fire?” 
“Yes, Headmaster. The whole temple seems to be on fire.” 
“That’s not possible!” The coffee cup nearly fell out of Pario’s hand. He was trembling, but not from the cold. 
“The Sisters of Mercy, Headmaster,” Jemmen called down to him. “It’s possible they—” 
Scope Five [?]
“Let me look!” Pario said.
He hurried up and over to his desk and set down his coffee before rushing over to scope five’s ladder and began climbing. “I can’t believe it. An attack? That hasn’t happened in years. No. An accident maybe?” 
“An attack fits the data,” Tillus said as Pario mounted the scope and placed his eye to the piece. His own heart pounded in his chest. The dial had read Menad! The giant moving platform for tracking the movements of earth never lied. Neither did the scopes. What Pario saw stole his breath away. 
The temple grounds were as they had seemed before, only now it was dark and they were lit with a huge fire burning, gutting the Temple. “Feathers,” he muttered. “Unholy feathers.” The temple wasn’t just one fire, it was gone, burnt completely up. Only the stones would remain. He pulled his eyes from the objective lens. His chest hurt. Pario wondered if this was how he died while riding a scope and a temple burning to the ground on Gearthrun.
“The data,” Pario said. “Is not ours to interpret.” But he couldn’t help shuddering. The job bringing the news down fell to him. He did not relish it. An attack. The gods and their Daughters would be incensed. Pario himself felt incensed, giving him, an old man, more work!
He trudged down the scope to the platform hoping he didn’t look like he was scurrying but he had been up and down too many times and seen others scurry too often to know he scurried. The temple in Menad was burning and he was scurrying down scope five for the second time this shift. If he didn’t know better he would say the scope had it in for his dignity. On the platform he straightened his robe and walked over to Jemmen. 
“I need to bring the news to the sacristy; you are in charge.” 
“So it is an attack?” 
“It is a fire. I know nothing more.” 
Jemmen raised his eyebrows at Pario, but nodded. “Yes, Headmaster.”
A Maze [?]
“Blooming flowers,” Pario half swore as he muttered to himself and headed for the ladder of the platform. If only he had gotten off the platform with his cup of coffee fast enough, then delivering this bad news would have fallen to Jemmen. Then he wouldn’t be so eager to replace me. But an attack did fit the data. 
A new Daughter had been found, then the temple where she was being held was burnt to the ground. Had Sisters of Mercy—what a cruel joke of a name—tried to capture the girl? It seemed possible. Pario had read the secret instructions first when he was made the assistant headmaster. It hadn’t told him much more than he and the astronomers suspected. Security was tight because Daughters were being kidnapped before they could be brought to Serathus and bound to the will of the Serath. 
Just last week the dark scope, the one used to monitor the Phoenix, revealed that the local star had been reborn. The last time that had happened had been just before the Leviathan had broken the Gaze Between the Worlds and tossed the Window into chaos. Coincidence maybe, yet Pario doubted these facts would be lost on the sacristy. 
“Please don’t let it be Seassarus still,” he prayed. Having to explain things to the red and black Serath was a trial, even the good news of the new Daughter had been met with skepticism and dismissed. The Golden Hearted was a cruel old bird for being one of the youngest. You are getting full of yourself, a voice in the head master’s head told him. Thinking ill of a god.
Muscles in his arms and legs vibrated nervously as he stepped off the ladder back to the solid earth the gods had named Serathus at the beginning of their dominion which he had learned started at the beginning of time. But Pario did not become the Headmaster Astronomer for the gods by accepting things so easily. Jemmen, Pario was certain had had similar thoughts even if they never talked of it. Such thoughts could be dangerous to speak aloud.
The halls outside the cavernous observatory were lit by alcove lamps. With the gradual slope the lighting slowly improved. The heart of the Altar Mountain hummed with the temple servants who worked and lived in the mountain and served the will of gods. The pinnacle of service was working the scopes in the observatory but at some point it seemed the servants had forgotten that. The lack of respect towards him and the other Astronomers was appalling. It was almost as if the gods and their cave dwelling helpers thought his job easy and didn’t take years upon years of study to master. 
Not a single person said hello as he headed for the sacristy; that was to be expected of the Serath and their Daughters, but not the maids and porters sweeping the halls and dusting the statue of the Serath in their marble alcoves. Or the lamp lightner and refuelers of the lamps and the hosts of cooks and launderers. Any idiot could do these jobs; only he could do his. And Jemmen.
Daughter Clooria stood guard at the door to the Holy Sacristy. Behind the large stone door bound with brass and on brass hinges, the gods prepared their Daughters for the binding. They would not be making that trip today. The Window between the worlds was closed. 
“You again, Pario.” 
“Yes, Daughter Clooria. Important news from Menad.” 
“Menad again? Haven’t you already brought the news from Menad? Going senile, Pario?” 
“It is Headmaster Astronomer Pario, Daughter Clooria. Fine minds like mine do not go senile.” 
“Then what is this other news from Menad, Headmaster Astronomer Pario?”
“I can’t say, not outside the sacristy. I’m afraid it is third-level secret, Daughter Clooria.”
“No it isn’t.”
“I shouldn’t even be telling you where my report is coming from, Daughter Clooria, now please see if they will admit me?” Pario took off his knitted blue hat and stared at the yellow moon and stars his mother had sewed into it.
The Sacristy [?]
“Wait here,” Daughter Clooria said testily and tapped on the door. It opened a crack and she slipped inside and the door closed. After several minutes it reopened and the woman stepped outside the sacristy. “They will see you.” She didn’t seem happy about it, but she showed him inside while she stayed without. 
The inside had not changed in the intervening hours. The lamps of burnished gold still lined the walls of gray worldwood paneling. The floor was worldwood and the carving on the walls were of the Serath and the World tree—not the one on Serathus. Pario had his own theory about that. 
Seasargus and one of his Daughters, Norina, stood waiting for him. The worldstone shined a bright golden light. The holy stone some claimed was the god of the gods. Others said it gave them eternal life. It was one of those mysteries Pario couldn’t himself quite penetrate. Anything could be the truth. Not too far from here the Heaven Hounds were kept, but he had never seen one. 
“Back again, Pario?” Seasargus’ Daughter, Norina said. “And with tales from Menad?” 
“Not tales, Daughter Norina,” Pario said, bowing before Seasargus. The god’s feathers were sharp looking and his large eyes had glossed over Pario as if he weren’t even there. His black claws were so deadly looking as any Sereth’s and Pario was just as happy not to have the High Lord’s attention. It was a good way of leaving the room dead. “I saw with my own eyes the unthinkable. The temple was burning.” 
“What part of the temple?”
“What part? The whole thing!”
“The Renegades,” Seasargus said, his feathers stirring. “They know the Phoenix Sun has been reborn? They are using it as a sign? Norina?” 
“How could they know, High Lord?” 
“How indeed, Norina?” Seasargus said, suddenly furious. “Tell me!”
“I’ll just let myself out,” Pario said quietly. 
“I don’t know,” Daughter Norina said as Pario closed the door escaping into the hallway. 
Pario heard Seasargus roar something that sounded like. “This is how it begins!” 
To Be Headmaster [?]
Then Daughter Norina screamed. 
Her loud scream cut short. Pario hurried down the hallway not even saying a word to daughter Clooria. Her wide eyes were on the door. Pario just wanted to get away. Old Norina was dead. 
Back in the Observatory, Pario climbed the platform and slumped into his chair. He stared into his hands, into the wrinkles. Jemmen walked over. 
“Nothing since you left, Headmaster. Scope five has continued it’s sweep.” 
Headmaster Astronomer Pario looked up. Concern lined Jemmen’s face. “You still want this job, Jemmen?” Pario asked softly. 
“What happened?” 
“Daughter Norina is dead.” 
“How did she die?” 
Pario slid his coffee mug across the desk towards Jemmen. “Get me hot cup and I’ll tell you.” Jemmen picked up the mug and Pario looked down at his desk. Unfinished reports and papers for the Society of Astronomers. Their work was legal on Serathus, but a deep current of heretical thought ran through the society. Members, members like himself, asked why was astronomy a taboo practice on Earth? Pario pushed his unfinished letter to the society under one of his unfinished reports. The Rebirth of the Phoenix Sun, an eyewitness account. It was for the Society. The letter was just a condensation of the account, a summary of just the juiciest bits. 
Jemmen returned with a steaming cup of coffee and set it down. He slid it forward and Pario picked it up and moved it closer to himself on the desk. “So what happened?” 
Pario told him everything, how he bypassed Clooria, how he found the sacristy, and how both Norina and Seasargus reacted to the news and to each other. And how he had run. And Clooria’s eyes. The fear. It was the same fear a servant might have for an angry Daughter. 
“And Seasargus, he said: ’This is how it begins?’” Jemmen asked. 
“He didn’t say it so much as shout it.” 
“What do you think it means?” 
“I don’t really know,” Pario replied. He had ideas but nothing that explained it. 
“A new Leviathan?” Jemmen asked, keeping his voice a whisper.
“A Leviathan from Menad?” Pario asked, “Seems preposterous, doesn’t it?” They had to look it up on the maps earlier. 
“But Seasargus didn’t think so?” 
“I suppose not,” said Pario. “But maybe his outburst was shock at one of the temples burning. I don’t think there is a single reference to a temple fire in the archives.” 
“I could find out,” Jemmen offered. 
“Do that,” Head master Astronomer Pario said. “I have a letter to write.” 
“And yes, Headmaster I would still like to be Headmaster one day.” 
“And when that day comes, Jemmen, I’m sure you’ll make a good one.” 
Jemmen padded off. 
“To My Family, Friends, and Colleagues” [?]
Pario pulled out a fresh sheet of paper, and addressed it to the Society of Astronomers. He wrote as the platform rotated with the movements of Seruthus and Gearthrun. The pages filled with words. Jemmen returned with a single sheet of paper in his hand. 
“Nothing, save for a reference in an Old High Orish book of apocrypha,” Jemmen said, handing over the sheet. On it, Old High Orish words were written using the standard characters. Under it was the translation. Jemmen explained, an archivist helped with it. The passage was in a precise hand. 
And so shall the Lamb set fire to the Swain,
And so shall the temple come down burning. 
As it was in the beginning, so shall it be in the end. 
“A book of apocrypha?”
“Yes, the archivist translated its name to something like: The Blind Crow.”
Pario put down the paper as he made a noise. “Uhahh!” He looked at Jemmen. 
“It’s nonsense,” he said, recovering his composure. “The Lamb and the Swain. That could mean anything.” 
“I know,” Jemmen replied. “I set the man to developing a more sophisticated translation. But I don’t need to tell you that the Leviathan was touched by the demons.” 
“A second coming of the demons,” Pario said. “Perhaps, that is what Seasargus meant.” 
“Perhaps,” Jemmen allowed. “I’ll let you get back to work.”
Pario’s pen scratched across the sheets like lightning filling them with his thoughts and remembered facts. He painted a picture with his words for those who could not spy the sun with their scopes. He wrote then reached the end, penning his conclusions and thanks. Then he retrieved the first sheet from the stack and turned it over to the front. All that was left was for him to title the letter. He wrote in the header. 
How It Begins [?]
The headmaster astronomer set down his pen, and reached for his coffee mug. He picked it up, but it was cold. He set it down, and realized he didn’t need coffee. He was warm and jittery from all the writing. He hadn’t written so much in one sitting before in his life. 
Jemmen came over and asked to read it. While he did, Pario watched his face as he reached certain points in the letter. When he finished, Pario asked, “What do you think?” 
“I’m not sure how the title fits in,” Jemmen said. “I mean, not without doing the same research we did.” 
“And only then if they fully appreciate the implications of the Phoenix’s rebirth,” Patio added.
“Ah, yes,” Jemmen said. “ I see. A hidden meaning. Clever.” 
“Is it?” Pario asked with a smile. “I thought it was just a catchy title.”
